Currency Trading:What BecameOf the DollarAs Safe Harbor?
Currency Trading: What Became Of the Dollar As Safe Harbor?
By Peter A. McKay
1,268 words
31 December 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
THE DOLLAR'S FALL from grace continues.
Once a haven for investors, the U.S. currency has slid throughout much of 2002 due to a mix of jitters over
the nation's economic recovery, the resulting flight of foreign investors from American stocks, and low
interest rates.
This month, the decline picked up steam, hitting three-year lows against the euro and the Swiss franc last
week, and a smaller decline against the yen. For the year, the dollar is down 17.6% to $1.0483 against the
euro, down 16.6% to 1.389 Swiss francs, and down 9.5% to 118.53 yen.
Now, instead of using the dollar for protection, investors are fleeing to other refuges like the Swiss franc and
gold, which is near a six-year high of $344.10.
Peter Schiff, for instance, several years ago began moving some of his own money and that of his clients into
Australian and New Zealand stocks and currency, gold mining stocks, Swiss francs, and other alternative
investments.
Now, Mr. Schiff's bearishness on the U.S. currency is in full bloom, and he says virtually none of his $1.5
million personal portfolio is in dollar-denominated assets, except for about $125,000 that he has offset with
hedges overseas.
That kind of skepticism represents a disappointing turnabout for the dollar, whose latest heyday for investors
began in the early 1990s, when buyers nervous about the Gulf War flocked to it, and continued through the
"dot-com" boom.
Now, most analysts and economists don't expect the dollar to recover anytime soon, which could mean that
the U.S. stock market will continue to struggle to attract investment and that the rest of the world may fare
better at its game of economic catch-up with America.
"You just haven't seen any rally in the dollar lately, when it normally would've [risen] under the conditions we
have now, with the war risk in the market and all that," says Mr. Schiff, president of Euro Pacific Capital Inc.,
which handles about $120 million in customers' money. "Trading dollars was something that used to work for
people, as something they did almost reflexively, and now it's just not working."
Not long ago, the dollar seemed much more resilient. In the months immediately following the stock market's
downturn, the dollar held its value, in part because foreign investors who still believed in the broader U.S.
economy were either the last to get out of the market, or clung to other dollar-denominated investments as
bets on an American comeback.
But some time in 2000 or so, analysts say foreigners finally began to lose confidence in the U.S. stock market
and to pull their money out en masse -- a trend that has only accelerated following a wave of corporate
scandals and military conflict.
At the same time foreigners were fleeing U.S. stocks, gold, which had languished near 20-year lows in the
late 1990s, began to come back into favor as a tangible asset in lieu of other paper investments. Its rally
solidified after the September 2001 terrorist attacks, continued steadily throughout much of this year, then
accelerated again in the last few weeks due to the dollar's woes.
Analysts say the metal also is benefiting from lower interest rates, to the extent that investors are staying
away from Treasury bonds as an alternative because they perceive the Federal Reserve can't cut rates much
more, and will likely have to raise rates sometime in the near future. That would hurt bond prices. Over the
last month, nearby gold futures are up 8.6% on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange.

--- Page 6 ---
Page 6 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"I think the weaker dollar has meant everything to the gold market," says Joe Foster, manager of Van Eck
International Investor Gold Fund. "You hear people talking more about Iraq driving gold, but I almost think
that's a side show compared to the currency market. People are looking for some place, anyplace else, to put
their money."
He also noted that, despite their relative strength against the dollar, the yen and euro both have fundamental
problems looming over them, which makes gold more attractive.
Mr. Foster says the euro still is viewed by many traders as "an experiment in progress," hobbled by
deepening economic problems at many of the nations using it.
The yen, meantime, has been undermined this year by talk among Japanese officials that they may weaken it
in order to boost the nation's sagging economy. Last week, Japan's finance minister repeated the
government's warnings against the danger of a strong yen, and one of his top staffers said the government
could take action to weaken the yen even on days when Tokyo's markets are closed.
---
Yesterday's Market Activity
Geopolitical tensions and disappointing economic reports kept the dollar vulnerable yesterday, with the U.S.
currency taking a sound thrashing from its major counterparts. But the dollar gained on the Mexican peso and
Canadian dollar.
The U.S. currency dropped about 1.5 yen from its intraday peak, thereby heightening the risk of currency
intervention by Japanese monetary authorities, while sinking yet again to multiyear lows against the euro and
the Swiss franc.
Blowing through levels that had been providing technical and option-related support, the dollar dipped as low
as 118.31 yen in New York from a global high around 120.00 yen in Asian trading. The yen emerged as the
day's big winner against the dollar as other majors posted more moderate advances.
However, the same factors that hurt the dollar against the big currencies -- risk aversion and worries about
the U.S. economy -- helped the U.S. currency gain on its neighboring currencies, the Canadian dollar and the
Mexican peso. Both the Canadian and Mexican economies are enormously dependent on the U.S., given the
volume of exports they send to the world's largest economy.
Late yesterday in New York, the dollar was trading at 118.53 yen, down 119.89 yen late Friday in New York.
The euro was changing hands at $1.0483, above the $1.0438 it was worth Friday.
Against the Swiss franc, the dollar fell to 1.3866 francs, lower than the 1.3902 francs it was worth Friday. The
pound rose to $1.6043, above the $1.6039 mark set late Friday.
Japanese financial markets will remain closed until next Monday in observance of the traditional New Year
holidays. With virtually all the world's foreign-exchange markets closed on Jan. 1, volume dries to only a
fraction of normal activity during the last days of the year. Thin market conditions cause exaggerated
movements and make it difficult for traders to determine short-term trends.
The dollar's broad weakness also tainted currencies from countries with close economic or diplomatic ties
with the U.S. Though it rose against the dollar, the pound fell to near its weakest valuation against the euro
since October 1999, with the common currency at 65.29 pence.
In Mexico, the dollar's weakness against other currencies played a large role in dropping the peso to its
weakest level against the dollar since January 1999. Late yesterday in New York, the dollar was worth
10.4439 pesos, up from 10.3114 pesos late Friday.
The Canadian dollar fell against the dollar, with the dollar purchasing C$1.5763 late yesterday, up from
C$1.5699 Friday.
-- Tyler Lifton

`Reflation' Comes Into Focus as U.S. Struggles to Battle Deflation
The Outlook
`Reflation' Comes Into Focus as U.S. Struggles to Battle Deflation
By Jon E. Hilsenrath
910 words
30 December 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
INFLATION. DEFLATION. Disinflation. The economic lexicon is garbled with so many 'flations these days, it's
getting a little hard to make sense of them all. During the 1970s, '80s and '90s, Washington policy makers
waged a persistent battle against inflation, broadly defined as rising consumer prices. But for most of this
year, as the inflation rate has fallen closer to zero, economists began to worry about the opposite trend -- the
possibility that the U.S. could face Japan-style deflation, or falling consumer prices.
As we enter 2003, here comes a new watchword: reflation. The term refers to government efforts to pump up
-- or reflate -- an economy that is stuck in a slump by flooding that economy with money. Essentially, reflation
is the intentional effort by governments to ward off deflation by boosting demand and underpinning prices.
Though it isn't quite the same as inflation, reflation can spark inflation problems if policy makers go too far.
The term is popping up often. In a recent report titled "The Reflation Trade," Morgan Stanley Co. chief
economist Stephen Roach argued that policy makers are now in a "full-scale frontal assault on the perils of
deflation." That has made him more bullish on the stock market in the months ahead. James Grant, in his
twice-monthly newsletter, Grant's Interest Rate Observer, writes about a "global reflation program" being led
by the U.S. and spreading world-wide. That, he said, explains why prices for gold -- a hedge against
inflation and his favorite investment -- are on the rise.
SOME ECONOMISTS who have been beating deflation drums are doing an about-face. "There has been an
important shift. The financial community is talking more about reflation . . . it is the thing to watch in 2003,"
says Paul McCulley, chief economist at Pacific Investment Management Co., the California bond-fund
manager that recently advised clients that government-bond yields will rise as result of today's reflation tilt.
Behind the reflation focus is blunt talk by Federal Reserve officials. Earlier this month, Fed Chairman Alan
Greenspan told economists in New York that while deflation is only a remote possibility, the central bank is
"extraordinarily sensitive" to the potential perils associated with it and is prepared to forestall it if the economy
moves further in that direction. In November, Fed governor Ben Bernanke laid out a series of unusual steps
the Fed could take to get out of Japan-style deflation if it did happen -- such as lending more aggressively to
banks to drive down private borrowing costs or buying foreign government bonds, which could weaken the
dollar and boost exports.
To Fed watchers, the eye-opening comments indicate the Fed's willingness to go to great lengths to head off
a broad downdraft in prices. "There has been a very radical rhetorical change," Mr. Grant says.
Economists worry about deflation because that price trend seems to be more difficult to tame than inflation.
The Fed controls the economy largely by raising and lowering short-term interest rates. But as short-term
rates drift toward zero, there has been concern that the Fed could run out of room to maneuver. The
benchmark federal funds rate is now at 1.25%, apparently leaving the Fed little room to cut short-term rates
further if the economy continues to slump and prices start falling broadly. When there is inflation, by contrast,
central bankers can raise interest rates as high as needed to fight it.
"It is much easier to get out of inflation than deflation," Mr. Roach says.
The outlook for prices is particularly vexing today because the economy is experiencing whiffs of both
inflation and deflation. While prices for services such as education and health care keep rising, prices for a
wide range of goods -- from power tools to children's shoes -- are falling. The overall consumer-price index is
rising by about 2% annually, though it dipped to 1% earlier this year.
THE REFLATION TALK has many economists rethinking which way they believe the economy is tilting in this
see-saw battle between rising and falling prices. Mr. McCulley, at Pimco, worried in the past that deflation
could become entrenched, which would be especially dangerous for companies with heavy debt loads. That's

--- Page 8 ---
Page 8 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
because when prices fall, it leaves companies with less revenue to pay off their debts and slices into
corporate cash flow.
But Mr. McCulley says the Fed's stance on deflation has changed his mind. "The Fed saw the beast that
we've been talking about for months and months and declared that it's going to kill the beast," he says. He
now believes the U.S. consumer-price index will tick up by 2.5% next year, compared with the 1%-2%
inflation rate that has prevailed for most of this year. "You should see some sort of pricing power restored to
globally traded goods markets," Mr. McCulley says.
But Mr. Roach, from Morgan Stanley, says that inflation worrywarts give the Fed too much credit for being
able to tackle today's economic burdens. With the economy just two years into a "wrenching post-bubble
shakeout," the risks of outright deflation haven't yet gone away, he says. Stock markets might cheer the
intensifying battle against pernicious deflation in the months ahead, but that beast will show its face again, he
says.

Ahead of the Tape
Ahead of the Tape
Ahead of the Tape
By Gregory Zuckerman
411 words
27 December 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
[Today's Market Forecast]
Cheap Grills at McDonald's
The heat is on McDonald's. But so far there's no sign of a turnaround.
A vicious price war is crippling profits. McDonald's has braced investors for its first-ever quarterly loss. Last
week, Moody's Investors Service said it may slash the company's debt rating amid two years of global
slowing sales. Long known for its growth, McDonald's is closing restaurants and firing employees.
McDonald's stock has tracked the price of its burgers, down sharply -- off 50% since May.
The secret recipe? Scrap the price cutting -- it isn't as if the french fries have been overpriced. Go healthier,
more upscale. You shouldn't feel guilty going to Mickey D's. And improve the taste of those burgers.
But the company is sticking with its Dollar Menu. Indeed, McDonald's will play up the cut-price strategy in
2003, according to an internal memorandum reviewed by Dow Jones Newswires. The company is content
tinkering with the taste of the seasoning of its beef patties, according to the memo. Let's hope there will be
more hamburger, not just helper.
Here's the big danger: The more McDonald's emphasizes its low prices, the more it will be known for its
cheap fare, rather than taste or its dining experience.
Stock Answer
Gold has surged to a 5 1/2-year high and the dollar is at its lowest level in almost three years. Bond yields
remain near 40-year lows. Behind all the moves: expectations of a war with Iraq, and looming tensions with
North Korea.
But stocks have shrugged it all off, retaining most of the heady gains achieved since early October.
What gives?
One view is that stocks are being affected by the war talk. If it weren't for all the hand-wringing, stocks would
be off to the races, says Tim Hayes, global strategist at Ned Davis Research. Stocks usually rally this time of
year, he notes.
Another view: A war with Iraq, especially a swift win, could be a boon. Such a conflict could keep interest
rates low and the dollar weak. It also would add a bit of needed inflation to the economy, allowing
companies to raise prices, says James Paulsen, Wells Fargo's chief investment officer.
Wishful thinking?

Main Street:A Tale of a BrokerAnd His ClientsAnd an Era's End---Market Bubble Was InflatedBy Many Ordinary People;Now, Loss and Accusat...
Main Street: A Tale of a Broker And His Clients And an Era's End --- Market Bubble Was Inflated By
Many Ordinary People; Now, Loss and Accusation --- `OK, Brion, I Totally Trust You'
By Jacob M. Schlesinger and Bryan Gruley
5,426 words
27 December 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
DALLAS -- When her husband died of a brain tumor in May 1999, Maria Walker, then 33 years old, was left
with a two-year-old daughter, no job and few marketable skills.
But there was hope. The bull market of the 1990s was in full stampede. And Ms. Walker's money was in the
hands of her late husband's friend, a Merrill Lynch & Co. broker named Brion Randall. Mr. Randall had
promised his pal that he would take care of the widow.
The broker invited her to his office on the top floor of a glass tower in Dallas. He told her that the stock market
would take care of her, just as it was taking care of his other clients. Through the magic of high-tech stocks
Ms. Walker had never heard of -- Ciena Corp., Xilinx Inc. and ADC Telecommunications Inc. -- her
inheritance soared by 50% in less than a year, exceeding half a million dollars. While the market roared, she
racked up monthly Visa bills that averaged more than $4,000. "You rock!" she told Mr. Randall again and
again.
Then stock prices imploded and so did their friendship.
In the search for what went wrong in the stock boom-gone-bust, debates have focused on greedy executives,
corrupt accountants and lax regulators. But the bubble never would have inflated without ordinary Americans
-- like Mr. Randall and some of his clients: Ms. Walker, divorce lawyer Robert E. Holmes Jr., and contractor
James Lundy Jr. and his son, J.R.
In the 1990s, the number of Americans owning stock swelled by 30 million to more than 80 million, a mania
unseen since the 1920s. The new national passion suffused the circle of investors who revolved around Mr.
Randall, 40. The native Texan was a bored banker who joined Merrill Lynch in 1995, just as the boom in tech
and telecom stocks was gathering force. He persuaded friends and family to join him on the ride to riches.
His clients cheered as their accounts ballooned. They went on shopping sprees and planned for early
retirements. But the bull market's end brought recriminations. Many investors aren't much worse off today
than before the bubble. Yet everything has changed. They're bitter and vindictive about losing the future they
thought was in their grasp. And they're grappling with where to lay the blame.
"He kept selling us more. And the more he gave us, the more we wanted. Then boom, crash," says Mr.
Holmes of his former broker.
At least 10 former clients -- including Mr. Holmes and Ms. Walker -- have filed formal complaints against
Merrill, alleging that Mr. Randall broke various rules in handling their accounts. He denies these charges.
Merrill has so far settled four of the cases, paying about $1.5 million to the investors.
The firm fired Mr. Randall earlier this year, and Mr. Randall plans to file an arbitration claim against Merrill. A
Merrill spokesman declines to comment on Mr. Randall's case, except to say, "We take all client complaints
very seriously and, consistent with our policy, review each complaint thoroughly and take what we believe to
be the appropriate action."
THE STORY begins in 1995. The Dow Jones Industrial Average was surging past 5000 and the Nasdaq was
breaking 1000. The initial public offering of Netscape Communications Corp. shot from $28 to a close of
$58.25 on its first day. Americans who had considered Wall Street a spectator sport decided to join the
action.
Rob Holmes wasn't one of them, at first. Eight years earlier, he'd given a few thousand dollars to a
stockbroker who insisted that the lawyer couldn't lose. Then came the Crash of '87.

--- Page 11 ---
Page 11 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Mr. Holmes, 50, cashed out his shrunken account and spent it. At the time he was a bachelor with a taste for
"nice cars and a nice time," and few worries about the future. But by 1995, he was starting his own law firm in
Dallas and shopping for a retirement plan when Mr. Randall called.
"He was pushy in his way, really aggressive, saying, `I can do this, I can do that,'" Mr. Holmes recalls. Mr.
Randall got the law firm's business, including Mr. Holmes's own $70,000 retirement account.
The Merrill broker was new to the stock market himself. He had grown up in Richardson, a thriving Dallas
suburb where his father, an executive at a real-estate development company, had helped build Telecom
Corridor, a stretch of office buildings occupied by Cisco, MCI, Nortel and other tech giants.
Mr. Randall had been a bank vice president when he decided he wanted bigger challenges and a chance to
make a better life for his wife, Amy, and their young son. He joined Merrill in April 1995, completed a two-year
training program in 13 months, and established himself as a whiz at assembling comprehensive financial
plans. The selection of individual stocks played only a small part in these plans, he says.
Mr. Randall worked in a tidy cubicle surrounded by other Merrill brokers. Customers met an athletic man,
unfailingly upbeat, who compared his philosophy of diversified investing to a highway. "I'm going to put you in
five lanes," he would say. "If one lane is stopped, the others are moving."
A few of Mr. Randall's investors had Alcoholics Anonymous in common. He had been a recovering alcoholic
since 1989. His wife, Ms. Walker and her husband were all recovering alcoholics. After AA meetings, Mr.
Randall would occasionally pick up the tab for dinner. "Everybody would be howling" at his stories and jokes,
Ms. Walker says.
ONE DAY IN 1996, Mr. Randall parked his black Nissan Maxima at an office building on Black Gold Drive in
Dallas.
He'd come to pick up a $1,000 check from his friend and newest client, 25-year-old construction-company
employee J.R. Lundy. "I told him, `Dude, I'm not worth your time,'" Mr. Lundy recalls. Mr. Randall not only
took the check and the account, he took Mr. Lundy to lunch.
Mr. Randall wanted help landing a more promising client -- Mr. Lundy's father, James Lundy Jr. The elder Mr.
Lundy owned a drywall and millwork firm that was flourishing thanks to a tech-driven construction surge.
James Lundy wasn't an easy sell, though. He had left high school after 11th grade and followed his father
and grandfather into construction work. To the extent he discussed investing with his family, it was about how
Wall Street was rigged against the little guy. The elder Mr. Lundy declined to be interviewed for this story.
His son had no such fear of the market. He was comfortable with the Internet and had come to see it as an
equalizer, a powerful tool to put detailed financial information in the hands of regular people. When he
received a bonus at work, he called Mr. Randall and said he was ready to start investing. Soon after that, he
introduced the broker to his father. At 45, the elder Mr. Lundy opened his first brokerage account.
Some experts, meanwhile, were growing uneasy. At the end of 1996, Federal Reserve Chairman Alan
Greenspan famously questioned whether "irrational exuberance" was inflating share values. Stocks briefly
swooned, but rebounded almost immediately. In 1997 and in 1998, the bull market stumbled under pressure
from the Asia crisis, the Russian debt default, and the collapse of a big hedge fund. Each time, the bull roared
back. At the end of 1998, the Dow Jones Industrial Average was near 9200 and the Nasdaq was near 2200.
MR. RANDALL HAD BEEN with Merrill three years when he took a call from an assistant to Dwight
Emanuelson Jr., a senior Merrill broker in Dallas.
Mr. Randall knew of him from the daily "goalpost" listings on his office bulletin board. They listed individual
brokers' "production," or how much they generated in commissions and fees. Mr. Emanuelson always
seemed to be at the top of the list.
Mr. Emanuelson had noticed Mr. Randall, too. The younger broker was winning new clients and awards,
including a statue of a cowboy riding a bull; it had been given him by then-Merrill brokerage chief John
"Launny" Steffens. He earned the award for his skill at developing financial plans.
Over breakfast, Mr. Emanuelson invited Mr. Randall to be his partner and pool their clients and fees. Mr.
Randall couldn't believe his luck. Mr. Emanuelson's annual production was in the millions of dollars, while Mr.
Randall's was in the hundreds of thousands.
Mr. Randall moved from his first-floor cubicle to a 20th-floor office with two assistants, a bank of computers
and a view of the Dallas skyline. "I've arrived," he thought. Mr. Emanuelson would handle most of their

--- Page 12 ---
Page 12 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
biggest clients and receive a larger share of the income. Still, in his first two years with Mr. Emanuelson, Mr.
Randall's annual income climbed to more than $400,000.
At the end of 1999, Mr. Randall and his wife moved with their son, Travis, and two-year-old daughter, Riley,
into a $580,000 brick-and-stucco house in suburban Plano. He leased a silver Jaguar, and put his cowboy
statue in his new home office. So many referrals poured in that he had to turn some away.
HE COULDN'T SAY no to Maria Walker.
Her late husband, Tuck, had been a fellow AA member. They barbecued and watched football together. The
Walkers' little girl, Emily, played with the Randalls' girl, Riley.
After Mr. Walker died in 1999, Ms. Walker holed up for two days to drink wine and beer, her first alcohol in
more than a decade. When she stopped, Ms. Walker says, Amy Randall sponsored her at the AA group she
and Mr. Randall attended. When Ms. Walker fell into a depression, the Randalls had her and Emily stay with
them for a week. Ms. Randall helped Ms. Walker learn to make beaded jewelry.
Mr. Randall followed through on his vow to care for Ms. Walker's finances. Her husband had no life
insurance, but he left a retirement account. Mr. Walker's family paid Ms. Walker for her late husband's share
of the family's medical-equipment business, and gave her more to set up Emily's college fund. After buying a
$156,900 house, she had about $350,000.
THE FIRST STOCKS Mr. Randall bought Ms. Walker were blue chips including Wal-Mart Stores Inc. and
International Business Machines Corp. Over time, though, he advised her to dump those in favor of hotter
issues. The account she used for household expenses remained invested in conservative bonds, but by
August 2000 her retirement account was composed entirely of tech stocks.
Ms. Walker delighted in hearing from Mr. Randall how much money she was making. When she heard that
another AA member had profited from IPO shares, she called the broker and asked, "What's an IPO? If that's
making a lot of money, I want one, too."
Rob Holmes, the lawyer, understood that envy. He had watched the dot-commers divvy up lucrative stock
options in divorces. And he'd seen new wealth blossom in the Preston Hollow neighborhood where he lived.
One day, Mark Cuban moved in. Mr. Cuban now owns the National Basketball Association's Dallas Mavericks
and co-founded Broadcast.com, which he sold to Yahoo! in 1999 for $5.7 billion. "His fountain makes mine
look like a water faucet," Mr. Holmes says.
By 2000, Mr. Holmes's $70,000 investment with Mr. Randall had more than doubled. The income from his law
practice began to seem inadequate. "All of these young guys were coming along going crazy making money,"
Mr. Holmes says, "making us little service-industry people look like fools."
That was enough to wipe away the reticence caused by his earlier misadventure in the markets. He turned
the bulk of his savings, about $100,000, over to Mr. Randall for another account. Mr. Randall put nearly all of
it in tech stocks.
Mr. Holmes, himself a certified financial planner, says he joked with co-workers that he was going to become
"an AOL millionaire." He bookmarked his stocks on his computer. "If I needed a little boost," he says, "I'd
check and go `Woo-hoo!'"
On Black Gold Drive, stock gossip joined fishing and hunting in coffee-break conversations at Lundy Services
Inc., James Lundy's firm. His son, J.R., stayed up late scrolling Web sites such as Motley Fool and watching
stock shows. "I loved Lou Dobbs," says the community-college dropout of the host of CNN's "Moneyline"
program. "It was the first time I started thinking I wished I'd gone to school so I could understand better."
James Lundy was pleased enough with Mr. Randall's handling of his money that he referred two construction
clients to the broker.
Mr. Randall says most of his clients maintained conservative, well-balanced portfolios. But for some, he set
aside the "five-lane highway" and concentrated almost entirely in the fast lane of high-tech stocks.
The broker says clients pressured him to do so. "I'd get calls from people saying, "My buddy's making more
money and I'm going to move this account -- my 8% mutual fund is a dog,'" he says. He steered others in that
direction himself, he says, because tech stocks "were where the growth was."
Network Peripherals Inc. -- a Fremont, Calif., maker of ethernet switches so obscure that Merrill analysts
didn't follow it -- became a favorite pick. Mr. Randall once told a friend he might put the company's stock
symbol, NPIX, on his Jaguar's license plate.

--- Page 13 ---
Page 13 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
By March 1999, the Dow had cracked 10000, the Nasdaq was threatening 2500, and online brokerage firm
Ameritrade was running ads starring Stuart, a red-headed slacker who goaded his boss into trading stocks
online.
Mr. Randall's clients didn't think of themselves as quite so wacky. "Merrill Lynch seemed safe," Mr. Holmes
says.
But rather than counseling against high-tech hysteria, Merrill got caught up, too. The brokerage firm had
lagged behind rivals in peddling stocks and winning investment banking in the fast-growing sector. As the
Nasdaq neared its peak in the first months of 2000, Merrill launched an Internet Strategies fund with more
than $1 billion in investor money. New York Attorney General Eliot Spitzer later alleged that Merrill's rosy
research on tech stocks had been intended to boost its banking business. Merrill has denied wrongdoing, but
it paid $100 million this past May to settle the charges and issued an apology to investors. Last week Merrill
agreed to pay an additional $100 million for investor education and research as part of a separate settlement
between regulators and 10 securities firms.
Mr. Randall says Merrill also encouraged brokers to raise as much money as possible in fees charged to
investors by making more trades and opening more accounts. He says that didn't necessarily conflict with the
interest of investors -- as long as the market was rising.
"There's no question that my investment philosophy changed, or was changed for me, when I entered the
inner sanctum," Mr. Randall says. "The more production you do, the more accolades you receive."
Not all Merrill accounts are charged commissions. Some of Mr. Randall's clients -- Ms. Walker, for example --
paid only a flat fee equivalent to 1% of the accounts' assets.
But one lucrative account was James Lundy's. Trading was so heavy that the account was turned over, on
average, every 12 days, according to a complaint Mr. Lundy has filed with the National Association of
Securities Dealers, the self-regulatory organization that hears most investor complaints. Mr. Lundy's tab on
trading commissions alone ran to $369,050 from December 1998 through July 2001.
Merrill, which regularly monitors accounts, noticed the activity. The firm says it called and wrote Mr. Lundy on
several occasions, and he repeatedly characterized himself as an aggressive trader who was pleased with
Mr. Randall's service.
In February 2000, through a combination of successful trades and cash infusions, Mr. Lundy's account --
worth $65,000 in the summer of 1998 -- hit $791,879. That was as high as it would get. The Dow had hit its
high and the Nasdaq was soon to peak. The bull market was at an end.
STOCK PRICES HAD CLIMBED for years on assumptions that the economy would keep thriving and,
indeed, that stock prices would keep climbing. In the spring of 2000, small tremors began to disturb those
assumptions.
In late March, Goldman Sachs's influential bull, Abby Joseph Cohen, urged investors to trim holdings a bit.
One week in April, the Nasdaq dropped 1125 points, or 25%, due in part to disappointing tech-company
earnings and inflation news. The market looked vulnerable.
In May, the Federal Reserve, worried that the stock market was overheating the economy, cranked up its
primary rate target by half a point. Over the summer, the economy's overall growth slowed, hurting traditional
stocks as well. The dead-heat presidential election created more uncertainty. A week after the Supreme
Court finally threw the contest to George W. Bush, the Nasdaq closed below 2500 for the first time in more
than a year.
That's when the exuberance surrounding Mr. Randall began to subside. In late 2000, one investor, a nurse,
filed a formal complaint against the broker and Merrill, accusing them, among other things, of churning her
account, or trading heavily to generate commissions, according to NASD records. Mr. Randall says the
complaint took him by surprise. Only that summer, the investor had given him high grades in a chat with a
Merrill official in Dallas, according to Merrill documents.
In the spring of 2001, Merrill paid $410,000 to settle the claim. Then a second Randall client filed an
arbitration claim for $522,000 in damages. Nervous, Mr. Randall told his wife, "The sharks are circling and
there's chum in the water."
"IT MUST BE TOUGH being you right now," Mr. Holmes told Mr. Randall one day as the market continued its
fall.
"Yeah," Mr. Randall joked. "I've got a bungee chord and I jump off the side of the building periodically to see
what it feels like."

--- Page 14 ---
Page 14 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Stocks that once made clients thousands of dollars in a matter of days or hours were now in free fall. Some
clients cashed out. Others refused, even when Mr. Randall advised them to sell, he says. But there were
some in the middle, anxious but unsure. When they sought Mr. Randall's expert advice, he often counseled:
Hang in there.
Mr. Randall says he, in turn, relied on Merrill strategists, economists and analysts, many of whom remained
bullish well into the bear market. Amid 2001's summer swoon, Christine Callies, Merrill's chief U.S.
investment strategist, appeared on CNNfn, CNN's financial channel. "It's days like this that we like to
encourage investors to step up and buy the dip," she said.
As the market fell, Mr. Holmes says he frequently suggested selling, and Mr. Randall talked him out of it. "I
kept hanging on until I was hung," Mr. Holmes says.
On Sept. 11, terrorists slammed jets into the World Trade Center and the Pentagon. Stock trading was halted.
When markets reopened, the Dow fell nearly 700 points, beginning its worst week since the Great
Depression. Shudders rippled through an economy that, it turns out, was already in recession. The public's
mood further darkened as the U.S. government launched war in Afghanistan and searched for an anthrax
killer at home. By the end of 2001, $4.7 trillion of stock-market value had disappeared since the peak.
When Maria Walker opened her year-end Merrill statement, she saw that her accounts, once worth more than
$500,000, were now valued at $75,813. Some of the loss was the result of her shopping. Her cash account,
which had been invested conservatively, was down by $66,460 since the summer of 2000. But she also had
big losses resulting from investment decisions. Her retirement account, now invested almost entirely in BEA
Systems, had fallen by $400,000. BEA, a San Jose maker of e-commerce software, was trading at $15.40 a
share, or less than one-fourth the $64.88 she had paid in January, according to the statement.
Mr. Randall invited her to meet in his office. He told her, as he says he had several times before, to cut her
monthly spending. He said she would be OK until the market bounced back.
"OK, Brion, I totally trust you," she recalls saying.
But Americans' trust in financial markets was dwindling. Enron Corp. had tumbled into bankruptcy amid
revelations that its earnings had flowed from dicey accounting. That touched off allegations of bookkeeping
shenanigans at other corporate leaders, from Global Crossing Ltd. to WorldCom Inc. With investors losing
faith in earnings reports, a brief market rally sputtered and died in the spring of 2002.
AS HER PORTFOLIO dropped, Ms. Walker began to feel resentful. "I was losing all my money and Amy was
still shopping," she says of the broker's wife. She also thought Ms. Randall was growing distant. "I'd ask
"What's wrong?'" Ms. Walker says. "She'd never say."
Ms. Randall was fretting about her husband.
Merrill officials had been calling Mr. Randall's clients -- including his father -- and asking if they were happy
with him. He'd heard that other clients might file complaints and had begun to doubt that Merrill was sincere
about defending him. He wondered if the firm was using him to shield his senior partner, Mr. Emanuelson.
He finally went to see the manager of his office, Cecil "Cap" Chesser. He says he told Mr. Chesser that he
had hired Roger Evans, a Dallas attorney who specializes in employment law, because he didn't trust Merrill
to defend his reputation.
A few weeks later, Merrill moved Mr. Randall from the 20th floor to the first floor. On March 11, Merrill fired
him because of "several customer complaints alleging unsuitability, discretion and churning," Merrill says.
The NASD "currently has an investigation under way" into the conduct of both Mr. Randall and Mr.
Emanuelson, an NASD official says. Mr. Randall's attorney, Mr. Evans, says that his client "is voluntarily
assisting the NASD in its investigation," adding that "Mr. Randall unequivocally and categorically denies any
part whatsoever . . . in any inappropriate or illegal activity by his former employer." A Merrill spokesman
declined to comment or make Mr. Emanuelson or Mr. Chesser available for interviews.
THE NIGHT HER HUSBAND was fired, Ms. Randall, Ms. Walker and two other friends gathered in Ms.
Walker's living room. As they sipped coffee, Ms. Randall talked about her husband's travails.
She said it looked liked any investors could allege most anything, and Merrill would pay without a fight. "Oh
my God," Ms. Walker said at the time, according to Ms. Randall. "Maybe I could get back some of the money
I lost."

--- Page 15 ---
Page 15 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
After her friends left, Ms. Walker says she grew angry and depressed. She had trusted Mr. Randall, she says,
but now she was hearing second-hand that he might have mishandled her money. She spent hours searching
the Internet for information on investor rights, she says, "just printing, printing, printing."
Mr. Randall called her the next day. They discussed the possibility that she would sue. Mr. Randall told her
he had done nothing wrong.
Then Ms. Randall called Ms. Walker and accused her of abusing their friendship. "I can't believe you would
do this," Ms. Randall said. They haven't spoken since.
TWO DAYS LATER, Ms. Walker dialed an attorney whose name she had seen on a Web site called
"investorfraud.com."
Tracy Pride Stoneman took the call at her sprawling home near Colorado Springs, Colo. Friends jokingly call
it "Prudential Palace" because Ms. Stoneman and her husband, an expert witness for investors, had won
millions of dollars suing Prudential Securities Inc. in the early 1990s for peddling risky limited partnerships.
Today's bear market has been a bull for Ms. Stoneman. She's handling 38 cases, triple her caseload three
years ago. "Many brokers led clients to believe that it's the market's fault," her voiceover said in a TV
commercial during a recent Dallas broadcast of "Divorce Court." "The good news is, you don't need to remain
a victim."
When Rob Holmes first started losing money, "I was blaming the market," he says. "I thought I should take
my losses." Then he heard Mr. Randall had been fired over customer complaints. He saw one of Ms.
Stoneman's ads in the Dallas Morning News, calling on investors who lost money on tech stocks with Merrill
Lynch. That described Mr. Holmes. His two accounts, once valued at about $300,000, were now, combined,
worth less than $20,000.
The news had been filled with reports of insider trading, sweetheart loans for executives, and stock
recommendations made to win investment banking. It got Mr. Holmes to thinking: "Maybe there's something
going on out there I didn't know about."
He called Ms. Stoneman on May 2. "Another Brion Randall client?" Ms. Stoneman said. She filed a claim
against Merrill with the New York Stock Exchange on behalf of Mr. Holmes, Ms. Walker and two other clients
of Mr. Randall.
DESPITE THE LITIGATION, these investors have wrestled over what or whom they should blame: Mr.
Randall? Merrill Lynch? Big companies? Themselves?
"Did I let it happen? Absolutely," says Mr. Holmes. He says he lost sight of his values and came to believe he
could make money without "grinding it out." When his stocks were soaring, he boasted to colleagues about
his plans for early retirement. "I'm so out of here," he would say.
Now, he says, he walks down the halls with his empty pockets turned out, pleading mock poverty. He
complains about the lost dreams as much as the money.
In choosing to lodge a complaint, Mr. Holmes concluded: "Somebody should have hosed us down and said,
`OK, let's have a point at which you stop.' That's what we were paying Brion Randall for."
James Lundy confronted the question of blame as well. Last year, the two construction-industry associates
he'd sent to Mr. Randall in 1997 decided to file complaints, and asked Mr. Lundy to join them.
He didn't at first. Mr. Randall says Mr. Lundy even warned him about the pending complaints one day over a
Tex-Mex lunch. As Mr. Randall recalls it, Mr. Lundy said he had discouraged the men, telling them, "`Brion
might've made some different calls, but at the end of the day you're the one making the final decisions.'"
But Mr. Lundy eventually chose to file a complaint with the NASD. The market plunge had wiped out his
account. He came to realize his losses were much steeper than in the market overall, says his son. Robert
Crotty, one of James Lundy's lawyers, says "there are substantial inaccuracies" in statements made by Mr.
Randall and Merrill about his client. He declined to cite specific examples.
J.R. Lundy also was wiped out, but decided against filing a complaint. He says his account, which never
reached $100,000, was too small. He owns no stocks now, but periodically checks his old ones -- at least
those that survived. "I tap a few symbols into my computer and snicker."
The vast majority of Mr. Randall's clients haven't taken any action against him. Scott Jessen, 45, is one. The
Telecom Corridor real-estate executive says he lost a lot of money, but filing a complaint never crossed his
mind.

--- Page 16 ---
Page 16 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"If you're going to trust somebody with your money and you don't take their advice, what are you paying them
for?" Of those who did take action, Mr. Jessen has this to say: "These people need to look in the mirror. If
they didn't like where it was going, why didn't they just fire him?"
WHEN MR. RANDALL LOOKS in the mirror, he sees a victim, betrayed by his employer, his former clients
and his dead friend's widow. Once relentlessly optimistic, Mr. Randall now shows flashes of anger.
He didn't lose his own money trading stocks because he didn't trade stocks for himself. Merrill restrictions
designed to prevent insider trading make it complicated for brokers to do so. But he says he has suffered
nonetheless. He lost his job, and says his family has been through emotional hell.
Mr. Randall refuses to accept blame for his clients' shrunken fortunes, except to say he was naive to trust
Merrill's research. He now works at a two-man firm linking clients with providers of estate planning and other
financial services. He charges flat fees -- no commissions.
He's preparing his own arbitration claim against Merrill, charging slander, libel, shoddy representation and
wrongful firing. But he still displays his Merrill cowboy statue in his new office.
Ms. Walker, meanwhile, recently had her Merrill Lynch debit card refused by a grocery store. She and her
daughter are living off of $2,668 in monthly Social Security checks, and what she can earn selling the beaded
jewelry that Ms. Randall encouraged her to make.
AA friends have upbraided Ms. Walker for violating a crucial tenet of the group, anonymity, by exposing the
Randalls as members in her complaint. She says it pained her, but she needed to show why she had trusted
Mr. Randall so much.
The boom brought Ms. Walker closer to Mr. Randall. They made money. Then they lost it. She faced a
choice: Eat her losses and take the rap for believing in the bubble. Or challenge her friend to try to get her
money back.
She chose the latter because, she says, "I have to take care of my family. I'm all my daughter has." And she
stews over the Randalls' contention that her actions destroyed their friendship. "Is that supposed to cost me
$250,000?" Ms. Walker says. "That kind of friendship? Is that how expensive it is?"
---
Kelly Spors and Christopher Conkey contributed to this article.

WEEKEND JOURNAL
Taste: Tony & Tacky

The Wall Street Journal

To Be or Not to Be: That's the question the Dallas Morning News says Shakespeare purists are asking about
a Texas high-school teacher's efforts to bring the bard to his students by trading in "Elizabethan couplets for
MTV-talk." In a new, special edition of Shakespearean plays, John Price reprints the standard text but
includes a running commentary geared to teens. Thus the plot line for "Romeo and Juliet" is described as
follows: "MacDaddy Romeo (or so he thinks!) is bummed about Rosaline, a babe he wants to hook up with,
because she ignores him. His cousin Benvolio (Benny) encourages him to get his groove on elsewhere."
Swan Song: If you think your Christmas outlay was less this year than last, perhaps you're right. PNC
Advisors, a financial-services firm that calculates the cost of the items in "The 12 Days of Christmas," reports
that its 18th annual index, for 2002, dropped. But the good news appears to be skewed by the decline in the
price of seven-swans-a-swimming to $2,100 from $3,500. "When you strip out the swans," says Jeff Kleintop,
chief investment strategist for PNC Advisors, "our core Christmas Price Index is on par with inflation." Gold
bugs take note: The price of five gold rings went up again, to $382 from $375.

Commodities Report:Crude Oil Continues Its AscentAs Venezuelan Exports Decline
Commodities Report: Crude Oil Continues Its Ascent As Venezuelan Exports Decline
By Masood Farivar
Dow Jones Newswires
597 words
27 December 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
B6
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- A four-week strike in Venezuela has yet to make a substantial dent in U.S. petroleum
inventories, but energy markets continue to act as though it will.
On the New York Mercantile Exchange, February crude-oil futures jumped 52 cents to close at $32.49 a
barrel, the highest settlement price for a front-month contract since late November 2000.
"A lot of people thought that the strike in Venezuela would be over in a week," said John Lichtblau, chairman
of the Petroleum Industry Research Foundation in New York. "Now, we have had it for four weeks, and there
is no end in sight."
Crude-oil prices have surged more than 20% since the start of the strike by oil workers and opposition
supporters in early December. Venezuela is the world's fourth-largest exporter of crude oil, supplying roughly
15% of U.S. oil imports. Venezuelan oil production has dropped to less than 200,000 barrels a day from a
prestrike level of about three million barrels a day.
Yesterday's rally came despite industry and government data showing U.S. crude-oil inventories rose
unexpectedly last week despite a near halt in Venezuelan oil exports to the U.S.
The Department of Energy's Energy Information Administration reported that crude oil in commercial storage
grew by 700,000 barrels, to 287.4 million barrels, last week, as refiners cut back on production. The American
Petroleum Institute, in a report released late Tuesday, had reported an even larger build of 2.7 million barrels
in crude-oil stocks. Analysts had projected a decline of about four million barrels in crude-oil stocks.
The EIA said that while the paralysis of Venezuela's oil industry has "significantly" reduced imports of crude
oil from that country, an increase in imports from other countries has partly offset the loss of Venezuelan oil.
"There is no question that our third or fourth largest foreign supplier of crude is almost completely out, and
there is no expectation that this will end soon," Mr. Lichtblau said.
Venezuela's proximity to the U.S. makes it especially important as a source of oil, Mr. Lichtblau said, noting
that shipments from Venezuela to the U.S. Gulf Coast take about five days, compared with 40 days from the
Persian Gulf. The strike in Venezuela comes at a time of heightened expectations of a U.S. war with Iraq.
Yesterday, U.S. and United Kingdom warplanes bombed Iraqi military command and communication targets
near Talil in southern Iraq, the U.S. military said. The military added that the strike was in response to the
Iraqi military's downing of an American unmanned surveillance plane on Monday. That incident helped lift
crude-oil futures above $31 a barrel earlier in the week.
"Between Iraq and Venezuela, the fundamentals are too supportive," said Mike Fitzpatrick, an energy analyst
at Fimat Futures Inc. in New York.
In other commodity markets:
NATURAL GAS: New York Mercantile Exchange January natural-gas contract ended lower as the market
broke through support at $5 per million British thermal units. January settled at $4.962 per million British
thermal units, down 18.4 cents.
SILVER: March silver prices at the New York Mercantile Exchange ended up 4.3 cents at $4.705 an ounce,
supported by strength in the gold market and thin market conditions. However, further gains in silver might be
limited since the metal lacks gold's safe-haven buying status.

Currency Trading:Threats of War, Nuclear Crisis Push Down Dollar
Currency Trading: Threats of War, Nuclear Crisis Push Down Dollar
By Tyler Lifton
Dow Jones Newswires
833 words
27 December 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
B7
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- In an extremely quiet day of trading that traditionally follows the Christmas holiday, the threat
of military conflict in Iraq and a burgeoning nuclear crisis on the Korean peninsula pushed the dollar to fresh
multiyear lows against some of its main counterparts.
The euro rose as high as $1.0375, its highest level in around three years, while the dollar slid against the
Swiss franc to as low as 1.4012 Swiss Francs, its weakest valuation since January 1999. Against the yen, the
dollar drifted mildly lower, though fears of intervention by Japanese officials to weaken the yen has kept the
dollar hovering around the 120-yen level recently.
With no new events to trigger a fresh outlook in foreign exchange, a lethargic market allowed the cloud that
has pummeled the dollar for weeks to stick around for another day.
"Obviously you can point to the issue with Iraq," as reason for the dollar's substantial weakness against the
euro and the Swiss franc, said David Solin, partner at Foreign Exchange Analytics in Essex, Conn.
The dollar tends to suffer in the face of anything that makes investors nervous. That is because risk aversion
curtails the flow of global capital, something the U.S. needs to finance its current-account deficit.
The greenback did receive a bit of good news on the economic front Thursday, as the Labor Department
reported that initial jobless claims fell by 60,000 to 378,000 in the week that ended Dec. 21. Economists had
forecast a decline of only 25,000.
But that report was unable to lift concerns about the U.S. economy, or keep the dollar from ending the day
weaker.
Late Thursday in New York, the euro was trading at $1.0374, up from $1.0323. U.S. financial markets were
closed Wednesday due to the Christmas holiday. The dollar was changing hands at 119.98 yen, down from
120.28 yen. Against the Swiss franc, the dollar was valued at 1.4015 Swiss francs, down from 1.4053 Swiss
francs, while the pound was valued at $1.5999, up from $1.5946.
The latest wrinkle to the diplomatic tensions that have pummeled the dollar over the past month came
yesterday when Mohamed ElBaradei, the director of the Vienna-based International Atomic Energy Agency,
said that North Korea's move "toward restarting its nuclear facilities without appropriate safeguards and
toward producing plutonium raises serious nonproliferation concerns and is tantamount to nuclear
brinkmanship."
As a direct result over the tussles between the U.S. and Iraq and North Korea, the dollar has fallen sharply
against the euro and the Swiss franc over the past four weeks.
On Nov. 27, the euro traded as low as 98.76 U.S. cents, 4.69% lower than the peak it reached Thursday.
Against the Swiss franc, the dollar topped out a month ago at 1.4925 Swiss francs, meaning it fell as much as
6.49% over the next month.
And while in past years investors have often plowed their money into the U.S. Treasury market in times of
uncertainty, that tendency may be diminishing.
Although the Treasury market has been posting gains in the past couple of weeks, "It appears gold is the
preferred safe asset class . . . and the dollar is suffering as a result," analysts at HSBC Securities wrote in a
report yesterday.
Looking to year end, it is doubtful that the dollar will find much respite.

--- Page 21 ---
Page 21 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"This time of year, there's not a lot of players in the market," so often whatever trend has been established
will remain in place, said Foreign Exchange Analytics' Mr. Solin.
Trading desks have been extremely short staffed this week due to the various holidays, including Christmas,
Boxing Day, and the emperor's birthday in Japan.
In other currencies, the Argentine peso strengthened after the central bank announced earlier this week that
it would ease currency controls introduced earlier this year. Late yesterday in New York, the dollar was
trading at 3.395 pesos, down from 3.435 pesos late Tuesday.
On Tuesday, Argentina's central bank said that beginning today it would cease the buying and selling of
dollars in support of its own peso benchmark rate until further notice.
The Venezuelan bolivar meanwhile was trading around 1,392.75 bolivars against the dollar late yesterday, a
bit weaker than 1,388.50 bolivars Tuesday in thin volume as banks remained shut for most of the day due to
the national strike against President Hugo Chavez's leadership, in its its 25th day.
Traders have said they expect the bolivar to weaken as dollar demand increases on speculation the
government will have to devalue the currency to meet its obligations in the coming quarter. Because more
than half of Venezuela's revenue is in dollars, weakening the bolivar gives the government more money to
pay its local currency obligations.

Friend or Foe:The Story of a Traitor to al Qaeda---Murky Loyalties in YemenUndo the Betrayer, WhoFinds Himself Betrayed---Ominous Words B...
Friend or Foe: The Story of a Traitor to al Qaeda --- Murky Loyalties in Yemen Undo the Betrayer, Who
Finds Himself Betrayed --- Ominous Words Before 9/11
By Andrew Higgins and Alan Cullison
4,334 words
20 December 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
SANAA, Yemen -- Fed up after two decades of Islamist plotting, the veteran Egyptian militant decided to jilt
the jihad. In early 1998, he walked into the heavily guarded offices of Yemen's intelligence agency, the
Political Security Organization, with a startling proposal: He could help unravel Osama bin Laden's network.
He disclosed the hiding places in Yemen of foreign terrorists, including one who would shortly become Mr. bin
Laden's chief lieutenant. He described the extremists' weaponry, security and violent plans for the future. He
revealed the locations of al Qaeda encampments in and around Marib, a desert region scattered with ruins of
the biblical kingdom of Sheba.
But instead of cracking down on the militants, members of Yemen's security service tipped them off. Mr. bin
Laden's acolytes grabbed their turncoat, grilled him about his treachery and made plans to send him to
Afghanistan to be killed. What should have been a triumph in a shadowy struggle against terrorism became
an intelligence coup for the terrorists. Safe in Yemen, they went on to launch a string of attacks there, from
the bombing of the USS Cole to an assault on a French oil tanker, the Limburg, this fall.
On Nov. 3, more than four years after the warning about camps in Marib, the desert region was targeted for a
lethal assault -- not by the Yemenis but by the Central Intelligence Agency. Monitoring satellite-telephone
chit-chat, the CIA tracked two Toyotas carrying suspected al Qaeda members across the desert. An
unmanned U.S. spy plane then fired a Hellfire missile that incinerated six people, including Qaed Salim Sinan
al-Harethi, a Yemeni suspected of helping organize the Cole attack.
The missile strike blew a hole in a diplomatic facade, as well. After Sept. 11, President Bush gave the world a
simple choice: "Either you are with us or you are with the terrorists." Yemen -- Mr. bin Laden's ancestral
homeland -- and other hotbeds of Islamist sentiment such as Pakistan and Saudi Arabia declared themselves
"with us." Their leaders pledged unequivocal support for the struggle against al Qaeda. But within these
nations' bureaucracies, not to mention their citizenries, the lines of loyalty are fuzzy.
The U.S.-Yemen relationship is unusually delicate today, after the U.S. asked Spain's navy last week to
intercept a North Korean ship heading for Yemen and carrying hidden Scuds. Those were the missiles made
famous in the Gulf War when Iraq, which was backed by Yemen, lobbed Scuds at Israel. After a tense
diplomatic exchange, Washington reluctantly permitted Yemen, which said it was the customer for the Korean
Scuds, to take delivery of them.
Over the past year, Yemen has detained hundreds of militant suspects, told clerics to purge extremism from
education and warned the public of terrorism's cost to its economy. State-run radio broadcasts a skit ridiculing
jihad hotheads. Yet months after Sept. 11, al Qaeda still looked to Yemen as a haven. Khalid Sheikh
Mohammed, a suspected mastermind of the hijack attacks, spoke this spring of plans to regroup in Yemen,
according to an al Qaeda operative captured by the U.S. And at Yemen's PSO, officers who monitored
extremists when the organization betrayed the informant remained at their posts.
Most unsettling of all, transcripts of monitored conversations raise the possibility that a Yemeni security
operative knew of the Sept. 11 attacks before they took place. Much mystery surrounds this security officer --
first because of indications he was the case officer who betrayed the informant, and second because he has
suddenly vanished. His family in Yemen claims Egyptian agents grabbed him. Some Islamists claim the CIA
has him now. The CIA, as is its policy, won't comment.
An account of divided allegiances within the Yemeni bureaucracy illuminates the muddle hampering the hunt
for al Qaeda. It's a story based on interviews with officials and Islamists, debriefing reports, court records and
documents on an al Qaeda computer obtained by The Wall Street Journal in Afghanistan in late 2001.
Tracking terrorists, says one Western official, "gets very messy when some of the hounds are on the same
side as the hares."

--- Page 23 ---
Page 23 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Yahya al-Mutawakil, deputy chairman of Yemen's ruling party and a former ambassador to Washington, says
that while some Yemenis support Islamist extremists for money and others out of conviction, this is "not a big
problem" at the Political Security Organization. He says the Yemeni government is committed to eliminating
terrorists but needs time "to catch such people." America, he complains, "always wants immediate action. We
will take action, but we have to do it our way."
Jihad has tenacious roots in Yemen, an impoverished land with a fragile central government, unruly tribes,
high illiteracy rates and three times as many guns as people. Tens of thousands of young Yemenis went to
Afghanistan in the 1980s as mujahedeen to fight the invading Soviet Union. They were encouraged and partly
financed by the U.S. After defeating the Red Army, they returned to join a domestic struggle for power.
Marxist South Yemen and American-backed North Yemen merged in 1990, an unhappy union that led to a
brief but vicious civil war in 1994. Yemenis who'd been to Afghanistan helped bring victory to the northern
forces of President Ali Abdulla Saleh, whose advisers include men with long ties to Mr. bin Laden. Soon, jihad
warriors from other Arab countries were pouring into Yemen.
Many were Egyptians. Ayman al-Zawahri, the Cairo surgeon who would later become Mr. bin Laden's chief
lieutenant, held meetings of his Egyptian Islamic Jihad at the Youth House, a government-run hall in the
capital of Sanaa, according to confessions of Islamists presented at a 1999 Cairo trial. The group's forger,
code-named Abu Saleh, lived in Sanaa and held classes on fabricating documents.
Yemen's tolerance of extremists infuriated Egypt, whose government waged a harsh campaign against
Islamic radicals during the 1990s. "There were always problems" with Yemeni security officials because "they
shared the same values of the people they were supposed to be arresting," says Nabil ad-Din, a former
officer in Egypt's state security.
Among the militants who flowed into Yemen in the mid-1990s was a veteran jihadi whom several Islamists
now identify as their traitor. His name is Ahmed Nasrallah.
A former Egyptian house painter, Mr. Nasrallah was widely admired in Islamist circles for his long service to
jihad and his self-taught mastery of difficult religious texts. His militant credentials traced to the 1970s, when
he was jailed in Cairo for his role in Takfir and Higra, a banned group blamed for murdering a government
minister. In the 1980s, he worked in Afghanistan, getting to know Messrs. bin Laden and Zawahri. At
Peshwar, Pakistan, near the Afghan border, he wrote a Mujahedeen Directory -- advice on travel, prayer, first
aid and the duties of jihad wives.
He arrived in Yemen about 1994. There, he and other Islamists formed a tight-knit fraternity, until betrayal
poisoned their brotherhood.
An outline of the events appears in a report stored in the al Qaeda computer the Journal obtained in Kabul.
The password-locked file describes the unmasking of a "traitor," telling how a trusted militant had offered his
services to Yemen's PSO intelligence agency. The turncoat was discovered, says the unsigned May 1998
report, when PSO members betrayed the betrayer, handing his debriefing notes to the very people on whom
he was informing.
Besides the computer report, the Journal has obtained 12 pages of the informant's handwritten notes from a
former jihad activist. Neither the debriefing file nor the computer report identifies the "traitor." Among those
who do is Hani al-Sebai, an Islamist now living in London. He says that a statement circulating in jihadi circles
in 1998 detailed the case of treachery and named Mr. Nasrallah as the traitor -- also giving his alias, Abu
Ibrahim al Masry. Mr. Sebai says he was shocked because he had always thought of Mr. Nasrallah as a
devout believer in the cause.
Another Islamist now in London, Yasser al-Sirri, had the same reaction. "Everybody trusted" Mr. Nasrallah, he
says. "Nobody could believe he would work for security services. It came as a big surprise."
Mr. Sirri says he has seen a videotape of the informant's debriefing, and while its quality is poor, he can
identify Mr. Nasrallah on it.
Against all odds, the informant in Yemen, after having been turned over to the militants, gave them the slip as
they were preparing to ship him to Afghanistan. According to a lawyer for Mr. Nasrallah, he is now in his
native Egypt. A Journal reporter spent a month there trying unsuccessfully to gain an interview with him.
Officials of the Egyptian government, which initially jailed Mr. Nasrallah after his return from abroad, decline
to comment.
It's not clear why Mr. Nasrallah would snitch. People who knew him say he sometimes complained about the
penny-pinching and uncertainties of life in the Islamist underground. They also say he was under pressure
from his wife, who, after years on the run from Egyptian police, wanted to be able to return to Egypt and live
there safely.

--- Page 24 ---
Page 24 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Early 1998 was a critical time for Mr. bin Laden's organization. Still a fledgling group, al Qaeda allied that year
with Egyptian Islamic Jihad and other militant groups. It adopted Afghanistan as its base, began expanding in
Europe, and issued a manifesto declaring the murder of Americans its principal aim.
Word of a traitor in Yemen prompted some militants to leave that country. Among them, the ace forger Abu
Saleh moved to Italy -- where his presence would eventually prove a treasure trove to counterterrorism
eavesdroppers.
Hundreds of others remained in Yemen. Some played a logistical role in the bombings of U.S. embassies in
Kenya and Tanzania: A Yemeni safehouse served as a relay station for al Qaeda messages. In December
1998 the Yemeni government's relations with the militants soured markedly, after a band of them kidnapped
16 foreign tourists, four of whom died in a bungled rescue attempt.
The U.S., eager to encourage this toughening, pursued a policy of engagement with Yemen. The Navy
started using the Yemeni port of Aden for refueling. But in October 2000, a boat packed with explosives
rammed a Navy ship, the Cole, and 17 American sailors died.
Yemen pledged full cooperation in finding the culprits. It put the PSO in charge of liaison with the FBI, which
sent more than a hundred agents to investigate the Cole attack. But the lead FBI investigator, John P. O'Neill,
complained bitterly of restricted access to witnesses and evidence. And America's then-ambassador, Barbara
Bodine, after receiving complaints from Yemeni authorities, refused Mr. O'Neill's request to re-enter Yemen
after a trip home. Mr. O'Neill later quit the FBI to become head of security at the World Trade Center, where
he died on Sept. 11. Former FBI Director Louis Freeh says cooperation with Yemen was "sometimes very
difficult" but generally "fairly good."
Fighting terrorism was a new and often uncomfortable duty for the Political Security Organization, staffed
mostly by ex-military men with no law-enforcement background. Its main job had been eliminating threats to
Yemen's long-serving president, Mr. Saleh. U.S. officials who've worked in Yemen say some senior PSO
officers took the antiterrorism task seriously, but presided over an outfit that tended to see Islamists as useful
allies against domestic opponents.
A militant with particularly close ties to the PSO, according to an Italian police report, was the forger Abu
Saleh (no relation to Yemen's president). A confidential Italian report, compiled this summer after a lengthy
investigation into Islamists in Italy and their contacts abroad, says the PSO provided "logistical and
intelligence assistance" to Egyptian Jihad.
Yemeni President Saleh has acknowledged the problems, signing a decree earlier this year to set up a new
National Security Agency that would counter the influence of extremist sympathizers in the PSO. Months
later, though, the project appears stillborn.
Perhaps wary of the PSO, the informant whom militants identify as Mr. Nasrallah first tried to cut a deal with
Yemen's interior ministry, which was more focused on law enforcement and had deported many jihadis for
immigration violations. The report on the al Qaeda computer says the traitor met the interior minister three
times in a Sanaa hotel but, unhappy with the minister's cautious approach, took his secrets to the Political
Security Organization.
"The traitor met the [PSO] deputy director and offered himself and his mission," says the report. It says the
deputy director kept him waiting in a reception room for four hours, using the delay to contact a security
officer with close Islamist ties, whom he asked to serve as the traitor's case officer.
"This man wants to trick his friends," the report quotes the deputy director as telling the case officer. "Find out
what he wants and warn your friends."
The PSO deputy director responsible for monitoring extremists was Mohammed al-Surmi. He holds the same
post today. A Western official describes him as "the great protector," whose "name comes up constantly as
someone linked to the wrong sort of people." However, an American who has worked with him says Mr.
Surmi has a good record of helping the U.S. and shows scant sign of Islamist zeal. Indeed, his home and
those of several other security officials were attacked earlier this year by a group claiming links to al Qaeda.
In a brief conversation with a reporter, Mr. Surmi, a heavyset, brusque man in his 50s, said he didn't recall Mr.
Nasrallah. Saying he couldn't talk without authorization, he abruptly ended a meeting in a Sanaa hotel.
The case officer assigned to handle the informant debriefed him in a rented flat. It was equipped with a
hidden video camera, says the report on the al Qaeda computer, installed by Abu Saleh, the forger. A grainy
videotape of the debriefing, according to Mr. Sirri, shows Mr. Nasrallah spewing out facts about terrorist cells
in Yemen.

--- Page 25 ---
Page 25 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The informant chided Yemeni authorities for not being tough enough on Islamists and urged them to follow
the Egyptian example of mass arrests. In handwritten notes, he ticked off the hiding places of nearly a dozen
radicals, including Dr. Zawahri, who he said was in the southern Yemen town of Taez, not in Afghanistan as
widely believed at the time. Tipped off, Dr. Zawahri fled to Afghanistan.
Boasting a vast knowledge of the jihad movement, the "traitor" offered to travel to Afghanistan to gather
intelligence on Mr. bin Laden's organization. He also proposed going to the mountains of southern Yemen to
spy on a band of militants led by Zein al-Abidine al-Mihdar, says the report in the al Qaeda computer. Mr.
Mihdar, whom Yemen later executed for the tourist kidnapping, was a relative of one of the Sept. 11
hijackers, Khalid Almidhar.
To help prove his credentials, the informant wrote an account of a journey he had taken as a guest of
militants. His handwritten notes described an arduous drive through Marib, the eventual site of the missile
attack by an American Predator drone. During his trip across this desolate region, inhabited by heavily armed
and feuding tribes, the driver kept changing direction to lose any pursuing cars. The informant wrote that
others in the car covered their faces, but he knew one as a former aide to Mr. bin Laden in Pakistan.
"I finally reached the gathering point, which included about 80 to 90 armed people, in addition to other groups
distributed around for guarding, monitoring and other purposes," he wrote. He said a leader explained plans
to declare a full-scale jihad, in the hope that Yemen could become a new Afghanistan.
"This is a serious incident unprecedented in the field of Islamic activities in Yemen," warned the informant,
who said the security agency should work to halt extremists' funding, particularly from Saudis, and should
infiltrate the militants' group.
Instead, it alerted them. The unmasking of the "traitor" led to bitter arguments over how to deal with the man.
The question: Execute him straightaway, or send him to Afghanistan first?
Favoring fast execution, says Mr. Sebai in London, was a group of militants who abducted the informant.
They chained him in a house for weeks and extracted a confession, which they filmed. Mr. Sebai says the
long captivity infuriated other militants, who sought to avoid trouble with Yemeni authorities. They wanted to
pack the traitor off to Afghanistan, where confederates would squeeze him for information, and then kill him.
The report on the Kabul computer appears written by someone in the more-cautious faction. "The advice of
the people here is to send him to you. Open the case and then close it," said the report. Calling the man
highly "dangerous . . . because of the volume of information he knows," it urged a "quick decision . . . so that
we don't lose an opportunity that came to us on a gold platter." The note requested that the "contractor" --
Mr. bin Laden -- send cash to pay for getting the captive to Afghanistan.
The militants bought him a one-way ticket there, according to a Cairo-based Islamist who lived in Yemen at
the time, and promised him safe passage. But the informant feared a trap and managed to flee Yemen for
Egypt, where authorities threw him in jail.
Now, what appears to be an eerie postscript to these events has emerged. In a report earlier this year to a
Milan prosecutor, Italian counterterrorism police provided transcripts of conversations between Abu Saleh,
the forger who had moved to Italy, and Yemenis with links to the Political Security Organization.
Among those Yemenis was Abdul Salam Abdul Rahman al-Hilah. A 34-year-old security officer and
businessman, Mr. Hilah is the PSO operative who mysteriously vanished in Cairo three months ago. And Mr.
Hilah also was the case officer who handled the informant and betrayed him to the militants, according to Mr.
Sirri in London. Mr. Sirri says Mr. Hilah is recognizable on the videotape of "the traitor's" debriefing.
The Italian counterterrorism report, citing information from foreign intelligence agencies, describes Mr. Hilah
as a PSO section chief "suspected of having harbored known terrorists belonging to Osama bin Laden's
group."
Traveling on a Yemeni diplomatic passport, Mr. Hilah visited Italy at least twice. In August 2000, Mr. Hilah
traveled via Rome to Bologna, where the forger Abu Saleh (whose real name is Abdel Kader al-Sayed)
picked him up at the airport. As they drove toward a sports center for an Islamist gathering, his host asked
about militant camps in Yemen. "They are in good hands. They are protected," Mr. Hilah replied, according to
a transcript from Italian police who had bugged Abu Saleh's Citroen.
Referring to an unspecified "program" designed by someone who "is a madman but . . . a genius," Mr. Hilah
predicted that "he will turn everyone to ice." According to the transcript, the Yemeni -- in oblique comments
peppered with references to aircraft, the sky and the U.S. -- also spoke of a "terrifying" event that "all
newspapers will write about." He said that "in the sky in international territory . . . the fire is already lit and is
just waiting for the wind."

--- Page 26 ---
Page 26 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Six months later, in February 2001, Abu Saleh phoned Mr. Hilah's number in Yemen and spoke to his
younger brother, Abdul Wahab al-Hilah. "I heard you were going to America," said Abu Saleh. "I'm sorry to
say we're not able to get in," replied Abdul Wahab. "It is our most important wish and our big target."
The conversations, too unspecific to set off urgent alarms when recorded, have been examined minutely by
American intelligence officials since Sept. 11, 2001, say Italian officials. Under scrutiny is the possibility of a
link between the hijack plot and operatives in Yemen's PSO intelligence agency.
Abu Saleh, who fled Italy a few weeks before Sept. 11, 2001, is thought to have been killed in fighting in
Afghanistan later. A radical mosque in Milan held a memorial event. Mr. Hilah continued his work in Yemen,
combining his security-service role with business ventures as sheikh, or leader, of a Yemeni tribe and as a
member of the ruling party.
This September, Mr. Hilah flew to Cairo to discuss a construction project with an engineering firm. He phoned
home after arriving on what was to be a brief trip. That was 11 weeks ago. His brother, Abdul Wahab, later
charged that Egyptian intelligence had abducted him.
Egyptian authorities denied knowledge of his whereabouts. They said that four days after his arrival, Mr. Hilah
left Cairo on a private plane bound for Azerbaijan. Mr. Sirri, the Islamist in London, and Arabic-language
media reports contend the plane was American and took Mr. Hilah to a U.S. interrogation center.
While American officials won't comment on that, within six weeks, the U.S. caught up with two important
Yemen-based al Qaeda operatives it had vainly sought for years. One was Abd al-Rahim al-Nashiri, who is
suspected of involvement in the African embassy bombings, the attack on the Cole and this fall's bombing of
the French oil tanker. The U.S. grabbed him at an undisclosed location. The other suspect was Mr. Harethi,
killed by America's Hellfire missile attack in the Yemeni desert.
On the same day as the Hellfire strike, unidentified gunmen opened fire at Sanaa airport on a helicopter
owned by America's Hunt Oil Co., which pumps oil in the Marib desert region. Two Americans were slightly
injured. The area around the airport is controlled by the vanished PSO officer's Yemeni tribe.
As for Mr. Nasrallah, the man identified by several jihadis as the "traitor" who barely escaped with his life, a
former inmate of an Egyptian prison tells of seeing him naked and with a shaved head, and of being told Mr.
Nasrallah was undergoing rough interrogation. The Egyptians freed him after 14 months. He sued the
government for wrongful imprisonment and won about $400, his lawyer says. Now nearly penniless, the
lawyer adds, Mr. Nasrallah lives in a garbage-strewn district of Cairo, eking out a living as a small-time trader.
