For Stock Listings, the U.S. Pull Gets Weaker---Foreign Companies Cite Disclosure,Say an ADR Is No Longer Crucial;Why Porsche Hit the Brak...
World Stock Markets
For Stock Listings, the U.S. Pull Gets Weaker --- Foreign Companies Cite Disclosure, Say an ADR Is
No Longer Crucial; Why Porsche Hit the Brake Pedal
By Craig Karmin and Kate Kelly
1,579 words
12 November 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
IS THE U.S. STOCK MARKET -- long the world's top destination for corporate-share listings -- losing some of
its appeal to foreign companies?
Throughout the 1990s, European, Asian and Latin American firms flocked to U.S. stock exchanges in hopes
of expanding their investor base and raising their profiles in the world's largest economy.
Most foreign firms list in the U.S. by offering American depositary receipts, which are securities held by a U.S.
bank and represent the actual company shares. ADRs provide a dollar-based security for U.S. investors who
might otherwise have avoided foreign firms because of currency or other risks. Big European companies,
meanwhile, queued up for a U.S. share in order to make all-stock acquisitions of U.S. companies.
But like the rest of the initial-public-offering market, ADR offers peaked in 2000, when foreign firms raised $30
billion in U.S. listings. Last year, that number plunged 70%, to $8.4 billion, and for the first 10 months of this
year, the figure has tumbled again, to $4.4 billion, according to Citigroup Inc., which tracks the annual figures.
(The overall IPO market is down 77% since 2000, in terms of capital raised, according to Dealogic LLC.)
The fallout from the ADR slowdown could be painful, hitting in particular the securities firms and stock
exchanges that profited from the new listings and from merger activity that now may not materialize. More
broadly, the pullback among foreign companies reflects a new queasiness with U.S. business, following a
spate of corporate scandals that have sent some foreign investors fleeing as well.
"Not along ago, you had to be listed in the U.S. to be seen as credible, and you had to use U.S. accounting
methods to be seen as credible," says John Wilson, a partner at the law firm Shearman & Sterling, which
works with foreign firms that list in New York. "This is changing."
But while most analysts expect the domestic IPO market to pick up once the economy improves, the outlook
for ADRs is less certain. Some experts think that even when better economic times return, ADR issuance
may have trouble returning to previous levels as a result of a global slowdown in merger activity and
disenchantment among companies that already list in the U.S.
New corporate-governance legislation in the U.S. is exacerbating the concerns of some foreign firms. The
Sarbanes-Oxley Act, enacted in late July, clashes with foreign corporate laws in many cases. The new
legislation includes provisions requiring chief executive officers to accept personal criminal liability in the U.S.
for the validity of their companies' account, banning company loans to corporate insiders, and establishing
tougher rules for audit committees.
German law, by contrast, dictates that a pair of corporate boards of directors certify financial results, not the
CEO and the chief financial officer, as the U.S. law requires.
As a result, German auto maker Porsche AG last month said it would postpone its planned New York Stock
Exchange listing. "We see no sense that the CEO or the [chief financial officer] have to swear alone to the
accuracy of the accounting," said Manfred Ayasse, head of investor relations for the company. "And for
Porsche, we have also [asked] the question, `Does it really make sense to be listed on the NYSE?' There is
no real pressure for Porsche [to have] the listing."
The Japanese brokerage firm Daiwa Securities Group Inc. also recently postponed a Big Board listing.
Because it isn't yet clear precisely how Sarbanes-Oxley differs from Japanese law, "We didn't know what the
rules of the game we'd be playing" would be, said Keiko Toshero, head of investor relations for Daiwa in
Tokyo. "So we wanted to know what we were getting into" before listing in the U.S.

--- Page 73 ---
Page 73 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Despite those cases, not everyone is convinced that the ADR slowdown represents much more than a natural
response to tough market conditions world-wide. "We see that ADR use as a capital-raising tool has fallen in
line with capital raising across the globe," says Kurt Schneiber, global managing director for ADRs at Citibank
in New York.
He points out that demand for a U.S. listing has receded in part because, as in the U.S., foreign companies
overinvested during the boom years. Because businesses are spending less, the need for new cash likewise
has declined, obviating the desire to launch new public offerings. Even companies that need to raise money
have found that lower interest rates often make debt offerings a more-attractive alternative.
Mr. Schneiber says he believes the number of deals will pick up as the economy rebounds. He notes the
current ADR pipeline shows especially robust interest from East Asian firms in China, Taiwan and South
Korea. "There is still a strong incentive to have an ADR," he says.
Yet even among the companies that already have done ADRs, the reviews aren't universally good. Some
foreign issuers haven't seen the trading volume they anticipated in their U.S. listing, says Benn Steil, a senior
fellow at the Council on Foreign Relations who studies market structure.
LVMH Moet Hennessy Louis Vuitton SA, the French luxury-goods firm, cited precisely that concern when it
decided to delist its shares from the Nasdaq Stock Market last month. "The ADR programme represents a
tiny proportion of equity capital with very thin daily volumes traded," the company said in a statement last
month.
Both the NYSE and Nasdaq declined to comment.

Cisco Sparks Technology Purge,As LSI Logic, Nvidia Also Sink
Abreast of the Market
Cisco Sparks Technology Purge, As LSI Logic, Nvidia Also Sink
By Shaheen Pasha
Dow Jones Newswires
957 words
8 November 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- As stocks fell sharply, with investors taking profits from Wednesday's Federal
Reserve-inspired rally, declining technology shares dragged down the broader market in response to Cisco
Systems' dismal fiscal second-quarter outlook.
Cisco shares fell 4.7%, or 61 cents, to $12.35 on Nasdaq. The company's revenue warning sent ripples
throughout the technology sector, with semiconductors and communications stocks leading the decliners.
Cisco is one of the biggest buyers of communications chips, so its warning spurred fears that estimates for
chip companies may be at risk.
Shares of all 16 members of the Philadelphia Stock Exchange Semiconductor Index closed lower, with the
index down 8.3%, or 27.22 points, at 302.62. Some notable decliners included LSI Logic, off 13%, or 90
cents, at 6.30, and Nvidia down 10%, or 1.63, at 14.55 on Nasdaq.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average fell 2.1%, or 184.77 points, to 8586.24, while the Nasdaq Composite Index
fell 3%, or 42.28, to 1376.71 -- near its session lows -- and the S&P 500 index fell 2.3%, or 21.11 points, to
902.65.
Charles Ryan, equity analyst at BB&T Asset Management, said tech companies have had a sharp run in
recent days and have now reached valuations that are no longer attractive. He said a breather for the group
was to be expected and Cisco's guidance simply clarified that tech stocks may have come too far, too fast.
Mr. Ryan added that the market has run out of catalysts with third-quarter earnings, the elections and the Fed
meeting out of the way, which will likely bring in a wave of consolidation. He said the market should remain in
a tight trading range ahead of the holiday sales season, which will be the next big catalyst to determine the
market's direction.
"The consumer has been carrying us for so long that there's fear that their shoulders are getting tired," Mr.
Ryan said. "The Street thinks it's going to be a horrible Christmas, and sales will be the next big thing we
watch."
Yahoo shed 10%, or 1.78, to 15.60 on Nasdaq after Prudential Securities downgraded the stock to hold from
buy, saying it is fairly valued at current levels. Additionally, Softbank sold some of its shares in Yahoo through
its U.S. subsidiary, lowering its stake to 4% from 7%.
Investors knocked down shares of home-building companies after Credit Suisse First Boston downgraded the
group to market weight from overweight, saying that increasing data indicate the housing market is
decelerating. Among the stocks on the NYSE taking a hit, D.R. Horton dropped 10%, or 2.04, to 18.16, while
Centex lost 6.3%, or 3.04, to 44.92.
October same-store sales were a mixed bag. Shares of Talbots rose 3.1%, or 89 cents, to 29.39 after the
apparel retailer reported better-than-expected October same-store sales growth of 1.7% and boosted its
third-quarter earnings guidance.
Kohl's climbed 3.4%, or 2.06, to 62 after it said its October same-store sales jumped 18%, higher than the
5.8% Wall Street had been expecting. Kohl's said the strong performance gave it renewed confidence in its
original third-quarter earnings guidance of 37 cents a share.
Abercrombie & Fitch gained 16%, or 2.88, to 20.79 on news that it expects to beat its prior third-quarter
per-share earnings forecast of 41 cents to 45 cents, due to a 3% decline in October same-store sales,
narrower than the 6.2% drop analysts had expected.

Art-supply retailers tumbled, with Michaels Stores down 24%, or 10.78, at 33.90 after the company cut its
earnings outlook for the year by five cents to $2.05 a share on weak October sales data.

forfeit $75 million in fees it collected that are now sought by plaintiffs in a class-action suit.

ASIA/PACIFIC
Economic Slump
Is Pinching Wages
Across the Region
Salary increases slowed at major companies around the Asia-Pacific region this year, as the global economic
downturn began to squeeze wages in developed economies. Workers in Hong Kong, Singapore and Japan
had the smallest salary increases in a survey of 11 economies by Hewitt Associates Asia Pacific, which
looked at salaries for five categories of workers at 947 companies in the service, manufacturing and high-tech
sectors. At the bottom of the list was Hong Kong, where wages of manual laborers -- the lowest-wage
category -- increased less than 1%. Wages of Hong Kong's senior managers, the highest-wage category,
rose less than 2%, according to the survey. That compares with increases in 2001 of 3% and 4%,
respectively. Developing countries such as Indonesia, China and the Philippines showed the largest pay
increases in 2002, averaging between 6.4% and 9.7%. That compares with pay increases in 2001 of between
6.7% and 11.4%.
The human-resources consulting firm polled human-resources departments for 2002 salary budgets and
2003 budget projections for the five wage categories. Most results suggest optimism that pay will rise in 2003
-- from a 2.1% projected increase for Hong Kong senior management to nearly a 14% rise for the same wage
category in Indonesia. The survey included Australia, China, Hong Kong, Indonesia, Japan, South Korea,
Malaysia, the Philippines, Singapore, Taiwan and Thailand.
Taiwan Reviews Bonus Programs
Taiwan's government has begun holding hearings about the unique employee bonus share plans used by the
island's technology companies, in one of the first signs that regulators could change the system. Under
Taiwan's accounting rules, companies can give stock to employees from retained earnings without counting
them as an expense on the income statement. U.S. rules, by contrast, treat such awards as a cost. Also,
unlike the stock options popular in the U.S., Taiwan's employee bonus shares can be sold immediately upon
issue and are taxed at par value, which is just 10 New Taiwan dollars (30 U.S. cents) a share, even if the
market price is several times higher.
SingTel Reports 26% Earnings Drop
Singapore Telecommunications said profit for the quarter ended Sept. 30 fell 26% from a year earlier as
overseas investments led by a one-time gain at Belgian associate company Belgacom failed to offset
shrinkage in its Singapore home market. The quarterly profit of 415 million Singapore dollars (US$235 million)
was lower than expected, mainly because of tax levels that surprised analysts. A consensus of analysts
polled earlier had forecast earnings of S$541 million, but they had wrongly assumed that a S$123 million tax
credit registered in the first quarter would be repeated. Investors reacted by selling. SingTel fell five
Singapore cents, or 3.5%, to S$1.36 in Singapore International Monetary Exchange trading.
BRIEFLY:
-- Retail sales in Hong Kong fell for a seventh month in a row in September as consumers remained
pessimistic about their personal finances, even as the unemployment rate steadily improves. Hong Kong's
retail sales fell a worse-than-expected 4.6% in September from a year earlier.

the pace of growth slowed from September, due to declining exports to other major trading partners, the
government said. The Ministry of Finance said exports last month grew 0.6% from a year earlier to US$11.5
billion, up from US$11.28 billion in September.

EUROPE/AFRICA
Monti Unveils EU Antitrust Reforms
European Union Competition Commissioner Mario Monti released details of what he called "radical" new
reforms. Until now, the commission has taken a maximum of five months to review cases. In the future, Mr.
Monti said antitrust officials would allow companies to obtain an extension of up to seven weeks, or to "stop
the clock." The commissioner also pledged to increase openness. Companies have often complained that
they didn't understand the EU's reasoning for blocking their deals. In response, Mr. Monti said he will allow
companies to defend themselves and confront challengers to the merger "at the earliest stages of the
investigation." Mr. Monti also said he wants consumers to have a bigger voice in merger decisions. He will set
up a "consumer liaison" to ease the involvement of consumer associations.
Miner Gold Fields Says Profit Tripled
South Africa's second-largest gold miner Gold Fields said its fiscal first-quarter profit more than trebled from
a year earlier, but the result still fell short of expectations. Year-on-year, profits boomed due to increased
production and higher gold prices. But, on a quarterly basis, earnings were damped by a stronger rand,
foreign-currency losses and modest drops in production at the company's Beatrix and Driefontein operations.
Gold Fields books most of its costs in local currency but sells in dollars. Net profit, excluding gains and losses
on financial instruments and foreign debt, rose to 735 million rand ($74.5 million), from 203 million rand a year
earlier. Quarter-on-quarter, earnings fell 13% to 735 million rand from 845 million rand at the end of June.
South African mining companies typically compare their results on a quarterly basis.
BRIEFLY:
-- Reuters Group plans to cut some jobs in its news divisions, a company spokeswoman said. In July,
Reuters posted its first quarterly loss since it became a publicly listed company in 1984 and said its core
business remained challenging. The company said it expected to know where and how big the cuts would be
by the end of the year.
-- Societe Generale will cut around 250 jobs from its cash equities and mergers-and-acquisitions operations,
in a further sign that the bloodletting among investment banks in Europe is far from over. The planned job
cuts represent 14% of the 1,800 employed by the French bank's investment-banking unit across the world.
-- The EU Commission kept its forecast unchanged at a subdued range of 0.2% to 0.5% for third- and
fourth-quarter euro-zone economic growth.

THE AMERICAS
Court Frees Ex-Cali Drug Lord
A Colombian court ordered one of the country's former top drug lords released from prison, as investigators
scrambled to find evidence to support further charges, and possibly his extradition to the U.S. Gilberto
Rodriguez Orejuela and his brother Miguel once controlled the Cali drug cartel, an empire that moved
multiton cocaine shipments across the globe -- including to the U.S. In yesterday's ruling, Judge Luz Amanda
Moncada forced the government to release Gilberto Rodriguez as Judge Pedro Suarez had ordered last
week. Judge Suarez also had ordered Miguel Rodriguez freed, but Judge Moncada ruled that he must stay in
prison to serve out a four-year additional sentence for a bribery charge. Unless U.S. and Colombian
investigators file additional charges against Gilberto Rodriguez, he was expected to leave prison within hours.
Antitrust Panel Chides Telmex
Mexico's antitrust commission ruled that Telefonos de Mexico used monopolistic practices in its local
telephone-service operations. In a resolution released Wednesday, the commission ordered the phone
company also known as Telmex to stop strong-arming the competition when doling out local phone lines and
services. The commission also said it will impose fines on the company. Telmex is a former state-held
monopoly that controls more than 90% of Mexico's roughly 15 million phone lines. A spokeswoman yesterday
said the company had no comment.

Defensive Puts See Brisk TradingIn Technology, Financial Sectors
Options Report
Defensive Puts See Brisk Trading In Technology, Financial Sectors
By Kopin Tan
Dow Jones Newswires
438 words
8 November 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
C11
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- Defensive puts traded actively in the financial sector, where investors wondered what affect
lower interest rates might have on banks' ability to lend profitably, and in technology stocks, where Cisco
Systems' guarded revenue forecast deferred hopes of a speedy recovery.
But optimistic investors saw stocks' pullback as inevitable after the recent run-up and took advantage of the
decline in stock prices to buy calls or call spreads to position themselves for what they hope will be a
continuing rally. In stocks where investors thought the options premium was rich, they sold puts to reap the
premium and set target prices at which they would be willing to buy stock. A call is an option to buy a security
at a specific price; a put is an option to sell a security at a specific price.
International Business Machines' options traded briskly after a Morgan Stanley analyst suggested that
investors consider reducing any "overweight" positions in IBM, pointing out, among other things, that Big Blue
could issue as much as $3 billion in debt during the fourth quarter.
In 4 p.m. New York Stock Exchange composite trading, IBM shares fell $2.59, or 3.2%, to $78.95 each. The
technology- sector bellwether's November 80 puts traded 9,248 contracts, compared with open interest of
12,599 contracts, and rose $1.10 to $2.75 each at the Pacific Exchange. IBM's November 80 calls traded
6,276 contracts, compared with open interest of 11,652 contracts, and fell $1.60 to $1.60 each at the Pacific.
During the first full session after the Federal Reserve cut benchmark interest rates, bank stocks fell as
implied volatility in the sector rose. While puts traded actively in the sector, they were particularly noticeable
in J.P. Morgan Chase. Grappling with credit losses and lackluster capital-markets revenue, the banking
company continued to find itself the target of speculation, including rumors that it suffered losses from gold
trading. A J.P. Morgan spokesman denied those rumors.
In 4 p.m. Big Board composite trading, J.P. Morgan shares fell $1.46, or 6.6%, to $20.60 each. Its December
22.50 puts, for instance, traded 2,364 contracts, compared with open interest of 5,805 contracts, and gained
$1.10 to $2.85 each at the Philadelphia Stock Exchange. Its January 20 calls traded 2,019 contracts,
compared with open interest of 8,968 contracts, and declined $1.20 to $2.30 each at the International
Securities Exchange.

Unsettling Scenario:Inside the Fed,Deflation DrawsA Closer Look---Stumped for a Cure, OfficialsStudy How to Keep PricesFrom Falling in Firs...
Unsettling Scenario: Inside the Fed, Deflation Draws A Closer Look --- Stumped for a Cure, Officials
Study How to Keep Prices From Falling in First Place --- A Dollar Worth 98.4 Cents
By Greg Ip
2,522 words
6 November 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Alan Greenspan and his colleagues at the Federal Reserve have spent their professional lives fighting
inflation. But in the fall of 1999, central bank officials gathered at a country inn in Woodstock, Vt., to talk
about the opposite: What would they do if faced with deflation, or widespread falling prices, and they already
had cut interest rates to zero?
Deflation is dangerous because it makes it hard to boost the economy by cutting interest rates, and because
it makes debt, now at a postwar high in the U.S., harder to repay.
At Woodstock, researchers brainstormed about possible ways the Fed could spur spending, such as adding a
magnetic strip to dollar bills that would cause their value to drop the longer they stayed in one's wallet.
At the time the chance of deflation in the U.S seemed remote. Inflation was low, but the economy was
booming and the Fed had lifted short-term interest rates above 5%.
Today, deflation no longer seems so remote.
Prices of consumer goods, as opposed to services, are falling for the first time since 1960. By the Fed's
preferred measure, overall inflation was just 1.8% in the year through August. Fed policy makers have cut
short-term interest rates to a 41-year low of 1.75%, and investors expect them to cut rates again in coming
months to as low as 1.25% -- perhaps starting at their meeting today.
The U.S. economy is struggling with the collapse of a gigantic stock mania. Sinking prices for telecom
services, to name just one conspicuous example, are already making it harder for some businesses to
support their heavy debts.
Overseas, Japan is in its fourth year of declining prices even with interest rates near zero, a result of a
decade of economic stagnation that followed the bursting of its real-estate and stock bubble. China has
experienced intermittent deflation since 1999 and a few economists think Germany may be close. The
International Monetary Fund projects that inflation in industrialized countries this year will hit 1.4%, its lowest
level in more than 40 years.
Fed officials and most private economists still think deflation is highly unlikely. While the Fed is expected to
cut rates either today or in December, that's more out of concern about slow growth, not deflation. Most Fed
officials feel that the 1.75 points of rate-cutting room they have left is plenty to get the economy growing
briskly again.
Still, they are all thinking more about deflation. "Whereas this possibility wasn't even on radar screens in past
recoveries, it is in the range of plausible risks now," Al Broaddus, president of the Federal Reserve Bank of
Richmond said last month. "We need to be alert to this risk."
Shouldn't consumers be happy when prices fall? That depends on what causes deflation. When technological
progress leads to rising productivity, or output per hour of work, the economy can produce more each year
with the same workers and equipment, and companies can cut prices while increasing sales. Between 1865
and 1879, manufacturing output rose 6% a year while prices fell by 3% a year. Wages were basically
unchanged.
Deflation is more worrisome when it results from declining demand, as it did during the Great Depression
when prices tumbled 24% between 1929 and 1933, bankruptcies mounted, thousands of banks failed and the
unemployment rate hit 25%.
It is a central banker's nightmare. William McDonough, the 68-year-old president of the Federal Reserve
Bank of New York, recalls his father taking him in the late 1930s to see breadlines and "people in jail who

--- Page 83 ---
Page 83 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
were there for stealing food for their families." The Depression, he said in a speech in March, "was a very real
thing to the people who created the Federal Reserve's mandate and they never wanted it to happen again."
The 1930s demonstrate that deflation is most dangerous when debt burdens are heavy, as they were in the
1920s and are today. "When a deflation occurs . . . without any great volume of debt, the resulting evils are
much less," Yale University economist Irving Fisher wrote in 1933. "It is the combination of both . . . which
works the greatest havoc."
A company borrows on the assumption that rising sales volumes and prices will enable it to repay the debt.
As prices fall, it becomes more difficult to make payments on debt. A company may be forced to cut wages or
jobs, or go bankrupt. The same applies to households that suffer falling income and have to cut spending to
service debts. If too many businesses and households do this, the result is depressed demand that fuels
further deflation. "The more the economic boat tips, the more it tends to tip," Mr. Fisher wrote.
Another risk from deflation is that consumers may delay spending because they expect goods and services to
get cheaper. However, there's little evidence that has ever happened -- even in Japan.
More troublesome is that deflation makes it impossible for a central bank seeking to jump-start the economy
to get inflation-adjusted interest rates -- the ones that economically matter -- below zero. (When inflation is
at 3% and the Fed cuts rates to 2%, the inflation-adjusted rate is minus 1%.) For that reason, modern central
bankers consider a low inflation rate -- typically between 1% and 3% -- ideal. The U.S. is now in the lower
part of that range.
Most economists say it would take another massive shock, such as the Sept. 11 terrorist attacks, or demand
persistently growing slower than supply to create so much excess capacity that prices fall. Macroeconomic
Advisers LLC, a St. Louis, Mo., consulting firm, estimates the economy would have to get so bad over the
next four years that it pushes unemployment up to 7.5% from the current 5.7%. "It's a pretty ugly scenario
that's required," says the firm's chairman, Joel Prakken.
The odds of deflation also are damped by the fact that inflation has been low and stable for years. As long
as businesses and consumers expect that to continue, and set prices and wages accordingly, the deflation
risks are diminished.
But a few economists think current economic circumstances are tilted toward deflation. The 43% plunge in
stock prices since early 2000 will pressure households for years to spend less and save more. Should home
values also see a major decline, families' ability to repay mortgages and spend on other items would fall
further.
Deflation doubters say the U.S. won't suffer deflation again because the Fed won't let it. "There's a much
exaggerated concern about deflation. It's not a serious prospect," asserts Nobel Laureate Milton Friedman,
now at the Hoover Institution. Mr. Friedman, the best-known proponent of the view that prices rise and fall
with the quantity of money in circulation, says, "The cure for deflation is very simple. Print money." At the
moment, with the money supply growing briskly, he argues, "Inflation is still a much more serious problem
than deflation."
Scholars blame the deflation in the 1930s on the Fed's refusal to accept responsibility for maintaining stable
prices. The Fed instead was focused on keeping the dollar's value in gold fixed, says Mr. Friedman, who
adds, "Today's Federal Reserve is not going to repeat the mistakes of the Federal Reserve of the 1930s."
Since at least 1997, the Fed's professional staff has been studying deflation and the problem of how a
central bank stimulates the economy once interest rates are already at zero. Mr. Greenspan and other Fed
policy makers have been thinking more about it lately. They devoted a chunk of their January policy meeting
to the subject. New Fed governor Ben Bernanke, a Princeton University economist who has studied the
1930s, is to give a speech on deflation later this month called "Making Sure It Doesn't Happen Here."
Deflation today would likely be more serious than when prices declined in 1949 or 1955 because Americans
are so much more in hock. Total debt, excluding the federal government, now equals 158% of gross national
product. The last time debt rose to that level was in the late 1920s. Indeed, both the 1920s and 1990s saw a
surge in new forms of debt-financed consumption -- installment plans in the 1920s, "cash out" mortgage
refinancings in the 1990s.
But the fact that consumer debt has doubled since the 1950s to 90% of personal income isn't of great
concern to Fed officials. They attribute it to a more sophisticated financial industry that has made credit easier
to get, and to the rise in home ownership which means many people have substituted mortgage payments for
rent.
During the Depression, mortgage default was a cause of considerable hardship, because of the structure of
the mortgage market, says Kent Colton of Harvard University's Joint Center for Housing Studies.

--- Page 84 ---
Page 84 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Homeowners generally had to pay the entire principal back in five to 10 years. If they couldn't refinance, they
defaulted. At the depths of the Depression, some 40% of mortgages were in default. The defaults and
declining home values also contributed to the failure of thousands of banks and thrifts.
Now, thanks to mortgage insurance and the creation of mortgage agencies Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac,
homeowners can pay their mortgages down over 30 years and easily refinance to take advantage of lower
interest rates. While delinquencies on both credit-card and mortgage payments are on the rise, the problems
have been concentrated among subprime borrowers, or those with poor credit.
The corporate debt burden, which has also doubled since the 1950s to 89% of revenue, is more worrisome.
One indicator of investors' concern about companies' ability to pay back the debt is that yields on
medium-quality corporate bonds are 2.7 percentage points higher than those on safe Treasurys -- the widest
spread since 1986. "Companies simply aren't coming up with the cash flow they thought they would when
they took on the debt," says John Lonski, chief economist at Moody's Investors Service.
Automobiles illustrate the pressures. In the 1950s, car prices rose about 0.5 percentage points a year faster
than inflation, says Sean McAlinden, chief economist at the Center for Automotive Research in Ann Arbor,
Mich. Car makers' productivity was rising rapidly, and sales were advancing 3% to 4% a year. In today's
dollars, manufacturers earned about $1,500 per vehicle. Today productivity is growing more slowly, the world
is awash in idle auto factories and a strong dollar is holding down the price of imported vehicles. As a result,
Mr. McAlinden says, new car prices have fallen 0.2% a year since 1996 and profits per vehicle are down to
about $400.
That is making investors increasingly nervous about auto makers' ability to repay huge debts, which are
mostly to finance customers' car purchases. As recently as 1980, Ford Motor Co. had the highest available
credit rating; now, its bonds trade as if they were junk. In the 1930s, Mr. McAlinden says, "we had both
deflation and absolute falling demand. This time we just have falling prices." So far. A renewed recession,
which would drive down auto sales, is "the most frightening prospect this industry cares about," Mr.
McAlinden says.
What would the Fed do if it confronted imminent deflation? A study by 13 Fed economists this summer
concluded that Japanese policy makers didn't see deflation coming until it was too late to prevent it. The
authors concluded that "when inflation and interest rates have fallen close to zero, and the risk of deflation
is high," policy makers should respond more aggressively than economic forecasts suggest. If the Fed lowers
interest rates too little, deflation could result, rendering the Fed less potent. If the Fed overdoes it, it can
always raise rates later to suppress the unwanted inflation, the study says.
Fed officials argue that they applied these lessons last year, cutting rates 11 times, and say the lessons are
less relevant now, with the economy growing, albeit slowly. But Martin Barnes, editor of the Bank Credit
Analyst, a Montreal-based forecasting journal, warns that prices received by most businesses are already
declining. While a near-term rate cut by the Fed might help, he says, it "is not going to prevent deflationary
pressure from intensifying over the next few months."
The federal government could also fight deflation by boosting spending or cutting taxes, though the recent
surge in the government's budget deficit could make that more difficult.
If the Fed cut its target for short-term interest rates to zero, and still feared deflation, its next steps are
largely untried. It could purchase large quantities of government bonds to lower long-term interest rates and
perhaps prompt investors to shift assets to stocks. It could purchase more government securities from banks,
leaving banks flush with newly created cash to lend. It could try to create inflation by purchasing foreign
currencies to drive down the dollar and push up the price of imports.
But Fed economists who studied these strategies in late 2000 were skeptical. If interest rates were zero,
then even if the Fed did pump banks full of cash by purchasing government securities, the banks would have
little profit incentive to risk lending out the money -- the return from leaving it in their vaults would be the
same. Indeed, the Bank of Japan has tried this "quantitative easing" for the past year, and the economy is still
in a slump. Driving down the dollar would fail if other countries tried to depreciate their currencies, too.
Other proposals, some possibly not legal, were for the Fed to lend to private companies or buy things such as
stocks, real estate or even goods and services, such as used cars. And then there are those magnetic strips.
Marvin Goodfriend, a top economist at the Richmond Fed, proposed at the Woodstock conference that a way
to stimulate the economy if interest rates are already at zero is to levy a fee on banks that keep cash on
deposit with the Fed rather than lending it out, and to find a way to make currency worth less the longer it
goes unspent -- thus the magnetic strips. When someone deposited a bank note, a "carry tax" would be
deducted according to how long it had been since it was withdrawn. These charges for holding on to cash
would effectively create negative interest rates.

--- Page 85 ---
Page 85 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"Asking people to carry around some one-dollar bills that are worth 99.4 cents and some that are worth 98.4
cents would be a terrible nuisance," Alan Blinder, a former Fed vice chairman, observed at the Woodstock
meeting. He added, "Prevention is far better than the cure."
(See related letter: "Letters to the Editor: Fed Could Fight Deflation With a Dollop of Keynes" -- WSJ Nov. 13,
2002)

A Split Fed?
A Split Fed?
By Brian Wesbury
839 words
4 November 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
A14
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
A fault line has opened inside the Federal Reserve. One side, apparently led by Alan Greenspan, thinks that
interest rates are too low. The other, which includes Dallas Fed President Bob McTeer, believes they are too
high.
The extent of this divide is made clear by the fact that after every meeting this year, the Fed's official
statement has used the word "accommodative" to describe current monetary policy. This means that a
steadfast majority of members have argued that short-term interest rates are already low enough to boost
the economy.
But in September, Mr. McTeer and Fed Governor Edward Gramlich dissented from the majority, calling for an
immediate rate cut. Some recently well-placed newspaper articles suggest that this minority may be growing
and the biggest split at the Fed in at least 15 years looks to be widening.
Those who believe that interest rates should be cut are in one of two camps. Either they believe that the
current level of interest rates is still too high, or they believe that while the Fed is not the primary problem
with the economy, lower interest rates wouldn't hurt. Those who hold the first view argue that the money
supply is not growing fast enough, or that a Japan-style deflation is a serious threat. This group thinks that
additional interest-rate cuts would counteract past or current deflationary pressures.
This debate started in the late 1990s, when the fears first arose of deflation if Fed policy became too tight. In
2000, with inflation at just 2%, the federal-funds rate was pushed to 6.5% and these fears were realized.
Higher real interest rates pushed the economy into recession, caused bankruptcies to soar, stock prices to
fall and commodity prices to collapse.
Following the Fed's 11 interest-rate cuts in 2001, real interest rates are now near zero and there is sufficient
liquidity entering the system. Total bank credit, which includes loans, leases and securities, has soared
25.1% at an annual rate in the past six months. However, commercial and industrial loans are down 7.3%
during the same time period. Something is hampering the transmission mechanism of monetary policy and
holding back economic growth.
That something appears to be uncertainty generated by war, terrorism, accounting fraud, midterm elections,
weak foreign trading partners, higher energy and insurance costs, a profligate congressional spending
pattern, the threat of a trade war, the phase-out of the tax cut in 2011, and many other developments. These
risks have made it difficult for businesses to commit to long-term investments, holding back the supply-side of
the economy.
But with the Fed holding interest rates at such a low level, the demand side continues to expand. New home
sales hit an all-time record high in September, and consumption continues to bolster GDP. This mixture of
weak supply-side growth and strong demand-side growth is a recipe for inflation and already some faint
signs are beginning to appear.
After falling last year, producer prices for finished goods are up 0.8% at an annual rate in the first nine months
of 2002. Pipeline prices indicate more pressure is building. "Core" intermediate producer prices are up 1.9%
at an annual rate since December 2001, while "core" crude goods prices are up 14.6%. The price of gold is
up 25% since last year and copper prices are up 10% in the past three weeks. Non-petroleum import prices
have climbed 0.4% at an annual rate so far this year, versus a decline of 4.5% during the first nine months of
2001. The dollar has weakened and the yield curve remains relatively steep. The lingering affects of past
deflation are clearly working their way out of the system.
As a result, to compare the U.S. to Japan is misleading. Japan increased the capital gains tax rate in 1990
and then boosted the value-added-tax. It spent trillions of yen in a misguided attempt to boost economic
growth and has attached many technically dead corporations to life support, diverting precious resources to

--- Page 87 ---
Page 87 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
lost causes. Most importantly, it took the Bank of Japan five years to lower real interest rates to zero and
even then it did not hold them there. The Fed accomplished this task in just one year.
Further interest-rate cuts in the U.S. will only serve to inflate the demand side. And as we learned in the
1970s, once out of the bottle, the inflation genie is not so easy to put back. It's the supply side of the
economy that needs help, not the demand side. Tax cuts and other incentives to increase the reward for
taking risks in this highly uncertain environment would help much more than further interest rates cuts. Fed
policy is not always the best therapy. This time, the fault does not lie with the Fed.
---
Mr. Wesbury is chief economist at Griffin, Kubik, Stephens & Thompson in Chicago.

ASIA/PACIFIC
BHP Billiton Says
Profit Slipped 9%,
Sees Flat Demand
Anglo-Australian natural-resources company BHP Billiton reported that profit for its fiscal first quarter ended
Sept. 30 fell 9% from a year earlier to $553 million, after losing an income stream when its BHP Steel unit
was spun off in July. Analysts had forecast profit of about $540 million. A year ago, the steel unit contributed
$60 million in earnings before interest and tax. BHP Billiton warned of continued flat demand for commodities,
saying the current quarter is likely to be "just as difficult as the last." BHP Billiton said that with the exception
of oil, prices for many of its key products remain at low levels.
But China's booming steel production remains a bright spot, providing support for the company's iron-ore and
metallurgical coal markets. Revenue rose 2.9% to $3.92 billion.
Shell Expects China Pipeline Pacts
Royal Dutch/Shell Group expects 11 agreements related to a 2,500-mile natural-gas pipeline in China to be
completed by the end of 2002 and that China's Foreign Trade Ministry will approve them next year, according
to Michael Sweeney, a managing director for Shell Gas in China. Shell, along with Exxon Mobil and Russia's
Gazprom, has formed a partnership with PetroChina to build and operate the pipeline from western China to
the country's eastern coast. A framework agreement on the joint venture was signed in July, giving each of
the foreign partners a 15% stake in the pipeline, which is expected to cost more than $5 billion. Finding and
extracting the gas is expected to cost an additional $3.3 billion.
China, Asean Form Framework Pact
China has agreed with the Association of Southeast Asian Nations on a list of hundreds of products on which
tariffs will be eliminated as part of plans to establish a free-trade area within 10 years. Beijing and the
10-member Asean, after six rounds of negotiations, have settled most broad questions about the free-trade
area, except when it should be fully implemented. They are leaving that to their leaders, including Chinese
Premier Zhu Rongji, who will choose a date and sign a framework agreement Monday during the annual
Asean summit in Phnom Penh, Cambodia.
China Reports Taiwan Trade Figures
Trade between China and Taiwan totaled $32 billion for the year's first nine months, China's official Xinhua
news agency reported, citing a Foreign Trade Ministry official. It didn't provide comparative figures for 2001.
Trade across the Taiwan Strait is expected to exceed $40 billion for the full year, the report said.

106.47 billion yen ($866 million), boosted by a special profit of 112.17 billion yen resulting from a shift to a
defined-contribution pension system. Sales rose 5% to 1.144 trillion yen.
-- Japanese workers' summer bonuses fell 5.9% from a year earlier to an average 412,853 yen ($3,358), the
Labor Ministry said. White-collar workers rely on bonus pay for as much as one-third of annual income.

EUROPE/AFRICA
Euro-Zone Data Signal Weak Growth
Business and consumer confidence in the euro zone and unemployment figures point to weak fourth-quarter
growth for the 12 countries that use the euro, but a worst-case scenario of recession is unlikely. But hopes
that economic growth might get a boost in the form of an interest-rate cut by the European Central Bank
were dashed by a slight rise in inflation. Euro-zone consumer prices in October rose 2.2% from a year
earlier, up from 2.1% in September and above the ECB's 2% target ceiling, the European Union's statistics
office reported. Meanwhile, the euro zone's industrial-confidence index rose two points in October to
minus-10, offsetting a three-point decline in consumer confidence, the European Commission said. That
consumers and businesses are going in opposite directions isn't surprising. But until now it has been
consumers carrying the load, while companies clean up their balance sheets through cutbacks in investment
and payrolls. Separately, France's jobless rate held steady at 9% in September for a fourth straight month,
the Labor Ministry reported.
AngloGold's Output, Profit Increase
AngloGold, South Africa's largest gold miner, reported third-quarter earnings that exceeded market
expectations, mainly due to an 11% increase in gold production. Profit rose 53% from a year earlier to $81
million. Earnings also rose 2.5% from the prior quarter, better than analysts' predictions of a
quarter-to-quarter decline. Gold output totaled 1.6 million ounces, up 200,000 ounces from the previous
quarter, with most of the increase coming from its Morila mine in Mali and from its larger stake in Cerro
Vanguardia in Brazil.
EU Fisheries Suspension Proposed
The International Council for Exploration of the Sea has proposed unprecedented restrictions on commercial
fishing in European Union waters. The Copenhagen-based ICES, which provides scientific advice to
fisheries-management authorities, says all cod and haddock fisheries in the North Sea, the Irish Sea, waters
off Scotland's western coast and the Skagerrak between Norway and Denmark should be closed for an
indefinite period to allow stocks to recover.
Amadeus Posts Profit, Lifts Outlook
Amadeus Global Travel Distribution, Europe's largest computer-reservations service for the airline industry,
raised its full-year outlook after a strong third quarter. Revenue for the year's first nine months rose 2.2% to
1.4 billion euros ($1.38 billion), bolstered by a 6.2% rise in third-quarter revenue. Profit excluding items rose
4% to 121.9 million euros; profit for the third quarter alone rose 32% to 27.6 million euros. Analysts said that
Amadeus's increasing diversification is helping the Madrid-based company weather a tough year for air travel.
The company raised its full-year forecast for growth in bookings to 2%, up from 1.2% projected earlier.
Briefly:
-- Britain's Imperial Chemical Industries said third-quarter profit rose 11% to GBP 80 billion ($125 million). Its
net debt fell to GBP 2.1 billion at Sept. 30, from GBP 3.1 billion a year earlier.
-- German stock-market officials said they would introduce new stock indexes next year but would keep the
blue-chip DAX index unchanged. Deutsche Boerse said the mid-cap MDAX index would shrink to 50 stocks
from 70 currently. A new TecDAX index will be introduced, with the 30 largest technology companies not in
the DAX as constituents.

Canada's economic growth in August was mildly disappointing but hardly foreshadows the sharp slowdown

Widens, but Cuts
In Costs Take Hold

U.S. and European Union regulators announced plans for merger candidates to undergo near-simultaneous
antitrust reviews on both sides of the Atlantic. The initiative shows U.S. and EU authorities going to great

Electric's proposed $43 billion acquisition of Honeywell after U.S. antitrust authorities had approved the deal.
The diverging positions over the proposed GE- Honeywell deal unleashed months of trans-Atlantic sniping.
EU and U.S. agencies will seek "consistent, or at least nonconflicting, outcomes," the commission said.
Bomb Attacks Rock South Africa
A series of bomb attacks by suspected right-wing extremists rocked South Africa, killing one person and
injuring three others, and sparking a massive hunt for more explosive devices. Police said 10 bombs
exploded around Johannesburg and Pretoria in less than 12 hours. Another bomb was defused. No one has
so far claimed responsibility for the explosions, and investigations are just beginning, but authorities suspect
the country's far right is behind the blasts, which mainly hit the black township of Soweto, near Johannesburg.
Britain's Tesco to Buy T&S Stores
U.K. supermarket operator Tesco agreed to buy smaller retailer T&S Stores in an all-stock deal valued at
GBP 377.3 million ($587.6 million), or GBP 4.55 a share, a 27% premium to T&S's closing price Tuesday.

convenience-store market to about 5%, will bring Tesco about 1,200 stores. Most will be converted to the
Tesco Express format or sold off.

Another Round? Honing Their Craft, Some Police Want To Get You Drunk --- Cops Learn Roadside
Tests For Alcohol and Drug Use By Studying Live Subjects --- Scouring a Heavy-Metal Crowd
By Russell Gold
2,114 words
29 October 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Cpl. Taft Green Jr., a white waiter's cloth tucked into the pants of his Texas highway-patrol uniform, poured
margaritas for a roomful of state employees. Four fingerprint technicians settled into a game of spades that
grew rowdier with each round of drinks. Others munched on peanuts while listening to the music of Texas
blues guitarist Stevie Ray Vaughan.
Soon, a dozen earnest state-trooper cadets gathered to scrutinize each of Cpl. Green's guests, politely asking
them to walk heel-to-toe for nine steps and then stand on one leg for 30 seconds.
Sgt. Doug Paquette of the New York State Police had a similar mission when he parked a Winnebago
camper at a heavy-metal rock concert in Albany. He put a sign in the window that said, "Volunteers wanted
for drug research." He recalls telling the handful of curious fans who knocked on the door that he was looking
for subjects to help police spot signs of illicit drug use. One reveler told him, "`I'm not screwed up now, but I'll
be back in a couple hours,'" says Sgt. Paquette. And some of them came back.
In the search for strategies to deal with the stubborn and deadly problem of driving under the influence, many
cops are turning to an unusual tactic: Recruiting volunteer drinkers and drug users to teach officers to
recognize impaired drivers. The tactic is drawing criticism from skeptics who think it isn't effective or ethical.
But advocates say there is no substitute for working directly with people who are drunk or stoned and that the
training helps bolster courtroom testimony by officers.
Texas and a majority of other states hold "wet workshops," in which bar-tending cops get volunteer subjects
drunk. Police in Minneapolis say they drive around looking for people on the street who appear to be on
drugs. Officers stop the wobbly pedestrians, promising they won't be arrested. If the subjects agree, the
police take them back to the precinct house, where other officers are waiting to inspect them.
The most common technique, used in Phoenix, Houston and other cities, is to intercept nonviolent arrestees
on their way into jail. Police offer food and an hour-long diversion from the holding tank, in exchange for their
services.
As part of a nationwide movement against drunk driving that blossomed in the early 1980s, authorities in
most states began broad use of roadside tests for alcohol. It is more difficult to diagnose drug use quickly,
and it requires more training to learn the craft. As a result, police say they are only now catching up on that
front -- and are eager to find human guinea pigs.
Tom Page, a former policeman in Los Angeles and Detroit who now advises departments around the country
on drug recognition, argues that some of these techniques are ineffective and ethically questionable.
"Volunteers" invited to be drug subjects may feel they have no choice but to cooperate, he says. The police,
for their part, may be enlisting the help of people they should be arresting. Looking for subjects at the county
jail fails for pedagogical reasons, Mr. Page asserts, because it is so likely that everyone will be drunk or on
drugs. He recommends to his clients that police learn to do evaluations in real-life settings, out on the road.
Authorities say they follow safeguards to protect volunteers and stay within the law. The National Highway
Traffic Safety Administration has issued guidelines for serving alcohol: Officers shouldn't carry firearms during
wet workshops and should screen volunteers to make sure they don't have a history of alcoholism.
"Volunteers should be released only into the custody of responsible, sober persons," the guidelines say.
Police can't administer illegal drugs, however, and there aren't broadly used guidelines for recruiting drug
users. That leaves police largely to their own devices.
Sgt. Paquette in New York has been scouring rock concerts for 14 years. The state police aren't condoning
drug use in order to train troopers to detect it, he says. "These kids are getting stoned whether we are there
or not." At least one other state, Massachusetts, says it recruits at rock concerts.

--- Page 95 ---
Page 95 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Sgt. Paquette recalls one time in the summer last year, when officers walked through the crowd outside of a
concert near Buffalo, seeking volunteers. "Some [music fans] were impaired at the time we talked to them.
Others said, `We'll see if we can help you out,'" and later stopped by a sheriff's substation to do so, he recalls.
California and some other states prohibit being stoned in public. But New York doesn't, as long as drug users
aren't endangering themselves or another, such as by getting behind the wheel of a car. Possessing drugs,
however, is illegal almost everywhere. Sgt. Paquette says he often offers concertgoers a friendly warning: "If
you come with drugs in your possession, you're ours."
Officers who pull over a driver they suspect is drunk typically administer a three-part initial test: walking a
straight line, standing on one leg and touching hand to nose. If a driver fails, that provides legally required
"probable cause" for police to ask to administer a breath test. Widely available breath-test devices can
reliably determine legal intoxication, which in most states starts at 0.08 grams of alcohol per 100 milliliters of
blood. Breath-test results generally are accepted as powerful evidence in court, accompanied by testimony
from arresting officers.
The analogous roadside drug test includes the walking, balancing and nose-touching steps, plus many more.
The additional elements include a range of medical questions and scrutiny of eye-pupil size and movement.
Controlled substances ranging from depressants, such as barbiturates, to stimulants, such as cocaine, can
have very different effects. There is no device for roadside drug detection comparable to breath testers.
Urine and blood tests reliably detect the presence of drugs. But police have to have probable cause to ask a
driver to take a urine test and getting the result can take a week or more. To prosecute someone for driving
under the influence of drugs, the authorities generally have to show not only the presence of the drugs but
actual impairment. That requires an arresting officer to testify about the defendant's behavior and reactions.
That testimony can be greatly strengthened if the cop is a state-certified drug-recognition expert, or DRE, and
has administered the 12-step test that most states now embrace. DREs were first certified in 1989. Today,
there are a total of about 5,400 in 36 states. Certification typically requires 72 hours of classwork and 12
accurate evaluations of actual drug users.
The human costs of driving under the influence are great. An estimated 17,448 people were killed in
alcohol-related car accidents in 2001, according to the most recent figures available from the National
Highway Traffic Safety Administration. The number of accidents involving alcohol has dropped sharply since
the early 1980s, but the annual figure appears to have bottomed in 1999 and has crept back up a bit since.
The government doesn't gather comparable figures for drug-related auto fatalities, but drugs and driving are a
problem. Based on a 2001 national survey, the Health and Human Services Department estimated that eight
million people had driven under the influence of an illicit drug in the previous year, compared with 25.1 million
persons who drove under the influence of alcohol.
Police agencies across the country say they are eager to improve officers' ability to turn up both drug and
alcohol use by drivers. The Texas Department of Public Safety in June offered its back-office employees a
paid day off to drink under scrutiny. Police also invited law-enforcement students from a local community
college. People with certain medical conditions exacerbated by alcohol, as well as those younger than 21 and
older than 65, were excluded. The allure of state-provided booze wasn't enough to fill the 35 slots, however.
Only 21 people showed up for a 10:30 a.m. session.
By mid-afternoon, secretary Lorna Meyer was visibly tipsy. As Cadet Dennis Redden administered the
sobriety test, she told him he would "be one hell of a trooper out in a little town no one has ever heard of."
She promised to attend his graduation. An hour later, she was in a deep slumber on the floor. (Mr. Redden
graduated, without Ms. Meyer in attendance, and is assigned to a barracks in Canton, Texas, population
3,466.)
The father of drug-impaired-driver testing is Dick Studdard, a now-retired Los Angeles Police Department
motorcycle cop who began adapting the technique from roadside alcohol-testing in the late 1970s. In 1985,
researchers at Johns Hopkins University in Baltimore checked his methods by having 80 volunteers take
marijuana, valium, amphetamines or a placebo. Mr. Studdard and three fellow officers pinpointed the correct
drug more than 90% of the time.
Interest in the Studdard approach grew slowly, with Arizona law-enforcement agencies embracing it the most
enthusiastically. Police from a dozen states, including Iowa, Rhode Island and Kentucky, have traveled to the
Maricopa County jail in Phoenix to complete their 12 required evaluations for DRE certification.
Sheriff's Department Officer Paul White supervises the training at the jail, the nation's fifth-largest municipal
lockup. Mr. White, 35, brings a personal zeal to his job: When he was a high-school senior, he was pulled
over in Columbia, Mo., for driving with a missing tail light. The state trooper didn't notice he had been smoking

--- Page 96 ---
Page 96 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
marijuana. "I remember thinking, `Wow, you have no clue how stoned I am.'" Mr. White says. "What if I had
gone down the road and killed somebody?"
On a torrid Friday evening in August, dressed in a black T-shirt with "Sheriff" in yellow letters, he was
scanning the crowd of incoming arrestees. "Welcome to jail. The wet bar is closed, but the sauna is open," he
said jovially.
Mr. White avoided subjects who appeared surly or completely zonked out. Instead, he focused on one in a
green shirt arrested for attempting to run out of a department store with a stack of blue jeans. Mr. White
shined a penlight into his eyes for a few seconds, then asked the man to close his eyes and estimate when
30 seconds were up -- a standard part of the 12-part test. Twelve seconds later, the arrestee's eyes popped
open, a possible sign of a stimulant such as cocaine. Mr. White looked at his watch, shook his head and both
men laughed. In short order, the arrestee admitted smoking crack two hours earlier.
After a quick explanation of the test and a promise of anonymity, Mr. White assigned the man -- nicknamed
"Polo" because of the insignia on his shirt -- to Arizona State Police Officer Kristi Shelley. She was unaware
Polo, 32, had just confessed his drug use to Mr. White.
First, Polo, was brought his reward: grape juice and a couple of plastic bags filled with white bread, turkey
slices, brownies and plums. Inmates who are brought in after 6 p.m. generally don't get fed until breakfast,
and Polo had arrived shortly after the cutoff.
Ms. Shelley then took his pulse and checked his pupils. She jotted down that his movement when walking
was "robotic," a possible sign that he's on a stimulant. He was free of needle marks but had blisters in his
mouth, which could have been from smoking a hot crack pipe.
After interviewing her subject -- today was the first time he tried to steal, Polo says -- Ms. Shelley checked her
notes one last time. Her conclusion: cocaine.
"It's that obvious, huh?" Polo replied.
---
Drivers on Drugs
Police use a 12-step test to determine whether there is "probable
cause" to ask a driver to take urine and blood tests. The steps
include:
-- Follow pen as it circles in front of face and touches nose. Police
check whether pupils track together. Failure to do so could be sign of
PCP or depressant use.
-- Walk and turn; balance on one leg. Inability to do physical tasks
while listening to instructions could be an indication of marijuana
use.
-- Close eyes for 30 seconds. Opening eyes too quickly could be sign
of stimulant use; taking too long could be sign of heroin use.
Source: International Association of Chiefs of Police

EUROPE/AFRICA
Russian Magnate
Berezovsky Faces
Auto-Fraud Charge
Russian prosecutors have charged self-exiled tycoon Boris Berezovsky with fraud, a move the businessman,
who now lives in England, dismissed as the latest stage of a government-sponsored witch hunt against him.
Russia's deputy prosecutor general, Vladimir Kolesnikov, said an investigation had revealed Mr. Berezovsky
and his business partners stole 2,033 cars from Russian auto maker Avtovaz in 1994-95. He said proceeds
from selling the cars were used to finance country houses and acquire stakes in Russian television channels
ORT and TV6. Mr. Berezovsky has since been stripped of his control of both channels. The case relates to
Mr. Berezovsky's role in the chaotic early years of Russian capitalism. Mr. Berezovsky, who already faces
charges of embezzling millions of dollars from Russian airline Aeroflot, says he has been singled out for
prosecution because, unlike other tycoons, he criticizes the Kremlin.
Prosecutors say they also have charged two of Mr. Berezovsky's closest business associates with fraud. Mr.
Kolesnikov said he expected arrest warrants to be issued for the men "in the near future."
German Bank HVB Swings to Loss
HVB Group, Germany's second-largest bank, reported it swung to a third-quarter loss and more than doubled
its provisions for bad loans as weak stock markets and higher-than-expected insolvencies hurt its bottom line.
HVB posted a net loss of 360 million euros ($352.2 million), compared with profit of 68 million euros a year
earlier. HVB also named Dieter Rampl, the 55-year-old current head of its corporate customers and markets
division, to succeed Chief Executive Albrecht Schmidt on Jan. 1. The bank, based in Munich, said
third-quarter provisions for bad loans rose to 1.23 billion euros, from 532 million euros a year earlier. For the
year's first nine months, HVB's risk provisions rose 87% to 2.5 billion euros, which was the bank's forecast for
provisions for all of 2002.
Pechiney Seals Corus Aluminum Deal
Anglo-Dutch steelmaker Corus Group agreed to sell most of its aluminum business to France's Pechiney for
about 750 million euros ($733.7 million), a sum that analysts said reflects weak aluminum prices. The deal for
rolled products and extrusions, if approved by regulators, all but removes Corus from its small aluminum
business. The deal involves plants in Germany, Belgium, Canada and China, and about 4,600 Corus workers
will join Pechiney. It excludes Corus's smelters in the Netherlands and Germany, for which Corus said it will
continue to seek buyers.

month to leave its key interest rate at 4%, the first time any members called for a rate cut since February,
according to minutes of the Oct. 9-10 meeting.

1.2 billion Swiss francs ($800 million) of new capital, but not without criticizing failed past strategies.
-- Dutch pharmaceutical and chemical company Akzo Nobel said third-quarter profit fell 3% to 232 million
euros. Sales declined 0.9% to 3.49 billion euros.

ASIA/PACIFIC
Placer Wins Control of AurionGold
Canadian gold miner Placer Dome has won control of Australia's AurionGold, increasing its stake to 55.73%
from 48.35%, according to a substantial shareholder notice issued yesterday in Australia. Placer is poised to
win full control of AurionGold after the Australian company's board this month gave up its five-month
resistance to Placer's takeover bid and recommended the offer to shareholders. With AurionGold's gold
production of one million ounces a year under its belt, Placer will become the world's fifth-largest gold
producer. Placer's offer is due to close Nov. 1.
Brilliance China Ex-Chief Files Suits
Days after Chinese prosecutors issued a warrant for his arrest for "economic crimes," Yang Rong, the
founder and former chairman of Brilliance China Automotive Holdings, said he was innocent and the victim of
a government move to take control of his business empire. Mr. Yang, one of China's richest businessmen and
the head of its largest minivan maker until he was forced out recently in a power struggle, also described two
lawsuits he has filed in Beijing against China's Finance Ministry and a quasigovernmental nonprofit
organization that is Brilliance's controlling shareholder. "I want my name cleared," he said from Los Angeles,
where he said he fled in May after receiving warnings he would be detained by police in Liaoning province.
Brilliance in 1992 became the first Chinese company to go public on the New York Stock Exchange. Mr. Yang
said he had learned that the Liaoning government accused him of fleeing China with 600 million yuan ($72.5
million) in state assets. "I haven't taken a cent," he said. Brilliance said it and its units "haven't been
investigated or notified" of any inquiry.

Brazil's Embratel Posts Wider Loss
Brazilian long-distance carrier Embratel Participacoes reported its third-quarter net loss widened to 549.6
million reals ($140.7 million), from 152.2 million reals a year earlier. Although Embratel cut costs, it continues
to face pricing pressures from competitors and a difficult economic environment may pose refinancing
problems. Embratel, a unit of WorldCom, which is in U.S. Chapter 11 bankruptcy proceedings, said it has
retained Banc of America Securities and a Brazilian consulting firm to help it design a refinancing plan for
about $800 million in debt maturing next year. Embratel has said it is financially independent from WorldCom.
But in a conference call with Embratel officials yesterday, analysts appeared worried that its status as a
Brazilian company may leave it struggling to gain access to credit markets, which are mostly closed at the
moment amid political worries and doubts about Brazil's debt-servicing ability. Revenue fell 7% to 1.79 billion
reals. Foreign-exchange losses stemming from the real's weakness resulted in 1.1 billion reals in charges,
which were partly offset by 309.2 million reals in tax credits.

Mexico's Trade Deficit Narrows
Mexico's trade deficit narrowed in September, helped by a sharp increase in petroleum exports. The Finance

Stocks' Four-Day Gain: 969 Points---But as Dow Industrials Rally,Bond Market Faces a Free FallAs Its Own Rally Turns Around
Tuesday's Markets
Stocks' Four-Day Gain: 969 Points --- But as Dow Industrials Rally, Bond Market Faces a Free Fall As
Its Own Rally Turns Around
By Gregory Zuckerman
1,337 words
16 October 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
BETTER-THAN-EXPECTED corporate profits sent stocks surging for the fourth straight day. But what was
good for stocks wasn't so good for Treasury securities -- as a bond rout raised new fears that the white-hot
market finally could be cooling.
Treasury securities, the haven investment of choice in recent months, suffered their worst day in almost a
year, leading analysts to question whether what some have called a bubble in bonds has been pricked. If it
has, interest rates could rise sharply from their historic lows. Yesterday, as bond prices dropped hard, the
yield on the 10-year Treasury note jumped to 4.04%, the highest in more than a month. The price on the
10-year note fell 1 31/32 points, or $19.69 per $1,000 invested. Just last week, the yield on the 10-year was a
skimpy 3.57%, a 44-year low.
That sharp increase for bond yields -- which move opposite to price -- means mortgage rates likely will rise
significantly in the days ahead. But corporate-borrowing rates may not be hurt as much if corporate bonds
become the hot investment and Treasurys continue to turn cold.
"Treasurys are in a free fall," says Kurt Harrison, head of government trading at Bank of America. "We're
wringing out the excesses of the past two or three months in bonds in just the past two or three days."
Stocks are a different story. During the past four days alone, the Dow Jones Industrial Average has added a
staggering 969.41 points, or more than 13%, its best four-day return since April 1933, confounding investors
who had been building more conservative portfolios in recent months.
Yesterday's surge was broad and the buying heavy. The Dow industrials leapt 378.28 points, or a heady
4.8%, to 8255.68, while the Nasdaq Composite Index jumped 5.07% to 1282.44 and the Standard & Poor's
500-stock index rose 4.73%, or 39.83, to 881.27.
The dollar, meanwhile, responded to Wall Street's strong day with a modest rally of its own. The U.S.
currency jumped to 124.77 yen, up from 124.29 yen late Monday, and strengthened on the euro, which fell to
98.05 cents from 98.70 cents late Monday.
Behind the stock surge: Bellwether companies under pressure lately, including Dow industrial stocks
Citigroup, General Motors and Johnson & Johnson, all reported better third-quarter earnings than expected,
suggesting the economy might be stabilizing. Other investors bet that stock prices have been so beaten up in
recent months that they already incorporate expectations of more earnings problems, putting a safety net
below stocks.
"Valuations finally became compelling and earnings projections were so pessimistic that the bar was very
low" for the earnings reports, says Jack Ablin, who manages stocks and bonds at Harris Trust in Chicago,
who did some buying yesterday.
And what of the investors and institutions that piled into bonds in recent months, looking for a safe
investment? They could be badly burned by the sudden downturn in Treasurys.
The reason some analysts have been worried about the Treasury market: In recent weeks many investors
began buying bonds on the bet that prices would climb higher, rather than because they were eager to own
bonds with slim yields, traders said. Other investors were trying to escape stocks or in fear of a deep
recession. Meanwhile, some big institutions, such as mortgage investor Fannie Mae, have been buying
bonds and interest-rate derivatives to adjust giant bond portfolios under pressure by rising refinancings, not
because they believed the Treasury rally would continue.

--- Page 101 ---
Page 101 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Overall, investors put $134 billion of new money into bond mutual funds through Oct. 9, most of it going to
funds that invest in the safest bonds. That makes this year the biggest on record for bond funds. In
comparison, stock funds have received just $14 billion of new investments.
All that buying was reminiscent of some of the buying that went on at the peak of the Nasdaq market, when
investors were buying stocks because they were going up, not because they believed underlying earnings
were set to rise.
A sample of messages posted on Yahoo's message board last week underscored the gloom that had helped
bonds soar. "There's no time to waste, the Dow is on the verge of a 1,500-point crash," according to one
posting. " A total of 80% of your funds should be invested in US Treasuries." Another posting: "Buy
three-month Treasurys, gold coins & a prayer book."
"It's absolutely been a bubble, there's no doubt in my mind," Mr. Ablin says. "It was a bubble because people
were buying Treasurys not for the sake of owning them but as a bet against the stock market."
Even big institutional investors, such as pension plans, had been buying bonds no matter the skimpy yield,
traders said, in part to make up for being underexposed to Treasurys compared with stocks in recent years.
"Yields seemed to be irrelevant" to investors in the past month, Mr. Harrison says. "But now the economy
doesn't seem to be rolling over and global stock markets are rallying," so bonds are in trouble.
It isn't at all clear that stocks will keep climbing, or that bonds will keep falling. Late yesterday, tech giant Intel
reported disappointing third-quarter earnings, which could help drag the market down today. Bonds could
very well rebound if evidence emerges of weakness in the economy, or the stock market takes another
downturn. Some say an extended selloff for bonds is unlikely, with an attack on Iraq by the U.S. still a strong
possibility and the U.S. economy still weighed down by heavy debts.
But yesterday some bond veterans began to warn that more good news on the economy, indications that
inflation is moving higher or that the Federal Reserve isn't going to be cutting interest rates any time soon
all could cause severe pain for bond investors.
What's the nightmare scenario for bond investors? Many point to 1994, when the Fed surprised investors by
raising interest rates. In 1994 the Lehman Brothers bond index dropped 3%, its worst calendar year return
on record. Corporate bonds fell 4%. Long-term Treasurys did worse that year, falling almost 8%.
Those drops pale in comparison to the tech and telecom losses for stock investors, but for bond investors,
who look to bonds for a safe investment, the losses were painful. Many large investors in the bond market
use leverage, or borrowed money, to juice their returns. As a result, many high-profile hedge funds suffered
heavy losses in 1994.
Ron Lara, president of investment-planning firm Lara, Shull & May in Vienna, Va., predicts the yield on the
10-year note will exceed 4.5%. He recommends investors edge into stocks, but not go overboard, because
the economy likely will stay under pressure. Bond investors should stick to short-term Treasurys that would
do better if the Treasury rout continues.

Currency Trading:Dollar Finishes Session Mixed,Little Affected by Bali Attack
Currency Trading: Dollar Finishes Session Mixed, Little Affected by Bali Attack
By Tyler Lifton
Dow Jones Newswires
577 words
15 October 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
B11
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- The dollar put in a mixed performance -- edging moderately higher against the yen and
modestly lower against the Swiss franc -- following the weekend bombing on the Indonesian island of Bali,
though more peripheral currencies including the Australian and New Zealand dollars did see some selling
pressure.
Aside from slight weakness against the classic safe-harbor currency -- the Swiss franc -- perhaps the most
remarkable currency reaction to Saturday's blasts was the fact that the dollar barely reacted at all.
Even with the increase in risk aversion following the Bali attack -- which President Bush said appeared to be
linked to al Qaeda -- the dollar strengthened against the yen and the pound and held its ground against the
euro.
No doubt partly accounting for the dollar's resilience were the extremely thin trading conditions in
foreign-exchange markets, as many financial professionals were out of the office with holidays in Canada, the
U.S. and Japan. But analysts said the dollar's lack of movement also stemmed from a sense that terrorist
events had to a certain extent, been anticipated.
The Indonesian rupiah crumbled to its weakest level in almost six months, while the attack also sent shock
waves through regional currency markets and knocked more than 10% off Jakarta stocks. The blast also
initially supported gold and oil prices. The rupiah, which is barely traded in New York, was quoted at 9,346
rupiah to the dollar late in the day, compared with 9,009 rupiah to the dollar on Friday, but off an intraday
peak of 9,350 rupiah to the dollar after Bank Indonesia, the central bank, intervened to stem the rupiah's
slide.
Late yesterday in New York, the dollar was trading at 124.29 yen, up from 124.12 yen late Friday in New
York. The euro was at 98.70 cents, just up from 98.68 cents late Friday in New York. Against the Swiss franc,
the dollar was worth 1.4815 francs, down from 1.4828 francs Friday in New York. Sterling was changing
hands at $1.5575, down from $1.5623 late Friday in New York.
Analysts said the attack could pose longer-term threats to the Australian and New Zealand currencies,
potentially hampering tourism and export demand. Australia, because of its proximity to Indonesia and the
large number of Australians among the casualties, is particularly vulnerable, some said. "It will have some
effect on the [Australian] economy," said Tim Stewart, chief currency strategist for Morgan Stanley in New
York. "Certainly you would expect a knock on consumer confidence."
The Australian dollar was trading at 54.71 U.S. cents late yesterday, down from 54.89 U.S. cents late Friday.
The New Zealand dollar was worth 47.82 U.S. cents, down from 48.14 U.S. cents late Friday.
Elsewhere, the other key focus for foreign-exchange markets was Brazil, after the central bank called an
extraordinary meeting and raised interest rates by three percentage points.
Nonetheless, the real failed to take heart from the rate increase, ending the day weaker as analysts
questioned the implications of the surprise rate increase both for Brazil's debt-servicing costs and the
economy. Late yesterday, the currency was quoted at 3.8506 reals to the dollar, weaker than 3.8095 reals to
the dollar late Friday.
