Guilt by Association:U.S. officials insist financial contagion is over; Not so fast
World Business (A Special Report)
Guilt by Association: U.S. officials insist financial contagion is over; Not so fast
By Pamela Druckerman
1,262 words
14 October 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
R7
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
CAN THE CONCEPT of financial contagion be tossed into history's dustbin?
The Clinton administration certainly didn't think so. When financial crises roared throughout emerging markets
in Asia, Russia and Latin America during the 1990s, the U.S. was quick to back big financial bailouts as
firewalls to keep the situation from spreading. Officials saw contagion -- the guilt by association that makes
one nation prone to economic upheaval just because another one is in crisis -- as a force to be reckoned with.
When the Bush administration took over, it insisted those days had ended. To prove the point, the U.S.
steadfastly withheld aid when Argentina fell into a financial crisis late last year. Contagion, U.S. officials said,
was just a passing intellectual fashion, made obsolete by better market information and the benefits of
experience. "We need to retire that fashion like a hula hoop," Treasury Secretary Paul O'Neill said.
Many economists aren't buying that line, though. They say contagion may be less virulent, since fewer
countries have fixed exchange rates, which can come violently unpinned in a crisis and deal a cataclysmic
blow to stumbling economies. But they warn that contagion is still a factor and say that, despite the
administration's official stance, it is still worrying Washington.
When Argentina's state finances collapsed in December in the largest-ever bond default by a government
borrower, other South American financial markets rumbled. But they experienced nothing like the
earthquakes that once brought a string of nations around the globe near financial collapse.
Is that a sign of contagion's demise? Not so, say some economists. They say Argentina's crisis was so many
years in the making that investors had plenty of time to react calmly, instead of stampeding out of other
markets in a panic, as they've done in the past.
They also point to the spread of financial problems to Argentina's Latin American neighbors this summer, and
to the U.S. reaction. Contagion concerns may have made investors quicker on the trigger in Brazil, whose
bonds and currency fell off a cliff this summer. Of course, a major catalyst for Brazil's crisis was the surprising
popularity of Worker's Party presidential candidate Luiz Inacio Lula da Silva ahead of the Oct. 6 election;
investors were concerned that he would undo pro-market reforms if he was elected. (As it turns out, Mr. da
Silva won about twice as many votes as his nearest rival, but faces an Oct. 27 runoff election.) But with
Argentina in default on much of its government debt, investors also focused on whether Brazil could sustain
payments on its own debt, regardless of the outcome of the balloting.
"Seeing Argentina, nobody wanted to take chances and give Brazil the benefit of the doubt," says Walter
Molano, head of emerging-markets research at BCP Securities Inc., a brokerage firm in Greenwich, Conn.
In August, the U.S. threw its support behind a $30.4 billion International Monetary Fund package for Brazil. It
also backed a smaller package to bolster the wounded banking system in Uruguay, which got caught in
Argentina's downdraft. Some economists say those moves belie the administration's professed confidence
that contagion is obsolete.
However, U.S. officials have barely budged in the contagion debate. Mr. O'Neill acknowledged that "intense
external pressures" from its neighbors on tiny Uruguay compelled the U.S. to intervene on its behalf. But he
and John Taylor, U.S. Treasury undersecretary for international affairs, attributed Brazil's troubles to the
domestic issue of pre-election jitters. "There's been nothing like the Russian contagion in Argentina's crisis,"
Mr. Taylor said on a visit to Mexico in August after the IMF package was put together for Brazil. "There's been
virtually no impact."
U.S. officials point out that interest rates, the usual measure of whether countries have been tainted by a
crisis elsewhere, hardly moved in the emerging markets as Argentina's economy went into a tailspin. By
mid-January, yields on Argentina's foreign bonds had soared, reflecting investors' perception of risk on the

--- Page 105 ---
Page 105 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
debt. But bonds from Mexico, South Korea, the Philippines and even Brazil were mostly steady. And bonds in
Malaysia and Russia actually strengthened, driving their yields down.
Interest rates have since edged still higher in Argentina and have more than doubled in Brazil. But rates
climbed only temporarily in other developing countries in response to Brazil's problems. In the Asian nations,
as well as in Russia and Mexico, rates are still near their January levels.
Economists say interest rates don't tell the whole story, though. "There are a lot of different channels for
contagion," says Morris Goldstein, senior fellow at the Institute for International Economics in Washington.
For one, economists point to direct-investment trends.
The Institute of International Finance, a Washington group representing commercial banks and financial
firms, estimates investment in hard assets like factories in emerging markets will fall 16% to $113 billion this
year, in part because of Argentina's troubles and its government's reaction. The government ordered a host
of dollar-denominated contracts, bank accounts and retirement funds to be swapped into pesos, in most
cases violating investment contracts and implying steep losses for companies. Electricity companies, for
instance, were forced to charge their customers in pesos rather than dollars, even after the peso had lost
much of its value.
"People are much more concerned about making longer-term investments, and they're reviewing contracts
backwards and forwards," says David Gould, director of global economic analysis for the Institute of
International Finance. There is a sense among international investors, he says, that once a country opens its
markets, "it doesn't mean they're open forever."
Analysts also are wary of the argument that better market information has helped render contagion obsolete.
Investors certainly have more information to chew on these days. In 1999, the IMF and the multilateral Bank
for International Settlements imposed new financial disclosure standards on borrowing governments, on the
assumption that well-informed investors are less prone to making rash judgments. To comply with the new
standards, central banks around the world have built Web sites offering daily data on their foreign-exchange
and gold reserves and on government debt levels, information they once doled out a few times a year.
Those who buy and sell emerging-market bonds praise the improved disclosure, saying they're less likely to
be blindsided by things like debt tucked away inside state enterprises. But economists don't believe that these
advances ensure that the U.S. won't have to intervene again at some point to stop contagion.
"Information is always going to be imperfect in the markets," says Barry Eichengreen, economics professor at
the University of California at Berkeley and author of a book on financial crises. "There may be times when
the markets don't get it right, or when the outcome is so uncertain we're reluctant to leave markets to their
own devices."
---
Ms. Druckerman is a staff reporter in The Wall Street Journal's New York bureau. She can be reached at
pamela.druckerman@wsj.com.

Beyond the Nest Egg:How much do you know about the various pieces that make up yourretirement plan? Probably less than you think
Encore (A Special Report): Money Matters
Beyond the Nest Egg: How much do you know about the various pieces that make up your retirement
plan? Probably less than you think
By Kelly Greene
3,593 words
30 September 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
R4
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
POP QUIZ: At what age will you qualify for full Social Security benefits?
Even if you think you know the answer, consider this: 56% of workers surveyed earlier this year said full
benefits are available at age 65 or before -- even though the majority of today's workers won't be eligible for
full benefits until they hit 67. It's a change Congress made almost two decades ago, and that question, which
the Employee Benefit Research Institute in Washington asks each year, continues to trip up lots of people.
"I keep waiting for the numbers [of people who are mistaken] to change, but the advances have been very
slow," says Dallas Salisbury, the group's president.
Most Americans know their nest eggs have taken a beating in recent months. Beyond that, though,
knowledge about the rudiments of retirement plans and finances -- about corporate rules governing matching
contributions and early retirement, about pensions and vesting, about health benefits in later life, rollovers of
savings plans, annuities and even one's own Social Security payments -- appears to be in remarkably short
supply.
"It's shocking," says Alan Gustman, an economist at Dartmouth College in Hanover, N.H., who has been
studying workers' familiarity, or lack thereof, with retirement planning for years. Even when it comes to
something as fundamental as identifying the difference between a defined-benefit plan and a
defined-contribution plan, many people "don't know if it's an account [or whether] it generates a benefit for
each year," Dr. Gustman says. "They just plain don't know."
Some of these failings might be forgiven. Workers now approaching retirement have lived through the
evolution of traditional pensions into 401(k) plans, along with other experiments in between. So even if you've
stayed true to the same company long enough to get a gold watch, your retirement plan may have changed
shape a few times. Frequent job hoppers, meanwhile, can be left with scattered savings -- an individual
retirement account here, an investment portfolio there, "a piece of a defined-benefit plan left behind
somewhere else," says Martha Terry, a principal in Boston with benefits consultant Towers Perrin. "People
are telling us, `I can't tell how all these pieces add up.'"
Your retirement savings, however badly bruised of late, are often just one part of a larger equation. We asked
benefits experts across the country to identify the most important things you probably don't know about your
retirement plan -- and where you can turn for help.
First, some basics. If you started working before the 1980s, chances are you've encountered more than one
type of retirement plan. In the 1950s, pensions became more widespread at big companies amid a period of
postwar prosperity, with employers providing defined benefits -- monthly retirement checks based on years of
service, salary and retirement age. But an obscure bit of federal legislation in the 1970s sparked a shift to
defined-contribution plans that were forever stuck with the unwieldy "401(k)" label. The plans spread quickly;
employers discovered they could cut benefit costs and save money, and workers were allowed to keep
retirement savings (or part of them) if they changed jobs.
More recently, a hybrid has emerged. So-called cash-balance plans resemble 401(k)s (you have an account
into which contributions are made) but act primarily like pensions, with a pool of assets and a formula used to
determine a retiree's eventual payout. Although the combination sounds great, the Labor Department has
criticized a number of companies for shorting workers when they convert traditional pensions to cash-balance
programs. Federal regulators estimated in a report last spring that 300 to 700 such plans may be underpaying
retirees by as much as $199 million a year.

--- Page 107 ---
Page 107 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Which plan do you have? And do you understand how it works? Ten years ago, when the last major survey of
workers' knowledge about their retirement plans was taken (as part of the University of Michigan Health and
Retirement Survey, supported by the National Institute on Aging), fully half of those with a pension in the
survey of 12,650 respondents approaching retirement age couldn't identify what kind of plan they had. Only
two in five could give the value of their retirement benefit. Today, researchers say workers may have a better
idea about what their plans are called or what form they take -- but still aren't sure how they operate.
"It's all too complicated for people to understand," Dr. Gustman says.
Perhaps the most important reason to be familiar with the mechanics of your retirement plan is the fact that
your benefits aren't set in stone. Although your employer can't take away retirement benefits you've earned
already, it can reduce them going forward. Thus, you need to watch for any amendments to your retirement
plan's description, and make sure you understand how they affect you. In the past year, for example, a
number of hard-hit companies including General Motors Corp., Bethlehem Steel Corp., and hotel chain
Wyndham International Inc. scaled back their 401(k) matching contributions.
"I don't think people understand that employers have the right under law to change their pension programs,"
says Larry Sher, the research director at Buck Consultants Inc. in New York. "I've seen employees who are
shocked when they realize it; they didn't think their benefits could be reduced. If they had understood, they
might have had more incentive to save on their own."
Where to start? Ask your benefits office for a copy of what's called a "summary plan description." You should
have received such a booklet when you became eligible for your company's retirement plan, but even if you
have an old copy, it's worth getting an update, which your employer has to provide, under federal law.
To get you to sock away a chunk of your pay in a 401(k) or other defined-contribution plan, your employer
typically will offer to match your savings -- often by 50% for contributions of as much as 6% of your pay. So, if
you put $60 of a $1,000 paycheck into your account, your company would kick in $30. But a lot of workers
misinterpret the math, mistakenly thinking they can put in 3%, or $30, and the company will match it by 100%,
says Amy Heiserman, a senior communication consultant for Mercer Human Resource Consulting in Denver.
You can also miss the match by stashing away large portions of your salary early in the year, says Wayne
Bogosian, president of PFE Group, a Natick, Mass., firm that provides corporate pre-retirement education.
"Most employers only make the match paycheck to paycheck," he explains. So, if you put the entire $1,000
paycheck in your 401(k) in January, your employer's match would remain $30. A few companies offer a safety
net: Through what's called a "true-up" provision, they tally your contributions at the end of the year to see if
they've matched the total percentage promised. If they haven't, they kick in the rest.
The safeguard could come in particularly handy if your employer decides midyear to increase the maximum
portion of your paycheck you're allowed to save. That's happening right now as companies respond to a law
increasing the maximum contribution employees can make to retirement savings this year to $11,000 a
worker -- or $12,000 if you'll be at least 50 years old by Dec. 31.
If you change jobs frequently, you may not get to keep the match. That's because of vesting requirements --
the varying schedules that companies have for giving employees full control of their retirement savings.
Among companies offering defined-contribution plans, 41% require employees to work at least five years
before they are fully vested, according to a 2001 survey of 400 companies by consulting firm Hewitt
Associates of Lincolnshire, Ill. Just a third have immediate vesting, it found.
"Keeping that vesting threshold in mind should be a key element in career planning," says James Delaplane,
vice president for retirement policy at the American Benefits Council, Washington, which represents
employers. "A difference of six months on the job can make a difference of thousands of dollars in your
account."
When you leave a job with a traditional pension, don't assume that you've lost the chance to collect it. You're
entitled to whatever benefit you've earned -- and you might even be entitled to take it now. "A lot of people
forget that they have it, or they think that by waiting until they're 65, they'll have a bigger benefit," says Mr.
Bogosian, the benefits planner. "But the value of that benefit is frozen the day you leave."
Your former employer should send you a certificate that says how much your pension is worth. If it's less than
$5,000, or if the company offers a lump-sum payout, it will generally cash you out, in which case "you should
take the money and run," Mr. Bogosian advises. It may not seem like much, but $5,000 invested over 20
years at 8% interest is $23,000. If your pension is worth more than $5,000, or your company doesn't offer the
lump-sum option, find out how much money you're eligible for at the plan's normal retirement age, along with
the earliest age at which you can collect the pension -- and the penalty for collecting it early. You'll probably
still come out ahead by taking the money now, he says.

--- Page 108 ---
Page 108 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Let's say you change jobs when you're 55 years old, and the pension plan at your former employer docks 3%
a year from your pension for the 10 years until you would turn 65. That means you would get 30% less for
collecting the money early. But if you invest it with at least a 3% return a year, you'll do just as well. If you
manage an 8% return, "you're considerably better off," Mr. Bogosian says.
Whether your payout comes from a traditional pension or a 401(k), make sure you roll it directly into an
individual retirement account without passing through your hands first. Otherwise, you'll get hit with a big tax
bill. "I've seen lots of employees get really dinged by that because they're confused, especially the older
employees who haven't had a 401(k) their whole career," says Ms. Heiserman of Mercer.
What if you left a job years ago, and you're realizing now that you unwittingly may have left behind a pension?
You can get some help with your sleuthing from the Pension Rights Center, a Washington advocacy group
that lists 11 regional pension counseling projects around the country on its Web site (www.pensionrights.org),
along with national agencies. The Pension Benefit Guaranty Corp., the federal entity that guarantees pension
payments, introduced an online search tool (pbgc.gov) in 1996 that has helped locate $47 million in lost
benefits for more than 12,000 workers. And the Social Security Administration (www.ssa.gov) maintains
employer identification numbers, which could help you find a plan that's been taken over by another
company.
Whether you have a traditional pension or a 401(k), retiring early costs more than you might think. With a
traditional pension, "there are multiple hits," says Michael Carter, a vice president with the Hay Group, a
Philadelphia consulting firm. "You don't get all your years of service, you lose out on salary increases, and
you take an actuarial deduction, and it's a tremendous hit."
Most people assume you take a proportional cut for leaving before your plan's "normal" retirement age,
typically somewhere between 60 and 65. For example, it might be assumed if you would have had 30 years
of service and you leave three years early, you would get a pension worth 27/30ths, or 90%, of the full
amount.
But that's not how it works, Mr. Carter explains. Instead, you have to take an actuarial reduction, determined
by the employer but often around 5% a year, for each of the years you plan to leave early. So leaving three
years early could leave you with only 85% of the total amount -- and if you add on the lost salary and years of
service, you could wind up slashing your pension payments in half, simply by cutting out a few years before
your pension plan thinks you should.
When you retire early with a defined-contribution plan, the problem is that you start spending investments on
which you could be earning interest for your future. If you retire when you're 55, for example, "that 10-year
period until you turn 65 will eat up about half" of your 401(k)'s value, says Ron DeStefano, an actuary with
Aon Corp.'s consulting unit in Baltimore.
Many people think health benefits for retirees are an absolute. A survey by the National Council on the Aging,
a Washington advocacy group, found that 49% of adults believe employers who provide pension plans are
also required to provide health insurance to their retirees. That's simply not the case.
A group of retirees from Harnischfeger Industries in Milwaukee were so outraged by the mining-equipment
maker's move to stop paying for their health coverage that they sued in U.S. district court in Milwaukee. But
they lost.
"Plaintiffs may be angry that their former employer has cut off the company funding for continuing health-care
coverage at a time in their lives when they likely need it most," Judge Lynn Adelman wrote in her July ruling.
"On the other hand, they can also see themselves as lucky to the extent that Harnischfeger continued
coverage as long as it did, when the plan documents and law did not require it to."
Although federal law protects pension benefits, it provides no guarantee for medical insurance. That appears
unlikely to change anytime soon. Retired workers from such corporate heavyweights as General Electric Co.
and the Baby Bells banded together last year to push for congressional action to stop large employers from
canceling or reducing an individual's health benefits after that person has retired. Their bill, however, is stuck
in committee with little Republican support. Meanwhile, many companies are phasing out health benefits for
retirees, no matter their age. In fact, a survey this spring found that 51% of 2,000 employers polled are likely
to increase retiree cost sharing for prescription drugs.
For younger workers, "there probably isn't going to be any coverage," says Sylvester Schieber, vice president
of research for Watson Wyatt Worldwide in Washington. "If you're working for an employer who offers a plan,
and you're 15 to 20 years from [retiring], you may want to check whether you're even covered. In many
cases, that coverage is frozen."
The choices aren't pleasant. If you're already retired, hold on to your corporate medical coverage as long as
you can -- even if the premiums skyrocket. They're still cheaper than the rates you would get on your own.

--- Page 109 ---
Page 109 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
And if you find that the health-insurance perks won't be there when you hit 55 or 60, make sure you factor
health-insurance premiums into any early-retirement plan -- and be realistic about whether you can still afford
to quit at a younger age.
"It may be that after you get your early-retirement reduction and have to pay for medical benefits, that's all
you can pay for," Mr. DeStefano says.
Yes, you can still receive reduced benefits at age 62. But whereas the reduction was once 20% of the full
benefit, it's gradually increasing to 30%, depending on your year of birth. And the reduction is permanent;
your monthly payment doesn't increase at 65, or any other age.
And speaking of 65. . . . Anyone born in 1938 or later no longer is eligible for full benefits on that magic
birthday. Rather, a sliding scale determines when people born in various years can get a full paycheck.
Anyone born in 1960 or later, for instance, isn't eligible for full benefits until age 67. (You can find the age you
qualify for full benefits, as well as the reduction in benefits for taking Social Security at age 62, at
www.ssa.gov.)
In contrast, Medicare will continue to kick in at age 65. You probably know by now that the federal
government's health program for the elderly and disabled doesn't pay for prescription drugs -- but you need to
know that it doesn't pay for long-term care costs, either. "Most people really believe that Medicare covers
nursing homes. We deal with that day in and day out," says Diane Piktialis, work-life product director for
Ceridian Corp.'s LifeWorks unit in Boston. "There are a lot of myths out there, and people really don't realize
the truth until they hit the brick wall."
With traditional pensions, your company mails you regular checks -- technically called annuitized payments.
But you still have one big decision to make: whether to elect a smaller payment upfront so that your spouse
continues to get a payment in the event of your death.
Mr. DeStefano recalls that when his father retired as a police officer, life-insurance agents tried to persuade
him to take the larger checks, which would end upon his death, and invest the difference in a life-insurance
policy. "But life insurance tends to get more expensive as you get older," he says. "It's usually a better deal to
take the reduced benefit in your plan" that will continue to support your spouse when you're gone.
A few traditional pension plans, and most cash-balance plans, offer you the option of taking your payout as a
lump sum instead of as a fixed annuity. "There is often a natural predisposition to take the cash, and that is
often the wrong decision," says Mr. Schieber of Watson Wyatt. A glance at the battered stock market shows
why: It's tough to predict investment returns, not to mention how long you're going to live, which means
there's a good chance you could outlive your money. Even if you have a 401(k) or other defined-contribution
plan, which automatically pays you a lump sum, you should still consider using at least part of that payment to
buy a fixed, immediate annuity on your own, he adds.
There is one time when it might make sense to take the lump sum instead of the monthly checks. When
interest rates are extremely low, as they are now, you may be able to get a large enough lump-sum payout
to buy an annuity from a commercial insurer that pays you more each month than you would get from your
company plan. Here's why: When interest rates are down, your company plan has to allot a larger amount
upfront to provide the needed return for your monthly check. And your plan would have to pay you that larger
amount as your lump sum.
But do your annuity shopping before you take the lump sum to make sure you can get a better deal, warns
Lynda Abend, a managing actuary for New York Life Insurance Co.'s investment management unit. This
strategy works on occasion for men, but almost never for women, because their long life expectancies usually
make commercial annuities costly, she notes.
You should generally avoid variable annuities, which often carry high fees and surrender charges that must
be paid if you pull money out early. Instead, look for fixed annuities in which there is a guaranteed rate of
return, either for your lifetime or for a period of years -- and no charge for cashing out at the end of that
period.
You've probably had it drummed into your head for so long that you're investing for the long term in your
401(k) that you don't realize that once you've selected an asset mix, you still need to keep buying and selling
so the proportions don't get out of whack. That's one reason so many workers wound up heavily weighted --
and then decimated -- by tech stocks. "If you put 15% of your portfolio in technology in 1996, it could have
increased to 70% of your portfolio in 1999," says Mr. Delaplane of the American Benefits Council. "If it tanks,
it affects 70% of your portfolio, not 15%."
One tool that can help you overcome your own inaction is a "lifestyle" or "balanced" fund geared toward your
age. "So if you're in your 20s, the investment options are more aggressive," he says. "But as you age, the

--- Page 110 ---
Page 110 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
fund becomes more conservative automatically. And it automatically rebalances itself to reflect market
conditions."
If you decide to invest in such a fund, put all of your retirement-plan money in it, says Mr. Bogosian.
Otherwise, if you make it one of a dozen selections, "you're undoing the strategy" of having one fund balance
out your investments.

encore@wsj.com.

History Lessons:Past Crises OfferHope for Economy,Warnings to Watch---Railroads, in 1870s, OvercameA Fiber-Optic-Style Glut;1929 Proved ...
History Lessons: Past Crises Offer Hope for Economy, Warnings to Watch --- Railroads, in 1870s,
Overcame A Fiber-Optic-Style Glut; 1929 Proved Fed's Fallibility --- What War Can Do to Budgets
By Bob Davis
2,550 words
26 September 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Corrections & Amplifications
JOHN UPDIKE'S poem "Superman" was originally published in 1955. A page-one article last Thursday about
lessons of past economic crises incorrectly said the date was 1965.
(WSJ Oct. 3, 2002)
With stocks sagging, public anger at corporations rising and fear of war growing, what's in store for the
economy? Is the U.S. in for a replay of the 1930s, when the economy snapped after a stock-market plunge?
Or is it more like the 1960s, when war unleashed inflation? How about the 1970s, when oil shocks battered
the economy?
No single example fits today's combination of circumstances. But past epochs yield insights for today. The
railroad boom and bust of the 1870s offers the possibility of a happy ending to the telecom glut. The bursting
of that earlier financial bubble was followed by a new era of growth. The 1920s, meanwhile, are a cautionary
tale of how policy-maker overconfidence can strangle an economy. The 1960s teach the risks of cutting taxes
and fighting wars simultaneously. The 1970s suggest that productivity counts for more than
headline-grabbing oil embargoes.
"The economy is constantly feeding us surprises," says former Federal Reserve Vice Chairman Alan Blinder.
"Economists get ideas of what questions to ask by looking at history."
The Gilded Age
In the early 1870s, the railroad drove economic expansion and created glittering wealth much as telecom and
Internet investment did in the 1990s. Intercontinental railroads turned the U.S. into a unified market from
coast to coast. Retailers expanded to supply immigrants building the tracks. Land values soared along the
routes. Cargo that had taken weeks to travel by boat and wagon moved in days, a speed-up as revolutionary
as the one that networked computers brought in the 1990s. Indeed, telecom companies lifted the word "trunk
lines" from their railroad forebears.
The railroad era was riddled with corruption and miscalculation. Rail barons worked political connections to
obtain federal land grants. Speculators grew rich and binged on credit. "I wasn't worth two cents two years
ago," a character in Mark Twain's "The Gilded Age" remarks. "Now I owe $2 million."
An 1872 scandal gave an early warning of trouble. Owners of the Union Pacific Railroad set up a subsidiary,
Credit Mobilier, ostensibly to build a line west from Omaha. Its larger purpose was to siphon money through
overcharges. Profits went to Union Pacific executives and prominent lawmakers. Rail executives were grilled
in Congress and some lawmakers were censured.
A year later, everything collapsed. An overextended railroad financier, Jay Cooke, declared bankruptcy,
setting off a panic on Wall Street. The New York Stock Exchange closed for 10 days. A fifth of the railroads
filed for bankruptcy. The economy fell into deep recession. Rail stocks sank by a third between the end of
1873 and the middle of 1877. But then the railroads once again powered an expansion, this one so explosive
that journalists for the first time used the word "boom" to describe the economy, says Vanderbilt University
economic historian Jeremy Atack. By 1880, technological gains, including mightier locomotives and better
signal systems, were reducing shipping costs for manufacturers and department stores. The 1881 arrival of
refrigerated rail cars helped create a national meatpacking industry. Montgomery Ward turned to railroads to
deliver catalogs and fill orders. As demand surged, mothballed railroads came back into service, and more
were built. Railroad stocks revived.

--- Page 112 ---
Page 112 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Today's bankrupt optical-fiber networks are as fallow as some railroad tracks were in the 1870s. But the
networks still have the potential to be the backbone of a high-speed global communications system for years
to come. "Over the long haul, the predominant costs in [telecommunications systems] are acquiring rights of
way," says Dale Hatfield, a former chief of engineering at the Federal Communications Commission. Those
costs have already been paid.
What's missing today, as with the railroads in the 1870s, is sufficient demand. But demand could rise with
changes in electronic commerce, medical imaging, genetic information, entertainment or other technologies
that would require super-fast communications.
So one lesson from railroads' tumultuous history is a positive one: The bursting of a technology-powered
financial bubble can obscure the economic power of the underlying technology. A nearly worthless asset
today can become the mainstay of an economy tomorrow.
The Great Crash
Over two days in late October 1929, the Dow Jones Industrial Average plunged 24.6%. Bad spells were
hardly rare on Wall Street, but this one was followed by the Great Depression, which eventually put a quarter
of Americans out of work.
Economists today largely blame the Federal Reserve for worsening the downturn by raising interest rates
instead of cutting them. But Fed officials at the time thought they were acting wisely. In today's lingo, they
were trying to get ahead of the curve, and the Hoover White House was encouraging them.
In 1928, the Fed had raised interest rates to prick what it saw as a speculative bubble. The subsequent
stock drop and economic slowing led the Fed to believe mistakenly that it had prevented an even worse
outcome. After the stock-market crash, Fed officials made another misstep. The key to economic stability,
they thought, was preserving the gold standard. So to prevent a rush of investors demanding gold for their
dollars, they raised interest rates in 1931 and 1932. The increases wound up strangling the economy.
Protecting gold seemed like an obvious move to policy makers then. "Gold was moral, principled and
civilized; managed money was the opposite," write economists Barry Eichengreen of the University of
California at Berkeley and Peter Temin of the Massachusetts Institute of Technology.
Sixty years later, the Bank of Japan made a similar calculation, pricking a financial bubble and then resisting
calls to cut interest rates for fear of producing a new speculative binge. Japan's economy has stagnated for
more than a decade.
Alan Greenspan, however, learned from his Fed predecessors. In October 1987, after stocks plunged 22.6%
in a day, his Fed informed banks it would provide as much credit as needed to keep the system going. And in
1996, although Mr. Greenspan warned of "irrational exuberance," he didn't deflate the stock market through
interest-rate increases. After the market bubble burst on its own, the Fed responded by repeatedly lowering
interest rates.
Yet a larger lesson of the 1920s is that policy makers aren't as smart or all-knowing as the rest of us may
hope, or as they sometimes pretend. So, what mistakes might the Greenspan Fed be making now that will be
recognized only later? Will Mr. Greenspan's comforting words that rising housing prices aren't a bubble prove
faulty? Is the Fed fueling a new round of inflation by keeping rates too low now? Or, on the contrary, might it
be underestimating the economy's continuing weakness and not cutting rates enough?
The Populist '30s
In 1935, with the economy in turmoil, Wall Street in disrepute and the Supreme Court hostile to his programs,
Franklin D. Roosevelt turned sharply left. Attacking "economic royalists," he endorsed a soak-the-rich plan to
raise taxes on the wealthy, a bill to protect workers' right to organize and legislation to break up utility holding
companies.
Today's business scandals have also produced strong anticorporate sentiment. The president and the
Treasury secretary have denounced wayward executives. Congress has toughened criminal penalties on
executives who mislead shareholders. About 71% of Americans think "more should be done" to crack down
on corporate fraud, according to a September Wall Street Journal/NBC news poll.
But to translate such sentiments into political change requires effective and charismatic politicians. FDR was
prodded to action by challenges from Louisiana Sen. Huey Long and a fiery radio priest known as Father
Coughlin, who warned his millions of listeners about "predatory capitalism." Historian Alan Brinkley says a
secret poll the Democrats took showed Sen. Long might win up to 10% of the presidential vote if he ran as a
third-party candidate in 1936, possibly handing the White House to the Republicans. Sen. Long was scoring

--- Page 113 ---
Page 113 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
points with a "Share Our Wealth" plan that would limit individual fortunes to about $4 million and take the rest
in taxes.
Sen. Long was assassinated in September 1935. But by then, FDR had proposed his own wealth tax and
other measures to pre-empt the challenge. He won in a landslide the next year.
Today, no national leader is pressing an anticorporate crusade that might force the political establishment to
embrace FDR-style changes. Ralph Nader's Green Party has only 225,000 members. The Reform Party
started by Ross Perot is riven by factionalism. Antiglobalization protesters haven't been able to agree on a
specific agenda for change.
The Democratic Party tries to tap into the discontent but also wants to show it is pro-growth. Liberal Sen. Paul
Wellstone of Minnesota, in a re-election struggle, had his attacks on corporate misbehavior turned against
him when a GOP leader accused him of being soft on the failings of unions. "People are upset with business,
but do they think the country is run by big companies? No, they don't," says Ruy Teixeira, a liberal
Democratic pollster.
Much of the public is outraged over the behavior of some corporations, accountants and executives. But
without a captivating populist, the anger may not translate into deep economic changes.
The Vietnam Deficits
In the 1960s, the U.S. economy was as healthy as it had ever been. Inflation was tame, unemployment was
quiescent and incomes were rising. In a 1965 poem, John Updike poked fun at growing American
abundance:
I drive my car to the supermarket,
the way I take is superhigh,
superlot is where I park it,
and Super Suds are what I buy.
But by the end of the decade, inflation was darkening the picture. Growing budget deficits, caused by the
Vietnam War's unexpectedly high cost, were largely at fault.
Last month, with the U.S. facing possible war with Iraq, President Bush warned of the Vietnam parallels. "We
cannot go down the path of soaring budget deficits," he said.
Like Mr. Bush, Lyndon B. Johnson came into office convinced of the power of a tax cut. In LBJ's case, it was
the Kennedy tax cut, which was passed after Mr. Johnson succeeded JFK and spurred the economy as its
architects promised. But as Vietnam spending pushed the economy beyond its limits, Mr. Johnson's advisers
urged tax increases to slow the pace of economic growth. He resisted, worried that tax boosts would
undermine support for his Great Society programs.
A financial crisis finally forced him to act, says University of Missouri historian Robert Collins. Fearing a U.S.
devaluation, foreign governments began demanding gold for dollars. Speculation was so intense the U.S.
considered airlifting gold from Fort Knox to New York, to reassure governments they would get gold if they
demanded it. Communist China's news agency announced that "the capitalist system has in fact collapsed."
President Johnson eventually agreed to tax increases and spending cuts. They came too late to curb
inflation. Just 1.3% in the first half of the 1960s, inflation hit 5.5% in 1969. Despite the wage-price controls
Richard Nixon imposed in the early 1970s, inflation was at double digits at the end of the 1970s. It took a
series of Fed interest-rate increases and the worst recession since the Depression to break inflation's back.
Today deficits are again rising, and war spending is threatening to widen them. An Iraq war could cost $100
billion to $200 billion the year it happened, says White House economist Lawrence Lindsey, although others
offer lower estimates. Meanwhile, Mr. Bush is as committed to phasing in the rest of his $1.3 trillion tax cut as
Mr. Johnson was to avoiding a tax boost 35 years ago.
The risk this time isn't so much inflation as a growing debt burden and dearth of savings that would make it
hard to pay for baby boomers' retirement and medical care. That demographic reality may be the most
significant force pushing on President Bush to raise taxes or defer planned tax cuts.
A lesson from the Vietnam era -- and from the 1980s as well -- is that Congress isn't likely to reduce spending
sufficiently to cover deficits that are rising steeply. If deficits keep growing, so does the pressure to raise
taxes.

--- Page 114 ---
Page 114 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Stagnation in the '70s
The U.S. economy was battered twice in the 1970s by violent events in the Middle East. The 1973
Arab-Israeli war led to an oil embargo of the U.S. by the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries and a
quadrupling of oil prices. Then in 1979, oil prices doubled after Iranians overthrew the Shah and took
Americans hostage. Both times, the oil shocks spurred inflation in the U.S. and dumped its economy into
recession. For the decade, stock prices declined about 40%, taking inflation into account, say researchers
Ibbotson Associates in Chicago.
Though neither of the oil crises packed the shock of Sept. 11, they were major blows to confidence, making
Americans wonder if the nation faced still more traumas. The future seemed so unsure that in 1979,
President Carter warned of a national sense of "paralysis and stagnation and drift."
But one reason the oil shocks were so damaging was that they hit an economy that already faced another
malady: a decline in the growth of labor productivity. Rising productivity, or output for each hour of work,
permits employers to pay people more without spurring inflation. And productivity had been rising rapidly
from the end of World War II until about the time of the first oil shock.
It is now clear that for two decades starting in 1973, productivity flattened out. Just why is still debated.
Economists initially blamed the oil shocks, but productivity also lagged when oil prices came back down after
each oil-price jump.
Now stocks are again doing badly and economic growth is sluggish. The economy could take another wallop
if a war with Iraq sharply cut the flow of Middle East oil or another major terrorist attack undermined consumer
and business confidence.
But the productivity situation is different today than in the 1970s. Since 1996, productivity has been rising at
brisk levels similar to that of the 1960s. Once again, economists aren't altogether sure of the reason, but the
consequences are clear: In 2002, hourly earnings, adjusted for inflation, have grown nearly as fast as in the
mid-1960s.
This strength in productivity makes the economy more resilient to unexpected shocks. Says Mr. Greenspan:
"With the growth of productivity well maintained and inflation pressures largely absent, the foundation for
economic expansion has been laid."

Jos. A. Bank Tailors Strategy for Growth
Jos. A. Bank Tailors Strategy for Growth
By Ellen Sheng
Dow Jones Newswires
1,136 words
25 September 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
B5O
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- Men's clothing retailer Jos. A. Bank Clothiers Inc. is loosening its tie while dressing up its
bottom line.
Undaunted by the sluggish economy and abundant corporate casual-dress codes, the Hampstead, Md.,
company has surprised many with its ability to continue posting strong sales and expand to new locations.
The 97-year-old company recently announced the opening of its 148th store. Ultimately, the retailer envisions
a chain of 500 stores nationwide with 12 more store openings in the next two months and 30 more in 2003.
In August, the company reported that sales shot up 20% from year-ago levels to $14.3 million.
By contrast, August sales at department stores, Jos. A. Bank's main competitor, were soft. Men's Wearhouse
Inc., a comparable menswear retailer, said sales decreased 7.7% from a year ago to $78.5 million.
"Clearly, they are taking market share," said Michael Via, an analyst at Anderson & Strudwick. Jos. A. Bank's
stock price has jumped accordingly, trading at nearly five times its 52-week low of $4.08 a year ago.
Industry watchers say the company's quick response to the changing men's casual market allowed it to get a
leg up on the competition.
The demise of the mandatory suit at many offices put plenty of men at a loss about what to wear to work.
Madison Riley, a principal at management-consulting firm Kurt Salmon Associates, said manufacturers
struggled "with helping the consumer understand what business casual is, and then making the appropriate
product for them to purchase and consume."
Chief Executive Robert Wildrick says the company's "secret to success" wasn't fighting the trend toward
casual. "While others were fighting it, we created corporate casual garments," he said. "After all, corporate
casual doesn't have to mean corporate sloppy."
Having captured a segment of the casualwear market, the company is leveraging its position to grow its
brand name and grab more market share. Men tend to be more loyal to a store and brand, Mr. Wildrick said.
With the return of many Wall Street offices to formal dress codes -- Bear Stearns Cos. being one of the latest
-- Jos. A. Bank is standing ready to embrace the growing numbers of customers coming in to buy suits. Suits
make up about 30% of total sales, but Mr. Wildrick says the number is slowly inching up.
Industry observers say the expansion plan is savvy because it takes advantage of low interest rates.
"They're striking while the iron's hot and getting good rental agreements," said Mr. Via of Anderson &
Strudwick. It's a good time to expand now because most competitors aren't in the financial position to do so,
he added. "Malls love them."
Mr. Wildrick agrees. Developers are "coming aggressively after us," he said. If developers "want something
other than pizza parlors, something so that a dad or boyfriend can come shopping with the family, right now
we are the only game in town."
In addition to opening new stores, the company is in the process of remodeling old ones. The new stores
feature larger display windows facing the street, brighter lighting and wooden floors.
Mr. Wildrick describes the stores as "upbeat" and "more like a gentleman's Banana Republic."

--- Page 116 ---
Page 116 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
To ease the shopping experience for the fashion-unconscious, Jos. A. Bank designs all of its clothes so that
they coordinate with one another.
This way, "customers can willy-nilly pick them and they all match, season to season," Mr. Wildrick said.
Also, to cater to customers of different age groups and budgets, the company categorizes its clothing by three
price points. Mr. Wildrick likens the system to that of credit cards that offer green, gold and platinum cards.
A suit with a 150-thread count might sell for $1,400 and be sold to a big boss, while a suit with a lower thread
count starts at $395 and targets those first starting out in the work force.
"Our objective is to pick up customers at college level and take them to retirement," Mr. Wildrick said.
Although the average age of customers at Jos. A. Bank is about 35, the company is trying to reach out to
younger buyers.
Mr. Wildrick said the company is in talks with fraternities, campus groups and college administrations to offer
discounts for students out to buy their first suit. The company's online sales also are attracting a younger
demographic.
It is no surprise that Jos. A. Bank is targeting this group, since they are bigger spenders. "Guys in their 30s
are reluctant shoppers," said Mark-Evan Blackman, chairman of the menswear-design department at New
York's Fashion Institute of Technology. But younger, college-age men "are really into clothes and into
grooming."
Of course, selling to a younger group and tending to changing trends might require some alterations.
"Jos. A. Bank has been catering to those people who went to Ivies and like a clean, classic look. Now, that
New England country club style has changed. People are keeping elements of that, but head-to-toe, it's seen
less frequently these days," Mr. Blackman said.
The company recently took a step away from its core of suits and corporate casualwear by introducing two
casual lines of clothing, Vacation In Paradise, dubbed V.I.P. for short, and David Leadbetter, a line of golf
apparel.
The lines, which sell within the stores, are both doing well, Mr. Wildrick said.
The company's efforts to cut out the middleman and get back into manufacturing have pleased both
customers and shareholders. This allowed the company to offer better quality clothing at a lower price.
"We work directly with the factory, then pass that savings to the customer," Mr. Wildrick said.
The move helped give the company tighter control over its product and has yielded higher profit margins,
noted Mr. Via, the Anderson & Strudwick analyst.
Jos. A. Bank now buys and designs the piece goods themselves, then contracts the manufacturing out. The
company doesn't actually own the factories but makes up 80% to 100% of production at the plants.
Despite the runup in its stock price, analysts see Jos. A. Bank as a company on the rise and forecast more
upside for the stock.
Preston Silvey, an analyst at First Dallas Securities, said the stock is trading at a "decent" multiple of about
14 times next year's earnings.
Also, he said, the company consistently raises its outlook.
Mr. Via, who has the stock as a top pick, said the stock is actually trading at something of a discount to other
retailers.
He agreed the company puts out reliable earnings, adding "they haven't missed a beat."

Small-Stock Focus:Abgenix, Meridian Gold and Skyworks Post Gains
Small-Stock Focus: Abgenix, Meridian Gold and Skyworks Post Gains
By Shaheen Pasha
Dow Jones Newswires
909 words
25 September 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
C8
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- Small-capitalization stocks fell, but managed to weather the storm in the U.S. stock markets
because of strength in biotechnology and other technology shares.
Still, the small-cap picture isn't much brighter than the large-cap one. The Russell 2000 remains at four-year
lows, and the Standard & Poor's SmallCap 600 index finished just two points above its one-year low. But
market trackers are giving small-caps some credit for holding up on a relative basis in an environment
plagued by profit disappointments, a sluggish economy, and the threat of war.
The Russell 2000 Index ended down 2.10 points, or 0.59%, at 356.58, while the Standard & Poor's SmallCap
600 Index dropped 1.11 points, or 0.6%, to 183.79. The Nasdaq Composite Index of both large and small
stocks finished down 2.76 points, or 0.23%, at 1182.17.
While most of Wall Street wasn't surprised with the Federal Open Market Committee's decision to leave the
federal-funds rate unchanged at 1.75%, there were hopes that the Federal Reserve's comments could
provide the market with some optimism. Those hopes were dashed, however, after the Fed acknowledged
the deepening slump in the labor market, a continuing decline in stock prices, and added more concern about
the heightened geopolitical risk's effect on expected pickup in production and employment.
While the broader market sold off sharply on the comments, small-caps managed to keep losses relatively
small.
Michael Sheldon, chief market strategist at Spencer Clarke LLC, said action among small caps is more
influenced by their valuation compared with large caps, the strength of the dollar, and whether the economy is
coming out of a recession.
Biotechs were the strongest performers of the session. Lehman Brothers initiated coverage on the sector with
a positive stance, saying the sector has dropped 45% this year and may be heading into a period of heavy
product launches by the end of this year or the beginning of next year. Credit Suisse First Boston initiated
coverage with an overweight rating. Abgenix jumped $1.02, or 18%, to $6.81, and Cell Therapeutics added
52 cents, or 12%, to 4.86.
Precious metals rose as the price of gold climbed on the back of the weak equity markets, a soft dollar, and a
rally in oil prices, spurring speculative and safe-haven buying of gold. Meridian Gold gained 23 cents, or
1.2%, to 19.73 as TVX Gold climbed 14 cents, or 0.9%, to 14.89.
Semiconductors were also among the session's top performers on an oversold bounce. The Philadelphia
Stock Exchange fell Monday to levels not seen since 1998, spurring some bargain hunters to come back into
chips. Skyworks gained 45 cents, or 10%, to 4.94. Asyst Technologies added 21 cents, or 2.8%, to 7.70.
But investors weren't so kind to diversified technology-services stocks after analysts downgraded Electronic
Data Systems and raised questions about its cash flow and weak earnings outlook. The pessimism spread to
smaller-cap stocks in the group, even though analysts said much of EDS's problems are company-specific.
KPMG Consulting dropped 1.12, or 15%, to 6.24.
Airlines stocks fell a second consecutive day. Crude-oil prices rose to their highest level in more than a year,
and investors remained cautious about the health of the industry and the possibility that UAL's United Airlines
will file for bankruptcy-law protection. UAL fell 22 cents, or 9.4%, to 2.12, and Atlantic Coast Airlines shed 64
cents, or 6.9%, to 8.69.
U.S. Physical Therapy shares hit a 52-week low, making the company one of the top percentage decliners on
the Nasdaq Stock Market after warning that third-quarter earnings would be lower than expected because of
a slowdown in patient visits. The stock tumbled 3.99, or 27%, to 10.77.

--- Page 118 ---
Page 118 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Radiologix of Dallas also fell to a 52-week low, after the company cut its 2002 earnings estimate, citing lower
procedure volume in July and August and an unexpectedly high level of physician vacations. Radiologix
(AMEX) dropped 3.35, or 41%, to 4.85.
Shares of small-cap Newport, which makes semiconductor capital equipment, shed 2.34, or 17%, to 11.56
after Credit Suisse First Boston downgraded the stock to underperform from neutral, saying
slower-than-expected semiconductor recovery will affect the company's growth.
Six Flags fell 27 cents, or 6.5%, to 3.90 after Morgan Stanley downgraded the stock to underweight from
overweight.
While preannouncement season has been pretty dismal so far, a number of companies bucked the trend by
raising their financial forecasts.
Marvel Enterprises (NYSE) raised its revenue and earnings forecast for the third quarter and full year,
primarily because of a "substantial" upfront cash payment and minimum revenue guarantees from a 10-year
games contract with Vivendi Universal. The stock gained 68 cents, or 11%, to 6.83.
Anadigics gained 20 cents, or 10%, to 2.21 after the company said it is on track to meet its previous
third-quarter projection for a loss of 33 cents a share before items, on revenue of $21 million, in line with
analysts' views. The company also plans to lay off 10% of its 450 employees.

Barrick Lays Out Plan to Boost Gold Output
Barrick Lays Out Plan to Boost Gold Output
By Mark Heinzl
452 words
18 September 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
B4
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
TORONTO -- Barrick Gold Corp. laid out a $2 billion program to increase substantially its gold production
with four new mines, and to double the company's earnings by 2006 from levels expected this year.
Production from the first new mine is expected to begin in 2005 with the newly discovered Alto Chicama
property in Peru, and by 2008, the mines will be contributing about two million ounces of gold to Barrick's
annual rate. The other mines are Cowal in Australia, Veladero in Argentina, and Pascua-Lama near Veladero
on the border with Chile.
Barrick has said it expects to produce about 5.7 million ounces of gold this year at an average cash operating
cost of $172 an ounce, and report net income "at the lower end" of the range of 42 to 47 cents a share, or
about $227 million to $254 million.
The new mines are expected to contribute a net increase of 1.2 million ounces to Barrick's production in
2006, when the company expects to reach 6.9 million ounces. Some of the new production is expected to be
offset by declining production at Barrick's Pierina mine in Peru, but the cash operating cost per ounce of the
new production is expected be about $125 an ounce, well below Barrick's current costs.
Analysts said Barrick's ambitious production and earnings targets appear achievable, but noted that the
largest project, Pascua-Lama, isn't expected to begin production until 2008. The project's "economics haven't
been spectacular," partly as a result of its high-altitude location far from infrastructure, said HSBC analyst
Victor Flores, adding that Argentina's economic woes are a concern.
Also, Barrick is basing its plan to double its earnings on a gold price of $325 an ounce, above the current
price of less than $317 an ounce.
Barrick also announced plans to reduce its amount of gold sold forward to about 12 million ounces by the
end of next year, from 17.9 million ounces currently.
Barrick cited a positive outlook for gold prices and less opportunity to create revenue from forward gold sales
as a result of lower interest rates. Analysts applauded the move. Investors "want to see the upside potential"
for Barrick's earnings, and "they don't want to see it capped" with locked-in prices, said Daniel McConvey, an
analyst with Goldman Sachs Group Inc.
Barrick's announcements didn't prevent its stock from declining along with others in the sector amid weaker
gold prices.
Shares of Barrick fell 38 cents to $17.08 in 4 p.m. New York Stock Exchange composite trading yesterday.

Thinking Things Over:A Monetary Bubble?
Thinking Things Over: A Monetary Bubble?
By Robert L. Bartley
1,054 words
16 September 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
A15
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
`What's a Bubble, Anyway?" I asked in an earlier column (Aug. 5), complaining that the term was often used
in lieu of analysis. I promised further thoughts on another day about whether monetary policy was the villain
in the 1999 boom and 2001 bust in the stock market.
Alan Greenspan pleaded innocent in his speech to the Fed's annual Jackson Hole coolout. In the weeks
since Paul Krugman threw his usual rotten fruit, Wall Street bears held a confab to talk up the idea of tighter
credit right now despite risk of a double-dip recession, and a prominent former Fed researcher wrote in the
Financial Times that a little boost in interest rates back in 1997 would have averted the pain investors felt in
2001.
Well, maybe. But a look at the dilemmas the Fed was facing tells something about the limits of monetary
policy.
First, what's the mission of a central bank? Serious people, in particular directors of the European Central
Bank, believe the mission is to mind the price level, period. By this measure, Mr. Greenspan's record is
impeccable. From 1996 to 2002, the years in question, increases in the consumer price index have ranged
from 1.8% to 3.4%. Since economists agree that the index overstates the increase by at least a percentage
point, we have achieved price stability.
Implicit in the "monetary bubble" argument, then, is a much more ambitious mission. While stabilizing prices
with one hand, the Fed is supposed to fight business cycles with the other. At Jackson Hole, Mr. Greenspan
suggested this was too much to ask. If the Fed had such perfect information, "so would private agents,
rendering the development of bubbles highly unlikely."
The best case for a monetary bubble is that during 1999 the "money supply" numbers grew rapidly, whether
measured by M-2 or the monetary base. This money had to go somewhere, the argument runs, and instead
of going into spending on goods and services it went into the stock market. This left consumer prices stable,
but created a market "euphoria" that couldn't go on forever. If the Fed had tightened in 1997, 1998 or 1999, it
would have stopped the run-up in stock prices and everyone would be happier today.
Central banking aficionados call it the "asset inflation" issue. If consumer prices are under control but prices
are soaring for assets such as stocks or real estate, should the central bank tighten by increasing its target
for short-term interest rates, presumably slowing monetary growth?
The Fed was debating this issue as far back as 1996. The transcripts of its monetary policy meetings
released after five years show that at the Sept. 26 meeting, then-Governor Larry Lindsey warned that stock
prices were too high, and suggested considering a move to "burst that bubble" before it "carries the economy
to stratospheric heights." Chairman Greenspan agreed that it was a problem to watch, but sketched a
dilemma.
If the Fed succeeds in stabilizing product prices, he said, "history tells us that price-earnings ratios under
those conditions go through the roof." There is a tension between consumer prices and asset prices; "if
monetary policy succeeds in one, it fails in the other." Ultimately, Mr. Lindsey voted with the majority in a
11-1 vote to leave target rates unchanged.
As a footnote, Mr. Greenspan did say the Fed could check the bubble by increasing margin requirements,
that is, lowering the amount of money stock buyers could borrow against their holdings. At Jackson Hole he
disavowed this belief, saying that margin sales holdings were not widespread enough that changes would
have a direct effect. A margin increase would curb prices only if it was seen as a psychological sign the Fed
might tighten monetary policy. In December 1996, Mr. Greenspan gave just such a psychological sign with
his noted remarks on "irrational exuberance;" share prices climbed on unabated.

--- Page 125 ---
Page 125 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
As stocks soared in succeeding years, other things were going on. Should the Fed have tightened in 1997,
when such a move would have exacerbated the currency crisis wracking Asia? It clearly was hinting at
tightening before Jackson Hole in the fall of 1998, but instead found itself easing with the Russian default
rattling through Long-Term Capital and the rest of the credit markets. Finally, it did tighten six times between
June 1999 and May 2000. This was the top of the boom; the Nasdaq peaked in March 2000; by the first
quarter of 2001 the real economy was shrinking.
Adding to the dilemma, during this whole period the dollar was going from strength to strength in the foreign
exchange markets. Tighter money would have driven it still higher, complicating problems in the rest of the
world and, by the way, attracting more foreign money into U.S. stocks, scarcely a way to "prick the bubble."
Similarly, if the Fed had tightened to curb asset prices, consumer prices might have fallen into outright
deflation. The strong dollar already suggested tight money, not easy money, as did gold and prices in foreign
nations linked to the dollar. Deflation can be even harder to fight than inflation. In December 1989 Yasushi
Mieno, newly installed as Governor of the Bank of Japan, set out to puncture the bubble with tight money; the
country still hasn't recovered.
The real-life forces and dilemmas before the Fed were a lot more complex than Mr. Greenspan's critics allow.
One lesson, it seems to me, is that international monetary events keep intruding. Even under floating
exchange rates, the Fed has to keep a more careful eye on the rest of the world.
Even more importantly, the Fed's been assigned every target on the horizon -- consumer prices, asset prices,
the value of the dollar, the business cycle. But it only has one shot to fire, changes in its short-term interest
rate targets. Right now, a lot of folks are expecting the Fed to jump-start the economy by cutting its target
rate, already the lowest in living memory. Too much to ask; a better bet would be a good tax cut.
(See related letter: "Letters to the Editor: Let's Pop the Myth About Market Bubbles" -- WSJ Oct. 1, 2002)

The Economy:August Retail Sales Rose 0.8%;Recent Consumer Sentiment Slips
The Economy: August Retail Sales Rose 0.8%; Recent Consumer Sentiment Slips
By Russell Gold
672 words
16 September 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
U.S. consumers are growing more fearful about future job prospects and the possibility of war with Iraq, but
those worries aren't stopping them from spending now.
The Commerce Department reported that retail sales rose a greater-than-expected 0.8% in August,
considered a strong showing after growth of a revised 1.1% in July and 1.4% in June. The record-setting
pace of mortgage refinancings seems to be giving consumers the confidence and cash to keep spending,
despite a weak stock market and little job growth.
At the same time, the University of Michigan consumer-sentiment index showed increasing concern about the
future. The index slipped in mid-September to 86.2 from 87.6 in August amid concern about the impact of
prolonged economic weakness on jobs and incomes. The preliminary survey is based on about 300 calls and
will be updated later in the month.
"There is what consumers do and what consumers think, and they don't always go together," said Diane
Swonk, chief economist at Bank One Corp. Talk of a coming war with Iraq by high-level Bush administration
officials and continued uncertainty about another terrorist attack are darkening consumers' outlook on their
future, she added. But "at the end of the day, what really matters is what's in your checkbook," and
consumers' checkbooks are flush with money from mortgage refinancings and a relatively low unemployment
rate of 5.7%.
As has been the case for months, consumers continued to spend freely on cars in August. Sales of
automobiles rose 2.2% in dollar terms, slower than revised growth of 4.3% the month before. What caught
economists by surprise was strong growth in other sectors: Excluding automobiles, sales rose 0.4% in
August, up from 0.2% a month earlier. Home-furnishing stores did well, as did general-merchandise
discounters. To be sure, some analysts say the early Labor Day holiday may have pushed up back-to-school
shopping and distorted the numbers.
Bruce Steinberg, chief economist at Merrill Lynch, expects strong consumer-spending growth to last through
the year-end holiday-shopping season and into next spring. That's because mortgage refinancings are
running at record levels and consumers typically enjoy a six- to nine-month boost in spending after filing a
refinance application. But when that runs out of steam, Mr. Steinberg cautioned, consumer spending could
drop quickly if the private sector hasn't begun to create enough new jobs to raise wages. "Income growth
won't be strong enough to sustain this kind of consumer spending," he said.
The University of Michigan consumer-expectations index, which measures attitudes a year ahead, slipped to
80 from 80.6, while the current-conditions index dropped to 95.9 from 98.5.
Separately, the Labor Department reported Friday that the producer-price index for finished goods, a widely
watched gauge of inflation, was unchanged in August, suggesting negligible upward price pressure. But,
again, the statistics paint a different picture for the future, as some price pressure appeared in the pipeline.
The price of intermediate goods, such as cement and lumber, increased 0.4%, rising for the sixth consecutive
month. The price of unprocessed commodities, such as steel scrap and soybeans, rose 1.6%.
This divergence -- steady prices for finished goods as the price of raw materials rises -- has been in evidence
for several months and suggests a squeeze on profit margins as competitive pressure prevents producers
from passing through their higher material prices.
---

Journal at WSJ.com/JournalLinks.

The Economy:Foreign InvestorsSee Little LusterIn U.S. Assets
The Economy: Foreign Investors See Little Luster In U.S. Assets
By Michael M. Phillips and Russell Gold
1,682 words
13 September 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Corrections & Amplifications
U.S. EXPORTS of goods rose to $172.7 billion in the second quarter, from $164.6 billion in the first quarter,
the Commerce Department reported. An article Friday about the current-account deficit incorrectly reported
the U.S. goods-export figures as $122.6 billion and $106.4 billion, respectively. Those figures are for the
deficit on U.S. trade in goods for the two periods.
(WSJ: Sept. 16, 2002)
Foreign investors are losing some of their enthusiasm for buying U.S. companies, factories and offices.
The drop-off reflects hard times in Europe and Japan, where companies have less cash on hand to invest in
the U.S., and the diminished appeal of American assets during a shaky U.S. recovery. It comes at a time
when the record trade gap is making it all the more important that the U.S. continue to attract enormous
amounts of foreign capital to finance business investment, to keep the dollar from collapsing and to prevent
interest rates from climbing.
Net direct equity investment in the U.S. dropped to $8.6 billion during the second quarter, the lowest figure
since 1995, the Commerce Department reported. With mergers-and-acquisitions activity in the U.S. drying up,
direct foreign equity investment measured just $19.6 billion during the first six months of the year. That is way
off the pace that resulted in $108 billion in foreign direct equity purchases during 2001, and much less than
the $246 billion during 2000.
Foreign direct investment "has gone from being a huge, huge positive for the U.S., to more neutral in the first
half of last year, and now to a definite negative," said Robert Sinche, head of global currency strategy for
Citigroup Inc. "It's unlikely you would see a turnaround on that in the very short term."
The decline in equity investment comes despite a sizable drop in the dollar; although foreign investors can
buy more U.S. assets for a given amount of local currency, they also run the risk of getting less back when
they repatriate dollars into their home currency, should the dollar continue to fall.
Direct investment, combined with foreign purchases of U.S. stocks and bonds, essentially allows Americans
to import more than they export. The Commerce Department said the current-account deficit, the widest
measure of the trade imbalance, hit a high of $130 billion for the second quarter, up from $112.5 billion during
the first three months of the year.
Imports of goods -- perhaps bloated because of importers' fears that a threatened West Coast port strike
might make it harder to bring goods into the country later -- shot to $295.3 billion for the second quarter from
$271.1 billion. Oil, cars and consumer goods led the way. Exports, aided by a somewhat weaker dollar, rose
to $122.6 billion from $106.4 billion.
The current-account deficit measured 5% of the country's domestic economic output, a figure traditionally
thought to be well above the danger point. Such a large trade gap sharpens the fear that foreigners might
suddenly grow wary of all U.S. assets, an event that could spark a plunge in the dollar and a rise in interest
rates. The dollar's fall might generate more exports, but it could also generate inflation. A market-driven rise
in interest rates could frighten consumers, who are the impetus behind the U.S. economic recovery.
For the moment, foreign investors, ducking U.S. stocks, are buying corporate bonds, Treasurys and bonds
issued by government-sponsored enterprises. Net foreign investment in all U.S. assets totaled $221 billion for
the second quarter, up from $113 billion during the first quarter, but behind the 2001 inflow pace.

--- Page 129 ---
Page 129 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The result is that the dollar, while steadier recently, has lost ground against the euro and the yen this year.
The euro was valued at 98.21 cents in late New York trading, up from .97.58 cents a day earlier. The dollar
bought 120.09 yen, compared with 120.36 late Wednesday.
"I think the dollar has taken its hit and will stabilize for the remainder of the year, because investors are going
to see that the U.S. economy continues to offer a better set of financial assets than those available in other
industrial-country markets," said Catherine Mann, senior fellow at the Institute for International Economics, a
Washington think tank.
But the falloff in foreign direct investment is a reminder that the U.S. economy and the dollar, which once
seemed invulnerable, are on unsteady ground.
In a survey of chief executives to be released this month, A.T. Kearney, a management-consulting subsidiary
of EDS Inc., found that for the first time since the report began in 1998, the U.S. isn't executives' top choice
for direct investment. Kearney won't yet reveal the top destination.
On a mission to Japan earlier this year, Pennsylvania Deputy Secretary for International Business
Development Michael Wolf found little interest among Japanese companies in locating facilities in his state.
To compensate, he has been working on drumming up interest among countries such as Singapore and
South Africa, with modest results.
Foreign investors are losing some of their enthusiasm for buying U.S. companies, factories and offices.
The drop-off reflects hard times in Europe and Japan, where companies have less cash on hand to invest in
the U.S., and the diminished appeal of American assets during a shaky U.S. recovery. It comes at a time
when the record trade gap is making it all the more important that the U.S. continue to attract enormous
amounts of foreign capital to finance business investment, to keep the dollar from collapsing and to prevent
interest rates from climbing.
Net direct equity investment in the U.S. dropped to $8.6 billion during the second quarter, the lowest figure
since 1995, the Commerce Department reported. With mergers-and-acquisitions activity in the U.S. drying up,
direct foreign equity investment measured just $19.6 billion during the first six months of the year. That is way
off the pace that resulted in $108 billion in foreign direct equity purchases during 2001, and much less than
the $246 billion during 2000.
Foreign direct investment "has gone from being a huge, huge positive for the U.S., to more neutral in the first
half of last year, and now to a definite negative," said Robert Sinche, head of global currency strategy for
Citigroup Inc. "It's unlikely you would see a turnaround on that in the very short term."
The decline in equity investment comes despite a sizable drop in the dollar; although foreign investors can
buy more U.S. assets for a given amount of local currency, they also run the risk of getting less back when
they repatriate dollars into their home currency, should the dollar continue to fall.
Direct investment, combined with foreign purchases of U.S. stocks and bonds, essentially allows Americans
to import more than they export. The Commerce Department said the current-account deficit, the widest
measure of the trade imbalance, hit a high of $130 billion for the second quarter, up from $112.5 billion during
the first three months of the year.
Imports of goods -- perhaps bloated because of importers' fears that a threatened West Coast port strike
might make it harder to bring goods into the country later -- shot to $295.3 billion for the second quarter from
$271.1 billion. Oil, cars and consumer goods led the way. Exports, aided by a somewhat weaker dollar, rose
to $122.6 billion from $106.4 billion.
The current-account deficit measured 5% of the country's domestic economic output, a figure traditionally
thought to be well above the danger point. Such a large trade gap sharpens the fear that foreigners might
suddenly grow wary of all U.S. assets, an event that could spark a plunge in the dollar and a rise in interest
rates. The dollar's fall might generate more exports, but it could also generate inflation. A market-driven rise
in interest rates could frighten consumers, who are the impetus behind the U.S. economic recovery.
For the moment, foreign investors, ducking U.S. stocks, are buying corporate bonds, Treasurys and bonds
issued by government-sponsored enterprises. Net foreign investment in all U.S. assets totaled $221 billion for
the second quarter, up from $113 billion during the first quarter, but behind the 2001 inflow pace.
The result is that the dollar, while steadier recently, has lost ground against the euro and the yen this year.
The euro was valued at 98.21 cents in late New York trading, up from .97.58 cents a day earlier. The dollar
bought 120.09 yen, compared with 120.36 late Wednesday.

--- Page 130 ---
Page 130 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"I think the dollar has taken its hit and will stabilize for the remainder of the year, because investors are going
to see that the U.S. economy continues to offer a better set of financial assets than those available in other
industrial-country markets," said Catherine Mann, senior fellow at the Institute for International Economics, a
Washington think tank.
But the falloff in foreign direct investment is a reminder that the U.S. economy and the dollar, which once
seemed invulnerable, are on unsteady ground.
In a survey of chief executives to be released this month, A.T. Kearney, a management-consulting subsidiary
of EDS Inc., found that for the first time since the report began in 1998, the U.S. isn't executives' top choice
for direct investment. Kearney won't yet reveal the top destination.
On a mission to Japan earlier this year, Pennsylvania Deputy Secretary for International Business
Development Michael Wolf found little interest among Japanese companies in locating facilities in his state.
To compensate, he has been working on drumming up interest among countries such as Singapore and
South Africa, with modest results.
