Many EconomistsSee Slim ChanceFor a Double Dip
Many Economists See Slim Chance For a Double Dip
By Russell Gold
626 words
10 September 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
An overwhelming majority of private-sector economists say there is no more than a 25% chance the economy
will sag back into recession.
The Blue Chip Economic Indicators, a monthly survey of more than 50 economists, said 55% of respondents
believe there is a one-in-four chance of a "double-dip recession" -- when an economy in recession returns to
growth in output, then posts more negative quarters shortly thereafter. An additional 41% of panelists thought
another downturn is even less likely. One economist cited growing corporate earnings and strong consumer
spending as reasons for sustained economic growth, assuming there isn't a rise in oil prices.
The economists' outlook isn't entirely sanguine, however; about two-thirds of those polled believe the
unemployment rate hasn't peaked, even though the government reported Friday that the U.S. jobless rate fell
to 5.7% in August from 5.9% in July. A commentary that accompanies the survey highlights a
larger-than-expected decline in August factory employment as a note of caution.
Meanwhile, anticipating a pickup in U.S. economic activity, wholesalers increased their inventories by 0.6% in
July, the Commerce Department reported, exceeding an 0.2% increase expected by Wall Street economists.
This follows an upwardly revised 0.4% increase in June, marking the first time wholesalers have expanded
their inventory of goods for two consecutive months since November and December 2000, at the tail end of
the economic expansion.
The reports signal an emerging optimism despite a rash of up-and-down economic data and stock-market
performances, says Vincent Boberski, chief economist at RBC Dain Rauscher. "The inventory correction is
over," he said. "We can be pretty comfortable that we are back to more solid growth in the broader economy."
More than 80% of the economists surveyed said they believe the Federal Reserve's Open Market Committee
won't lower interest rates to stimulate the economy before year end. Their consensus puts the federal-funds
rate target at 3% at the end of 2003, up from 1.75% now.
The consensus forecast for real gross-domestic-product growth in 2002 remained at 2.3% for the second
straight month. Economists are cheered by record home sales in July, strong car sales in August and a stock
market that has risen from its midsummer lows. But economists worry the manufacturing sector isn't coming
back to life as hoped. They expect growth to accelerate somewhat next year; the consensus forecast calls for
3.2% GDP growth in calendar 2003.
The wholesalers' report showed inventory rebuilding replacing inventory liquidation. The inventory-to-sales
ratio is holding steady at a record-low 1.23 months, suggesting the expected growth in final sales would
quickly translate into increased production. The ratio is a measure of how long it would take a wholesaler to
deplete its inventory at the current sales pace.
Separately, the Federal Reserve Board reported a surprisingly sharp annualized 10.8% increase in
credit-card debt in July, up from 6% a month earlier. For much of the last year, consumers had been keeping
the revolving debt in check in part by paying off credit-card balances with cash extracted during mortgage
refinancing. Nonrevolving debt, such as automobile loans, grew an annualized 5.3% in July, down from 6.4%
a month earlier.

Currency Trading:Dollar Gains on Jobs Data, but Clouds Still Loom
Currency Trading: Dollar Gains on Jobs Data, but Clouds Still Loom
By John Parry
Dow Jones Newswires
878 words
9 September 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
C18
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- A more upbeat-than-expected jobs report last week restored some confidence for the
economic recovery and helped the dollar climb, but underlying uncertainties could thwart a sustained
improvement for sentiment.
Late Friday, the dollar was at 118.54 yen, up from 118.24 yen late Thursday in New York. The euro was at
98.04 U.S. cents, down sharply from 99.32 cents Thursday. Against the Swiss franc, the dollar was at 1.4865
francs, up from 1.4707 francs, while sterling was at $1.5568, down from $1.5675.
The dollar rallied after the August jobs report showed the unemployment rate fell to 5.7%, its lowest level
since March, and a bigger increase in nonfarm-business payrolls than expected. Even if the report represents
a turning point for the economy, the dollar may not benefit if growth remains sluggish, currency strategists
said. "What we now have are four consecutive months of very modest employment gains," said Robert
Sinche, chief currency strategist with Citibank in New York. For the dollar, that "leaves the status quo," in
place, he added.
The U.S. currency should remain within the range of 117.5 and 119 yen, while the euro is expected to hover
between 97.8 and 99.9 cents this week, strategists said. The dollar may tread water for a time as currency
traders digest the change of outlook for monetary policy.
One factor that supported the dollar was expectations the Federal Reserve would ease interest rates,
accelerating U.S. economic growth. These expectations have changed. A Dow Jones/CNBC survey of
traders taken after the jobs report found all expected the Fed to keep its short-term interest rate unchanged at
1.75% at the Federal Open Market Committee meeting scheduled for Sept. 24. A majority expect the
federal-funds rate to end the year at the same level.
Jason Bonanca, foreign-exchange analyst with Credit Suisse First Boston in New York, said the Fed leaving
interest rates unchanged may fail to spark economic growth, which "over the longer haul isn't good news for
the dollar.
The Fed's monetary stance could hurt the dollar if investment flows dribble out of the Treasury-bond market,
of which investors outside the U.S. own about 40%. After Treasury-bond yields -- which move inversely to
prices -- fell to nearly four-decade lows last week, the sector is showing signs of strain. Some bond-fund
strategists are recommending shifting funds from short-dated Treasurys into short-dated
European-government bonds.
Thomas Sowanick, Merrill Lynch's chief global fixed-income strategist, said in a research report last week he
sees Europe as the most-favored government-bond market, based on "the risk of weaker economic growth . .
. which could prompt investors to begin to discount the need for the European Central Bank to ease"
monetary policy.
European sovereigns are likely to benefit as investors come to the conclusion, "We are probably at a point
where Treasurys . . . are not the place to be," said Amy Falls, global fixed-income strategist at Morgan
Stanley in New York. A shift could be negative for the dollar, which is more dependent on inflows of foreign
capital than its rivals the euro and yen.
The U.S. current-account deficit, which is about 4.3% of gross domestic product, continues to weigh on the
dollar. Japan and Europe have current-account surpluses. The release this week of a reading of the U.S.
current account for the second quarter will likely show it widened to record levels, Mr. Sinche said; which
would likely weigh on the dollar.

--- Page 134 ---
Page 134 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Europe's economic rebound appears to be hitting stumbling blocks, and despite indications measures
designed to help Japan is in the offing, analysts remain skeptical the world's second-largest economy will pick
up the pace as a result.
Japan's Economy Minister Heizo Takenaka said in Italy on Friday the Japanese government is set to unveil
overhauls including tax cuts to address some of the country's structural problems.
Overshadowing such considerations for the currency markets are prevailing geopolitical concerns. Opinions
are mixed about the potential effects of a U.S.-led attack on Iraq. Some analysts say the dollar priced in most
negative effects of the initial stages of a campaign, although if such a conflict became protracted, it might
drive investors into havens such as the franc and gold.
"The risks of an Iraq war appear to be nearly fully embedded in the [fixed-income and foreign-exchange]
markets right now," said Even Berntsen, a managing director with J.P. Morgan Chase in New York. "In itself, I
don't think a war will propel the dollar to go weaker from here," he said.
Others disagreed. "There is no doubt that one effect of an attack on Iraq or news of any imminent plans to do
so would be to enable the euro to break [above] parity" with the dollar, while against the yen, the dollar would
likely weaken to about the 116 yen mark, said Paresh Upadhyaya, currency analyst with Putnam Investments
in Boston.

The New CredoOn Campus:`Just Charge It'---Credit-Card Companies TargetStudents Before Freshman Year;Should Your Kid Get One?
The New Credo On Campus: `Just Charge It' --- Credit-Card Companies Target Students Before
Freshman Year; Should Your Kid Get One?
By Ruth Simon And Christine Whelan
1,215 words
3 September 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
D1
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
WHEN LAURA CACCAVONE set off for her first semester of college last month, she brought along her
favorite stuffed animals, hot pink sheets -- and a Capital One Visa with a $300 credit limit.
"I know I have it if I need it," says the 18-year-old freshman at Drew University in Madison, N.J., who also
uses the card to buy gas and the occasional must-have accessory.
As if there weren't already enough temptations at college, students are using plastic to pay for more of their
purchases. In all last year, students used cash for just 42% of expenditures, compared with 53% the previous
year. Students now pay more of their bills with debit, credit or ATM cards or even their college IDs.
In addition, more students who have credit cards are getting them before they set foot on campus. While
campuses have always been prime territory for credit-card marketers, many students are now getting
targeted earlier. Forty-eight percent of college students with major credit cards in their own name got them
before their freshman year, according to a spring 2002 survey by Student Monitor, a market-research firm in
Ridgewood, N.J. That's up from 34% four years earlier.
The student market is a small part of the overall credit-card business, but it's one of the few places where
card issuers can find a fresh set of customers each year. Marketing to students also allows lenders to build
relationships with people who are likely to need auto loans, home mortgages and other financial services in
the future. And with most students carrying just one or two cards, competition to be the first card in the
student's wallet is keen.
Capital One sends direct-mail offers to parents of 16- to 18-year-olds pitching them credit cards for their
children. Citibank, Discover Financial Services and Wells Fargo offer cards to students once they turn 18,
generally the minimum age to have a card in your own name. MBNA, which has affinity relationships with
roughly 700 colleges and universities, sends mailings to about 100,000 freshmen before they arrive at school
each year. On campus, issuers offer enticements such as T-shirts and sunglasses.
The deluge of credit cards comes as many students are taking on more debt to pay for college itself. The
result: Students are taking on "substantial debt loads that will clearly affect them for the rest of their lives,"
says Michael Olivas, a law professor at the University of Houston.
Already, many students can't keep up with their bills. Students are nearly three times as likely to be at least
90 days delinquent on their credit-card payments as older adults, according to a recent study by the
Georgetown University Credit Research Center. They also are more likely to pay late fees and over-the-limit
fees. Still, credit-card issuers seldom take big losses on student accounts, both because the balances are
smaller and because parents often bail out their children.
Roughly 15% of schools no longer allow credit-card companies to solicit on campus, Discover Financial
Services estimates. But such moves don't always deter solicitors. At John Jay College of Criminal Justice in
New York, vendors bring their own portable generators and set up shop across the street from campus,
offering promotional T-shirts, mugs and candy bars.
The issue has raised some concerns in Congress as well. The Senate Banking Committee plans to hold
hearings Thursday on the marketing of credit cards to college students.
Lisbeth Minoso, a John Jay senior, says she was 17 when she was first solicited by credit-card vendors on
her way to classes. "I believed if they were outside the college, the college had granted them permission,"
says Ms. Minoso, who was forced to drop out of school for two years after her credit-card charges
skyrocketed. She still has about $10,000 in credit-card debt and an additional $23,000 in student loans taken
out to pay down debt.

--- Page 136 ---
Page 136 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The credit-card industry says such cases are the exception and that cards provide students an opportunity to
learn financial responsibility. Issuers also note that they provide special educational materials designed to
help students better manage their money.
Parents should shop carefully because rates vary widely, from as low as 8.9% on Blue for Students, an
American Express card, to nearly 18% on a Discover Card pitched to students. Credit cards marketed to
students typically carry lower initial credit limits, often $300 to $1,000. Some cards also have special features.
MBNA, for instance, recently introduced a credit card that allows parents to set the credit limit, receive a copy
of their child's bill and monitor charges online.
Some students may be better off with a debit card or a stored-value card, which allows students to spend up
to a preset amount. Marcus Demott, a Rutgers University junior, opted for a debit card instead of a credit card
when he started college. "I'm afraid I would end up using the credit card way too much," he says.
Indeed, debit cards and ATM cards are more popular than credit cards, accounting for 15% and 11% of
spending, respectively. Credit cards are used to pay for 9% of monthly purchases, Student Monitor found. In
addition, a growing number of colleges now give students identification cards that can also be used to buy
everything from books to pizza, with the school getting a slice of the revenue.

Open Wallets:In Uneasy Times, ConsumersBoost a Fragile U.S. Economy---Now, Can Purchases of Homes,Cars Continue to OffsetScant Busine...
Open Wallets: In Uneasy Times, Consumers Boost a Fragile U.S. Economy --- Now, Can Purchases of
Homes, Cars Continue to Offset Scant Business Spending? --- `Jump Now and Think Later'
By Greg Ip and Russell Gold
2,256 words
3 September 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Immediately after Sept. 11, it looked as if the U.S. economy might buckle. Americans hunkered down for war,
stock prices plunged, and business investment, already weak, slumped further.
But it turns out the terrorist assaults marked the ending phase of a recession, not its intensification. How?
Consumers such as Christine Grable in Austin, Texas, kept buying.
In the wake of Sept. 11, Mrs. Grable and her then-fiance cut back on fancy restaurant meals, concerts and
what she calls "joy spending." But their somber mood faded quickly. The couple bought a house in April and
jetted off for a Hawaiian honeymoon the following month. Though her employer, Dell Computer Corp., was
shedding jobs, Mrs. Grable felt confident she would keep hers -- and she has. "It was kind of, jump now and
think later," says the 34-year-old marketing specialist.
If Sept. 11 seemed to signal the American way of life was under attack, American-style spending seemed the
proper response. "The American economy will be open for business," President Bush assured the nation the
evening of Sept. 11. General Motors Corp., kicking off a campaign of no-interest financing on new cars, urged
consumers to "Keep America rolling."
They did -- and have continued to do so in the year since. The latest evidence came Friday, when the
Commerce Department reported consumers had increased their spending by 1% in July, the largest advance
in nine months. Even as Americans' incomes, which include wages, interest and government benefits, were
flat in July, rising home values, booming mortgage refinancing and low interest rates kept consumers
reaching for their wallets.
The vital question now is whether consumers -- whose spending accounts for two-thirds of all U.S. economic
activity -- will continue shelling out for cars, appliances, furniture and services. As businesses remain
reluctant to increase their expenditures, and economic trouble abroad endangers U.S. exports, consumers
are more critical than usual to the economy.
Last fall, consumer purchases saved the U.S. economy from deep recession. The Federal Reserve played a
big role by cutting short-term interest rates to a 41-year low. So did the Bush administration and Congress,
with a tax cut that began last summer with $300 rebate checks and continued with reduced tax withholdings
earlier this year. Auto makers and retailers helped with sharp discounts.
But none of that would have been sufficient if consumers hadn't continued to spend liberally. "We had an
event with a powerful psychological impact, with the potential to really cut into consumer spending at a time
the economy was already weak," says Laurence Meyer, who retired as a Federal Reserve governor in
January. "It's very surprising it had such little impact on the consumer. Maybe it really is true: We love to
shop."
What explains Americans' willingness to keep spending? Why weren't people as distracted as they were
when Iraq invaded Kuwait back in 1990, setting the stage for the Gulf War and a U.S. recession?
The burst of patriotism in response to the first attack on U.S. soil since Pearl Harbor played a role, as did the
quick victory in Afghanistan and the fact that oil prices fell rather than rose as they did in 1990. But that
doesn't explain the entire mystery. Simple economic calculations have also been at work. Over the past year,
Americans have sized up their financial situations and liked what they saw. Paychecks proved more important
than paranoia.
Take-home pay has risen. Mortgage rates have tumbled. Inflation has been tame. Although unemployment
has risen, it remained lower than it had been for most of the 1990s and 1980s. The stock market plunged,
causing many people to rethink retirement dates and abandon fantasies of exponentially expanding 401(k)s.
But for a lot of people, stock losses were offset by the rising value of their biggest asset: their home. Although

--- Page 139 ---
Page 139 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Americans aren't as upbeat about their finances as they were in the heady days of 1998, surveys by the
University of Michigan find consumers more optimistic about their financial future than at all but a handful of
occasions in the past three decades.
As the Sept. 11 anniversary approaches, the vitality of consumer spending is once again in question. A wave
of corporate scandals has pummeled the stock market, wiping out more wealth and, in some ways, shaking
Americans' faith in the nation's institutions to a greater extent than the terrorist attacks did.
The boost to spending from falling mortgage rates and last year's tax cuts eventually will dissipate. Pessimists
argue that if the economy goes through a prolonged period of slow growth, then rising unemployment,
stagnant wealth and weak income growth could erode the optimism the last decade's prosperity fostered. In
that case, the higher debts that have financed much of the recent spending will be harder to support.
"Consumers are hardly even aware we've had a recession," says Edward Leamer, a forecaster at the
University of California at Los Angeles. "There's certainly no evidence they've come to grips with the fact that
the future isn't what it used to be. That adjustment has to be made."
But even if consumers slow their spending for a while to rebuild savings, a jarring collapse of consumer
spending seems unlikely -- unless the U.S. economy suffers another devastating blow, such as a far steeper
plunge in the stock market or a sustained surge in oil prices. For example, auto sales surged in July and
appear to have done so in August as well, in response to a new round of no-interest-financing offers.
Over the course of the recession, which began in March 2001 and appears to have ended in December,
inflation-adjusted consumer spending grew at an annual rate of 3.2%, better than in any of the previous five
recessions. This year, consumption has accelerated to an annual rate of nearly 4%. Without the burst of
spending that followed the attacks, the recession would have lasted longer, and the unemployment rate
would have risen higher.
The most important factor in consumers' buy-now, worry-later attitude was continued gains in their purchasing
power. In most downturns and the periods that immediately follow them, layoffs and deferred raises constrain
household incomes and thus consumers' ability to spend. This time, inflation-adjusted aftertax incomes eked
out a small gain during the recession and then rose strongly afterward. During the 12 months ended in July,
inflation-adjusted income increased by 4%. At a comparable point in the previous two recoveries, it didn't
grow at all.
The Bush tax cut accounted for some of that increase. But consumers also benefited from a more permanent
economic change: Gains in productivity growth, which began in the mid-1990s, continued through the
recession. With workers producing more for each hour they work, companies can pay higher wages without
raising prices. After-inflation hourly earnings are now growing about 2% a year. They hardly grew at all
through most of the 1980s and 1990s.
One particularly comforting development for consumers, according to monthly surveys by the University of
Michigan, is a steady decline in inflation to its lowest levels since the 1960s. Consumers see it at the cash
register, as Wal-Mart, Costco, Target and other discounters have used their buying clout and efficient
inventory management to hold down prices.
Falling monthly mortgage payments have been a major spur to consumer spending, too. Mortgage rates
tumbled after Sept. 11, rebounded and fell to new lows this summer. That has made it easier for Americans to
buy homes and, just as important, to refinance mortgages and pocket the leftover cash.
In October, Matthew Pallatto, a mechanical engineer in Southbury, Conn., went onto Priceline.com and
quickly found a deal to cut a full percentage point from his mortgage rate. He knocked $50 a month from his
monthly payments, even though he borrowed an additional $13,000, which he is using to remodel his
basement. He hasn't been happy to see the losses in his mutual funds, but they are for his retirement. "I'm
not that worried -- I can't touch them for 30 years anyway," says Mr. Pallatto, 32.
After Sept. 11, U.S. businesses put on a fire sale. Not only did they mark down prices, but they offered
imaginative promotions that many consumers couldn't pass up.
Steve Ward, an assistant district attorney in Charlotte, N.C., cut back in small ways. Instead of ordering drinks
with dinner, he and his wife now have one at home before leaving. They no longer take their 10-year-old
daughter and her friends to expensive amusement parks, figuring a movie will do just as well.
Mr. Ward could have held off on a new car, too. But his local Volvo dealer offered two free airline tickets if he
picked up the car in Europe. By dealing directly with the factory, he saved about $3,000 in markups that
normally would have been charged for importing that vehicle. "They dangled that carrot out there and I bit on
it," says Mr. Ward, who used the money he saved for a two-week European vacation. To prepare for the trip,
he spent more than $300 here at home, including $120 for a new suitcase.

--- Page 140 ---
Page 140 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Even rising unemployment didn't shake consumers as much as in past recessions. The unemployment rate
has climbed from a 30-year low of 3.6% in 2000 to 6% now, but it's still lower than it was during the growth
years of the 1980s and early part of the 1990s.
For those who lose their jobs, the prospects for being rehired elsewhere are strong. Although a net average
105,000 jobs a month have been lost since March 2001, Labor Department data released Friday suggest that
more than four million people change jobs every month through the normal process of hiring, firing and
quitting. "Even in tough times, the U.S. labor market is enormously fluid," says Robert Mellman, an economist
with J.P. Morgan Chase & Co.
After surviving four rounds of layoffs at ICG Communications Inc., a telecommunications company, Stacy
Ferrell lost her job in June as a repair coordinator. Undaunted, she used her severance package to pay off
several medical bills and move from Irvine, Calif., to Carson City, Nev. There, her husband landed a job as an
auto mechanic, and she quickly turned up five openings for telephone repairers. The Ferrells plan to buy a
house by the end of the year. "People always will need to get their cars and their phones repaired," Ms.
Ferrell says.
The two-worker family provides an important cushion. In 2000, both husbands and wives worked in 56% of
married couples, up from 50% in 1986. If one spouse gets laid off, the other may still be working. Charles
Chandler lost his job as a computer-programming consultant at Sprint Corp. in Overland Park, Kan., a few
weeks before Sept. 11, but his wife, Pam, kept working as a teacher.
The couple cut back on dining out. But he didn't try to get back a $500 deposit he had put down for a new
BMW Mini Cooper before being laid off, even though the $19,000 car was so much in demand the dealer was
charging full retail price. "I had my heart set on this car since 1968," says Mr. Chandler, who took delivery a
month before getting a new job as an information-technology manager at a bank. "It's not like we were
destitute."
Many economists had predicted that the loss of wealth from the fall in stock prices -- which began in 2000,
gathered steam after Sept. 11 and resumed amid corporate scandals this summer -- would prompt sharp
spending cutbacks. But that hasn't been the case. While many people have seen their late-1990s winnings
erased, the market gyrations so far haven't inhibited spending nearly as much as anticipated. Many
consumers hold their stocks in retirement accounts and haven't factored the fallen value into day-to-day
spending.
Counting both stock losses and housing gains, the less affluent 80% of households saw their net worth rise
from 2.8 times their annual income in 1995 to 3 times in 1999, and then fall back to 2.8 times by early 2002 --
hardly much of a change in either direction, according to Fed data. For the wealthiest 20%, net wealth shot
from 5.6 times income in 1995 to 7.6 times in 1999, then slipped to 6.1 times.
Less affluent buyers have continued spending at their usual rates despite the stock market's drop, according
to Vadim Zlotnikov, equity strategist at Sanford C. Bernstein & Co. It has been the affluent who have socked
away more money than usual in savings. Their spending also has risen, but at a slower clip than that of the
larger middle class. So discount chains, such as Dollar General Corp., are reaping double-digit annual gains
in sales, while tony department stores, such as Neiman Marcus Group Inc. and Saks Inc., are reporting
declines.
---
Ken Gepfert contributed to this article.

Futures & Options:Workers Unite
WEEKEND JOURNAL
Futures & Options: Workers Unite
By Robert J. Hughes
1,208 words
30 August 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
W2
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
LABOR DAY signifies back to school, the unofficial start to fall and the winding down of summer. But more
than that it's a time to remember laborers and workers. Around the country, many museums document the
lives and struggles of American workers in many industries, from ironworkers in Michigan to silk workers in
New Jersey to miners in Colorado.
Here are some of the country's salutes to the average hero.
DEBS MUSEUM
451 N. Eighth St.
Terre Haute, Ind.
812-232-2163
www.eugenevdebs.com
EUGENE V. DEBS wouldn't give up. This labor organizer was a Socialist Party candidate for U.S. president
five times between 1900 and 1920, excluding 1916, when he ran for Congress instead. The home in which he
lived is a National Historic Landmark and commemorates his work on behalf of American workers and against
child labor. The house's interior offers a snapshot of how an American Indian family might have lived in the
1920s, and at the rear of the house are the Virgil Morris Memorial Gardens, where plaques honor American
labor-movement pioneers like Mother Jones. Open Wednesday to Sunday 1 p.m. to 4:30 p.m. Free
admission.
MINT HILL COUNTRY DOCTORS MUSEUM
4311 Hillside Dr.
Mint Hill, N.C.
704-545-5766
libweb.uncc.edu/archives/doctors/welcome.htm
THE COUNTRY DOCTOR labored long and hard, but at least he, or much more rarely, she, didn't have to
deal with HMOs and insurance forms. This museum preserves the heritage of the rural family physician. It
contains a physicians' office, an apothecary and rooms with medical instruments and equipment, including
ones used to amputate the arm of Confederate Gen. Thomas "Stonewall" Jackson following the Battle of
Chancellorsville. Open 2 p.m. to 6 p.m. the second Sunday of every month. Free admission.
HENRY FORD MUSEUM
20900 Oakwood Blvd.
Dearborn, Mich.
313-982-6100
www.hfmgv.org
HENRY FORD pretty much invented the modern assembly line, and assembly-line workers were a significant
part of the U.S. labor force and labor movements. The museum's collection documents innovation and

--- Page 142 ---
Page 142 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
change in the work lives of Americans in the 19th and 20th centuries. It also houses a collection of Americana
related to transportation, manufacturing, home life and entertainment of the times. Museum admission is
$13.50; $20 includes admission to adjacent Greenfield Village. Open 9 a.m. to 5 p.m. Monday to Saturday;
noon to 5 p.m. Sunday.
SLOSS FURNACES
First Avenue North & 32nd Street
Birmingham, Ala.
205-324-1911
www.slossfurnaces.com
HERE IS HISTORY to touch and smell. Sloss Furnaces produced pig iron, which fed Birmingham's foundries
and mills for just under a century. It's a National Historic Landmark that serves as a museum of industry. With
its massive furnaces, web of pipes and tall smokestacks, Sloss tells of Birmingham's rise to prominence as
the South's foremost industrial city, a kind of living museum frozen in time. Open Tuesday to Saturday 10
a.m. to 4 p.m., Sunday noon to 4 p.m.
MR. STRATTON'S BUSINESS: HIS LIFE & MINES
Western Museum
of Mining & Industry
1025 North Gate Rd.
Colorado Springs, Colo.
719-488-0880
www.wmmi.org
LEWIS AND CLARK explored the West, but miners also played a big role in westward expansion, and for an
obvious reason: There was gold in them there hills. This exhibition examines the life of W.S. Stratton and his
Cripple Creek Mining District operation. The museum has vignettes of the 19th-century mining era, illustrating
home life in mining communities. Kids can pan for gold amid scale models of equipment and mines. Open 9
a.m. to 4 p.m. Monday to Saturday. Free tours at 10 a.m. and 1 p.m. Admission is $7.
SAUGUS IRON WORKS
244 Central St.
Saugus, Mass.
781-233-0050
www.nps.gov/sair
IRON MAKING provided the infrastructure for the rise of other Colonial industries. This site features the first
major ironworks in North America, which operated from 1646 to 1668. It includes a blast furnace, a forge and
a restored 17th-century house. There's also a riverside open-air museum, with working waterwheels. It all
explores early industrial manufacturing in the Massachusetts Bay Colony, and the social, political and even
environmental effects of the era's iron making. Open daily through Oct. 31, 9 a.m. to 5 p.m., to 4 p.m.
November through March. Free admission.
LABOR DAY CELEBRATION
Sept. 1
Pullman Village
Pullman Visitor Center
11141 S. Cottage Grove Ave.
Chicago

--- Page 143 ---
Page 143 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
773-785-3828
members.aol.com/pullmanil/index.html
GUEST SPEAKERS from the Illinois Labor History Society will discuss the meaning of Labor Day here, as
well as significant developments in labor history, such as the Pullman Strike of 1894, the first national strike in
the U.S. Pullman was a company town built by George M. Pullman (of the railroad cars) in the 1880s. The
Pullman strike, which resulted from the company cutting both wages and hours, led to greater awareness of
labor problems. Ceremonies begin at 3 p.m.
SCRANTON IRON FURNACES
159 Cedar Ave.
Scranton, Pa.
570-963-3208
www.phmc.state.pa.us/bhsm/toh/scranton
THIS HISTORIC SITE is about workin' in the coal mine. In the 19th century, coal was critical to the
industrialization of Pennsylvania and the U.S. Its use, mainly in ironworks, fed the growth of transportation
and manufacturing, and public awareness of workers' hardscrabble lives eventually led to labor reforms. This
site features four huge 19th-century stone blast furnaces of the Lackawanna Iron & Coal Co. The furnaces,
fed by local coal, were at one point the second-largest iron producer in the country. Open daily 9 a.m. to 5
p.m.
THE AMERICAN LABOR MUSEUM
Botto House
83 Norwood St.
Paterson, N.J.
973-595-7953
www.paterson.k12.nj.us/labormuseum/index.html
UNLIKE MOST MUSEUMS, this one is open Labor Day. This National Historic Landmark, a 1908
Victorian-style home, belonged to silk mill worker Pietro Botto and his wife Maria. It was the meeting place for
more than 20,000 silk mill workers during the 1913 Paterson Silk Strike. The museum has restored period
rooms and gardens, and a labor and immigrant library, as well as exhibits. Its Labor Day parade starts
midday. Admission is a suggested $1.50. Open Wednesday to Saturday 1 p.m. to 4 p.m., or by appointment.
B&O MUSEUM
901 W. Pratt St.
Baltimore
410-752-2490
www.borail.org
THE RAILROAD INDUSTRY is intertwined with the labor movement. This museum seeks to commemorate
American railroading through the history and stories of railroad companies such as the B&O Railroad, the
Western Maryland Railway, and other railroads of the Mid-Atlantic. On display now are "Portraits of American
Railroading in the 19th Century," from the collection of the Smithsonian's National Portrait Gallery. It features
portraits and images of America's railroad barons, as well as artifacts from the museum's collection. Open
daily 10 a.m. to 5 p.m. Admission is $8.

Hedge Funds Grew Madly -- Now, the Shakeout --- Cooperman Sustains A `Difficult' Year Amid Market Woes
Hedge Funds Grew Madly -- Now, the Shakeout --- Cooperman Sustains A `Difficult' Year Amid Market
Woes
By Gregory Zuckerman
1,201 words
26 August 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
English
Copyright 2002 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
IN A SIGN OF HOW brutal this bear market has become, even Leon Cooperman is suffering.
Mr. Cooperman, one of the most respected names in the hedge-fund business, is going through his roughest
period in years. Mr. Cooperman's $2 billion hedge-fund firm, Omega Advisors, was down almost 13% for the
year, through Aug. 22, due to losing bets on companies such as Tyco International Ltd. and Calpine Corp.
and
For many stock-market investors, that kind of decline would hardly be surprising, although not welcome,
news. During the same period, the Standard & Poor's 500-stock Index was down 16%, while the Dow Jones
Industrial Average was down 10%.
But Mr. Cooperman's losses nevertheless are humbling for a money manager known for predicting
stock-market trends and one who steered his fund to profit even as the stock market tumbled in 2000 and
2001. His results are especially poor for the hedge-fund business, which charges investors high fees and
forces them to keep their money locked up, often for as long as a year, with the promise of top-notch returns
in all kinds of markets. By way of comparison, the hedge-fund industry as a whole is flat for the year, through
the end of July.
Mr. Cooperman, known on Wall Street for his cocksure attitude, acknowledges the difficulties of the past few
months. But he says a recent rebound for his fund in August is a sign of better things to come.
"This is my most difficult year in equities, it's the first year we've ever been down" in stocks, says Mr.
Cooperman, 59 years old. "The 2 1/2-year bear market has finally caught up to Lee Cooperman but we're
here from seven in the morning until nine at night. We're committed to turning this around."
Mr. Cooperman's backers say the recent losses are simply a stumble in the long career of a stock picker who
has consistently beaten the market since he opened his hedge fund in 1992. "Tiger Woods had a bad third
round at the British Open, but I'm not sure I would have fired him," says Mario Gabelli, chairman of Gabelli
Asset Management Inc. and an investor in Omega. While "the hedge-fund community prides itself on being
able to deliver positive absolute returns in any market," Mr. Gabelli says. "Lee is the kind of investor you want
to bet on over the long haul."
Still, Mr. Cooperman's losses stand out. For one thing, they are in the same ballpark as those that in recent
years forced hedge-fund kings Julian Robertson Jr. and George Soros to bail out of the business.
Because hedge funds usually make the bulk of their money from a so-called incentive fee, under which they
reap 20% of all the fund's gains in any year, any losses are painful. While Omega also charges investors 1%
of the assets under management, the juicy performance fee is how Mr. Cooperman and other hedge-fund
managers can attract top talent to the firm. Until this year's losses are erased, Mr. Cooperman says his team
won't earn those incentive fees.
But Mr. Cooperman scoffs at the notion that his firm is hurting. "You don't need to light any candles for me,"
he says.
Mr. Cooperman's recent losses are affecting employees at Goldman Sachs Group Inc. (Mr. Cooperman is a
former senior stock strategist at Goldman). Omega is one of five hedge funds that Goldman offers its
employees as an investment option in its 401(k) retirement plan. And some retired Goldman executives
remain investors, according to someone close to the situation. If Mr. Cooperman's fund doesn't turn around it
could be dropped by Goldman as part its annual evaluation of investment options for employees, according to
people close to the matter.

--- Page 145 ---
Page 145 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
A Goldman spokeswoman wouldn't comment on the evaluation of any funds by the investment committee
except to say "we're not singling out any one investment, employees have a choice, Omega is one of 39
investment options across the entire risk spectrum."
Still, even if Goldman decides to drop Omega it likely wouldn't hurt Mr. Cooperman's firm very much -- he and
his Omega partners control about $400 million, or 20%, of the firm's $2 billion in assets.
Well before the bear market began just over two years ago, Mr. Cooperman was telling traders at his firm that
the market was heading lower. Worried about sky-high prices for stocks, he set up Omega's portfolio in a
conservative manner, helping the fund gain 14% in 2000 and 3% last year.
But this year Mr. Cooperman made a few mistakes that have come back to haunt him. Omega's biggest
investment is in Tyco with more than 5% of the firm's assets. The stock of the industrial conglomerate has
fallen sharply amid allegations that the company's top executive evaded taxes and the company used
aggressive accounting. Mr. Cooperman bought more shares at what he thought were bargain levels, but the
tumble has crippled his returns nonetheless.
For Mr. Cooperman, who has long criticized corporate buybacks that help boost executive pay, the Tyco
losses are galling. "Tyco is my biggest mistake in 35 years of doing this," he says. "I've been railing against
corporate greed and I got caught with [this]. It's embarrassing."
Mr. Cooperman has turned around his portfolio in the past, and he says it will happen again this time, once
Tyco rebounds. The stock already is recovering after falling late last month. Now that the company's former
chief executive officer, L. Dennis Kozlowski, has quit, "Tyco is going to come back," Mr. Cooperman says.
"It's got great assets and I'm very confident."
Mr. Cooperman, a classic value investor who likes to pick up out-of-favor bargains, also made a mistake
betting on independent power companies like Calpine, Williams Cos. and AES Corp. He has dramatically
slashed Omega's positions in this sector, although he has kept some of his AES shares, betting on a
rebound. "By the time we cut our position [in the sector] we had lost substantial amount of money," he says.
Another misstep: buying bonds of WorldCom after they had fallen, but before the company filed for
bankruptcy-law protection and fell still further. The firm has made big gains on Oxford Health Plans Inc.,
Freeport-McMoRan Copper & Gold Inc. and Tyson Foods Inc., among other stocks, as well as a prescient
bet that interest rates would fall.
Mr. Cooperman is skeptical the market's recent gains will continue and that a new bull market has begun.
Still-high stock prices and a growing aversion to stocks by investors means the market will remain in its
current range for the foreseeable future, he argues.
"The probability is very high the stock market is going nowhere," he says.

Wary" -- WSJ Aug. 26, 2002)

Color Line:A Fashion HouseWith an Elite AuraWrestles With Race---Some Minority Employees SayThey Don't Fit SmoothlyIn Ralph Lauren's Empire-...
Color Line: A Fashion House With an Elite Aura Wrestles With Race --- Some Minority Employees Say
They Don't Fit Smoothly In Ralph Lauren's Empire --- `We Have to Correct This'
By Teri Agins
2,963 words
19 August 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- Fashion magnate Ralph Lauren says he first became aware of racial tension within his
company after an incident in 1997 at a Long Island sportswear boutique.
A regional manager with Polo Ralph Lauren Corp. dropped by the new Polo Sport store in anticipation of an
inspection by an important visitor: Jerome Lauren, Ralph's older brother and the executive overseeing Polo
menswear. The Roosevelt Field Mall where the boutique was located attracts a middle-class, racially
integrated clientele. But the regional manager concluded that the store's ambiance was too "urban," meaning
black, former Polo officials say.
The manager ordered two black and two Hispanic sales associates off the sales floor and back into the stock
room, so they wouldn't be visible to Mr. Lauren, according to the ex-officials. The sales associates followed
orders, but they later hired a lawyer and threatened to sue Polo for discrimination. The company reached
confidential settlements with the four, the former executives say.
Ralph Lauren says he learned about the incident several weeks after it happened and "was just sick" about it.
The regional manager, Greg Ladley, was ordered to undergo racial-relations training but wasn't fired, Mr.
Lauren says. Mr. Ladley, who still works for Polo, didn't respond directly to numerous requests for comment.
A company spokesman says that Mr. Ladley's recollection is that some sales associates working on inventory
were asked to move to the stock room because they weren't "dressed appropriately."
After the episode, Mr. Lauren says, he told subordinates, "We have to correct this. Let's make a change." But
executives who worked at Polo at the time say their boss didn't make clear what changes he wanted.
The incident illustrates how one large company has wrestled for years with the issue of race in the workplace.
The grievances and tensions over racial relations that have played out inside Polo are common across a wide
range of corporations. They are particularly sensitive in the fashion industry, whose very product is tied to
image and appearance.
Polo, like some of its rivals, presents a multi-racial face to the world, with black models in some of its ads and
a following of young black consumers wearing its familiar logo of a horseman wielding a polo mallet. Yet
internally, big fashion houses tend to exude an exclusiveness that is uninviting to many nonwhites. Few
blacks or Hispanics have penetrated the upper ranks of major clothes manufacturers and retailers.
Mr. Lauren is an unusual example of a CEO willing to address the subject in detail, offering a rare look at how
a highly visible company tries to manage race relations. In response to complaints that have flared up at Polo
over the last five years, he has met with lawyers, hired lieutenants to overhaul company personnel practices
and embraced "diversity" training. But he says he has left the details to others, as he is usually preoccupied
with design work at his headquarters studio. "For me it has always been about the clothes," he says.
If any corporation is the standard bearer for American fashion's fantasy of life-as-country club, it is Polo. In
much of its advertising and retailing, the company sells clothes, home furnishings and cosmetics with images
of old-money privilege: English country mansions, prep-school reunions and summers on Nantucket. British
accents, even among American employees, aren't rare.
This aura of Anglo-Saxon elitism is the elaborate creation of Polo's founder, Mr. Lauren, 62 years old, who
remade himself as he rose from modest roots in the Bronx, to become the chairman and CEO of a fashion
powerhouse. As teenagers in the 1950s, he and his brother, Jerome, 67, Americanized their last name from
the Russian "Lifshitz." Polo retail supervisors routinely tell salespeople to think Hollywood. "Ralph is the
director," the instruction goes, "and you are the actors, and we are here to make a movie." But some black
and Hispanic employees say the movie seems to lack parts for them.

--- Page 147 ---
Page 147 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
In 1999, the U.S. Equal Employment Opportunity Commission began looking into complaints by about a
dozen minority Polo workers, according to current and former company executives. The EEOC probe ended
this spring without the agency itself suing Polo, the company says. But in August 2001, the EEOC cleared the
way for a private discrimination suit subsequently filed in federal court in New York by a pair of ex-Polo
employees. The agency's "letter of determination" said that "testimony from several witnesses suggests that
[Polo] managers sought to maintain a `blond-hair-and-blue-eyes' image of employees, who often advanced
the image over more qualified and experienced minority employees." The EEOC says that in 2001, it issued
this kind of determination of "reasonable cause" in response to 7.5% of the roughly 29,000 race-based
complaints it received.
The suit, filed in February 2002 by an African-American retail salesman and a Filipino human-resources
employee, both of whom had resigned in 1999, alleged that Polo denied them raises and promotions
available to whites. The legal action was settled confidentially in April. Polo and the plaintiffs decline to
comment on it.
As for the EEOC's three-year probe, Mitchell Kosh, Polo's senior vice president for human resources, says
the agency "never asked us to do anything specific." The EEOC was impressed by "the programs and
diversity initiatives we had put in place," he adds. It isn't unusual for discrimination claims to crop up from time
to time in a company the size of Polo, which has 10,000 employees world-wide and reported revenue of
$2.36 billion for its fiscal year that ended March 30. The EEOC, following its policy, declines to comment.
Mr. Lauren says he is color-blind when it comes to hiring talent. He frequently points out the wide visibility he
has given Tyson Beckford, the striking shaven-headed black fashion model who has appeared in Polo ads
since 1994. "Tyson is not just in jeans," Mr. Lauren says. "We put him in a pinstripe suit, in our best Purple
Label [brand]. Tyson is in the annual report, in our advertisements on TV."
Polo declines to provide the racial makeup of its work force or executive ranks. At least three black or
Hispanic executives have the rank of vice president or higher, out of a total of more than 100. Last year, Polo
appointed the first minority director to its 10-member board: Joyce Brown, president of the Fashion Institute of
Technology in New York.
Mr. Lauren, whose thick white hair and deeply tanned face are familiar to many because he models his
clothes in luxury-drenched advertisements, didn't grow up amid affluence. As a child, he lived with three older
siblings in a two-bedroom apartment in the Bronx. His father painted houses and murals for a living. In high
school, Ralph played basketball on public courts but dressed like an Ivy Leaguer -- a look that helped him
land a sales position at Brooks Brothers.
He founded Polo in 1968 to make "classic American" clothes and earned a fortune as a pioneer of what
became known as lifestyle merchandising. Few large corporations bear a stronger imprint of their founders.
Employees pride themselves on divining whether a particular tweed jacket is "very Ralph" or "not Ralph
enough."
Sales employees are required to wear ensembles of Polo clothes, purchased at steep discount and approved
by supervisors. For a time in the late 1990s, one supervisor at Polo's flagship Manhattan store offered two
curly-haired saleswomen -- one white and one black -- weekly styling sessions to get their tresses
straightened, according to other people who worked there at the time. Polo officials today say they have no
knowledge of the hair styling.
The casual opulence that is Polo's trademark at first appealed to Sikita Smith. She arrived at Polo in 1997, 22
years old and fresh from prestigious Wellesley College in Massachusetts, where she had earned a bachelor's
degree in philosophy. Ms. Smith, who is black, started as an $8.50-an-hour sales associate at the company's
flagship store in a renovated 19th-century mansion, 12 blocks uptown on Madison Avenue from the corporate
office. She says she dreamed of one day moving to headquarters as an executive.
She learned the store's rituals: serving shoppers champagne and cookies, even passing out treats to their
dogs. Her client list and commissions grew. But she says she saw white co-workers with even less
experience promoted ahead of her. She concluded that her supervisors' assessment of her was: "She has a
look, and she can sell the product. But we're not looking for her to do anything else."
In 1998, an incident occurred at the store that disturbed Ms. Smith and other minority employees. Exactly
what happened is in dispute. Ms. Smith and others who worked there at the time say that a fellow employee
overheard a store supervisor, Electra Preston, questioning why a certain black male employee was working
on the sales floor, rather than back in the stockroom "with the rest of them" -- the other minority workers.
Ms. Preston says, "I did not ever make a racial comment." She says that the employee was "in the wrong
place, and he wasn't wardrobed properly."

--- Page 148 ---
Page 148 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Nevertheless, Ms. Preston's remarks caused such distress among the staff that Charles Fagan, a senior Polo
retail executive, says he called a special Saturday-morning meeting at the store. Mr. Fagan says he made a
speech about racial tolerance. Ms. Preston offered a tearful apology, according to Ms. Smith and others who
were present.
Ms. Preston says that "the company made me apologize for something perceptual."
Ms. Preston remained a Polo employee, but Mr. Lauren says she was instructed to seek outside
racial-relations training. Ms. Preston denies that she ever received "racial counseling." She has since left Polo
but has done consulting for the company.
Ms. Smith wasn't satisfied by the company's response to the episode. "It was a performance -- a cop-out. We
knew that what they were doing was damage control." She quit her job after only a year. "There were things I
wanted to do at Polo, and I think that I would still be there, but [after the Preston incident], I just didn't want to
go through any of that," she says. She now works in merchandising for Nike Inc. in New York.
Mr. Lauren says Polo "is a leader to do the right things to bring in the people who are the best in the industry."
Some of his subordinates complain, however, that it is difficult to find black and Hispanic applicants with the
credentials for design jobs coming out of the New York fashion schools where Polo usually recruits.
In the mid-1990s, Mr. Lauren's Polo Sport line, originally aimed at people who play golf, tennis and other
sports, became popular with young black hip-hop aficionados -- and then their MTV-fed white suburban
imitators. Like his rival, Tommy Hilfiger, Mr. Lauren adjusted his marketing to encourage the incorporation of
his jeans and polo shirts into a rap-tinged youth wardrobe. Mr. Beckford, the black model, lent street
credibility to Polo Sport clothing. More recently, upstarts such as Fubu and Sean John have given Polo and
Hilfiger more competition in the hip-hop market.
When asked about these developments, Mr. Lauren volunteers the story of Lacey Moore. A Polo staffer
recommended in 1998 that Mr. Lauren meet Mr. Moore, a 20-year-old African-American from Brooklyn, who
had taken some college-level communications courses and had aspirations to be in the music business.
Since high school, Mr. Moore had worn Polo Oxford shirts and knit tops with flashy gold chains and a hip-hop
attitude: precisely the sort of hybrid image Mr. Lauren hoped would draw younger customers. "Lacey is edgy
-- he gets it," Mr. Lauren recalls thinking, snapping his fingers for effect. He hired the young man as a design
assistant.
By all accounts, the new recruit's rap-influenced personal style and lingo confounded his co-workers. Mr.
Moore felt isolated. He says he understood that in any competitive workplace, "there are people who don't
like you." But in Polo's cliquish and overwhelmingly white Madison Avenue headquarters, he says co-workers
made it clear he wasn't welcome. "I kept getting this bad vibe," he says. He quit in 2000.
Shocked, Mr. Lauren telephoned him at home. "Lacey, I want you to come back," he recalls saying. After
listening to Mr. Moore's complaints, Mr. Lauren says he made it clear to the young man's white colleagues, "I
want you all to work this out." A couple of weeks later, Mr. Moore returned, but warily.
Polo has received advice and pressure on the race issue from a variety of outside counselors and advocates.
After the incident at the Long Island Polo Sport boutique, the company's then-main outside law firm, New
York's Paul, Weiss, Rifkind, Wharton & Garrison, tried to heighten Mr. Lauren's personal awareness of
diversity issues. The law firm arranged a meeting in 1998 with A. Leon Higginbotham, a retired
federal-appeals-court judge and authority on civil rights who was "of counsel" to Paul Weiss.
When he met Mr. Lauren in New York, Mr. Higginbotham, an African-American, brought the fashion mogul
copies of his 1996 book, "Shades of Freedom: Racial Politics and Presumptions of the American Legal
Press." Mr. Higginbotham spoke about how achieving a truly diverse workplace requires more than hiring a
few black employees, according to two other people who were present. The meeting soon became awkward,
however, as Mr. Lauren spent most of the time talking about Mr. Beckford, the black model, with whom the
retired judge appeared not to be familiar, the other attendees say.
Mr. Lauren says he doesn't recall any specifics about the meeting. Mr. Higginbotham died in December 1998.
During this time period, some disgruntled minority employees enlisted the aid of the Rev. Dennis Dillon, a
black activist minister in Brooklyn and publisher of the New York Christian Times, a weekly newspaper. Mr.
Dillon says he met with company officials and helped some of the minority workers reach confidential
settlements with Polo. The company declines to comment.
By sometime in 1998, Polo hired Mr. Dillon as a paid consultant. Roger Farah, who became the company's
chief operating officer in 1999, ended that relationship soon after his arrival, the company says. But Polo has
continued to provide funding for events sponsored by Mr. Dillon's newspaper, including a conference this
spring in New Jersey called "And We Still Rise." Polo says the sponsorships promote "community relations

--- Page 149 ---
Page 149 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
and outreach." Neither the company nor Mr. Dillon will discuss how much he or his organization has been
paid over the years.
In a separate venture, Polo agreed in 2000 to contribute $5 million to help build a center for breast-cancer
care and prevention -- long a Lauren cause -- in New York's predominantly black and Hispanic Harlem
section. The center will bear Mr. Lauren's name.
Mr. Farah, 49, had held executive posts at Federated Department Stores Inc. and Venator Corp., the
athletic-shoe marketer now known as Foot Locker Inc. He says Mr. Lauren hired him specifically to
modernize Polo. The fashion house had operated as a private outfit for 30 years before going public in 1997.
Mr. Farah introduced Polo's first formal policies for job postings and employee reviews, as well as a toll-free
hot line for employee complaints. He hired two human-resources managers he had worked with at Venator:
Mr. Kosh and Paul Campbell. Mr. Campbell, who is black, became a Polo vice president. He had experience
handling racial-relations issues in previous posts and launched a "fair employment" program, at Polo, says
Mr. Kosh.
In 2001, Mr. Farah hired as a consultant Roosevelt Thomas, an Atlanta-based ethnic-diversity expert he had
worked with previously. Mr. Thomas, who has a Ph.D. in organizational behavior from Harvard, has led
two-day seminars attended by hundreds of Polo senior managers. Mr. Lauren participated in one of the first
sessions.
Some Polo employees who have attended the seminars say that one of the lessons they learned is that in
order to get along in the office, you don't have to like another person -- or even abandon negative views of a
person's ethnic group. That was essentially the message Mr. Lauren issued to subordinates two years ago
when he brought back Mr. Moore, the young black design assistant.
Today, Mr. Moore says his colleagues seem friendlier. Mr. Campbell, the black human-resources vice
president, has given Mr. Moore reassurance. "You feel there is someone looking out for you," says the young
assistant, who helps prepare for fashion shows and consults on clothing design.
Mr. Lauren says he is paying more attention to what he sees at work. For example, he recalls that at a
company Christmas party in 2000, he was surprised that a group of blacks and Hispanics had congregated in
a separate room. "Why is that happening?" he says he wondered. "What's not welcoming" to those
employees?
Mr. Lauren says he didn't approach his workers to ask them, however, and is still wondering about the
answers to those questions.

False Bottom? Stocks May Decline Further---Market's History ShowsRecent Rally May NotSpell Long-Term Relief
Abreast of the Market
False Bottom? Stocks May Decline Further --- Market's History Shows Recent Rally May Not Spell
Long-Term Relief
By E.S. Browning
2,056 words
5 August 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
INVESTORS ARE FACING one of their most nettlesome puzzles since the bear market began more than two
years ago: Did that 1,000-point rally in the Dow Jones Industrial Average mark an important turning point from
which stocks will make significant gains? Or was it like every other rally for the past two years -- one that may
last a few weeks or a few months, but which then will lead to new lows and more disappointment?
Was it the lady or the tiger? That makes a big difference to many investors, who have been holding money
out of the stock market and want to know whether it is safe to venture back in.
The answer isn't very reassuring. Many of the experts do think the 6% surge that the Dow Jones Industrial
Average recorded July 24, with record New York Stock Exchange trading volume, marked at least a
temporary bottom, from which stocks can post further gains. But if it was a longer-term bottom -- finally
signaling the return to a lasting bull market -- then it was an exceptional one, because it didn't have some of
the hallmarks of investor panic, "capitulation" and bargain-basement stock prices that characterized the end
of past major bear markets.
In short, experts worry, stocks may need to get cheaper, and investors may need to become more nervous.
"It was a bottom," says Phil Roth, chief technical market analyst at New York brokerage firm Miller Tabak.
But, he adds, "I don't know if it was the bottom."
That kind of skepticism was reflected in stock performance late last week, as investors wiped out more than
one-third of the 1,000-point Dow rally that began July 24.
A 230-point drop in the Dow industrials Thursday was followed by a decline of 193.49 points, or 2.27%, to
8313.13 Friday. That offset most of Monday's big gain and left the blue chips up a bare 0.59%, or 48.74
points, for the week. Still, they are up 8% since the rally began but down 17% for the year.
The reason investors are unsure whether stocks truly have hit bottom is that the market is starting to show
some signs associated with a bottom, just not all the signs. Big Board trading volume, for example, hit a
record July 19, when the Dow industrials fell 4.6%. Then it made another record on July 24, when the blue
chips soared 6%.
To many investors, that constituted a selling panic followed by a buying panic, the kind of turn that can signal
a bottom.
The theory is that a bottom occurs when weak-willed investors finally give up and sell their stocks, or
"capitulate," at which time more-confident buyers start stepping in, creating a rebound.
Another signal of a bottom: the "VIX" index of market volatility, which moved above the 50 level, a mark
sometimes seen at other market bottoms. The VIX measures demand for "put" options, or options that permit
people to sell stocks at a prearranged price, to guard against losses. When it gets high, it means investors
are panicking about the risk of further losses. On top of that, mutual-fund redemptions rose sharply in July.
Both signs are associated with the kind of fear found at a bottom.
"Investors need to remember that at the bottom, when the despair is the greatest, as we have seen recently,
the selling pressure gets to the good companies as well as to the bad," economist Donald Straszheim of
Straszheim Global Advisors wrote in a commentary to clients last week.
Studies of past market bottoms -- and false bottoms -- suggest that the latter kind occur when economic
fundamentals prove too weak to sustain stock prices, Mr. Straszheim wrote. That makes him bullish. As long
as the economy stays out of a double-dip recession, he concluded, the market's sharp July swings may signal
a lasting bottom from which stocks can move ahead.

--- Page 154 ---
Page 154 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
But others who have studied past bear markets are less hopeful. To them, this looks like a head fake.
Heavy trading volumes and rising investor pessimism aren't enough to signal a turning point, says technical
analyst Paul Desmond, president of Lowry's Reports, an independent research firm in North Palm Beach, Fla.
His research suggests that a market turnaround normally requires a more extreme situation. He measures
that by tracking what he calls "90% days," which are rare events.
A 90% down day, for example, is one where 90% of trading, measured in share volume and in dollar value,
comes on declines. That is a day when sellers are overwhelmingly in charge. A 90% up day is the opposite.
Mr. Desmond's work suggests that significant market shifts tend to come between a 90% down day,
representing a selling panic, and a 90% up day, marking a buying panic.
As it happens, July 24 was a 90% up day. But it wasn't preceded by a 90% down day, which makes Mr.
Desmond skeptical that the rebound will be more than a temporary bounce.
"The patterns here are very similar to what they were in September 2001," Mr. Desmond says. "We expect
that a rally of approximately the same kind could potentially occur here," although it might not last as long, he
says.
He fears that stocks aren't cheap enough yet to provoke extensive buying. The Standard & Poor's 500-stock
index, for example, trades at 30 times its companies' earnings for the past 12 months, and 17 times forecast
earnings for the coming 12 months. That remains above the historical average, which is in the mid-teens.
Even many bulls, who consider the S&P 500 attractively priced because interest rates are unusually low,
don't argue that it is exceptionally cheap.
"Historically, we have never had a major market bottom start with valuations at the levels that they currently
are at," Mr. Desmond notes.
Mr. Roth of Miller Tabak sees some other things missing.
Although corporate insiders were selling stock in their companies before the turn, they haven't started buying
in large amounts since then, he says, and insider buying usually is a signal of a market turn.
Although individuals have moved money out of stock funds, money managers at mutual funds and pension
funds haven't built huge cash positions, he says. That leaves them limited room to add to their stock
positions.
He thinks the recent market swings have been fueled by short-term traders, not long-term investors. The
traders panicked in the first part of July, and then funneled money back later. A real market shift should be
driven by longer-term investors, he says.
"If you looked at all the indicators, you would conclude that you can't make a long-term bullish case," Mr. Roth
says. "Right now, I am saying: four to eight weeks of recovery out of this."
What gives some analysts pause is that the bubble of the late 1990s was in many ways more extreme than
anything the country witnessed since the 1920s. The reaction to that bubble has been more extreme than
many analysts expected, and that has them worrying that some of the guidelines they have been using may
not work this time.
By some measures, the stock market should already have turned up, noted analyst Ned Davis of Ned Davis
Research, of Venice, Fla., in a report to clients Friday. In every bear market since the early 1930s, the stock
market has risen within a year of aggressive Federal Reserve rate-cutting. This time, the Federal Reserve
began aggressively cutting interest rates 18 months ago and stocks are down. Since the 1920s, the stock
market always has turned up within six months of the end of a recession, but not this time.
Does that mean that a double-dip recession is coming, or that we are in a "secular" bear market that could
drag on for years to come?
Whatever the case, Mr. Davis said, "this market has now done two things that are almost unheard of in a
normal bear market," by not rising after the rate cuts and by not rising after the recession. "I submit that these
two factors alone should be a wake-up call that the aftermath of one of the greatest bubbles in history is a
dangerous condition. I have described it as moving from a so-called Goldilocks economy -- not too hot, not
too cold, just right -- to a `Humpty Dumpty' condition, where we are not sure `if all the king's horses and all the
king's men can put Humpty Dumpty together again.' "

we had early in the week on economic data that was worse than economists predicted is reasonable. This is
what the bottoming process is like."
Dow added that the market had held too strong of a valuation on the market so the disappointing data are

Bugs That Eat BugsAre Scratching OutA Growing Niche---Jan Dietrich's Critters KillPests, Are Seen on TV;Maggots by the Gross
Bugs That Eat Bugs Are Scratching Out A Growing Niche --- Jan Dietrich's Critters Kill Pests, Are
Seen on TV; Maggots by the Gross
By Bill Richards
Special to The Wall Street Journal
1,085 words
2 August 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
VENTURA, Calif. -- Jan Dietrich got the call in April: Fox TV needed 5,000 maggots, ASAP, for a pilot of its
reality show, "Truth or Consequences."
"But they had to be clean maggots," says Ms. Dietrick, general manager of Rincon-Vitova Insectaries Inc.
"They said, `Are they sterile? The contestants have to eat them.'"
Ms. Dietrick, a 56-year-old former dietician, got to work. "We said, `We'll wash 'em, put 'em in deli containers,
and have them ready for you in the morning.' "
The bug business is booming these days -- and gross-out TV shows are just the tip of the antenna. Ms.
Dietrick has handled calls on everything from organic growers ordering predatory mites by the millions to a
film company needing gallons of ladybugs -- that's 72,000 per gallon -- to pour over the actress Thandie
Newton for her bug-ridden role in the 1998 movie "Beloved."
The Association of Natural Biocontrol Producers, a Santa Ana, Calif.-based trade group that represents the
30 biggest insectaries in the U.S. and Canada, estimates that about $200 million in commercial bugs are sold
each year -- with demand growing about 10% annually. Behind the uptick: Bugs that eat other bugs, in
demand from farmers as an alternative to chemical pesticides. More bugs are growing resistant to pesticides
and more consumers are buying organic. "We're moving toward a strategy of good bugs eating bad bugs,"
says Maclay Burt, the association's executive director.
David Bunn, assistant manager of Crown Packing Co. in Salinas, Calif., says his company buys ladybugs and
green lacewings to control insect pests on 200 acres of organic artichokes, lettuce, celery and cauliflower.
"We've gone from zero organic farming around here to nearly 5%," he says of the rich farming lands around
Salinas.
In Hereford, Texas, Ralph Diller uses an ultralight aircraft to spray 12.5 million wasps and lacewings over his
1,100 acres of organic corn, wheat and soybeans. Mr. Diller says the bugs repel moths that arrive from South
Texas in flights so heavy they can be picked up by radar.
John France, who raises 250 acres of organic mandarin oranges, prunes, walnuts and table grapes on his
ranch near Porterville, Calif., says he began buying bugs to control harmful insects nearly a dozen years ago.
"It was a massive decision," he says. "We kept some chemicals until we were sure it worked."
Now, Mr. France is a bug believer. "If we have a bug that needs to be dealt with, we figure out what kind of
other bug will do the job and buy it."
Recently, Mr. France and his work crew were busy scattering millions of tiny wasps, called Aphytis melinus,
through his orchards. The wasps, about the size of a pepper grain, attack California red scales, heavily
armored parasites that plague citrus growers. The wasps punch holes into the parasites, insert their eggs,
and the larvae feed from the inside out.
Mr. France says he used to spray his fruit with Lorsban, an insecticide he calls "pretty heavy stuff." These
days he and his workers carry cardboard cups, each containing 25,000 of the tiny wasps, and tap them out
onto the leaves of his trees.
"It's not a real high-tech operation," he says, "but it works."
There is big money in bugs. Growers can charge as much as 40 cents each for some ladybugs, although
farmers can pick up 1,000 run-of-the-mill ladybugs for just $1. Some insectaries charge up to $1 each for
certain weed-eating bugs.

--- Page 157 ---
Page 157 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Syngenta Bioline's bug farm in Oxnard, Calif., sits behind a locked gate, marked only by a weathered sign.
Daniel Cahn, president of the unit of Swiss agrochemical concern Syngenta AG, says the low profile is to
discourage visitors who might be after his bug-propagation secrets. "There are people who think this is a gold
mine," he says. "They're tripping over themselves to get in."
Raising bugs is a fairly simple business. Mr. Cahn's greenhouse is filled with bean plants and spider mites,
which feed on the plants. While the spider mites are eating the beans, predator mites eat the spider mites.
The tricky part is collecting the 15 million predator mites Syngenta ships in a busy summer week. Mr. Cahn
declines to show a visitor around his greenhouse or to say how he collects the bugs. "They move upwards,
and we exploit that," he says.
Ms. Dietrick, Rincon-Vitova's general manager, is less secretive. The insectary, on the site of a former
farm-labor camp, operates out of a labyrinth of 26 pale-green cargo containers and a couple of flyspecked
workrooms tucked away among a field of oil derricks.
Ms. Dietrick opens a cargo container and out pours a rush of barley odor along with the musky smell of
millions of breeding bugs. The room is filled with six-foot-long bags hanging from the ceiling. In the bags,
cereal moths lay their eggs on barley kernels. The moth eggs will be fed to green lacewings, known as "aphid
lions" for their predatory gusto.
Ms. Dietrick refers to another line of odiferous storage bins as "fly alley." Inside, 14 million flies are growing
on a diet of bran, milk and sugar. In other containers, maggots grow in piles of woodchips and an army of
ladybugs feeds on a table of rotting squash.
Ms. Dietrick's father, Everett "Deke" Dietrick, a white-bearded former entomologist at the University of
California at Berkeley, started the business in 1960. Mr. Dietrick, now 82 years old, began by peddling bugs
to West Texas cotton farmers out of the back of his Chevy pickup in the early 1960s.
"I'd keep a box of bugs on ice to slow down their growth, and a box heated up -- to speed up the hatch and
show what they could do," says Mr. Dietrick. He competed head-to-head with salesmen from chemical
companies. On occasion, he recalls, competitors would swoop down in crop dusters and release clouds of
insecticide as he was putting his bugs through their paces in the fields.
"The chemical companies won the battle then," says Mr. Dietrich, who still pads around the place keeping
track of his bugs, "but we're struggling back."

World Watch
International
World Watch
Compiled by David I. Oyama
1,385 words
31 July 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
A12
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
EUROPE
Finland to Probe
Sonera Write-Offs
Of Mobile Outlays
Sonera, Finland's largest telecommunications company, has written off more than four billion euros ($3.9
billion), mostly because of third-generation, or 3G, wireless investments that failed spectacularly. The Finnish
government, which owns 53% of Sonera, yesterday said it will investigate what happened. The prime
minister's office and Justice Ministry feel "it is imperative the inquiry is conducted thoroughly and as quickly
as possible," the ministry said. Sonera last week posted a second-quarter loss of 2.8 billion euros after writing
off the value of its 3G investments in Italy and Germany. That followed earlier write-offs of about 1.5 billion
euros. Sonera plans to close down its third-generation operations in Germany. Sonera spent more than four
billion euros on third-generation wireless licenses.
Communications Minister Kimmo Sasi said that ministry has also been conducting its own inquiry into
Sonera's decision-making process. Sonera, formerly known as state-owned Telecom Finland, is to be taken
over by Sweden's Telia later this year.
Euro Zone, U.K. Rate Rises Unlikely
Turbulent stock markets will likely persuade central banks in Europe to keep interest rates lower for longer.
Just a few months ago, the European Central Bank and the Bank of England began laying the groundwork
for rate increases as the U.S. and global economies recovered from the worst of last year's downturn. Many
economists at that time expected both central banks to have acted by now, but a move by either bank seems
unlikely when they make their separate monthly monetary-policy announcements tomorrow. Some
economists believe the ECB and the Bank of England will keep interest rates on hold for the rest of the year.
Schroder, Chirac Discuss EU Future
German Chancellor Gerhard Schroder and French President Jacques Chirac launched an effort to bring the
two countries' positions closer on the future of the European Union and subsidies to farmers, but their summit
was overshadowed by Germany's Sept. 22 parliamentary elections. The leaders, meeting in Schwerin, in
eastern Germany, agreed to set up working groups examining the EU's system of farm subsidies, EU
expansion and changes ahead of the 15-nation bloc's December summit that is expected to give the final
go-ahead to 10 new EU members, a German official said. But with the German vote less than eight weeks
away, and Mr. Schroder's Social Democrats floundering in the polls, difficult decisions were deferred.
