EUROPE/AFRICA
WTO Again Delays
Fixing U.S. Fines
For Export Breaks
The World Trade Organization delayed for the third time a decision on fining the U.S. potentially billions of
dollars for offering its exporters improper tax breaks, European Union officials said. Both the U.S. and the EU
welcomed the postponement, playing down the dispute amid concerns that it could derail attempts to end the
standoff over the Bush administration's decision in March to raise steel-import tariffs. The tax dispute centers
on a U.S. program that has helped scores of U.S. companies gain tax breaks on exports. The WTO ruled last
year that the U.S. program violates world-trade rules, but it still must determine the penalties. The EU has
demanded $4.04 billion. The U.S. believes it should pay $956 million. A decision on penalties was scheduled
to be announced this week but won't come before mid-August, EU Trade Commissioner Pascal Lamy's office
said.
The WTO contends the delay is due to translation difficulties and the complex calculations on how much
damage EU companies suffered. But diplomats privately say the real reason for the delay is to avoid
exacerbating trans-Atlantic trade conflicts.
Cantor Wins Partial Victory in U.K.
British securities firm ICAP illegally enticed a broker to leave rival U.S. bond-trading firm Cantor Fitzgerald
Securities, a British judge ruled. The decision was a partial victory for Cantor Fitzgerald, which was shattered
by the deaths of 658 employees in the Sept. 11 terrorist attacks on the World Trade Center. Cantor had sued
ICAP, accusing it of plotting to exploit the tragedy and lure away key employees. High Court Judge Richard
McCombe ruled ICAP had offered an "unlawful inducement" in encouraging broker Luigi Boucher to break his
contract with Cantor. "There can be no doubt that the tragic events of Sept. 11" provided "the backdrop to
what happened," the judge said. But he dismissed allegations that ICAP acted illegally in the cases of Edward
Bird and Spencer Gill, two other brokers who left Cantor. The judge will determine financial penalties against
Mr. Boucher and ICAP later.

Australia's trade deficit in June swelled to 1.09 billion Australian dollars (US$583 million), the widest gap in

exports. The Bureau of Statistics said exports declined 2%, while imports rose 2%, nearly doubling the trade

The Past Is Prologue,Says a 91-Year-OldWho Knows Bubbles---Mr. Kindleberger Saw SelloffComing in Seminal Book;`Dubious' About Big Rally
The Past Is Prologue, Says a 91-Year-Old Who Knows Bubbles --- Mr. Kindleberger Saw Selloff
Coming in Seminal Book; `Dubious' About Big Rally
By Jon E. Hilsenrath
1,428 words
25 July 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
LEXINGTON, Mass. -- In a well-manicured Boston retirement community, Charles P. Kindleberger has
watched the stock-market turmoil unfold during the past two years with a sense that he has seen it all before.
Mr. Kindleberger, a retired economist, wrote the 1978 economics classic "Manias, Panics, and Crashes: A
History of Financial Crises." The book, required reading for many Wall Street trainees and students of
economic history, documents four centuries of boom-and-bust financial cycles. It ranges from a fleeting
bubble in the market for Dutch tulips in 1636, to rampant speculation and subsequent collapses in railroad
shares in 1847 and 1857, to the Depression in the 1930s, to the rise and fall of Japan's property market in the
late 1980s and early 1990s.
At the age of 91, Mr. Kindleberger, who taught economics at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology for 33
years, is one of the few retirees unshaken by the current market turbulence -- with the Dow Jones Industrial
Average down 18% from the start of the year, despite yesterday's big rally. "I'm ashamed to say I enjoy the
decline in the stock market," he says. "It is what we call schadenfreude, a joy in the troubles of others." Mr.
Kindleberger is gloating because he warned readers in the foreword of the third edition of "Manias, Panics,
and Crashes," released in 1996, of what looked "suspiciously like a bubble in technology stocks." Paul
Samuelson, a colleague and Nobel prize-winning economist, admonished readers on the book's cover,
"Sometime in the next five years you may kick yourself for not reading and re-reading Kindleberger's [book]."
"It is one of the most important books for people on Wall Street to read," says Richard Sylla, who teaches a
course on financial history at New York University's Stern School of Business and has his students spend two
weeks examining the book.
Just knowing that he was right is reassuring, says Mr. Kindleberger. "You get to sleep easier." Mr.
Kindleberger said he was "very dubious" that Wednesday's rally signaled much of anything. Investors might
rush in to a market in the midst of a downturn, he says, but it takes a more sustained rally to signal a real
bottom.
Mr. Kindleberger's own savings are in certificates of deposit, money-market funds and bonds. His fortunes
are also helped by a recent uptick in sales of his book, according to John Wiley & Sons, the book's publisher.
A fourth edition was released in late 2000, in the wake of Asia's financial meltdown in 1997 and 1998.
Mr. Kindleberger says he would love to write a fifth edition full of details from the latest corporate scandals, or
a book about a housing-price bubble he sees developing, but he doesn't feel up to it. Still, he hasn't stopped
working. In November, he wrote an essay on how to invest when you're 90 and contemplating death. As you
get older, he says, it makes sense to become more risk-averse because there is less time to ride out market
volatility. His advice: "subtract your age from 100, and that is the percentage you should have in equities."
A widower with four children, he still drives a 1989 Ford Escort, which he uses to visit the barber and friends
such as 94-year-old former Harvard University professor John Kenneth Galbraith, another giant among
economic historians and author of "The Great Crash of 1929." A few weeks ago, he met with Mr. Galbraith. "I
said, `What should we talk about?' and he said, `Enron! Enron!' " Mr. Kindleberger says, adding: "I love that.
We all love talking about Enron." Mr. Kindleberger says that history offers little consolation for victims of
swindles such as Enron because the victims rarely get their money back. "Lawyers get most of it," he says.
"Manias, Panics, and Crashes" lays out the familiar pattern of boom-bust cycles. They start with a
fundamental change in the real world, such as a war or new technology, which creates new profit
opportunities in some sectors. Investment expands, often fed by easy bank credit. Before long, however,
investment becomes speculation, then becomes totally detached from reality and turns into mania,
sometimes spreading internationally. Ultimately, he says, it ends in a crash and "revulsion" in which investors
flee falling markets. Authorities are left to wrestle with how to stabilize and then fix the financial system.

--- Page 165 ---
Page 165 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Mr. Kindleberger's stories of financial crises feature legendary swindlers such as Robert Knight, who helped
cook the books of the South Sea Company. The 1720 British equivalent of an Internet stock, it had no profits
but big plans for trade in slaves to Latin America. Its shares soared more than fivefold in four months' time
and then flamed out as copycat companies multiplied and insiders started selling shares. Knight fled England,
ended up in an Antwerp jail and then broke out.
The Dutch tulip bubble offers another example of how these cycles have played out. It started with the
development of exotic new breeds of tulips and was fueled by a booming Dutch economy. At its height,
investors traded land, houses, farm animals, paintings and gold for colorful tulips. One Viceroy tulip bulb,
writes Mr. Kindleberger, commanded a down payment of eight pigs, a dozen sheep, two oxheads of wine,
four tons of butter, a thousand pounds of cheese, a bed, clothing, some wheat and rye, and a silver beaker.
Some great crashes of history, such as the one during the time of the railroad boom, did set off recessions.
The severity and depth of the downturn, Mr. Kindleberger says, depends a lot on how aggressively authorities
fight it.
Federal Reserve Chairman Alan Greenspan has done a masterful job fighting off previous crises, including
the 1987 market crash and the 1998 collapse of hedge fund Long Term Capital Management, Mr.
Kindleberger says. Today, he's not convinced the Fed is up to the job because the bubble was so big and
consumers are heavily indebted after bingeing on loans tied to equity in their homes.
"I think we're going to bounce along the bottom for a while," he says.
The object of his greatest fascination today is the real-estate market. For weeks, Mr. Kindleberger has been
cutting out newspaper clippings that hint at a bubble in the housing market, most notably on the West Coast.
Nationwide, median home prices are up about 7% from a year ago, even though the stock market has tanked
and the economy has floundered. Over the long term, economists agree, housing prices can't continue to
outpace growth in household incomes. Mr. Kindleberger says he isn't certain there is a housing bubble yet,
"but I suspect it is."
The trick with spotting real-estate bubbles, he says, is that they don't always spread. In 1925, for instance,
real-estate prices in Florida soared and crashed, but that didn't spread to the rest of the country. Yet he notes
that something is distinctly different about the nation's housing market today, when compared with 1925.
Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac, two large government-sponsored enterprises, own or guarantee nearly $3
trillion in mortgages, helping to keep the mortgage market liquid with cash. That is a boon to homeowners,
but Mr. Kindleberger says he fears that Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac's deep nationwide presence in the
market is fueling a speculative fire.
"Banks will make a mortgage and sell it to them. It means that the banks are ready to mortgage more and
more and more and more. It's dangerous, I think," he says.
A Fannie Mae spokeswoman describes the argument as "preposterous," and notes Mr. Greenspan dismissed
the chances of a housing bubble in testimony to Congress last week. Robert Van Order, chief international
economist for Freddie Mac, says home prices might decelerate in the months ahead, but they're unlikely to
crash because interest rates are so low, the inventory of unsold homes is also low and the economy has
proven surprisingly resilient.
Yet Mr. Kindleberger isn't convinced. "If I was 30 years younger," he says, "I'd write a small book on Fannie
Mae and Freddie Mac."

Stocks Are Still an Oasis
Stocks Are Still an Oasis
By Jeremy J. Siegel
1,672 words
25 July 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
A10
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Frank J. Williams, a great stock trader of the early 20th century, said, "The market is most dangerous when it
looks best; it is most inviting when it looks worst." Well, certainly the market cannot look much worse than it
has over the past several weeks, Yesterday morning, the decline of the Standard & Poor's 500 Index
surpassed the horrible 1973-74 bear market, before posting a strong afternoon rally. All this has occurred
despite the fact that the real economy has done far better than almost anyone had expected following the
Sept. 11 terrorist attacks. Why is this happening?
In a nutshell, the lack of dividends and disbelief that the earnings reported by firms are real and can substitute
for this cash return. In past bear markets, the dividend yield served as a natural cushion for falling stocks. As
long as investors believed that the dividend payment was safe (and cash dividends declined very little during
bear markets), they would buy when stocks fell to levels where the dividend yield became attractive.
But as dividend yield fell towards zero during the bull market of the 1990s, stock prices became solely
dependent on their earnings. And as long as faith in those earnings could be sustained, stock prices
continued to rise. But this rise was very precarious. As the faith in earnings faded, there was nothing that
investors could fall back on to support prices. The traditional cushion has been taken away, and the landing
was bound to be very hard.
The roots of the current malaise stem from two sources: first, management-defined earnings that differed
from reported earnings and could not be adequately monitored; second, managements' exclusive focus on
delivering capital gains, instead of cash dividends.
Earnings manipulations actually began in the 1990-91 recession when firms realized that huge write-offs
connected with discontinued operations, which caused reported earnings to fall sharply, were greeted with joy
by Wall Street analysts. "Operating earnings," which excluded all sorts of special "one-time" charges and
which had no formal definitions in accounting standards, became management's new target. Investors sent
stock prices higher if management exceeded earnings estimates and sold mercilessly if earnings fell short (or
only "met") management's targets.
During the latest bull market, firms also found that paying dividends bestowed little benefit to the stock price.
This was fine with managers since they didn't have to turn operating earnings into hard cash. The inability to
deduct dividends from corporate taxes encouraged management to use debt financing, where interest was
deductible. Furthermore, tax-sensitive investors greatly preferred the lower-taxed (and often long-deferred)
capital gains instead of receiving fully taxable dividends.
Stock options worsened the trend towards substituting capital gains for dividends. Options became valuable
only when the price of the stock rose; dividends were of no use to the option holder.
The focus on earnings instead of dividends stood in sharp contrast to the entire history of stock returns. Until
the last 10 years, about three-quarters of stocks' real return has come from dividends and not capital gains. In
fact, dividends were the primary means that firms had to show that earnings were real. Investors care little
about how firms defined their earnings as long as hard cash flowed to investors.
Federal tax law played a large role in creating the wrong incentives for both management and investors.
Allowing deductibility of debt interest, but not of dividends, rationalizes the heavy use of debt. Furthermore,
options grants were encouraged because they could be deducted from taxes, but not reported as expenses. I
do not wish to exonerate executives who looted their companies, auditors who committed fraud, and analysts
who hyped stocks because of banking fees paid to their firms, but many of the problems that we have today
have their roots in our Byzantine tax code that distorts incentives and invites fraud.
Yet amid all the gloom, there is reason for optimism. To restore credibility of earnings reports, Standard &
Poor's has proposed a new standard for measuring earnings in a white paper released in May entitled

--- Page 167 ---
Page 167 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"Measures of Corporate Earnings." S&P, with the help of Warren Buffet, Prof. Eli Bartov of New York
University and top accountants from major Wall Street firms, has defined a new tough measure called "core
earnings" that indicates the ongoing profitability of a company's core businesses. Core earnings subtracts
options expenses, portfolio gains and most restructuring charges from reported earnings, while eliminating
the "write down" of asset values, which the committee rightfully regarded as incidental to ongoing profitability.
The core earnings standard is extremely demanding. A review of all S&P 500 firms showed that core
earnings in 2001 were only 83% of reported earnings (under generally accepted accounting principles) and
only 66% of operating earnings (assuming JDS Uniphase, which took a whopping $56 billion write down, is
excluded). Options expenses comprised almost all the 17% difference between core and reported earnings,
as the net effect of other adjustments (including exclusion of the gains from pension portfolios) almost exactly
offset each other.
The core earnings concept allows us to take a tough look at real earnings. "Top down" estimates for reported
earnings for the S&P 500 Index for 2003 (estimates made by macroeconomists, not the "bottom-up"
estimates for operating earnings that are more than one-third higher and are made by analysts with
rose-colored glasses), are $45.00. If we subtract 17% from these reported earnings for option grants, we are
left with $37.35 core earnings. With the S&P 500 now below 800, stocks are trading at about 21 times core
earnings.
But core earnings next year are apt to be higher than $37.35. I believe that option grants will go the way of
the bear market and will be far lower in future years. Options grants were necessary for many firms to attract
employees, especially in the technology sector, during the height of bull market. Today, many of those same
employees should be thankful to have a paying job. If options expenses decline by one-half next year, the
price/earnings ratio of the market is 18.6, and if the tech sector is excluded (which, despite the beating it has
taken, is still in many cases too richly priced), the P/E ratio of the 420 nontech firms falls below 18.
The bears will say a P/E ratio of 18 is still too high since the average is 15 over the past 50 years and P/E's
often drop below average in a bear market. But that average is not measured against this gold standard
"core earnings" definition I have used. Furthermore, my research suggests that the proper P/E level for
today's market, with its low transactions costs, low inflation, and favorable tax rates for capital gains, should
be in the low 20s, not 15.
What about underestimated pensions costs? Bears maintain that the market decline confirms that the return
estimates used by most firms on their pension portfolios are far too high. But there is good news and bad
news in the market decline. Yes, the value of the pension portfolios has fallen. But from these depressed
levels, the return estimates made by many pension funds are finally close to being correct. As ridiculous as a
12% return estimate was at the market peak in March 2000, a 6% to 7% return estimate is far too low from
current stock market values, which I believe will approach, if not exceed, the 7%-plus-inflation that has been
realized over all long-term periods.
The bears say that history shows that there are long bull markets followed by extended periods of stagnant or
even declining markets. Even if market history can be divided into bull and bear segments (and I doubt they
are as regular and predictable as some historians believe), it is patently wrong to warn investors about stocks
now that they have declined by more than 50%.
There have been six major stock market peaks (including the 1929-32 stock market crash) in the past 100
years. To be sure, investors would have suffered significant negative returns if they invested at these market
peaks. But after the market has dropped by 40%, subsequent five year returns have averaged 8.6% per year
above inflation and none has been negative. And all subsequent 15-, 20-, and 30-year returns have not only
been positive, but have also been above the 7% long-run average real return on stocks.
But will stocks necessarily be more rewarding than alternative assets? I can hear the bond enthusiasts
reminding investors that the 8.7% real returns on U.S. Treasury bonds since 1982, when the great bull market
began, have now topped stocks' 8.4% return over the same period. But that is because interest rates on
long bonds fell from 14% to just below 4%. It is mathematically impossible for bonds to repeat those returns
over the next decade, even if interest rates fall (God forbid!) to Japanese levels of 1.5%. I dare any bond
enthusiast to look me in the eye and maintain that an investor who buys the 10-year U.S. Treasury bond at
the current yield of 4.4% will outperform an investor who buys a diversified portfolio of common stocks over
the next decade.
None of the above analysis guarantees that we are at the bottom of this bear market. But many of the current
fears about earnings quality are overdone. By the toughest definitions of earnings, the prices of stocks are
now more than reasonable. History is definitive that once investors have suffered this much pain, subsequent
stock returns will be very rewarding. Amid all this gloom, the market looks very inviting to me indeed.

Mr. Siegel is a professor of finance at the Wharton School and author of "Stocks for the Long Run" (McGraw
Hill, 1998).

Delivering the News:Children InjuredOn Paper RoutesOften Go Uninsured---`Little Merchants' on BikesPay Own Medical Bills,Don't Get Death B...
Delivering the News: Children Injured On Paper Routes Often Go Uninsured --- `Little Merchants' on
Bikes Pay Own Medical Bills, Don't Get Death Benefits --- In Memory of 11-Year-Old Sean
By Patricia Callahan
2,236 words
19 July 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Nelson Chong Gum was zipping across the street on a motorized scooter one Sunday in January, delivering
the Honolulu Star-Bulletin, when a van hit him. The 14-year-old paperboy spent 12 days in the hospital. The
injury left him with a disfigured leg and medical bills so far totalling more than $80,000. The Star-Bulletin,
owned by closely held Black Press Ltd. of Canada, isn't legally obligated to pay those bills.
The paperboy long ago became an American icon, and generations of kids have gained important experience
-- not to mention income -- delivering newspapers. "Why be a newspaper carrier?" asks an industry recruiting
advertisement. "The lessons learned can carry over into a bright future." The ad goes on to name more than
100 famous former carriers, including billionaire Warren Buffett, astronaut John Glenn, and Olympic
speed-skating gold medalist Chris Witty.
But largely unknown is what happens when a child gets hurt or killed delivering newspapers. In most cases,
the newspaper is not responsible for paying medical bills or providing workers' compensation benefits. The
industry has argued for decades that the children aren't employees, but are "little merchants," independent
contractors who buy papers wholesale and sell them at retail prices. This independent status saves
newspapers money on workers' compensation insurance and essentially absolves them of any responsibility
for the welfare of their child deliverers -- a position that dismays some legal and workplace experts.
That kids can work in the newspaper business at all is an unusual exception to federal law. The industry won
an exemption to child-labor laws in 1949, and delivering newspapers is one of only six kinds of employment
legally available to children 13 years old and younger. They can also act, babysit, do certain kinds of farming,
work for some family businesses or make Christmas wreaths at home. The list of jobs children 14 and older
are permitted to hold grows less restrictive as they reach age 18.
In 1990, kids made up 68% of the home delivery force. With newspapers getting heavier, and morning
delivery deadlines earlier, that number dropped to 35% in 2000, according to the Newspaper Association of
America. Still, that means that roughly 140,000 youngsters are tossing newspapers on to front lawns and
doorsteps, according to the latest industry figures.
The job has its hazards. Sixteen youths under age 18 were killed delivering newspapers or collecting fees
from subscribers between 1992 and 2000, the most recent year for which figures are available, according to
the U.S. Department of Labor's Bureau of Labor Statistics. No national data are compiled for how many kids
are injured on their newspaper routes.
Last month a 12-year-old papergirl was sexually assaulted in Ogden, Utah, while delivering the closely held
Standard-Examiner. In 1994, Andrea McNeil, also 12, got struck and killed while delivering the family-owned
Daily Times Chronicle by bike in Reading, Mass. A year earlier Jeralee Underwood, 11, was abducted and
murdered while collecting money from subscribers for the family-owned Idaho State Journal in Pocatello. That
same year 14-year-old Mark Caljouw was hit and killed by a van while riding his bike to pick up a bundle of
Patriot Ledger newspapers to deliver in Hanover, Mass.
Of the six job-related deaths of youths in Massachusetts between 1991 and 1999, half were newspaper
carriers, according to Letitia Davis, director of the state's Occupational Health Surveillance Program. It was
only while studying those deaths that Ms. Davis, an epidemiologist, learned that the young paper carriers
were not covered by workers' compensation, child-labor laws or standards set by the U.S. Occupational
Safety & Health Administration. They were not even required to wear bike helmets, she says.
"It was surprising to us that the youngest and most vulnerable of our workers were the least protected," she
says.

--- Page 170 ---
Page 170 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Marc Linder, a University of Iowa law professor who has studied the issue, says he can't "understand how
anyone with legal training can accept that a 13-year-old boy is running a delivery service like UPS."
In other industries, state and federal regulators have been cracking down on employers of under-age
workers. In March the Illinois Department of Labor refused to let kids under age 14 work as paid umpires at
little league ball games. (Responding to irate parents, the state last month passed a law allowing 12- and
13-year-olds to work as umpires under certain conditions.) Last year, the U.S. Department of Labor fined Red
Robin International of Englewood, Colo., for employing 14- and 15-year-olds at prohibited times or for too
many hours at 26 of its burger restaurants. Two years ago, federal regulators fined the Waltham Public
Library in Massachusetts for paying 14- and 15-year-olds to shelve books on school nights for more than
three hours and past 7 p.m., two violations of child-labor laws.
Federal law doesn't limit the hours child newspaper deliverers can work and even allows them to work in the
middle of the night. Some states have stricter guidelines.
Meanwhile only a handful of states, including New York, Wisconsin, Nevada, Kentucky and Maryland, require
workers' compensation coverage for youth carriers. (Most adult carriers also work as independent contractors
and are not covered by the insurance, although the latter four states require it.) Workers' compensation laws
require employers, or their insurers, to pay medical bills and some lost wages to injured workers, as well as
death benefits for employees who are killed on the job.
In other states, legislators have tried without success to force newspapers to provide workers' compensation
to carriers. Publishers this year convinced Rhode Island lawmakers to drop a provision from a bill that would
have required coverage for paper carriers at daily newspapers. It was the second time in recent years that
newspapers successfully fought such a law in Rhode Island.
John Howell, owner of the biweekly Warwick Beacon, says that if he were required to carry such insurance,
his 120 carriers -- 85% of whom are under age 18 -- would be out of a job, because he would be forced to
mail the paper. Mr. Howell says he knows of no Beacon deliverer who has ever been hurt on the job.
State legislators in Nebraska tried in 1994, 1996 and 1997 to pass laws that would have required newspapers
to provide workers' compensation benefits for paper carriers. But the Nebraska Press Association argued that
such a law would dry up job opportunities for young people, and the bills never passed.
At least one paper voluntarily takes financial responsibility for injured child carriers. Under no statutory
pressure to do so, the family-owned Columbus (Ohio) Dispatch for two decades has made its 1,100 youth
carriers, about half of its total delivery force, employees instead of independent contractors, thus covering
them with workers' compensation insurance. When 11-year-old Sean Allen was struck by a car and killed
delivering the paper in 1997, the Dispatch paid his medical bills, as well as $6,200 in death benefits. Life
insurance the paper buys for its carriers paid an additional $5,000. The boy's mother, Linda Allen, was so
moved by the paper's support that she kept her son's route and now delivers it herself. "I keep doing it in
Sean's memory," she says.
Over time, parents have challenged the newspaper industry's "little merchants" position in court, with mixed
results. Twelve-year-old Jenny Larson was struck by a car while delivering the Fremont (Nebraska) Tribune in
1991. Reduced to a persistent vegetative state, she must be watched round the clock, fed through a tube in
her stomach and have her diapers changed. When her medical bills neared $1 million, her parents filed a
workers' compensation claim.
The paper and its insurer said the girl, now 23, was not eligible for such coverage. Hometown
Communications Inc., which then owned the Fremont Tribune, no longer exists. Rupert Phillips, a part owner
of the company at the time, says he had a limited role in the case, but noted that "the vast majority of
newspapers considered carriers independent contractors."
It took four years and four courts to resolve the issue. A Nebraska workers' compensation judge ruled that
Jenny was the paper's employee and was eligible for workers' compensation benefits. "It is beyond sophistry
and closer to outright dishonesty to characterize a 10-year-old party to a contract as a `little merchant' and
thus an independent contractor," the judge wrote. The newspaper appealed, and the Nebraska Supreme
Court ultimately affirmed the lower court decision in 1995.
But many courts have ruled against families. Ron Da Silva was delivering the News-Times in Danbury, Conn.,
in 1983 when someone in a passing car threw a snowball, hitting his right eye. Mr. Da Silva, 14 years old at
the time, underwent seven operations and was left legally blind in that eye. Initially his medical bills were
covered by accident insurance he had purchased from the News-Times for 50 cents a week. But that
insurance was capped, leaving him with $35,000 in unpaid medical bills. His parents had no insurance at the
time of his accident, so Mr. Da Silva filed a workers' compensation claim.

--- Page 171 ---
Page 171 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The News-Times challenged the claim. Rob Sypek, its circulation director, said in an interview that the
paper's carriers are all independent contractors who each run "their own little business." He says the paper
didn't want to pay in Mr. Da Silva's case and thus set a precedent that its carriers were employees. "It wasn't
just for the News-Times, it was to stand up for every newspaper," says Mr. Sypek.
Scott McCarthy, Mr. Da Silva's attorney, argued that the paper had a duty to cover its young workers. "The
characterization of newsboys as independent contractors is an outdated legal fiction used by publishers to
avoid responsibilities to minors and runs contrary to the public policy of this state," Mr. McCarthy said in a
court brief.
But the News-Times prevailed at the Connecticut workers' compensation review board and the Appellate
Court of Connecticut. That court ruled in 1995 that as a paperboy Mr. Da Silva was an independent contractor
because, among other things, he kept all money and tips above the wholesale cost of the paper, was billed
each week by the paper even if subscribers failed to pay him, and was free to deliver other papers as well.
At that point, about 13 years after the accident, medical-bill collectors tried to garnish Mr. Da Silva's wages as
a textile-plant worker. In 1997, at the age of 28, he filed for personal bankruptcy protection.
The News-Times is owned by Dow Jones & Co., publisher of The Wall Street Journal, through its Ottaway
Newspapers Inc. unit. A Dow Jones spokesman declined to comment further on the Da Silva case.
About 15% of the carriers for Dow Jones's community newspapers are kids under 18 years old, and the
company provides workers' compensation coverage for about half of them because the papers they work for
operate in states that require the coverage, the company says. The other children are offered accident
insurance, but it is not as comprehensive as workers' compensation coverage.
Dow Jones says it does not know if any youth carriers deliver the Journal. About 31% of the newspaper's
home-delivery circulation is handled by adult employees, who are covered by workers' compensation. The
Journal contracts the rest of its deliveries to independent distribution firms and other publishers, and also
mails some papers.
In Hawaii, the family of Nelson Chong Gum, the 14-year-old hurt delivering the Honolulu Star-Bulletin, hasn't
tried holding the newspaper responsible for his injury. Nelson's father, Ibang Chong Gum, says he didn't think
he could get help from the paper. Don Kendall, the publisher and president of the Star-Bulletin, says that
Nelson was employed by an independent distributor at the time of the accident. Phil Paragoso, the distributor,
says that the boy's contract stipulated that his company was not liable for injuries suffered delivering papers.
The personal-injury insurance of the driver who hit Nelson covered $20,000 of his medical costs, but that left
more than $67,000 in unpaid bills. Neither the boy's father, a security guard, nor his mother, a restaurant
kitchen worker, has insurance. They have hired a lawyer to sue the driver of the van and the cleaning
company she was working for at the time. The suit has not yet been filed, and the family's attorney says he
has not ruled out filing a workers' compensation claim.
Meanwhile, Mr. Chong Gum, whose six other children also deliver the Honolulu Star-Bulletin, has applied for
and received state assistance for uninsured kids. "To me, my children work for the newspaper company, even
if they say they are independent," Mr. Chong Gum says. "Without the kids, their customers are not going to
get the newspaper."

A Late Rally Saves Wall StreetFrom Steepest Drop of the Year
A Late Rally Saves Wall Street From Steepest Drop of the Year
By Craig Karmin and Jeanne Cummings
1,452 words
16 July 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Stocks staged the deepest dive of the year for the Dow Jones Industrial Average, nearly touching their
post-Sept. 11 low -- before roaring back even faster and leaving investors shaken and uncertain.
A remarkable afternoon rally of nearly 400 points in 92 minutes offered some momentary relief, and rare good
news, for investors slogging through the worst down market in three decades. But few analysts expected that
momentum to hold.
Pessimism after such a powerful comeback was one more sign of the broadening lack of confidence. The
rally came after a morning in which a rare big merger announcement and a presidential speech aimed at
restoring confidence failed to stem the losses of the previous six trading sessions.
Wall Street pros say yesterday's trading -- which saw the Dow industrials tumble 439.66, or 5.1%, at one
point -- reflects the market's current state of play, as investors made jittery by a spate of corporate scandals
look for any reason to sell. "I don't take away a lot of confidence from this," said Greg Rosenberg, head of
U.S. cash trading for Deutsche Bank Securities in New York. "The trend remains downward, and volatility
remains high."
The wild day brought spectator-sport drama to markets that have become increasingly accustomed to bumpy
rides. After opening low in the morning following weakness in Europe, selling accelerated sharply around
midday, following a speech by President Bush on the economy and corporate governance. By midafternoon,
the industrial average was within 10 points of breaking through the lowest close it hit after the terrorist attacks
last September. It briefly joined other indexes by reaching bear-market territory, more than 20% below its
March 19 high of 10,635.25
But instead of falling through that level, the market suddenly braked, and spent the rest of the day on a
boomerang course. Some market pros say the turnaround had the hallmarks of computerized program
trading, which kicked in once the market hit a predetermined low, and of buying among short sellers, who
decided it was time to take their profits on their bets that stocks would fall.
Yesterday's trading was particularly intriguing for investors looking for signs of what comes next. The drama
could well continue today, as Federal Reserve Chairman Alan Greenspan is likely to touch on the markets
and corporate governance when he delivers his semiannual report to Congress. Mr. Greenspan testifies
today before a Senate committee and tomorrow before a House committee.
For many investors, though, Washington's ability to soothe the markets is limited: The selloff started picking
up steam after President Bush gave his speech from Alabama. Big market and economic issues obviously
are beyond the White House's ability to control. But many investors, not to mention many Democrats, now
contend that Mr. Bush's economic team has failed to create the aura of confidence and competence enjoyed
by President Clinton's advisers, led by former Goldman Sachs & Co. co-chairman Robert Rubin. Mr. Bush's
outspoken Treasury Secretary, Paul O'Neill, has tended to roil markets with unpredictable comments more
than he has calmed them.
"Maybe the president should stop making speeches for a couple of days," declared Democratic Sen. Joseph
Biden of Delaware. "He's spoken twice and the market went down. . . . People are looking for real things to
happen." Even some Republicans winced privately yesterday at Mr. Bush's characterization of the business
crisis as a "hangover" from an "economic binge" during the 1990s.
Overseas investors, in particular, have been unimpressed with the administration's attempts to gain control of
the corporate scandals in the U.S. While Wall Street was swooning yesterday, the dollar fell on foreign
exchange markets, hitting parity with the euro -- where $1 equals one euro -- for the first time in more than
two years.

--- Page 173 ---
Page 173 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
European stocks tumbled, clocking their biggest one-day percentage drop since Sept. 11. The dollar's
weakness has been encouraging foreign investors, especially European investors, to pull money out of the
U.S. market, where returns are falling and look even worse when they are combined with currency losses.
For anyone who tuned in only at the end, the market's close would have looked more like a languid summer
Monday than what almost was the sixth-worst point decline in the history of the Dow industrials. The blue-chip
industrial average ended the day down just 0.52%, or 45.34 points, to 8639.19, while the S&P 500 stock
index closed down 0.38%, or 3.46 points, at 917.93.
But those closing numbers masked the frenzied action. Trading on the New York Stock Exchange was the
fifth-heaviest this year, and investors flooded mutual-fund companies to see how their accounts were holding
up. At T. Rowe Price Associates, a Baltimore fund firm, call volumes were 57% above average, a
spokeswoman said.
"Emotion is running pretty high and it seems like selling is pretty indiscriminate," said Andy Brooks, head of
stock trading for T. Rowe Price. He added that many investors appeared to be unloading even the stocks in
their portfolios that have held up best during the bear market, which is often a sign that the bottom may be
near. "But right now," he said, "people are only focusing on the negative."
One disquieting element of the selloff yesterday was that it came despite events that in the past would have
sent stocks up. The president's speech -- showing that grappling with corporate abuses is clearly taking
center stage at the White House -- could, in a different era, have been taken as a sign that the worst of the
scandals could be over.
Another potential positive on that front that was ignored in the morning: News that Coca-Cola and the
Washington Post Co. would begin treating employee stock options as expenses -- a move meant to increase
the accuracy of companies' accounting. Other companies were expected to follow suit, but an expected
market boost didn't appear to materialize.
Even Pfizer Inc.'s $60 billion acquisition of Phamacia Corp. -- the biggest corporate combination in more than
a year and the sort of deal that fueled big rallies during the 1990s -- seemed only to further undermine market
sentiment. Under the harsh light of the bear market, investors fretted that such large mergers failed to create
shareholder value more times than not. "There was a clear vote of no confidence," said Christoph Bianchet,
U.S. economist and stock strategist for Credit Suisse Asset Management.
In a sign of the depth of the recent selloff, all 18 sectors of the Dow Jones Total Market Index -- whose
components represent 95% of the market's total capitalization -- now are in negative territory for 2002 for the
first time, even after the late-day comeback. Telecommunications, media and technology stocks, all down
more than 30% for the year, have been the hardest hit. Traditionally defensive sectors, meanwhile, such as
food and beverages and basic resources are also down in 2002. Even gold stocks, the classic safe haven,
ended the day lower.
Around $7 trillion in stock-market capitalization has been erased since March 24, 2000, as measured by the
Wilshire 5000 index, and $2.4 trillion this year alone.
One of the biggest losers yesterday was Pfizer, whose shares tumbled 10.6% in response to the Pharmacia
acquisition. Pharmacia shares, however, rose 20.4%. Tech-heavy Nasdaq stocks weathered yesterday's
storm relatively well. The Nasdaq Composite Index ended the day slightly higher, up 0.66%, or 9.12 points, to
1382.62. Tech shares rose 2.2% and telecom closed unchanged.
So what now?
On one hand, the fact that the market clearly repelled the Dow industrials from hitting its Sept. 21 closing low
of 8,235 will be seen as a welcome sign of resistance. For all the market's current woes, even skeptics have a
hard time arguing that the market in particular -- and the economy generally -- is in as precarious a state as in
the days following the terror attacks last fall, when more attacks were widely feared and a deep recession
was deemed likely.
On the other hand, the attacks came before the current wave of corporate scandals, which now have
engulfed companies ranging from Enron Corp. and WorldCom Inc. to Martha Stewart Living Omnimedia Inc.
Analysts say that it will take a flushing out of those scandals, as opposed to the breaching of some new
statistical barrier, to be a precursor for the market finally starting to turn.
---
Aaron Lucchetti, Jacob M. Schlesinger and Shailagh Murray contributed to this article.

ASIA/PACIFIC
Bank of China Wins
Federal Fraud Case
Ahead of Unit's IPO
A federal jury in New York supported Bank of China's claim that customers and a former staffer defrauded the
New York branch of $35 million over nearly 10 years. In a decision announced by the U.S. court for the
southern district of New York on July 11, the jury awarded the bank $35 million in compensatory and $98
million in punitive damages. The bank is expected to ask that the compensatory damages be trebled. In
reaching its verdict, the jury also rejected countercharges made by the defendants of negligence at the Hong
Kong unit of Bank of China. That outcome removes a major cloud over the bank as it prepares to sell 25% of
its Hong Kong arm. This week, senior officials of the Bank of China arrive in New York as part of the
roadshow promoting the $2.8 billion initial public offer.
Bank of China had brought the case in February of last year, charging that two customers, John Chou and his
wife, Sherry Liu, had defrauded the bank through false trading schemes and fake invoices. Lawyers for Mr.
Chou and Ms. Liu say they will ask the judge, Denny Chin, to set aside the verdict. The jury also found that
Patrick Young, a former bank employee, breached his fiduciary duty to the bank in connection with the case.
Mr. Young wasn't immediately available for comment.

period of time" because of the uncertainties surrounding the U.S. economy, he said. The dollar closed
stronger Friday at 1,182.8 won compared with Thursday's close of 1,179.5 won.

Wholesale PricesRose 0.1% in June,So Fed Can Relax
Wholesale Prices Rose 0.1% in June, So Fed Can Relax
By Russell Gold
538 words
12 July 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Prices for wholesale goods remained tame in June, but the government's latest report contained a hint of
future inflationary pressure.
The Labor Department reported that the producer price index rose a seasonally adjusted 0.1% in June.
Excluding the food and energy sectors, the index rose 0.2% -- the largest increase in seven months but too
slow to put any significant pressure on the Federal Reserve Board to raise interest rates soon, economists
said. "The Fed can take the summer off and not worry about inflation or interest rates," said Sung Won
Sohn, chief economist at Wells Fargo.
But basic industrial materials, excluding food and energy, rose 1.6% in June, following monthly gains of 3.4%
and 3.6% in May and April, suggesting some upward pressure on prices may occur in coming months.
Overall, wholesale prices for finished goods are down 2.1% over the past 12 months, the Labor Department
said. The falling prices have fueled consumer spending, which has remained healthy throughout the
downturn. Economists are more worried about corporate profits, and depressed wholesale prices will
continue to hamper improvements in the bottom line.
At the same time, evidence is piling up that the labor market has stabilized. The Labor Department reported
that initial claims for unemployment insurance rose 16,000 to a seasonally adjusted 403,000 last week, but
the rise was attributed to the shutdown of several automobile plants for annual maintenance.
The four-week moving average, a longer view intended to smooth out weekly fluctuations, inched up to
395,000 from 393,000 the previous week. The job-market stability means there is very little upward pressure
on wages, which is helping keep inflationary pressure at bay.
New York state saw the largest increase in initial unemployment claims, but that was attributed to the end of
the school year.
Elsewhere, automobile manufacturers General Motors Corp. and Ford Motor Co. furloughed workers as part
of a temporary shutdown of plants for scheduled maintenance. The meager improvement in the job market
could be seen in the decreasing number of workers filing continuing claims for unemployment. The claims
dropped to 3.64 million for the week ended June 29 from 3.67 million a week earlier.
Separately, the weekly Dow Jones-Bank of Tokyo-Mitsubishi business index of the economy's overall
strength dropped 0.3% in the week ended June 29. A steep drop in mortgage applications and coal
production led the decline. Truck and auto sales rose, and electricity output increased. A version of the index
that tries to adjust for weekly volatility rose 0.2%, the fifth consecutive gain.
---
Journal Link: The core index of producer prices, which excludes food and energy, registered the biggest
increase in seven months. See the latest economic reports in the Online Journal at WSJ.com/JournalLinks.

EUROPE/AFRICA
French Budget
Relies on Growth,
Includes Tax Cut
Relying on increased economic growth to make ends meet, France's new center-right government of Prime
Minister Jean-Pierre Raffarin rejiggered the 2002 budget to cut income tax despite a soaring deficit that has
raised concern among European Union partners. The revised budget was based on an increased deficit
estimate of 46 billion euros ($45.8 billion) and projected economic growth this year of 1.4% to 1.5%, officials
said. The deficit is equivalent to 3.2% of gross domestic product, but the government is counting on surpluses
in local authority finances and other areas to bring the total public deficit below the 3% limit of the EU's
Stability and Growth Pact.
The revamped budget increased the previous Socialist-led government's deficit prediction by 50%, or about
15 billion euros, and included provisions for a 5% cut in income tax this year totaling about 2.5 billion euros. It
set aside a similar amount to finance welfare measures introduced by the previous government.
Liberty Extends Telewest Bond Offer
Liberty Media said it extended its offer to buy the bonds of Telewest Communications by one week to July 19,
after a group of the U.K. cable company's bondholders filed a lawsuit alleging the offer was illegal. Liberty
said a federal court in Manhattan postponed until July 18 any decision regarding the lawsuit. Liberty said it
had received tender offers for 17% of Telewest's nonconvertible bonds outstanding as of Tuesday, short of
the 20% it needs to complete the offer.
Munich Re Adds to Terror Provisions
Europe's insurers are still feeling the pain of the Sept. 11 attacks on New York's World Trade Center. German
reinsurer Munich Re, which mainly provides risk coverage for other insurance companies, said it would set
aside an additional $500 million for future claims related to last year's terror attacks, raising its total provisions
for the disaster to $2.7 billion. Munich Re also said that it would inject $2 billion into its U.S. affiliate, American
Re, to boost the unit's reserves. Chief Financial Officer Joerg Schneider said he expects Munich Re to post
second-quarter profit, before special items, slightly below the 700 million euros ($696 million) posted for the
first quarter.

completed by today.

Swedish firm agreed to concessions, including the sale of its Finnish wireless operations and cable-TV unit.

ASIA/PACIFIC
NTT DoCoMo Posts Fiscal-Year Loss
Japanese wireless operator NTT DoCoMo reported financial results for the fiscal year ended March 31 based
on accounting rules set by the U.S. Securities and Exchange Commission. The company posted a group net
loss of 116.19 billion yen ($986 million) based on U.S. accounting standards, compared with profit of 862
million yen it had reported under Japanese accounting rules. Under U.S. rules, the book value of DoCoMo's
acquired overseas assets were larger, increasing the investment losses the company posted for the fiscal
year, a company spokesman said.
Indonesia, APP Creditors Seek Plan
Indonesia's government has agreed with a number of foreign creditors of Asia Pulp & Paper to work together
to reach a restructuring plan by Sept. 30 for the ailing company's $13.9 billion in debt and obligations. APP's
creditors' steering committee, which represents hundreds of international creditors, said Indonesia has signed
an agreement with the credit-export agencies of the U.S., Germany and Japan to work with all creditors to
reach a debt-workout plan. APP, based in Singapore, declared a debt standstill in March 2001. The
Indonesian Bank Restructuring Agency, which is separately owed $1.25 billion by APP's parent, Indonesia's
Sinar Mas Group, had said previously it would act alone to get its cash back. The agreement with the
export-credit agencies came one day before the first scheduled hearing in Singapore's High Court of a
petition filed by Deutsche Bank and BNP Paribas seeking the removal of Indonesia's Widjaja family from
APP's management.

officials to bring the problem under control, according to a report issued by Ernst & Young. The global
economic slowdown, inconsistency in dealing with the problem and lender complacency are among the
factors fueling the increase, it said.

THE AMERICAS
Expert Panel to Counsel Argentina
The International Monetary Fund announced the selection of a panel of four prominent outside advisers that
will be sent to Argentina to counsel the government on monetary policy. The IMF said the team includes
Andrew Crockett, general manager of the Bank for International Settlements; former Bundesbank President
Hans Tietmeyer; John Crow, former governor of the Bank of Canada; and Bank of Spain ex-governor Luis
Angel Rojo. The team will visit Argentina July 22-24, and will advise both the government and the IMF on
formulating monetary policies for Argentina, such as controlling inflation and safeguarding central-bank
independence.
IMF, O'Neill Back Brazil's Efforts
The International Monetary Fund said Brazil's economic fundamentals "continue to be strong," following a
meeting in Washington between Brazil's Central Bank governor, Arminio Fraga, and IMF Managing Director
Horst Kohler. Treasury Secretary Paul O'Neill, who met with Mr. Fraga earlier yesterday, said he was
"convinced" Mr. Fraga and Brazil "are pursuing sound, forward-looking policies that lay the basis for
economic and financial stability."

Wyeth, Medtronic Fall BackAs Blue Chips Continue Slide
Abreast of the Market
Wyeth, Medtronic Fall Back As Blue Chips Continue Slide
By Robert O'Brien
Dow Jones Newswires
910 words
11 July 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- The assault on stock prices continued unchecked as investors pilloried Wall Street again.
A host of the sectors that had already felt the lash found new levels of pain in a session of pitched trading.
Shares of drug maker Wyeth fell to a 52-week low, off $1.55, or 4.2%, to $35.75, extending the fallout from
the latest study to cast doubts on the medical value of its hormone therapy. Merck dropped 2.18, or 4.8%, to
43.57, after the drug maker, sent reeling in recent trading, postponed its plans to split off its Medco Health
Solutions distribution unit.
The rest of the health-care sector also collapsed. Medical-devices maker Medtronic declined 3.41, or 8.7%, to
36, while Johnson & Johnson faded 2.32, or 4.4%, to 50.30, both hitting fresh lows for the year. Hospital
operator Tenet Healthcare sank 1.72, or 3.6%, to 45.76, and health insurer Cigna slid 3.07, or 3.3%, to 89.83.
Shares of Qwest Communications International shed 83 cents, or 32%, to 1.77 after the
telecommunications-service giant disclosed it is under a federal investigation.
Shares of auto makers General Motors, which dropped 3.53, or 6.9%, to 47.61, and Ford Motor, which fell
1.12, or 7.4%, to 13.99, pulled back sharply following some critical comments from a Wall Street analyst.
PNM Resources fell 3.50, or 16%, to 18.92, touching a new low for the year. PNM, the parent of Public
Service of New Mexico, issued an earnings warning. Another utility concern, Pinnacle West Capital, parent of
Arizona Public Service, sank 3.66, or 9.8%, to 33.50, sliding to a 52-week low, after some analysts reduced
their views on the stock.
While some fundamentals drove the action in a number of sectors, the character of the session was forged in
the broad market's descent. Traders described the kind of indiscriminate selloff that smacked of real
capitulation on the part of already anxious equity investors.
Shares of manufacturer ITT Industries, just two weeks removed from its high of the year, fell 1.90, or 2.8%, to
65.85.
Traders spoke of the rumors of widespread selling by mutual funds facing redemptions from fed-up investors,
but couldn't say whether that accounted for the cause or the effect of the day's declines.
That crisis showed itself in the way equity averages behaved. The Dow Jones Industrial Average sank 282.59
points, its worst point loss since Jan. 28, down 3.1%, to 8813.50, the lowest it has finished since Oct. 1 last
year.
The Nasdaq Composite Index sank 35.11 points, or 2.5%, to 1346.01, the lowest it has closed since May 19,
1997.
An attempt to pinpoint a rebound in fundamentals helped some network-equipment makers in the session.
Small cap Extreme Networks gained 60 cents, or 6.4%, to 9.93, while Cisco Systems added 37 cents, or
2.8%, to 13.51, both on the Nasdaq Stock Market, after an analyst raised ratings on the stocks, citing
expectations that corporate clients would start to ramp up their spending on network gear.
But fundamental progress itself always enough to support stocks. Shares of MGIC Investment, for example,
fell 4.25, or 6.6%, to 60.15, even though the mortgage insurer reported second-quarter results that topped
Wall Street's forecasts. SunTrust Banks recorded on-target results for its second quarter, but investors sent
the stock down 1.13, or 1.7%, to 64.37.

--- Page 183 ---
Page 183 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Meanwhile, some exigent circumstances that surprised Wall Street contributed to the tumult. The keepers of
the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index shuffled the composition of the blue-chip indicator, tossing some
components with overseas headquarters in favor of new entrants with domestic domiciles.
Gone were: Unilever, down 4.15, or 6.4%, or 60.97; Royal Dutch Petroleum, off 5.16, or 9.2%, to 50.73;
Alcan, less 1.76, or 4.9%, to 34.20; Barrick Gold, lower by 34 cents, or 1.7%, to 19.20; Placer Dome, off 11
cents, or 1%, to 11.34; and Inco, weaker by 2, or 8.9%, to 20.45.
Already-reeling Nortel Networks absorbed another body blow, falling 22 cents, or 14%, to 1.33, slumping
back to its lows for the year.
On the other hand, the replacements notched successful campaigns. They included shares of United Parcel
Service, which said it expected to live up to earnings prospects, and gained 2.51, or 4.1%, to 63.25. EBay
(Nasdaq) added 1.78, or 3.1%, to 58.83. Principal Financial Group rose 1.34, or 5%, to 28.34. Goldman
Sachs improved 64 cents, or 0.9%, to 71.55. Prudential Financial rose 28 cents, or 0.9%, to 30.59. Sungard
Data Systems inched up 25 cents, or 1.1%, to 22.40.
Shares of Halliburton fell 57 cents, or 4%, to 13.55. A legal watchdog group sued the oil-services concern, as
well as Vice President Dick Cheney, the company's chief executive from 1995 to 2000. In the lawsuit, the
Washington-based group Judicial Watch charged that accounting fraud at the company led to shareholder
losses.

ASIA/PACIFIC
Hong Kong Unit
Of Bank of China
Plans Local IPO
Bank of China Hong Kong (Holdings) said it would raise between US$2 billion and US$3.2 billion in an initial
public offering in Hong Kong representing up to a 25% stake in the bank. In addition, Standard Chartered
Bank will take a US$50 million strategic stake in Bank of China Hong Kong, said Liu Mingkang, who is
chairman of the Hong Kong unit of Bank of China, the mainland's largest international bank. The listing, if
successful, will clear the path for overseas listings of other big Chinese banks, which the mainland
government considers a crucial step as it readies its financial institutions for competition from foreign banks.
Under rules governing China's accession to the World Trade Organization, foreign banks will be allowed to do
business with Chinese companies within two years.
Bank of China Hong Kong didn't give a timetable for its much-anticipated New York listing, which originally
was going to be concurrent with the Hong Kong listing, following tradition for big Chinese companies. "We
think it is right to list in our home market" first, said Liu Jinbao, Bank of China Hong Kong's chief executive.
The offering will be priced at between HK$6.93 and HK$9.50 a share (89 U.S. cents and US$1.22), with a
listing date of July 25. The base offer represents 21.74% of Bank of China Hong Kong's shares. Should
strong demand lead to an overallotment option being exercised, the total offer will represent 25% of shares.
About 10% of the base offer is set aside for retail investors, but the bank said that portion could be raised if
demand is strong. Bank of China Hong Kong, already the second-largest bank in Hong Kong by assets after
HSBC, would become one of the dozen or so largest stocks listed in Hong Kong.
Fujitsu Focuses on Trimming Debt
Fujitsu aims to cut its outstanding debt to 1.5 trillion yen ($12.46 billion) on a consolidated basis by March
2005 from 1.761 trillion yen as of last March, the company said Friday. The major Japanese electrical
equipment maker will step up efforts to improve its finances after booking a group net loss of 382.54 billion
yen in the fiscal year ended March. The company said its debt increased by 124.40 billion yen in the
just-ended fiscal year. A Fujitsu spokeswoman said part of 250 billion yen raised through the issuance in May
of bonds with warrants will be used to repay debts.
Malaysia Leader Suggests Next No. 2
Prime Minister Mahathir Mohamad yesterday endorsed his defense minister to become Malaysia's No. 2
leader after Mr. Mahathir steps down. Najib Razak is considered a front-runner to succeed Abdullah Ahmad
Badawi, currently the country's deputy prime minister. Mr. Abdullah is slated to take over as the head of the
government after Mr. Mahathir resigns in October 2003. Mr. Mahathir, speaking during a visit to Thailand,
said Mr. Najib was well-qualified, but said his comments were "not binding" and Mr. Abdullah had the right to
pick his own deputy.

EUROPE/MIDEAST
June Traffic Soars at easyJet
Britain's easyJet on Friday reported strong growth in June traffic numbers, confirming the current strength of
budget airlines, and said its order for new airplanes is still up for grabs. Passengers numbers rose 51.5% last
month to 958,857 from 632,908. Load factor, indicating how full its planes are, rose slightly to 86.6% from
86.5%. Traders applauded the news as easyJet shares surged 19% to GBP 3.70 ($5.65) in London trading.
EasyJet said talks continue with Boeing and Airbus for an estimated $6 billion contract that could be the
largest order for new aircraft in 2002, but it didn't say how many planes it wanted to buy. EasyJet Chief
Executive Ray Webster said Boeing has an advantage in the negotiations, as easyJet already flies Boeing
737s, but either company can win the order.
Saudi, Oil Leaders Set More Talks
Saudi Arabian ministers will hold further talks next week in Jidda with top foreign oil executives on the
kingdom's gas investment initiative, as both sides rush to save the deal after face-to-face meetings with Saudi
Arabia's de facto leader last month. The Saudi ministerial negotiation committee will meet July 14 and 15 with
Royal Dutch/Shell executives, and then it will hold separate talks with an Exxon Mobil team July 16 and 17,
said an executive close to the talks. The executive said Crown Prince Abdullah, who won't attend the
upcoming talks, set a one-month deadline for the resolution of differences over the landmark $25 billion
investment plan, which would have foreign companies explore for, and produce, gas while investing in
industrial, power and water projects.
Deutsche Post to Buy Rest of DHL
Deutsche Post aims to buy the remaining shares in air express courier DHL International, cementing its
expansion into the global-parcel business. Deutsche Post, Europe's largest postal company, which is 70%
owned by the German government, said Friday that it would buy airline Lufthansa's 25% stake in DHL for 610
million euros ($593.6 million). The deal won't change how DHL is run, because Deutsche Post already owned
50.6% and controlled the management. Deutsche Post said it is now targeting the remaining shares in DHL,
which are held by Japan Airlines and a number of institutional investors. Brussels-based DHL is the largest
air express courier outside the U.S., but it trails Federal Express and UPS in the U.S. market.

June Jobless Rate Slips in Canada
Canada's seasonally adjusted unemployment rate declined to 7.5% in June from 7.7% in May, the

a June jobless rate of 7.6%. Analysts said the result is another sign of a booming economy that means the
Bank of Canada is almost certain to raise interest rates again later this month. Statistics Canada said that
since the Canadian labor market rebound began in January, employment has increased 303,000, or 2%.
Neighbors Stand by Argentina
South America's trade bloc on Friday rallied behind Argentina's beleaguered economy, but stopped short of
forging a common front to appeal for funds from the international lending community. The presidents of the
Mercosur trading nations -- including Argentina, Brazil, Paraguay and Uruguay -- issued a communique at the
end of two days of meetings in Buenos Aires expressing solidarity with Argentina during its worst ever
economic crisis. The leaders didn't go as far as Argentina's president, Eduardo Duhalde, had wanted. He had
pressed for a united lobbying front to campaign on the country's behalf with the International Monetary Fund.

EUROPE
Germany's E.ON
Wins Exemption
For Ruhrgas Deal
E.ON is expected to win the German government's approval today to take over Ruhrgas, the country's largest
natural-gas distributor. The approval would make E.ON, Europe's largest listed utility, also the continent's
biggest listed natural-gas company. E.ON's takeover required a special Economics Ministry exemption after
Germany's cartel office and monopoly commission ruled against the deal, saying it would give E.ON a
dominant position in German natural-gas and electricity distribution and undermine efforts to liberalize the
sectors. But E.ON's chief executive, Ulrich Hartmann, successfully lobbied the government for the exemption,
citing Germany's need for a powerful gas supplier able to hold its own in international markets.
Following a meeting between Mr. Hartmann and Alfred Tacke, secretary of state for economics, E.ON
yesterday said it had arranged to pay Royal Dutch/Shell Group, Exxon Mobil and Germany's TUI AG a total of
4.1 billion euros ($4.04 billion) for the 40% of Ruhrgas it doesn't own. Mr. Tacke's office is expected to
confirm approval of the E.ON-Ruhrgas deal today, according to people in the government. Berlin is also
expected to require concessions from E.ON.
ECB Keeps Interest Rates Steady
The European Central Bank, by keeping interest rates low despite upward pressure on prices, is
consciously putting at risk its fledgling reputation as an inflation fighter. But for the central bank for the euro
zone's 12 countries, it appears to be a risk worth taking, given the uncertainty still facing the recovery of the
region's economy. The ECB, at its monthly policy meeting yesterday, kept its key interest rate unchanged.
ECB President Wim Duisenberg suggested the bank is still leaning toward raising the current 3.25% rate, but
the timing of any move is further off than seemed likely several months ago. "We do believe the risks" to
inflation "are still somewhat on the upside," he said. "But uncertainties are too large to come to a decisive
decision already at this stage." Meanwhile, the Bank of England yesterday also kept its key interest rate on
hold at 4%, even though British housing prices continue to soar.
Russia Issues Poultry-Import Rule
Russia's Agriculture Ministry said it will require that new veterinary certificates accompany all poultry imports
from the U.S. beginning Aug. 1. It warned that if the U.S. wasn't ready to comply with the requirement, Russia
would bar all imports of U.S. poultry. But Russian officials said they are ready to continue talks with the U.S.,
which wants the deadline to be pushed back to Oct. 1. Moscow imposed a ban on U.S. poultry imports in
March, citing concerns about sanitary conditions at U.S. plants. The ban was lifted a month later, but
bureaucratic hurdles have delayed a full resumption of imports.
BBC-Led Group Wins U.K. Licenses
The British Broadcasting Corp. cemented its dominance over the country's television as Britain's Independent
Television Commission awarded a BBC-led consortium the over-the-aerial digital-TV licenses once held by
ITV Digital, a now-defunct pay-TV platform operated by Granada and Carlton Communications. The
consortium also includes British Sky Broadcasting, controlled by News Corp., and Crown Castle International.
Some BSkyB channels will be offered as part of the free service to customers who buy a set-top box.

-- France is likely to abandon its current bans on television advertising by retail chains, publishers and film

Placer Dome, Canada's second-largest gold producer, extended its bid for AurionGold to July 24 from July

(US$1.1 billion), or A$4.51 a share, when it was launched May 27, has been unsuccessful in winning support
from AurionGold or the Australian miner's key shareholders.
PLDT Files Suit to Block Stake Sale
The battle over who should own Philippine Long Distance Telephone ratcheted up a notch after PLDT said it
filed a suit against its parent, Hong Kong conglomerate First Pacific, in federal court in New York for allegedly
violating securities laws. The suit, which PLDT said was filed Wednesday, was apparently aimed to block the
sale of a controlling stake in PLDT to a consortium led by Philippine businessman John Gokongwei Jr. PLDT
said First Pacific's failure to submit a copy of its agreement with the Gokongwei group was a violation of
disclosure rules on information that could affect a listed company. PLDT's American depositary receipts are
listed on the New York Stock Exchange. A spokeswoman for First Pacific said it hadn't seen the suit and had
no comment.
Warner Buys Shanghai Cinema Stake
Chinese state-owned cinema chain Shanghai Paradise Film Show Management said it agreed to sell a stake
in one of Shanghai's largest cinemas to the Warner Bros. unit of AOL Time Warner. The deal, which requires
government approval, would expand the tiny foreign presence in China's film industry. Beijing agreed to
partially open the sector to foreign companies as a condition of its World Trade Organization entry last year.

THE AMERICAS
Brazil, Mexico Sign Trade Accord
Brazil and Mexico signed a long-awaited trade agreement Wednesday that reduces import duties on about
800 agricultural and industrial products, in what analysts said was an important step toward strengthening ties
between Latin America's two largest economies. Brazilian President Fernando Henrique Cardoso and
Mexican President Vicente Fox also expanded an accord that aims to create a free market for automobile
trade between the countries by 2006. Mr. Fox met with Mr. Cardoso in Brasilia Wednesday before the two
headed to Argentina for a meeting of the Mercosur trade bloc, which includes Argentina, Brazil, Paraguay and
Uruguay; Bolivia and Chile are associate members. Mexico is a partner in the North American Free Trade
Agreement with the U.S. and Canada. Separately, Mr. Fox and Argentine President Eduardo Duhalde
yesterday signed a trade pact allowing Argentina to boost its auto exports to Mexico to 50,000 units a year
from 19,000. Mr. Fox said it will add momentum to talks for a free-trade pact between Mexico and Mercosur.

The Economy:GDP Growth Rate Revised Upward---Economy in First QuarterSizzled at 6.1% Annual Pace;Jobless Claims Fell in Week
The Economy: GDP Growth Rate Revised Upward --- Economy in First Quarter Sizzled at 6.1% Annual
Pace; Jobless Claims Fell in Week
By Russell Gold
1,032 words
28 June 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
The government revised its estimate of U.S. economic growth in the first quarter to a sizzling 6.1% annual
rate -- the best quarter since 1999 -- thanks largely to a rush by businesses to sell off unsold goods.
Once that inventory-liquidation boost is stripped away, economists say, the economy is showing signs of a
more moderate, but still sustainable, recovery, capable of generating a 2.5% second-quarter growth rate.
In another report, unemployment claims fell last week, suggesting gradual improvement in the lagging job
market.
Yesterday's latest and final revision of first-quarter gross domestic product, up from a preliminary 5.6% rate,
was basically a glance backward. But the new data nonetheless suggest the gradual emergence of a
restrained economic recovery. Putting aside inventory fluctuations, GDP increased 2.6% in the first quarter,
better than the preliminary 2.0%, as measured by final sales data. Final sales figures are a measure of U.S.
economic growth factoring out changes in inventory levels. The GDP measures the total value of goods and
services produced and is the widest gauge of U.S. economic health.
What's more, the Commerce Department data offered evidence of solid consumer and corporate demand.
Personal-consumption expenditures rose 3.3% in the first quarter and are expected to remain solid. While
retail sales in May were off in an earlier report, the June figures appear to have made up lost ground.
On the corporate side, business spending decreased 6.2%. While corporate spending remains depressed,
the decline was a marked improvement from the precipitous 13.8% drop in the fourth quarter and economists
expect a better performance in the second quarter as improved profits from current production are
re-invested. In fact, the Commerce Department reported the good news that software and equipment
purchases increased, albeit by a meager 0.1%. Business spending on computers and related peripheral
equipment shot up 25%.
"In first-quarter numbers, the hemorrhaging in business investment came to an end. That was a significant
drag on GDP, which is no longer a drag. It's not adding to growth, but it's not subtracting either," says Mark
Zandi, chief economist for Economy.com, a West Chester, Pa., economic-consulting firm. "The recovery got
out of the gate very quickly and has been fading since."
The first-quarter data also suggested that price pressures aren't as distant as previously thought. The
personal-consumption expenditures price index, a measure of the prices paid by U.S. residents, increased
1.1% in the first quarter, revised up from 0.8% in the preliminary estimates. The index increased 0.5% in the
fourth quarter.
While rising, economists say this widely followed measure of consumer goods-and-services inflation is still
too low to put any pressure on the Federal Reserve Board to accelerate its timetable for raising interest
rates.
In fact, minutes of the Federal Reserve's May policy meeting released yesterday show officials there left the
short-term interest-rate target at a 40-year low of 1.75%, in part out of concern that an increase "would be
premature and would incur an undue risk to a healthy expansion." The Fed again left the target at 1.75% at
its meeting this week.
Fed officials acknowledged such a low rate was "inconsistent" with low inflation over time, but for now,
inflation pressures were "subdued" and "expected to remain so for a considerable period," providing
adequate opportunity to "tighten policy as needed later," the minutes said. That suggests a boost in rates
could still be several months off, as many Fed observers had anticipated.

--- Page 191 ---
Page 191 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
While growth had slowed from the first quarter, officials expected it to pick up but saw continued
uncertainties, especially in regard to business investment, where they saw a "sluggish and delayed upturn,"
the minutes said.
Economists say 3.5 percentage points -- slightly more than half -- of the exceptional first-quarter growth in
GDP can be attributed to companies liquidating inventory. With that trend expected to slow considerably,
inventory liquidation should boost the annual rate by less than one percentage point in the current quarter.
The Commerce Department reported that private businesses reduced inventories by $27.7 billion in the first
quarter, slightly larger than the $25.7 billion previously reported. A theme of gradual growth also can be seen
in the snail's pace improvement of the labor market. The Labor Department reported that initial claims for
unemployment insurance for the week ended June 22 dropped to 388,000 from the revised 398,000 posted a
week ago. The four-week moving average, which helps smooth out weekly fluctuations, dropped to 391,000
from 397,500. Additionally, continuing claims for unemployment insurance dropped by 43,000 to 3.75 million.
The decline signals companies are beginning to hire, but economists don't think enough jobs are being
created to keep pace with the expanding labor force. Maury Harris, chief U.S. economist and managing
director at UBS Warburg, expects the unemployment rate to tick up to 5.9% in June from 5.8% in May, when
the data are released next week. "You don't want to get excited about the unemployment rate going down,"
Mr. Harris says.

Greg Ip contributed to this article.
