Fed Holds SteadyDespite AnxietiesAmong Investors---Stock Market ComplicatesCentral Bank's ChallengeTo Revive U.S. Economy
Fed Holds Steady Despite Anxieties Among Investors --- Stock Market Complicates Central Bank's
Challenge To Revive U.S. Economy
By Greg Ip and Michael M. Phillips
1,607 words
27 June 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
WASHINGTON -- A month ago, the economic recovery appeared to be moving according to script. Now a
crisis of confidence -- intensified by WorldCom Inc.'s accounting scandal -- is tanking stocks and the U.S.
dollar, threatening to push the country back into recession. That poses a big challenge for policymakers
scrambling to figure out what, if anything, they can do.
Federal Reserve Chairman Alan Greenspan yesterday put on a brave face, refusing to cut interest rates or
even to signal that the central bank was considering such a move anytime soon. He and his fellow Fed
policymakers didn't mention stocks or foreign-exchange moves in a statement issued after their regular
monetary policy meeting, indicating they think gloomy markets don't reflect the improving economy.
"Economic activity is continuing to increase," the Fed officials said, adding that they expect "the rate of
increase of final demand to pick up over coming quarters."
Even if the Fed wanted to act, a factor complicating its job is that, with short-term rates already at a 40-year
low, there's a limit to just how much more the central bank can do to give the economy an additional boost.
President Bush and Congress, meanwhile, seem in disarray over how to respond to the spreading investor
pessimism and distrust, or how to help the economy more broadly. Sensing a mounting political storm, Mr.
Bush, traveling in Canada for a summit of world leaders, made a point of telling reporters that his
administration would step up enforcement of accounting laws: "When we find egregious practices, such as
the one revealed today, we'll go after them," referring to WorldCom's disclosure that it discovered $3.8 billion
in accounting irregularities on its books. Congressional Democrats said that wasn't enough and called for
tough new legislation.
Both sides continue to set bond markets on edge by failing to overcome the political gridlock preventing an
increase in the federal debt limit. Treasury Secretary Paul O'Neill has said if Congress doesn't act by Friday,
the government may be unable to pay its bills.
After a tumultuous day, U.S. stock markets closed little changed. The Dow Jones Industrial Average, after
spending much of the day below the 9,000 level for the first time since November, ended the day at 9120.11,
down just 6.71 points. The Nasdaq Composite Index, which had fallen below 1400 for the first time since
September, ended the day up 5.34 points to close at 1429.33.
The euro, after nearing parity, or one for one, with the dollar for the first time since February 2000, was
trading at 98.20 cents late in New York. Against the Japanese yen, the dollar fell to 119.99, the first time it
ended below 120 since October.
The risk is that, at some point, the cumulative impact on business, investor and consumer confidence of
declining stock prices and the drumbeat of news of corporate malfeasance takes a toll on growth.
"Every time a company announces it has overstated earnings or another piece of bad news comes out, it has
everyone going back into the shelter," said Pete Sinisgalli, president and chief operating officer of CheckFree
Corp., Atlanta. "People are more and more skeptical and less committed to moving forward." While
CheckFree continues to invest in computer capacity to support its business of providing electronic billing and
bill payment over the Internet, it now is reviewing market-growth figures "weekly and daily" instead of every
several months.
"It's a tough little situation," said Paul Mangano, president of Rheometric Scientific Inc., a Piscataway, N.J.,
maker of analytical equipment. The damage to investor confidence from the corporate scandals is making it
harder for his company's own stock price to recover from a steep drop that began last summer and intensified
after Sept. 11, dragged down by market weakness. The company had planned to use its stock to finance
acquisitions of other companies in the life-sciences industries. But with its shares trading for less than $1

--- Page 193 ---
Page 193 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
apiece, the company will try to "fund our growth on our own . . . and this is going to require us to get leaner
than we would have wanted" by cutting back on other investments, Mr. Mangano said.
Fed officials themselves yesterday took pains not to be too upbeat in their assessment of the economy. They
noted that the "growth in final demand" appears "to have moderated" since officials last met in May. And they
noted that "the degree of the strengthening" of the growth they expect in coming months "remains uncertain."
They also pointed out that the current Fed funds rate -- the rate at which banks lend to each other in
overnight markets -- "is currently accommodative," meaning that monetary policy should continue to
stimulate the economy, even without further rate cuts.
For all of Wall Street's doom and gloom, there are also plenty of signs that the economy -- aided by last
year's aggressive Fed rate cuts and President Bush's tax cut -- is continuing its recovery from last year's
recession.
The Commerce Department reported yesterday that new orders for durable goods rose 0.6% in May from
April and that new-home sales rocketed 8.1% in May to a record level. That suggests that growth, while less
vigorous than at the start of the year, continues to chug along.
The durable-goods order was the fifth increase in the last six months. Tech-related product orders were up
1.0% in May, building on a strong 2.9% showing the month before.
"The stock market has disconnected," asserted ISI Group, a New York-based research firm, in a report
yesterday. ISI economist Nancy Lazar said the selloff parallels 1962 -- when a confrontation between
President Kennedy and steelmakers combined with the Cuban Missile Crisis to send stocks down while the
economy powered ahead -- and 1987, when the stock-market crash wasn't followed by recession, as had
been feared.
There actually is a silver lining to the financial turmoil. Investors fleeing stocks have sought the safety of
bonds, driving their prices up and yields down. The 10-year Treasury bond yielded 4.74% late yesterday,
close to its lowest since last fall. Lower bond yields will hold down mortgage rates, underpinning housing
activity, as yesterday's booming new home sales report showed.
Furthermore, the weakness in the dollar should help U.S. companies regain market share both at home and
abroad from foreign competitors, and boost profit margins. "If all of our exporting member companies were in
one room right now, the collective sigh of relief would be deafening," said Mike Baroody, executive vice
president of the National Association of Manufacturers. "A robust recovery for U.S. manufacturing is just not
possible without a realistic market value for the dollar."
Indeed, one reason the dollar has been falling is a view by some traders that it's what the Bush administration
secretly wants. Asked about the dollar earlier this week, President Bush suggested that his economic team
would do nothing to slow its fall. "As you know, my position is that the dollar will seek its own level based
upon market forces and based upon whether or not our country can rein in spending, can recover, can
revitalize our manufacturing base," Mr. Bush replied.
But there's also a risk for the administration and for the Fed. The dollar is as much a vote of confidence in
American policies as it is in the economy. Foreign investors have been disenchanted by perceptions that the
current administration has succumbed to political expediency in passing protectionist measures such as steel
tariffs, tariffs on Canadian softwood lumber and huge new farm subsidies. "If you track the dollar's decline, it
began with Bush's call on steel" in March, said David Hale, chief global economist at Zurich Group. "The
world fears that Karl Rove now controls U.S. international economic policy," he added, referring to Mr. Bush's
political advisor.
Furthermore, while the dollar's decline thus far has been orderly, there's a risk that it could become
disorderly, if foreigners rush to dump stocks and bonds. If that happens, Mr. Greenspan would have a much
harder time giving the U.S. economy a new boost, since a central bank rate cut would only likely serve to
further depress the dollar. That's because lower official rates reduce returns on American investments.
"We're right on the cusp between a benign decline, which is positive for the U.S. and many other countries,
and the growing risk of a sharper decline, which would be disruptive for the U.S. and many other countries,"
said Robert Hormats, vice chairman of Goldman Sachs International.
Stephen Roach, chief economist at Morgan Stanley, said: "The dollar was America's last bubble, and it does
appear to be popping at this point."
---
Russell Gold, Andrew Caffrey and Ken Gepfert contributed to this article.

Credit Error to Repair? Call Sisyphus
Personal Finance
Credit Error to Repair? Call Sisyphus
By Brad Reagan
Special to The Wall Street Journal
1,393 words
26 June 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
D2
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
An inaccurate credit report has never been more dangerous for consumers, but fixing credit errors can be
treacherous as well.
Insurers and mortgage bankers increasingly rely on credit scores to determine premiums on policies or
interest rates on loans. So any mistake that lowers a consumer's credit score can be costly. An extra
one-half of a percentage point on a $250,000 30-year fixed-rate mortgage can mean more than $20,000 over
the life of the loan.
More than a quarter of credit reports contain mistakes that could result in denial of credit, according to U.S.
Public Interest Research Group, a nonprofit consumer-advocacy group -- though the credit-reporting industry
says the amount is much lower. Errors can range from a bill wrongly listed as delinquent to outright identity
theft. And it is up to the consumer to identify inaccuracies and get the reports fixed.
Correcting errors yourself can mean a frustrating, time-consuming chain of letters and phone calls to
credit-reporting agencies, consumer advocates say. "They put you in voice-mail jail," says Edmund
Mierzwinski of U.S. PIRG in Washington.
But the alternative, hiring a so-called credit-repair company to do the legwork, can be worse. Regulators warn
that all too often such companies use illegal tactics and charge exorbitant fees. Many even promise to fix bad
credit -- rather than just correct errors -- but regulators say no one can do that.
"We've never seen a legitimate" credit-repair company, says C. Steven Baker, director of the Midwest region
of the Federal Trade Commission. He says his agency has shut down more than 50 credit-repair companies
over the past decade. Victims of fraudulent credit-repair companies can see their credit histories suffer
additional damage and in some cases face criminal charges as a result of actions taken by the company in
their names, Mr. Baker says.
Credit reports track the borrowing and payment habits of anyone who has ever applied for credit. These
reports are kept by three major credit-reporting companies -- Experian, Equifax and Trans-Union -- based on
information from lenders and merchants. Other lenders then use them to determine whether to issue more
credit and under what terms.
Credit-repair companies often claim unique insight into the dispute process and even boast that they can get
all derogatory items off a report, according to Mr. Baker. Among the tactics he has seen used: repeatedly
contesting accurate items hoping the reporting agencies will eventually fail to meet the 30-day requirement for
responding. At that point, the reporting agency must remove the item. Or, Mr. Baker says, companies will
establish a new credit history for the consumer through an employee-identification number, a nine-digit
number usually used by small businesses for tax purposes. This practice, called "file segregation," is a felony
under federal law since it essentially creates a false identity.
Nevertheless, the concept of hiring an expert is attractive to many consumers.
Richard Wahba of Brooklyn, N.Y., says his credit report was tarnished several years ago when someone
embezzled money from him. But he found it hard to deal with the reporting agencies himself.
"I don't have the head for things like that," he says. So he enlisted Diana Nichols, president of Gold Key
Consulting in Milford, Conn. Mr. Wahba says it cost him $2,500 to clear up about a dozen incorrect items.
"They were relentless. It was worth the money," he says.

--- Page 196 ---
Page 196 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Ms. Nichols says her clients are mostly busy professionals. "The more complicated a person's life is," she
says, "the more possible it is for something to slip through the cracks." The process of clearing up a credit
report takes three to eight weeks and costs between $300 and $2,500, Ms. Nichols says.
She says Gold Key is a consulting firm and avoids the disreputable techniques used by credit-repair
companies.
However, the company was recently the subject of several complaints to the Connecticut attorney general's
office, and a spokesman there says the agency is looking into the complaints. Ms. Nichols says she believes
the complaints are from former clients with legitimately bad credit who felt the company should have fixed
their credit anyway.
The Federal Trade Commission and the credit-reporting agencies say it is best for consumers to handle
credit-report errors themselves. Here is how:
First, get copies of your reports from the three major reporting agencies; reports are available through the
agencies' toll-free numbers or Web sites. If you have been denied employment or credit during the past 60
days, the reports will be free. Otherwise, they will cost about $9 each.
Next, check them for errors. It isn't uncommon for accounts of relatives or people with similar names to show
up on the wrong report. Make sure each entry accurately reflects your payments.
If you find a mistake, write a letter to each agency explaining the discrepancy and include copies (not
originals) of documents that support your claim. You can also contact the agencies by phone or e-mail, but
you should always create a paper trail. If the information is wrong, the report must be corrected and you can
request that a new version be sent to anyone who received your report in the past six months. Rod Griffin, a
spokesman for Experian, says the company tries to respond to calls to its toll-free number within 24 to 48
hours. The entire process is "very easy and usually very effective," he says.
Consumer advocates and some busy professionals disagree.
"They just don't have to care and they don't," says U.S. PIRG's Mr. Mierzwinski.
Eric Brown, a business-development officer for a medical company in Denver, says he experienced that
firsthand. Someone with a similar name ran up more than $40,000 of charges in Mr. Brown's name that
resulted in his inability to get credit. He says he sent hundreds of documents to the reporting agencies
seeking to rectify his report -- to no avail.
"It's like having a truck go back over you and then go forward again," he says of the dispute process. He sued
the reporting agencies and ultimately received an out-of-court settlement.
The credit-reporting agencies declined to comment on Mr. Brown's case. But Norm Magnuson, executive
director of the Consumer Data Industry Association, a trade group, says 80% of all disputes are resolved
within 10 business days. He says fraud cases make up a small percentage of total disputes and frequently
take longer to resolve because they may involve law enforcement.
---
What's in a Score?
Credit bureaus come up with a credit score using a formula devised by
research firm Fair, Isaac & Co. The score, called a FICO, can be broken
down into five general categories:
-- Propensity to look for new credit: 10%
Someone who frequently applies for new credit is considered a risk.
-- Length of credit history: 15%
At least five years is preferable.
-- Debt ratio: 30%
The amount of debt outstanding versus amount available.
-- Credit mix: 10%
How much installment and revolving debt.
-- Bill-payment history 35%
Weighted to emphasize the past 12 months.
Here are some steps you can take to improve your credit score:
-- Pay your bills on time. Your bill-payment history is the No. 1
factor affecting your credit score.
-- Keep accounts open. Instinct says you should close open accounts
to improve your credit, but this could actually hurt you. Reporting
agencies say a debt-to-available-credit ratio of less than 50% is

--- Page 197 ---
Page 197 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
preferable, and closing accounts will increase that percentage.
-- Get a mix of credit. Reporting agencies like to see a variety of
revolving and installment debt, but most important is establishing
some sort of credit history. A person without credit cards is
considered a greater risk than someone who manages several responsibly.
-- Fix the mistakes. Check your credit report at least once every two
years and make sure all the accounts listed are actually yours and the
report accurately reflects your payment history.
-- Avoid credit-repair companies. According to the Federal Trade
Commission, most use fraudulent techniques that can land you in hot
water.
Sources: Fair, Isaac & Co., Federal Trade Commission

Commodities Report -- Gold Rush:Producers, Who Helped GoldRally, Could Bring Price Down
Commodities Report -- Gold Rush: Producers, Who Helped Gold Rally, Could Bring Price Down
By David Elliott and Gavin Maguire OsterDowJones Commodity News
493 words
26 June 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
C13
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
[Second of Two Articles]
LONDON -- Gold producers, who played a major part in the recent rally in the commodity's price, could also
help pull out the rug.
Many gold-mining companies had long protected themselves against price swings by using hedges in the
futures market -- selling their output through contracts pegged to faraway months. When gold prices started
picking up, some lifted those hedges by buying equivalent amounts of gold, boosting gold prices at the same
time. Gold Fields Mineral Services, a research company, estimates that the global gold producer hedge book
contracted by a net 147 metric tons, or around 4,285,000 troy ounces, during 2001 compared with 2000.
Howard Patten, precious-metals analyst at Barclays Capital in London, estimates that additional "unhedging"
in 2002 will add an extra 350 tons to demand.
In turn, the spot gold price jumped from $254 a troy ounce in February 2001 to a high of $330.55 this month.
Yesterday, gold on Comex settled at $322, up $1.80.
Among others, South Africa's Anglogold has said it will continue to reduce its hedge book in 2002 at the same
rate as in the previous two quarters at just over 50 tons, while Australia's AurionGold has said it plans to
reduce its hedge book further over the next 12 months.
Although gold companies have pledged to continue to lift hedges, some traders say it is only a matter of time
before hedging activity picks up again -- because a number of the factors that triggered the unhedging trend
are expected to diminish over the next few months.
Firstly, low interest rates over the past year have led to a reduction in the "contango" on the gold market,
the difference between the nearby and forward contracts, lowering the relative gain from selling forward, said
Stephen Briggs, metals analyst at Societe Generale in London.
The contango got to the point where almost no premium could be gained from selling gold forward in the
short to medium term.
If, as expected, interest rates are lifted in the U.S. toward the end of 2002 in reaction to signs the global
economy is beginning to recover, the contango would widen and could tempt producers to sell, said Mr.
Briggs.
Coupled with this is that in some instances mines are forced to hedge forward production to secure loans
from banks, said Kamal Naqvi, analyst at MacQuarie Bank in London. This is mostly to finance new mines
and could result in increased hedging if the volume of fresh mine production outweighs expired mines.
The obvious temptation for producers to raise hedging activity is the fact that the spot gold price is just off its
highest level in 2 1/2 years of $330.55 a troy ounce.
---
Enza Tedesco of OsterDowJones Commodity News contributed to this article.

Consumer Confidence Declines,Hurt by Job-Market Weakness
Consumer Confidence Declines, Hurt by Job-Market Weakness
By Russell Gold
746 words
26 June 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
A steady drumbeat of news about corporate malfeasance, coupled with a weak job market, helped drive
down consumer confidence in June, but not substantially enough to affect the key housing market.
The New York-based Conference Board's monthly index of consumer confidence dropped to 106.4 in June
from a revised 110.3 last month. It was the lowest level since February. Lynn Franco, director of the board's
consumer-research center, attributes the decline to "weak labor market conditions, generally soft business
practices, and waning public confidence in questionable business practices."
Consumers reporting that jobs were "hard to get" increased to 23.1% from 21.8%, and their outlook for the
next six months, measured in a separate part of the index, also declined. Confidence will remain close to
current levels until more businesses begin hiring, says Ms. Franco. While accounting and other corporate
scandals "may cast doubts looking ahead," she says, "all that can change by an improvement in the labor
market."
The Conference Board index is closely watched because confidence is closely tied to consumer spending,
which in turn accounts for about two-thirds of the nation's economic activity.
Still, the drop in confidence is less-than-expected after a sharp drop in a separate consumer-sentiment
survey earlier this month, and, most economists say, does signal enough personal spending to support a
gradual recovery. "The only way it would look weak is to compare it to the height of the bubble economy,"
when the index reached an all-time high of 144.7 in May 2000, says Robert McGee, senior vice president with
UFJ Bank.
Moreover, consumer spending should be helped by the still-robust housing market, which buttressed the
economy during the recent recession. In a separate report yesterday, the National Association of Realtors
said sales of existing homes fell 0.3% in May from April to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 5.75 million
units. Despite the moderate drop, the rate is the fourth-highest month ever for existing home sales; the top
four months have all been recorded this year.
Yesterday's reports are unlikely to affect the outcome of a two-day meeting of Federal Reserve policy
makers, which wraps up today. The Fed is expected to leave its target for the federal funds rate at 1.75%, a
40-year low, and to say that risks remain balanced between economic weakness and inflation. While there's
little chance the Fed will cut rates in response to weakness in stocks and some economic reports, as some
have speculated, its statement accompanying its rate decision might acknowledge the softer tone to the
economy in recent months, suggesting no rate increase for some time to come.
Bank One Corp. economist Diane Swonk says she is encouraged that consumers still are "building and
buying homes" despite being buffeted by bleak news about world-wide terrorism and U.S. corporate
scandals.
Historically low interest rates and the improving economy are helping sustain the strong housing-market
activity. The slump in the stock market also is making real-estate investment seem more attractive. "Certainly,
real estate has become a relatively more attractive place to put your funds," says David Lereah, chief
economist at the National Association of Realtors.
The strong housing market should help furniture and home-appliance makers, as well as building-material
sellers, as new homeowners typically spend on these items soon after moving into an existing home. But it is
unclear how long the housing market can remain as vibrant as it is now. Mr. Lereah suggests the current
pace can't be sustained indefinitely and predicts a gradual decline over the coming months. But UFJ's Mr.
McGee argues that since many May sales were contracted in March when interest rates peaked, lower rates
since then "will stimulate even stronger housing demand."

--- Page 200 ---
Page 200 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Meantime, house prices nationally continue to rise. The median home price in the U.S. increased 6.6% in
May from a year earlier, to $154,600. The median is the midpoint, where half the homes sold for more and
half for less.
Those gains are important, says UBS Warburg chief U.S. economist Maury Harris, because they help offset
"the potential constraint on consumer spending from sharp declines in equity wealth."
---
Greg Ip contributed to this article.

Commodities Report -- Gold Rush:As Dollar and Economy Tarnish,Gold Glitters Anew -- How Long?
Commodities Report -- Gold Rush: As Dollar and Economy Tarnish, Gold Glitters Anew -- How Long?
By Gavin Maguire and David Elliott OsterDowJones Commodity News
595 words
25 June 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
C13
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
[First of Two Articles]
NEW YORK -- Gold prices, after being pounded for years, have risen 15% since the beginning of the year to
just under $325 a troy ounce.
While many investors and traders don't believe in the rally, there are reasons behind the upward trajectory
that are expected to persist.
Since flying above $800 an ounce during the late 1970s, the metal has been in a 21-year downward trend
that shook out all but the most loyal gold bugs. Indeed, the market has had more comebacks that fizzled than
a 1970s rock band. What could be different this time, analysts and market observers say, is the combination
of supports: continued weakness in the U.S. dollar, less producer hedging, persistent tensions in the Middle
East, the uncertain outlook for the U.S. economy and investor concerns over corporate scandals.
Part of gold's renewed attraction is the lack of appealing alternatives in other markets, with stocks falling and
low interest rates on bonds.
Gabelli Asset Management's Gabelli Gold Fund is proof that for investors, gold has returned from the
wilderness. The fund, which invests primarily in gold-company stocks, has grown from less than $30 million
at the beginning of the year to more than $100 million currently, with a year-to-date return of around 68%.
Caesar Bryan, the fund's manager, says, "Certainly, the outlook for gold prices is good, and this fund is still
very small so it has a lot of room to grow."
The increased attention to gold is there. According to Commodity Futures Trading Commission data, open
interest (or amount of outstanding contracts) in gold futures on the Comex division of the New York
Mercantile Exchange during the past six months has grown by around 60,000 contracts or 55% -- or by about
six million ounces.
On the producer side, the current phase of consolidation in the industry will likely lead to additional reduction
in the amount of selling in the futures market that comes from gold companies themselves.
Not everyone in the gold market holds a positive outlook and those that don't are focusing on the potential for
significant economic turnaround and recovery for the dollar. Societe Generale says the dollar will weaken
further during the next three months but that it will "reassert its strength" in the final quarter of 2002 and into
2003.
For this reason, SocGen's metals analyst, Stephen Briggs, predicts that gold will test $340-$350 during the
next few weeks before starting to drift back when the dollar regains upward momentum.
Yesterday on the Comex, after rallying nearly $3 during the morning's stock-market swoon, gold prices
finished 30 cents down on the day, to $324.30.
In other commodity trading yesterday:
CORN: Prices at the Chicago Board of Trade hurtled to a five-month high, with traders frantically installing a
premium in response to heat in the Midwest and thoughts that a high-pressure front will further deprive the
late-planted crop. By the close, the spot July contract had climbed 7.75 cents to $2.18 a bushel.
CRUDE OIL: Prices rose at the New York Mercantile Exchange on talk that oil ministers will attempt to clamp
down on quota cheating when the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries meets tomorrow. The
August future climbed 65 cents to $26.47 a barrel.

Minnesota Sues Pharmacia Over Drug Pricing
Health
Minnesota Sues Pharmacia Over Drug Pricing
By Russell Gold
462 words
19 June 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
D3
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Minnesota became the latest state to sue a pharmaceutical company for allegedly inflating prices, charging
Pharmacia Corp. illegally raised the price the state paid for chemotherapy drugs.
The lawsuit, filed yesterday in state court in Minneapolis, is part of a widening effort by states to rein in rising
pharmaceutical prices. Minnesota prosecutors have visited colleagues in California, Florida and Texas to
"exchange information and strategize" on this and similar cases in the works, says Minnesota Attorney
General Mike Hatch.
One of the main topics at a meeting of state prosecutors that began yesterday in Farmington, Pa., is tactics
and possible cooperation in future lawsuits. Drug companies also are facing pressure from federal
investigations and consumer-group lawsuits that contend companies have employed deceptive tactics to
drive up prices.
The Minnesota lawsuit against the Peapack, N.J., company contends the state and its citizens paid too much
for cancer-fighting drugs such as Adriamycin. For instance, in 2000, the suit alleges, Pharmacia reported an
average wholesale price of $241.36 per dose for the drug, while it was selling the drug to oncologists for as
little as $33.43. Adriamycin is generally administered by physicians during office visits, instead of being
dispensed by a pharmacist.
The prescribing physician keeps the difference between reported and actual wholesale price, called the
spread. By allegedly manipulating the reported price, the suit says, Pharmacia created an incentive for
doctors to choose Adriamycin rather than other chemotherapy drugs. The result is the state allegedly
overpaid for the drug through programs such as Medicaid, a state and federal health-care program for the
poor and disabled. In addition, seniors who purchased the drug through Medicare, the federally funded
program for seniors, also overpaid, the suit alleges.
Minnesota is seeking between $5 million and $10 million in reimbursements in addition to requiring drug
companies to be subject to periodic price audits, a potentially significant development because drug
companies are bitterly opposed to giving government officials access to competitive pricing strategies. He
also says the additional lawsuits against other companies are likely this summer.
Pharmacia spokesman Paul Fitzhenry declined to comment, saying the company hadn't been served with the
complaint. He did say the chemotherapy drugs listed in the lawsuit are no longer protected by patents and
thus subject to competition from generic versions. The drugs represent a small fraction of company revenues
or profits, he added.
A similar lawsuit, involving alleged price inflation for asthma inhalers by Dey Inc., Roxane Laboratories Inc.
and Warrick Pharmaceutical Corp., is set for trial next year in Texas; California is contemplating similar legal
action. Pharmacia was named in pricing-related suits filed separately earlier this year by the Nevada and
Montana attorneys general.

French Election Result May Bode Well for Stocks
World Stock Markets
French Election Result May Bode Well for Stocks
By Craig Karmin
1,285 words
12 June 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
C14
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Once French investors get over the country's early elimination from the World Cup soccer tournament, they
may realize they have something new to cheer about.
The conservatives' overwhelming victory in Sunday's parliamentary elections could open the door to a new
slate of pro-business legislation, including pension overhaul, tax cuts and tinkering with the 35-hour French
workweek. Analysts say that enactment of these and other changes could benefit the financial- services
industry, defense contractors and many of France's small and midsize companies.
"The election results are generally good news," says Steve Auth, director of global portfolio management for
Federated Investors in New York. "They are also indicative of a broader trend of European politics moving
back toward the middle. That is positive for long-term investment in Europe."
French stocks were among Europe's biggest winners yesterday as the CAC 40 Index advanced 2.1%.
Surprisingly upbeat comments from mobile-phone maker Nokia helped boost technology and
telecommunications shares throughout Europe, where most blue-chip indexes registered strong gains. Asian
and Latin American markets, meanwhile, were mixed. Overall, the Dow Jones World Stock Index fell 1.04
points, or 0.63%, to 164.94. Excluding the U.S., the index rose 0.9 point, or 0.72%, to 125.31.
After five years of sharing power with a left-leaning government, center-right candidates supporting President
Jacques Chirac trounced the socialists and are poised to consolidate their success in Sunday's final runoffs.
The conservative victory in French parliament -- coming a few weeks after President Jacques Chirac himself
easily turned back far-right candidate Jean-Marie Le Pen -- marks the latest European country to move
politically, and likely economically, to the center-right. Dutch voters recently backed a conservative
government, joining Spain and Italy in the right-leaning camp. Even German Chancellor Gerhard Schroder of
the Social Democrats has displayed a recent willingness to move to the center ahead of his re-election bid in
September.
Global investors, for the most part, have welcomed these more business-friendly governments and are
pinning their hopes on more market-friendly platforms that could also bolster the euro, which is already near a
16-month high compared with the dollar. "The trend re-enforces the case why we think Europe will outperform
the U.S. over the next 12 months," says Mr. Auth.
In France, Mr. Chirac is expected to address the 35-hour workweek provision enacted under the socialist
government. He has indicated plans to increase the number of hours allowed for overtime and may even free
certain companies, such as those with fewer than 500 employees, from the 35-hour constraint. While this
move would be especially beneficial for small and midsize companies, some analysts believe it would boost
the economy as a whole.
"We expect a jump in both income and productivity from the relaxation of these laws," Carl Weinberg, chief
economist for High Frequency Economics in Valhalla, N.Y., wrote this week.
The heart of Mr. Chirac's program is $28 billion in personal and corporate tax cuts, though it also includes a
loosening of labor laws and incorporating a greater private component into the pension- fund system. John
Tribolet, an international fund manager for Loomis Sayles in San Francisco, thinks these changes could help
the share price of financial-services company BNP Paribas, which rose 1.3% to 57.35 euros yesterday, and
insurer Axa, up 2.1% to 19.99 euros.
Moreover, Mr. Chirac's preferences for a stronger French military could improve the fortunes of defense
companies like European Aeronautic Defense & Space Co. and Thales. Both share prices edged higher
yesterday.

--- Page 209 ---
Page 209 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Still, analysts note, French politics can be unpredictable and unions are sure to oppose much of Mr. Chirac's
pro-business agenda. Mr. Weinberg also worries that an ambitious new policy platform, including an increase
in the minimum wage, could spark inflation by the end of next year. Stimulative new fiscal policies could also
conceivably put France in violation of a European Union rule that a country's budget deficit cannot exceed 3%
of gross domestic product.
"That would test a legal mechanism that has never been tested before," Mr. Weinberg says. "It's not clear
how [the EU] would resolve this."
In TOKYO, the Nikkei Stock Average finished up 0.7%, or 79.23 points, at 11449.44. Many technology shares
ended higher, even after profit-taking pulled them down from intraday highs. Hitachi finished up 0.8% at 867
yen, Fujitsu climbed 1.7% to 900 yen and Sanyo Electric rose 1.9% to 585 yen. Toshiba was up 0.6% at 509
yen and NEC was ahead 0.7% at 907 yen. Banks also advanced in thin trading. Sumitomo Mitsui Banking
climbed 2.7% to 730 yen, UFJ Holdings gained 2.9% to 360,000 yen and Mitsubishi Tokyo Financial Group
closed up 1.2% at 977,000 yen.
In HONG KONG, the Hang Seng Index ended down 0.7%, or 73.82 points, at 11215.02. Johnson Electric
was the session's biggest blue-chip loser, tumbling 12% to 9.25 Hong Kong dollars after announcing
worse-than-expected results. The company reported net profit fell 24% to US$110.8 million during the year
ended March 31. Property shares surrendered Monday's gains; analysts said the market had already priced
in benefits from the government's new policies. Hysan Development lost 2.4% to HK$8.10, Henderson Land
Development fell 1.7% to HK$34.00 and Hang Lung Properties dropped 1.6% to HK$9.00.
In LONDON, the FTSE 100 Index rose 0.1%, or 6.6 points, to 4934.8. Chip maker ARM Holdings led the
gainers, ending the day up 10.5 pence, or 6.2%, at 180 pence. Software company Sage rose four pence, or
2.4%, to 170 pence while battered stock Logica closed up three pence, or 1.4%, at 218.5 pence. Telecom
stocks also advanced. BT Group at the helm, rising 10.75 pence, or 4%, to close at 279 pence. Vodafone
finished up 2.5 pence, or 2.6%, at 99.5 pence. In the media sector, Pearson rose 17 pence, or 2.1%, to close
at 825 pence, while Reuters gained 6.5 pence, or 1.5%, to 437.5 pence.
In TORONTO, the TSE 300 Index fell 0.8%, or 61.88 points, to 7398.83. Banks were mostly weaker. Toronto
Dominion Bank, with large exposure to Tyco International, fell 88 Canadian cents to 36.10 Canadian dollars.
Royal Bank of Canada lost C$1.24 to C$56.36 and Bank of Montreal gave up 62 cents to C$36.68. But gold
stocks extended their recent rally. Barrick Gold rose 71 cents to C$31.20, Placer Dome gained 17 cents to
C$19.77 and Goldcorp added 77 cents to C$15.90.

Unocal to FaceTrial Over LinkTo Forced Labor
Unocal to Face Trial Over Link To Forced Labor
By Peter Waldman
801 words
12 June 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
FOR THE FIRST time, an American judge has ordered a U.S. corporation to stand trial for alleged
human-rights violations committed by a joint-venture partner overseas.
In a case with potentially far-reaching implications, Los Angeles Superior Court Judge Victoria Chaney ruled
from the bench Monday that Unocal Corp. may be held liable for the conduct of the government of Myanmar,
formerly known as Burma, Unocal's partner in the Yadana gas field in southern Myanmar. A trial is scheduled
for September on the allegations raised in the suit, which was filed by several Myanmar villagers in 1996.
They charge that they were forced to work on the oil project in slave-like conditions by Myanmar's military.
Already, U.S. District Judge Ronald S.W. Lew has ruled that Unocal knew about and benefited from forced
labor in Myanmar. Despite those findings, Judge Lew dismissed the villagers' claims against Unocal in 2000,
on the ground that Unocal didn't control Myanmar's military and thus wasn't liable for its conduct under U.S.
law. Judge Lew remanded the case to state court for application of state law.
Applying California law, Judge Chaney ruled Monday that Unocal may be liable under the legal doctrine
known as "vicarious liability," plaintiffs lawyers say. That doctrine holds that joint-venture partners bear
responsibility for each other's actions involving their common business. Her ruling -- if upheld by higher courts
and applied in other jurisdictions -- could subject a long list of U.S. companies to lawsuits in American courts
as human-rights groups seek to expand the reach of American tort law to foreign soil.
"This court's position affirms that simply moving your operations overseas to a lawless environment does not
immunize you from the law," said Terry Collingsworth, whose group, the International Labor Rights
Foundation in Washington, D.C., has filed six human-rights suits against various U.S. corporations --
including the one against Unocal -- on behalf of overseas plaintiffs.
In Los Angeles, a Unocal spokesman confirmed the company's motion for dismissal was denied and said the
company now expects the case to go to trial. He added, however, that the judge may limit the trial's scope in
terms of plaintiffs, time frame and other issues. And he reiterated Unocal's position that the company never
participated in, condoned or benefited from any human-rights abuses in Myanmar.
The Unocal case, like several other human-rights suits filed against multinational companies in U.S. courts in
recent years, was initially brought under the federal Alien Tort Claims Act of 1792. That law, originally written
to prevent the U.S. from becoming a safe haven for seafaring pirates, permitted foreigners to sue one
another in U.S. courts.
It was expanded to apply to modern human-rights violators in a 1979 case known as Filartiga. In that case
filed in a U.S. court in Brooklyn, N.Y. , the family of a 17-year-old boy who had been tortured and murdered in
Paraguay successfully sued the policeman who perpetrated the acts.
Since then, human-rights lawyers have filed at least a dozen suits against U.S. corporations attempting to
apply the novel jurisdictional standards from the Filartiga case to U.S. companies doing business overseas.
Most of these suits are still pending at various levels of discovery and appeal, with no clear judicial trend
evident as to whether any or all of them will proceed to trial.
Judge Chaney's ruling Monday in Los Angeles was the first time a state judge has ordered a foreign
human-rights case to go to trial, lawyers say. Several other state judges, including one in a Louisiana case
involving Freeport McMoRan Copper & Gold Inc.'s mining activities in Indonesia, have dismissed similar
human-rights claims on jurisdictional grounds.
Mr. Collingsworth, the plaintiffs' lawyer in the Unocal case, says he is hopeful Judge Lew's findings about
Unocal will become binding in the state trial.

--- Page 211 ---
Page 211 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
That could be bad news for Unocal. In his 41-page order granting summary judgment in favor of Unocal,
Judge Lew quoted extensively from depositions, company records, consultants' reports and State Department
cables to show that Unocal and its partners were well aware, for many years, of human-rights abuses being
committed to benefit the Yadana project.
Judge Lew quoted one industry consultant, for example, who reported to Unocal in 1995 that forced labor
was common on the project, as was "imprisonment and/or execution by the army" of those who resisted.
"Unocal, by seeming to have accepted [Myanmar's] version of events, appears at best naive and at worst a
willing partner in the situation," Judge Lew quoted the Unocal consultant as writing.

Bullish on America
Bullish on America
By David Malpass
1,128 words
7 June 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
A10
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
First came Enron, then Arthur Andersen, now Dynegy, Adelphia and Tyco. Is it any wonder that Wall Street is
in a sour mood? Given the poor performance of equities, it's easy to jump to the conclusion that the economy
is fragile. Some think we're walking a tightrope -- risking a weak dollar and inflation if monetary and fiscal
stimulus continue, and a double dip into recession if they are withdrawn.
In reality, the likelihood is that the U.S. has entered a broad, durable economic sweet spot, not a fragile
interim. The economic recovery shouldn't be all that sensitive to interest rates, mortgage rates, business
confidence or the foreign demand for U.S. assets. Growth is being driven by other, more powerful factors: the
welcome exit from the deflationary macroeconomic policies of the late 1990s, and the proven flexibility and
entrepreneurism of the labor force, which have added immensely to our earning potential.
The recovery should also be sustainable over a wide range of values for the U.S. dollar. Clearly, a return to
the ever-strengthening dollar policy of the 1990s would be harmful, as would the acceptance of an
ever-weakening dollar. But this leaves open a wide range of acceptable values for the dollar given its very
strong starting point. I think an explicit "strong and stable" dollar policy would be the most pro-growth, but
other formulations will also promote economic recovery as long as they don't permit a new trend, strong or
weak, in the dollar's value.
The recent rise in the price of gold and the dollar's "weakness" against other currencies is actually a central
part of the bullish case for the U.S. The low price of gold in the late 1990s and the dollar's appreciation
against other currencies indicated a shortage of liquidity, a scarcity of dollars. The recent moves in the dollar's
value are an almost audible sigh of relief that the deflationary forces have abated. Something similar
happened in July 1982: Gold rose sharply when Fed Chairman Paul Volcker loosened his disinflationary
monetary policy. Within a month, equities had begun a strong rally, knowing that this was relief from
deflation, not an inflation threat. With the dollar still very strong today, we are likely to see a low inflation
environment for years to come, a key component of the economic sweet spot.
Best of all, our current recovery is global in nature. Europe should begin to bounce back soon. And Japan is
finally breaking out of its deflation spiral, with the Bank of Japan printing huge quantities of yen and the yen
price of gold, commodities, and wholesale goods rising. I expect Japan to begin adding to world GDP in
2003, a major improvement from the 1990s. Many emerging markets will also benefit, helping reverse some
of the polarization of the world economy that so marred the past decade.
Business leaders and the equity market are having trouble seeing the recovery because they are reeling from
two blows -- the unaccustomed nature of the deflationary pressures at work and a welter of microeconomic
problems including sweeping litigation threats, accounting mistakes, bankruptcies, security costs, insurance
issues and Washington's protectionism.
The damage from currency weakness and inflation is well known. In contrast, sustained currency strength --
as the U.S. experienced in the 1990s with the 30% increase in the dollar's value after the December 1996
change in monetary policy imbedded in Alan Greenspan's "irrational exuberance" speech -- is rare and has
almost always led to deep, prolonged recessions. Since the world left the gold standard in 1971, only Japan
and the U.S., among major industrial countries, have gone through massive currency strength. Japan's
deflationary episode lasted from 1985 through 2001, with the results evident in a decade-long stagnation.
The U.S.'s deflationary episode lasted from 1997 to 2001, culminating in an actual decline in the GDP
deflator, the broadest measure of prices, in the fourth quarter of 2001, the first in 50 years. Fortunately, the
economy is likely to emerge in relatively good condition.
First, deflation stopped deepening soon after Sept. 11. The Fed added liquidity and finally let real interest
rates fall. The price of gold and commodities began to recover some of their losses, making clear the
improvement in monetary policy. Gone almost overnight were the recessionary policies of the late 1990s --

--- Page 213 ---
Page 213 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
high real interest rates, heavy taxes on the private sector to pay off the national debt, and the U.S.
encouragement of the OPEC cartel and its $30-per-barrel oil.
Second, the economy and labor force have proven their flexibility and resilience. Productivity growth
strengthened in the 1990s and stayed strong in the recession, suggesting that the average U.S. growth rate
over the next decade will be well above historical norms. Worries about the consumer's balance sheet have
proved false, with evidence that the personal savings rate was much higher than government statistics
showed and that delinquencies in this recession stayed well below expected levels.
Overlooked in the current gloom is the structural improvement in the U.S. unemployment rate in the 1980s
and 1990s. If the U.S. unemployment "floor" is indeed 4%-6% rather than the 6%-8% predicted by the Phillips
Curve, then the lifetime earnings of U.S. workers is trillions of dollars more than earlier estimates. The U.S.
has a growing labor force employed mostly in private-sector, flexible jobs. This has massive positive
implications for the U.S. and for the wide and widening U.S. advantage over Europe, which has a shrinking
labor force employed mostly in government and unionized jobs.
Bears argue that the 4% unemployment rate of the late 1990s was an aberration. I don't think so. The big
obstacles to low U.S. unemployment in earlier decades were the inflation and rising tax rates of the 1970s,
the false economic theory that low unemployment was inflationary, the now-redesigned welfare system, and
inflexibility in the workplace. These barriers are gone, and it's hard to defend a "jobless recovery" theory.
Paul Krugman's June 4 New York Times diatribe asked "who will save the malfunctioning corporation called
the U.S.A.?" The answer is clear enough: better macroeconomic policy, labor flexibility, very low inventories,
and the intense focus on corporate malfeasance we have seen in recent months.
In short, the likelihood is that macroeconomic mistakes in the late 1990s which caused the boom and bust
have been corrected. The microeconomic aftershocks, while serious, are passing quickly, and are not likely to
cause lasting damage to the U.S. economy's ability to create prosperity.
---
Mr. Malpass is chief global economist at Bear Stearns.

Commodities Report:Was That the End of the Gold-Price Rally?---As the Metal Takes a 2% Tumble,Gold Investors Start to Worry;Price Remai...
Commodities Report: Was That the End of the Gold-Price Rally? --- As the Metal Takes a 2% Tumble,
Gold Investors Start to Worry; Price Remains Up 15% for Year
By Peter A. McKay
866 words
6 June 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
GOLD BARS DON'T TARNISH, but some investors worry that the recent gold rally soon might.
After reaching their highest price since October 1997 earlier this week, gold prices plunged yesterday, as
investors who had flocked to the metal as a haven grew confident enough to put their money elsewhere.
Gold fell $6.70 a troy ounce, or 2%, to $322.10, on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile
Exchange.
Analysts said it was no coincidence that the gold drop -- the largest price decline on Comex in more than a
year -- came on the day when major U.S. stock markets rallied, with the Dow Jones Industrial Average seeing
its first 100-point gain since mid-May.
The drop damped, but didn't totally extinguish, an enthusiasm among gold buyers that began early this year.
Even though yesterday's decline is unusually steep for a market that normally moves in much smaller steps,
analysts say they expect gold prices to stay relatively strong. That is a reflection of continuing investor jitters
stemming from weak stock markets, fears of terrorism in the U.S. and a military standoff between India and
Pakistan.
Gold also has been a prime beneficiary of a weakening U.S. dollar, which has fallen 5.4% against the euro
and the yen this year, and 0.2% against the British pound.
For the year, gold is still up an impressive 15% on Comex, and analysts believe this could be the first time in
years that the downtrodden metal sustains a run over $300.
"A certain amount of profit taking is normal and I'm actually glad to see it so the market doesn't get ahead of
itself," says Joe Foster, manager of Van Eck International Investor Gold Fund. Gold bull markets usually last
three to five years, he says, and the current one is about 18 months old.
James Glassman, chief U.S. economist for J.P. Morgan Securities, says that while a bump in gold prices is
understandable, considering the political and market uncertainties around the world, he said he "doesn't
understand" how the fundamentals of supply and demand justify this month's rise.
"People would usually be buying gold if they were worried about inflation, which hardly seems like a worry
right now," he said.
He also said low interest rates work against gold, although if the Federal Reserve begins raising them later
this year as expected, that would be bullish for the metal.
Robert Weinberg, managing director of institutional investment for the industry-sponsored World Gold
Council, acknowledged that investors who want to start buying gold may see little benefit in the short term,
although he believes the metal will perform well over time.
He noted that the ratio of gold's price to the Dow Jones Industrial Average has fallen from about 40-to-1, to
30-to-1 this year, suggesting that even if the metal has skyrocketed and dropped slightly, it has still held its
long-term value better than blue-chip stocks.
"I'm not suggesting you should buy gold because you're going to double your money overnight," he said. "I'm
suggesting you ought to buy it for insurance against your other investments."
In terms of demand -- despite gold's run-up on financial exchanges in New York and London -- the need for
physical gold for jewelry, coins and other items is actually dropping. It fell to 749.5 tons in the first quarter,
compared to 830.5, according to WGC -- a trend that may help explain the recent tempering of the gold rally.

--- Page 215 ---
Page 215 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
George Gero, a veteran trader at Comex, said such supply and demand trends will continue to affect the gold
price more than any political crisis, which could actually hurt prices.
"People think there's supposed to be a lot of upside to gold when there's bad political news. But a lot of times
the market prices in its expectations ahead of time, then sells off on the news," Mr. Gero said, noting that
gold fell when the Gulf War began in 1991.
Yesterday's gold selloff began overnight in Asia and London before spilling over to Comex, where other
precious metals also fell.
Silver prices dropped 18 cents to $4.95 a troy ounce. Platinum fell $3.20 to $549.80 and palladium closed
$4.05 lower at $349.50.
In other commodity trading yesterday:
SOYBEANS: Chicago Board of Trade prices gained on hopes of strengthening demand. Premiums paid for
soybeans in Brazil rose amid a lack of supply caused by a shrinkage of Argentina's exports. The July contract
rose four cents to $5.1375 a bushel.
CRUDE OIL: Prices weakened at the New York Mercantile Exchange on news of increasing U.S. stockpiles.
Crude stores rose by about six million barrels last week, industry and government data showed. The nearby
July contract dropped 44 cents to $24.89 a barrel.

Cranky Consumer:Dodging the Late Fees on That Credit-Card Bill
Personal Finance
Cranky Consumer: Dodging the Late Fees on That Credit-Card Bill
By Charles Passy
1,267 words
4 June 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
D3
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
You might be forgiven for thinking that credit-card companies would prefer people to pay their bills late every
month, instead of on time.
Card issuers have been tightening the rules about due dates and racheting up late fees -- by an average of
7% last year alone, according to Consumer Action, an advocacy group. Miss the deadline? Expect a late fee
as high as $35. And be prepared to see your interest rate skyrocket, too.
It adds up to a windfall for credit-card companies, who collected $7.3 billion in late fees last year, a
remarkable increase from $1.7 billion just five years ago, according to CardWeb.com, which tracks the
industry. With the loss of other sources of income -- most notably, those once-standard annual fees -- and
with the need to keep interest rates competitive, companies are on the prowl for fresh ways to squeeze more
revenue out of their card-carrying members.
To gauge what it takes to avoid paying the penalty for mailing in a late credit-card payment, we ran up
charges on five cards -- then mailed the check just one business day before it was due. We called each
company to ask what to expect. Then, if we got socked with a late fee or finance charges, we called again to
ask to have them reversed. Our logic was self-serving, but defensible: We technically "paid" on time since we
mailed the check a day early; it could, theoretically, arrive on time, right? (As if to prove our point, in one case
it actually did!)
So, how flexible are credit-card companies? It turns out they're willing to drop the fees -- as long as you're
willing to call up to ask. Several cards still hit us with penalties that climbed as high as $70 (finance charges
included), but we were able to talk our way out of every situation.
Remarkably, two cards -- Citibank's Drivers Edge Platinum MasterCard and MBNA America's LL Bean
Platinum Plus Visa -- actually have an undisclosed one-day grace period. So much for the death of courtesy.
But not all were that considerate at first. When we first contacted Chase, we were told the due date is the due
date, no exceptions. Chase did give us the chance to pay the $2,134.59 bill via phone or the Internet -- a
common last-minute option with most issuers. We mailed our check, which was credited to our account three
days past the due date. The result was a late fee of $29 and finance charge of $41.99. However, all it took
was a minute or two on the phone to have all the penalties revoked.
The situation repeated itself with MBNA America, which levied a $15 late fee on our account -- for a mere
$49.27 bill. (We avoided finance charges since we were on a special interest-free promotion.) The company
does have a one-day extension of the grace period, but that didn't help when our payment was credited to the
account two days late. Nonetheless, after calling to dispute the fee, a representative called us a "valued
customer," and said we were off the hook.
American Express also played tough at first, saying we could expect a $25 late fee. But when our $389.58
payment landed a day late, all we got fined was a measly $3.04 finance charge. The company seemed
positively happy to remove that once we called. "You will see a credit on your next month's statement," the
representative said, sounding as if the mistake was theirs, not ours.
Two companies didn't levy penalties -- even though they may have been justified. Bank of America warned
about the possibility of a $15 late fee, but didn't assess anything when our payment arrived two days late.
Citibank showed itself to be the most understanding: When we called in advance of mailing our payment, it
offered a five-day extension of our grace period. Ironically, their kindness wasn't even needed -- our check
landed in the account on the original due date.
Why are the companies so accommodating? "It's the squeaky wheel game," says Robert B. McKinley of
CardWeb.com.

--- Page 217 ---
Page 217 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Just don't squeak too much. All five companies said they can't guarantee they'll extend the same courtesy for
habitual stragglers. "It's a judgment call," says a spokesman for MBNA America.
---
Company: Citibank (Driver's Edge Platinum MasterCard)
Late-fee Policy: Payment must be made by due date, but there's an
`undisclosed' one-day grace period.
What Happened: When we called before mailing the check, Citi offered
a 5-day grace period. (Ironically, the check actually made the
original deadline.)
Service: Only company to offer an extension upfront. Downside: The
phone rep launched into a big sales pitch for a balance-transfer
option.
Other Payment Options: $9.95 for payment by phone; free for online.
Comment: Top Choice. Granting the extra grace period solved the
problem before it even became one.
Company: Bank of America (Platinum Visa)
Late-fee Policy: Payment is expected on the due date, otherwise late
fees of $15 and up start to kick in.
What Happened: Our $39.56 payment landed two days late, but we
weren't charged a late fee.
Service: The rep didn't give the impression of wiggle room. `Your
grace period [ends with] your due date,' we were told. But BoA turned
out to be forgiving.
Other Payment Options: $5 for payment by phone (free if you use
automated service); online is free.
Comment: Our experience reflects the old way card companies operated,
when customers weren't automatically hit with a late fee.
Company: American Express (Costco Platinum)
Late-fee Policy: Payments after due date subject to $25 fee ($29 on
some non-Costco American Express cards).
What Happened: Payment landed a day late. No late fee, but we did get
a $3.04 finance charge (which they dropped).
Service: When we phoned before mailing the check, the rep promised to
note that the check was in the mail; this may be why we didn't get hit
with the late fee, a spokesman later said.
Other Payment Options: Payment by phone or online is free.
Comment: Company says it may be flexible about penalties when it
comes to customers "in good standing."
Company: Chase (Toys `R' Us
Gold Visa)
Late-fee Policy: Late payments face penalties ranging from $15 to
$35, depending on size of bill.
What Happened: Hit with a $29 late fee plus $41.99 in finance charges.

Lean Times:The Surprising RiseOf a Radical Diet:`Calorie Restriction'---Most Would See It as CloseTo Starvation, but IsLonger Life a Result?...
Lean Times: The Surprising Rise Of a Radical Diet: `Calorie Restriction' --- Most Would See It as Close
To Starvation, but Is Longer Life a Result? --- A Muffin Tips the Scales
By Laura Johannes
2,394 words
3 June 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
If there were a magic elixir that extended life to 150 years, most people would drink it gladly.
But as scientists begin to uncover the secrets of longevity, they are finding a prescription for long life that few
will want to take: a diet so low in calories that to most Americans, it would feel close to starvation.
The diet, dubbed "calorie restriction" in the clinical parlance of science, would be called severe deprivation in
any other lexicon. Calorie restriction was first shown to create exceptionally long-lived rats in the 1930s. It
later had the same effect in guppies, water fleas, yeast, spiders and a microscopic water invertebrate called
the rotifer. Last month, Labrador retrievers became the first large mammals to join the list.
Now, scientists appear on the verge of a finding that calorie restriction also extends the lifespan of monkeys,
who share more than 90% of their genes with humans. At the National Institutes of Health, where researchers
have been studying a colony of 120 rhesus monkeys for 15 years, evidence for calorie restriction is mounting.
The control animals, fed a healthy lowfat diet, are dying at a normal rate, while animals fed 30% less appear
to be living far longer -- and avoiding age-linked maladies. One of the underfed monkeys is 38 years old, the
human equivalent of 114 years.
"Calorie restriction has worked in every species in which it has ever been tested," says Massachusetts
Institute of Technology biologist Leonard Guarente. "I'd be shocked if it doesn't work in humans."
Calorie restriction appears to create biochemical changes in the body that have a more-profound effect on
lifespan than simply avoiding diseases caused by excess fat. No one knows for sure how it works. It might
lower the levels of free radicals, or potentially toxic particles created by the breakdown of food. Other
scientists believe it triggers a state of emergency called "survival mode" in which the body eliminates all
unnecessary functions to focus only on staying alive.
If scientists could figure out what the changes are and bottle them as a drug, "we'd have it made," says Roger
McCarter, a scientist at the University of Texas Health Science Center in San Antonio.
Encouraged by the animal research, the NIH plans to spend $20 million to test the effects of calorie restriction
on hundreds of Americans. Some people have seen enough evidence already and have started
self-depriving. Bob Cavanaugh, a 54-year-old landscaper from Morehead, N.C., has trimmed his intake to two
meals a day, totalling 1,500 calories. Breakfast consists of one cup of quick oats, two tablespoons of toasted
wheat germ, one cup of skim milk and blueberries. For dinner, he eats vegetables, fruit and a small portion of
fish. "I'm hoping to see my great, great grandchildren," he says.
Mr. Cavanaugh's diet may sound extreme, particularly since at 5-feet 9-inches tall and 158 pounds, he isn't
overweight. But over the next several years, if the monkey results hold up, they could represent a major shift
in how we view food and nutrition. The U.S. Department of Agriculture says the average sedentary woman
should eat 1,600 calories a day and the average man 2,200 -- benchmarks already significantly overshot by
most Americans.
But those guidelines are based on ideal weight, and a calorie-restricted diet has nothing to do with weight.
Rather, the NIH monkey experiments limit food intake to the minimum necessary to prevent negative effects
on health -- or at least 30% less than the current "healthy" diet. Translated into human terms, that would be
1,120 calories a day for the average woman, or 1,540 for a man.
For the average American, eating at that level would create deep hunger pangs. One meal at McDonald's -- a
Big Mac, supersize fries, and small Coke -- weighs in at 1,450 calories. And if a woman on 30% calorie
restriction had a cappuccino and a large muffin during her morning commute, she would already have
consumed 75% of her allocation for the day, says Cathy Nonas, director of the Van Itallie Center for Nutrition
and Weight Management at St. Luke's-Roosevelt Hospital in New York.

--- Page 219 ---
Page 219 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
In the NIH tests, which will last as long as three years, volunteers will be asked to cut their current intake by
20% to 30%. Since many of those chosen will likely be overeaters, theirs will be a modest effort by
comparison with the NIH monkeys. Still, to ensure compliance, the Pennington Biomedical Research Center
in Baton Rouge, La., will initially require volunteers to eat only food provided by the scientists -- and two of the
three daily meals must be eaten in the laboratory cafeteria. Scientists will catalog the test subjects'
metabolism and other biochemical markers, such as blood sugar, lipid levels and body temperature.
The goal of the tests, says Evan Hadley, head of geriatrics at the National Institute on Aging, is to give
scientists insight into how deprivation changes body chemistry. Residents of developing countries eat very
low calorie diets. Their nutrition is so poor, though, that any positive effects are masked by medical problems
caused by malnourishment, scientists say. But a study of the Japanese island of Okinawa -- whose 1.3 million
inhabitants have traditionally eaten a spartan, but nutrition-packed diet of 1,800 calories a day -- provides
some evidence for calorie restriction.
On Okinawa, where the diet consists of soy, vegetables and small amounts of fish, meat and rice, there are
34 centenarians for every 100,000 people -- more than triple the U.S. rate, says Bradley Willcox, a
gerontologist at Beth Israel Deaconess Medical Center in Boston. The oldest person in the world,
113-year-old Kamato Hongo, lives on a nearby island, he adds.
The Okinawa data fall short of a controlled experiment, because genetics or other factors could be at work. In
rats, the effects of calorie restriction have been shown repeatedly, and they are dramatic. University of
Wisconsin researcher Richard Weindruch says that broadly speaking, a 30% calorie restriction results in a
30% increase in maximum lifespan. Translated into human years, that would mean that the oldest members
of the species would make it to about 150 on such a diet.
Even more striking is that the caloric intake of the rat, not its weight, matters most. Well-fed rats kept lean by
regular exercise are less likely to die prematurely of disease than well-fed sedentary rats -- but their
maximum lifespan remains the same.
Primates, similar enough to humans that they have been used to study everything from congenital vision
defects to Alzheimer's disease, remain the gold standard for proof when human experiments aren't
practicable. But monkeys haven't been easy to study, because they live to be around 25, compared with
three years for rodents.
At the NIH facility in Poolesville, Md., in a bucolic area about 40 miles from downtown Washington, scientists
are growing increasingly excited about the results they are seeing. The experiment began in 1987, with
monkeys of various ages. They were divided into two groups. One group was fed a normal low-fat diet,
equivalent to the healthy diet recommended by nutritionists for humans. The other group received 30% less
than that, or just barely enough to stave off starvation.
And that's the group that's thriving. Today, 14% of the calorie-restricted monkeys have died, compared with
22% of the monkeys on the normal healthy diet, says Mark Lane, a co-investigator on the study. Those
figures exclude monkeys whose deaths were deemed accidental, such as when a batch of overcooked food
caused a fatal stomach ailment called gastric bloat.
"We're very excited," says Dr. Lane. "We think it's working."
Not only do the calorie-restricted monkeys appear to be living longer, they also seem to be healthier. Only
14% of them have developed an age-related disease, such as cancer, cardiovascular disease, diabetes or
failing kidneys, compared with 32% in the control group, Dr. Lane says.
Also, calorie restriction staved off the normal age-related decline in a multifunctioned hormone called
dehydroepiandrosterone sulfate. DHEA, sold as a dietary supplement, has touched off a craze among
Americans even though many scientists say there is no proof it will forestall aging.
It's too early to predict what the maximum lifespan will be in either group. But one of the calorie-restricted
monkeys, a rhesus from Indian stock who goes only by the name given him by his breeders, C58, turned 38
in January.
That makes him one of the oldest rhesuses ever recorded. The University of Wisconsin, widely believed to
have housed the oldest-ever rhesus, says the oldest monkey for which it had a firm date of birth lived to be
36. Another animal, still alive but without a well-documented birthdate, is believed to be about 39 years old.
Neither of those monkeys were on calorie-restricted diets.
C58 looks thin but not gaunt. He weighs 17 pounds, compared with an average of 24 pounds for elderly
control monkeys. Other than a touch of arthritis and a cataract, he appears in excellent health. Each day, he
eats about 3.7 ounces of monkey chow -- dried pellets compressed from wheat, corn, soybean, alfalfa, fish
and brewer's yeast.

--- Page 220 ---
Page 220 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
In his younger days, scientists say, C58 was an aggressive "alpha male," reaching out of his cage to grab
passersby. But he's mellowed in his old age. On a recent day, he sat quietly, munching contentedly on a
handful of chow and gazing out of his cage with mild curiosity.
A small group of humans are practicing C58-style calorie restriction in the hopes that it will provide a fountain
of youth. These people, who communicate through an online chat group with 800 participants, call their
philosophy "Calorie Restriction with Optimal Nutrition." Its practitioners, who dub themselves Cronies, follow
their own personalized diets, which share the common goal of minimal calories.
Many draw their inspiration from the Biosphere 2 project in the early 1990s. Volunteers attempting to live a
self-contained existence in a glass-enclosed community were forced to reduce their calories sharply when
food became unexpectedly restricted. One of the volunteers on Biosphere 2 was Dr. Roy Walford, a scientist
at the University of California Los Angeles who is one of the pioneers of calorie restriction.
Michael Rae, a six-foot-tall 31-year-old from Calgary, Canada, weighs a gaunt 115 pounds after three years
on a strict calorie-restricted diet. "I'd much rather weigh 50 more pounds, but I want to live longer and this is
the only proven way to do it," says Mr. Rae. "Every calorie you eat is a second off your life."
Cronies monitor their vital signs carefully. Like the Biospherians, their blood sugar, weight, blood pressure
and cholesterol levels have dropped. They draw hope from the fact that they have few colds and flus, and
that their bodies seem to be changing in ways similar to animal models. For example, just as the body
temperature of rats and monkeys drops with calorie restriction, Mr. Rae's has fallen to 97 degrees, below the
normal human temperature of 98.6 degrees.
But many Cronies become irritable and snappish. Testosterone drops, causing some of the men to lose
interest in sex. Several men have developed early signs of osteoporosis -- a disease of brittle bones
commonly found in elderly women. One Cronie suddenly found himself severely anemic. He had to start
taking iron supplements and eating more red meat.
Families sometimes resent the enormous amount of time it takes to maintain the Cronie lifestyle. For the most
part, prepackaged food is out -- because it would shoot the daily limit quickly, while providing inadequate
nutrition.
Dean Pomerleau, a 37-year-old technology entrepreneur from Wexford, Pa., grows sprouts -- bean, alfalfa,
broccoli, arugula and a dozen other kinds -- in his basement. "If you like arugula, you'd really like arugula
sprouts," says Mr. Pomerleau, who is 5-foot-8 and weighs 127 pounds.
Terry, his wife, isn't enthusiastic. She told her husband the time-consuming preparation of his giant salads
was dirtying the kitchen, he says. The solution: He spent $1,000 to build a mini-kitchen for himself in the
basement, adjacent to the sprout farm.
If scientists could discover what makes calorie restriction work, people might be able to enjoy the same effect
without the hassle, and without the deprivation. One theory is that the lower body temperature caused by
near-starvation somehow extends life. In case low temperature is in fact the secret, Mr. Rae avoids putting on
a sweater even when he feels chilly.
There is mounting evidence for another favorite theory -- that lower food intake results in fewer free radicals,
or unstable particles created as a result of the breakdown of food. These particles can seriously damage
genes and proteins, resulting in potentially fatal diseases. Advocates of this theory got a major boost when
samples of thigh muscles from the calorie-restricted monkeys at the University of Wisconsin were shown to
have suffered remarkably little free-radical damage, says Dr. Weindruch.
NIH scientists have also found preliminary evidence for the "survival mode" theory. The scientists found that
human and rat cells grown in the blood of calorie-restricted monkeys are enormously resistant to heat and
toxins -- suggesting there is something in the blood that is fighting dangers aggressively.
Several groups of researchers are now racing to find which genes are "expressed," or turned on, during
calorie restriction. In mice, the "gene-expression profile," or the list of genes whose functions are turned on
and off, is strikingly similar in calorie-restricted animals to younger animals -- indicating that calorie restriction
may be directly reversing age-related biochemical changes.
In monkeys, so far, dozens of genes have been found turned on or off as a result of calorie restriction. But in
preliminary data, the gene-expression profile of the restricted monkeys doesn't appear to mirror that of
younger animals, says Stephen Spindler, a professor of biochemistry at the University of California at
Riverside.
