Midas Touch: Gold Portfolios Surge---Returns Vary Widely,And Sector's Stock PricesAre Notoriously Mercurial
Fund Track
Midas Touch: Gold Portfolios Surge --- Returns Vary Widely, And Sector's Stock Prices Are
Notoriously Mercurial
By Aaron Lucchetti
775 words
3 June 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
C17
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
It's enough for investors to want to turn into prospectors.
Gold-stock mutual funds continued their hot streak in May, extending a run that has left just about all other
funds in the dust this year. Funds that focus on precious-metals shares returned an average of 19.06% last
month through Thursday, the highest one-month gain for any fund category since technology funds soared
more than 25% in February 2000, according to preliminary May data from Lipper Inc. In the first five months
of the year, gold-oriented funds have gained almost 72%.
"I have the feeling that the high-tech guys had in 1999," says Jean-Marie Eveillard, co-manager of the First
Eagle SoGen Gold Fund. After a long and steady decline during the 1990s, the assets of Mr. Eveillard's fund
have spiked, doubling since the end of March to $61 million.
Diversified U.S.-stock funds lost ground in May, slipping an average 2.13%, while the Dow Jones Industrial
Average, including dividends, was about flat for the month, according to the preliminary Lipper figures.
Investors tempted to jump on the gold bandwagon should realize that the performance of such funds is
notoriously fickle and can vary widely even among gold portfolios themselves. Returns of the three dozen
gold funds ranges between about 50% so far this year to about 125%.
Indeed, gold stocks are even more volatile than the price of the actual metal. And while gold-stock funds are
having a good run now, they haven't had a great year since 1993. They have also lost more than 10% in six
different years since 1990. Over the 15 years ended in April, the average gold fund gained only about 3% a
year, far below the return of most other investments, according to Morningstar Inc.
Still, the metal is giving a silver lining to some portfolios. In May, gold rose while just about all types of stock
funds fell, according to Lipper's data. Even funds focusing on small, undervalued companies -- which have
been attracting gobs of new money recently -- slipped about 3% in May through Thursday.
Investors are starting to believe again that "gold is legitimate as insurance against something bad
happening," Mr. Eveillard says. Cash and inflation-protected bonds also play a defensive role, he says, but
aren't likely to soar as much during tough periods for stocks. "The last two years have been the opposite of
the previous 20," the portfolio manager says. "You've had the bursting of the Internet bubble, Sept. 11 and
Enron. The cult of equities might be dead for a few years."
Some alternatives to U.S. stocks did well in May, as Japanese-stock funds rose 6.43%, bringing that
long-suffering category to a gain of more than a 12% for the year. Bond funds remained in the black for the
year, as funds focused on corporate and mortgage issues performed well in May, according to Andrew Clark,
a senior research analyst with Lipper in Denver.
The price of gold, which has shot up above $320 an ounce from about $265 a year ago, has driven much of
the rally in the stocks of gold-mining companies. Bill Martin, manager of American Century Global Gold
Fund, says that a further increase in the price of gold-mining stocks depends in large part on a sharper rally
in the price of the metal. That's more likely if the U.S. dollar continues to weaken and gold mining companies
unwind more of their hedges, which in the past have held gold prices lower.

and the $1.6 billion Vanguard Energy Fund, will retire at year end.

Vanguard. Rather, he is a senior vice president and partner with Wellington Management Co. of Boston,
which manages $321 billion for Vanguard and other clients.
Taking up Mr. von Metsch's mantle on the Wellington fund will be Edward P. Bousa, 43, currently a vice

several portfolios in the energy and natural resources sectors, will take over Mr. von Metzsch's duties on the

Investors Consider Defensive Plays
World Stock Markets
Investors Consider Defensive Plays
By Michael R. Sesit
1,155 words
3 June 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
C10
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
LONDON -- How much of the malaise afflicting global stock markets is due to economic factors, and how
much is due to political and security risks? While strategists don't agree on the answer, they are convinced
the political mood could very well get worse, with potential crises ranging from terrorist attacks to a war
between India and Pakistan.
Still, there are things investors can do to protect themselves.
Beyond any doubt, the possibility of another terrorist attack is a factor for most investors. From a strictly
financial standpoint, any attack clearly would be bearish for the stock market. But it would be particularly
bearish for certain sectors, such as airlines, hotels and leisure companies -- just about anything related to
travel, says Gerald Holtham, chief investment officer at Morley Fund Management in London. Insurance
stocks also could take a hard hit.
For investors most concerned about another attack, David Fuller, global strategist at Stockcube Research
Ltd. in London, recommends an investment portfolio that is 50% in gold and other precious-metals shares
plus some silver futures. "I'd have 30% in a short-only fund -- most likely a hedge fund -- trading U.S. stocks,"
he adds. With the remaining 20%, he recommends purchasing U.S. Dollar Index put options, which trade on
the New York Board of Trade.
For investors who aren't convinced there will be an attack but are nervous and want some shelter, Mr. Fuller
suggests a portfolio mix of 30% gold, precious metals and silver futures, 30% high-quality -- single-A and
higher -- corporate bonds, and 40% Japanese stocks.
Why Japanese stocks? "They have the least correlation with Wall Street among major stock markets, and
nobody is going to attack Japan in all likelihood," Mr. Fuller says. "And Japan is undergoing a fashion change
at the moment as people swing money away from Wall Street and Europe." Propelling a good chunk of the
cash toward Tokyo are institutional investors who have long been underweight in Japanese stocks relative to
standard benchmarks and are afraid of being caught out by a rising market.
Other strategists, like Mr. Holtham, say it is a ripe time to employ hedging strategies. "It's fairly cheap to
insure your position at the moment, because volatility in the equity market has been very low this year," he
says. That, in turn, means options are very inexpensive.
At Global Asset Management in London, Graham Wainer, group head of portfolio management, measures his
clients by their fear factor. For the most anxious, "an appropriate strategy would be a portfolio comprising
substantially sovereign bonds and double-A and triple-A corporate bonds -- and predominantly in the major
currencies: the dollar, euro and sterling," he says. He adds that nominal and inflation-adjusted yields on
bonds are reasonably attractive.
For the client who isn't as anxious, GAM's recommended portfolio would hold some fixed-income
investments, such as hedge funds that trade in currencies and commodities. "These funds have proven to do
very well when markets have been tested in political or broad economic crises," Mr. Wainer says.
For the least-nervous investors, Mr. Wainer suggests "quality blue-chip stocks in the quality markets." All
portfolios, he says, "should have an exposure to the big blue-chip global stocks -- Coca-Cola, Procter &
Gamble and Microsoft -- which should be able over the long run to withstand market shocks."
Other trouble scenarios, including an Indian-Pakistani shootout, would probably have little direct impact on
most investment portfolios, apart from those held locally in the region. While their populations are huge, the
two countries' markets are relatively small -- minuscule in Pakistan's case. "What matters are the regional and
global political consequences, particularly the reactions of its neighbors -- the Islamic world to the west and
China -- and also the U.S.," says Mark Cliffe, chief economist at ING Financial Markets in London.

--- Page 224 ---
Page 224 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
And what if the U.S. decides to take on Iraq's Saddam Hussein? The gurus advise buying oil futures or
energy stocks. Markets, they predict, should react as they would following a terrorist attack -- a flight to safety
-- but with an added fillip: the price of oil would jump to $35-to-$40 a barrel.
It is a given that Iraqi oil exports will halt. But the bigger "risk is that some of the Arab regimes in the Middle
East are a little fragile," Morley's Mr. Holtham says. "The Saudi regime, for example, couldn't afford to be
seen supporting the U.S.; they'd have to worry about their own people and the situation in the streets."
For big-hitter investors, Stockcube's Mr. Fuller recommends a portfolio mix of 30% gold and silver, 50%
corporate bonds and 20% Japanese stocks.
The flight to quality also could force U.S. and European bond yields lower. But "if you get a reasonably long
period of $40 oil, bonds won't like the implications of that," Mr. Holtham says. "So yield curves could
steepen."

Friday's Market Activity
Asian markets closed lower, while technology gains sparked a rally in European markets. Overall, the Dow
Jones World Stock Index rose 0.07%, or 0.12, to 171.60. Excluding the U.S., the index fell 0.11%, or 0.14, to
128.14.
In TOKYO, the Nikkei Stock Average fell 0.05%, or 6.33 points, to close at 11763.70. Auto makers were down
on concern over the strengthening of the yen. Toyota Motor fell 2.9% to 3,390 yen, while Honda Motor shed
2.8% to 5,300 yen.
In LONDON, the FTSE 100 Index closed up 0.8%, or 44.3 points, at 5085.1. Retailer Dixons Group jumped
six pence, or 2.8%, to 216 pence as Credit Suisse First Boston upgraded its recommendation to "buy" from
"hold" on valuation grounds.
In PARIS, the CAC 40 Index was up 0.3%, or 14.50 points, at 4274.64. Vivendi Universal shares closed up
4.9% at 33.60 euros. Late Thursday, credit-rating agency Standard & Poor's said it had taken the media
concern off its list of companies threatened by a potential cash crunch.

EUROPE/AFRICA
Norway Bank DnB,
Insurer Storebrand
Outline Deal Terms
DnB Holding, Norway's largest bank, and the country's largest insurer, Storebrand, unveiled preliminary
financial terms of a friendly takeover of Storebrand. DnB Holding, the holding company for Den norske Bank,
will offer 1.33 of its shares for every Storebrand share and is aiming for 20% of the value of the share swap to
be in cash. At current share prices, the offer is equal to about 56.5 Norwegian kroner for each Storebrand
share, valuing Storebrand at about 15.7 billion kroner ($1.94 billion), analysts said. That is less than an
all-share offer DnB made last year of 62 kroner a share.
Analysts said the offer price was in line with expectations, but warned value for DnB shareholders is limited.
They were also skeptical about the estimated synergies from the deal of about 750 million kroner a year.
U.K. Transport Secretary Resigns
Britain's transport secretary, Stephen Byers, resigned following a series of train accidents and accusations of
misleading Parliament that had put a dent in Prime Minister Tony Blair's government. Mr. Byers said he left
because he had become a "distraction" to the government, which is wrestling with trying to improve public
services and whether to join Europe's common currency. Although Mr. Byers wasn't a heavyweight within the
Labour government, his departure is a blow to Mr. Blair; he was loyal and an enthusiast for taking Britain into
the euro zone. But after a series of missteps in which he was forced to defend himself against charges of
lying, Mr. Byers had become the target for opposition claims that Mr. Blair's government is arrogant and
obsessed with "spin." Mr. Byers told reporters yesterday that the "people that know me best know that I am
not a liar." But he added: "I know I must leave."
E.ON to Sell Veba Oel Stake to BP
German utility conglomerate E.ON said it would sell its remaining 49% stake in Veba Oel to British oil major
BP in July for 3.3 billion euros ($3.04 billion), as E.ON sharpens its focus on core operations. E.ON will
exercise a put option for the remaining Veba Oel stake, effective July 1, Chief Executive Ulrich Hartmann told
shareholders at E.ON's annual meeting. "We will then collect the agreed purchase price of 2.8 billion [euros]
plus a purchase-price adjustment of approximately 500 million" euros, he said. E.ON sold 51% of Veba Oel to
BP in February through a capital increase, and received 1.9 billion euros from BP to cover loans to Veba Oel.
E.ON will then pay BP 2.4 billion euros for BP's stake in Ruhrgas, provided E.ON receives government
approval to take control of Ruhrgas.

-- The European Commission announced plans for a radical overhaul of the European Union's fishing
industry, calling for cuts of as much as 60% in EU fishing fleets -- amid overfishing and declining stocks --
despite tough opposition from EU member states.
-- Anglo-French engineering company Alstom said it is launching a 636 million euro capital increase, in a
move to patch up its balance sheet.
-- The German Federation of Labor Unions approved Michael Sommer as its new chairman. Mr. Sommer
succeeds outgoing chief Dieter Schulte.

ASIA/PACIFIC
Japan Telecom Posts Fiscal-Year Loss
Japan Telecom posted a group net loss of 65.97 billion yen ($529 million) for the fiscal year ended March 31,
compared with profit of 17.55 billion yen a year earlier. Japan Telecom, which is 66.7%-owned by Britain's
Vodafone, said it plans to spin off its fixed-line operations on Aug. 1 and adopt a holding-company structure
as part of its efforts to return to profitability. Japan's third-largest telecommunications company posted a
fiscal-year loss for the first time since its 1994 stock-market listing, as it wrote down assets and faced rising
sales costs in its fixed-line business. Revenue rose 16% to 1.7 trillion yen.
Conoco Makes Gulf Indonesia Offer
The Conoco Canada Resources unit of U.S. oil company Conoco said it plans to acquire the shares of Gulf
Indonesia Resources it doesn't already own for $13.25 a share in cash, or $329 million, in a move to
consolidate Conoco's Southeast Asian oil and natural-gas holdings ahead of its merger with Phillips
Petroleum. Conoco acquired its existing 72% stake in Gulf Indonesia one year ago, when it bought Gulf
Canada Resources for $4.3 billion. The offer represents a 23% premium over Gulf Indonesia's closing price
Friday on the New York Stock Exchange, Conoco said. Conoco plans to merge with Phillips in an $18 billion
transaction that would create the third-largest U.S. oil company.
Japanese Business Lobbies Merge
Japan's two largest business groups merged to give member companies more influence with the government
at a difficult time for Japan's economy. Hiroshi Okuda, chairman of Toyota Motor, took the helm of the Japan
Business Federation, created from the Japan Federation of Employers Associations, or Nikkeiren, which he
had chaired, and the Federation of Economic Organizations, or Keidanren. The new lobby has 1,534 member
firms and industry groups, covering nearly all listed companies.
BRIEFLY:
-- Japan's vehicle production in April rose 2.7% from a year earlier to 777,497 vehicles, the first rise in three
months, the Japan Automobile Manufacturers Association said. Passenger-car output rose 5.3% to 660,561
cars.
-- Singapore's industrial output in April jumped 8.1% from a year earlier, compared with a 2.9% decline in
March, the government said.
-- AurionGold shares rose 1.6% in Australian trading yesterday, as investors bet that Canada's Placer Dome
won't be the only bidder for the Australian gold miner. Some analysts say Placer's global rivals won't allow its
two billion Australian dollar (US$1.1 billion) bid to be completed easily.

THE AMERICAS
Mexico Probes Wal-Mart Allegations
Mexico's antitrust regulator is looking into allegations of anticompetitive behavior by the Mexican unit of
Wal-Mart Stores, agency officials said. The preliminary investigation follows complaints by rivals that
Wal-Mart de Mexico allegedly strong-arms suppliers into giving Wal-Mart deep discounts. The watchdog said
it would look at the case in the next few months to decide if there are grounds for a full investigation. A
Wal-Mart spokeswoman said the Bentonville, Ark., company hadn't been formally notified of any investigation
by the agency and couldn't comment. Wal-Mart de Mexico accounts for about half of Mexico's supermarket
sales.
Bank of Nova Scotia Profit Rises 11%
Bank of Nova Scotia reported fiscal second-quarter profit rose 11% from a year earlier, as Canada's
fourth-largest bank benefited from a strong showing by its domestic-banking unit and managed to contain its
bad-loan provisions. Scotiabank said net income for the quarter ended April 30 was 598 million Canadian
dollars (US$390 million), compared with C$539 million a year before. Its chief executive said the bank still
faces challenges in Argentina and credit quality. Its profit was hit earlier this year by a charge that effectively
wrote off its entire investment in its Argentine unit, Scotiabank Quilmes.

Commodities Report:BHP Billiton Cuts Production;Copper Hits Two-Month Highs
Commodities Report: BHP Billiton Cuts Production; Copper Hits Two-Month Highs
By Allen Sykora OsterDowJones Commodity News
598 words
29 May 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
C13
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
An unexpected production cut by Anglo-Australian miner BHP Billiton Ltd. sent high-grade copper futures to
their highest level in eight weeks.
Funds were reported to be buying, and buy stops -- orders to enter the market when specified price levels are
hit -- were triggered as the futures moved past both Friday's high and the high for the month set May 14,
market watchers said.
Analysts suggested the move could further encourage technicians -- traders who take positions based on
chart patterns -- to continue probing the upside. Based on supply and demand fundamentals, however,
analysts were more cautious, with one even suggesting the market may have overreacted to the
production-cut news and that demand will have to improve before prices can rise much higher.
July copper rose 1.8 cents to 75.7 cents a pound at the Comex division of the New York Mercantile
Exchange, having traded as high as 76 cents, its most muscular level since April 3.
For some time, traders and analysts have been describing copper as a range-bound market. While prices
have been supported by a belief that an economic recovery will eventually mean improved demand for the
metal, the upside has been limited by stubbornly high warehouse inventories and awareness that copper
demand usually wanes during the summer.
The market got the news it needed to break out of its recent range when BHP Billiton told the Australian
Stock Exchange that it will cut production from its Escondida mine in Chile by 80,000 metric tons through the
end of the year. That would bring the company's total copper-output reduction to 250,000 tons, including
170,000 tons announced in November.
"That was very much unexpected by the market and brought in fund buying," said Dave Meger, senior metals
analyst with brokerage firm Alaron Trading.
Some buy stops were triggered as the July futures climbed through Friday's 74.40-cent high, Mr. Meger said.
More stops were hit as prices moved through the May 14 high for the month of 75 cents, he said.
Looking ahead, Mr. Meger said while Tuesday's price surge was constructive from a chart standpoint, a key
for the market may be "whether anybody follows suit" after the BHP Billiton output cut.
There still isn't a lot of buying of physical copper, said Jim Steel, director of research in New York with Refco.
"We have to wait for industrial demand to really kick in and start reducing the stocks."
While London Metal Exchange stocks declined by 1,225 metric tons Tuesday, they remained at a lofty
954,675 tons. Meanwhile Comex warehouse stocks ended last week at 333,943 short tons.
In other commodity markets:
GOLD: Comex futures gained, with banks and speculative funds buying on the back of the ailing dollar, which
makes the dollar-priced commodity cheaper to buyers. The June contract climbed $3.40 to $324.10 a troy
ounce. Tensions between India and Pakistan also offered support, bolstering the role of gold as a safe
haven.
SOYBEAN OIL: Chicago Board of Trade futures added to recent gains on hopes of export demand and
strength in Malaysian palm oil. The July contract leapt 0.56 cent to 18.26 cents a pound. Market watchers
said U.S. prices are more competitive than they usually are at this time of year. There were also rumors India
has bought soy oil from Brazil to stockpile it in the event of war with Pakistan.

Deposit Insurance Folly
REVIEW & OUTLOOK (Editorial)
Deposit Insurance Folly
610 words
28 May 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
A18
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Last week the House of Representatives, by a vote of 408-18, raised the limit on federal deposit insurance to
$130,000 from $100,000 per account. Somewhere Freddy St Germain must be laughing.
If only more of the Members remembered Freddy, they might not have repeated this horror movie. Mr. St
Germain was the House Banking Chairman back in 1980 when, as a favor to the thrift lobby, he pushed
through a midnight increase in deposit insurance to $100,000 from $40,000. The media overlooked that
increase too. But it put taxpayers on the hook for billions of dollars of new liabilities, which they eventually
paid when the savings and loan industry went bust. Freddy left Congress in disgrace, and the Members
swore never again.
Memories sure are short on Capitol Hill. With November elections approaching, today's House Members
apparently see the deposit insurance increase as one way to pay off the small-banker and insurance lobbies.
The small banks argue that they need a higher cap to compete with bigger financial institutions, even though
big banks get the same increase. But the Members can oblige by helping the small banks right away, while
taxpayers don't notice their greater liability -- until the next banking crisis.
As the history of the S&L meltdown shows, raising deposit insurance is a gold-plated invitation to what
economists call moral hazard. Once Congress socialized deposit risk, thrifts were able to attract cheap
deposits to roll into increasingly bad investments, mainly real estate deals. When high inflation rates later
popped the bubble, taxpayers had to pick up the tab.
This is why most financial institutions and nearly all regulators oppose an increase. Federal Reserve
Chairman Alan Greenspan testified in April that raising the ceiling would simply "increase the government
subsidy to banking," without providing any real public benefits. He suggested that if anything the current
$100,000 cap was too high.
Treasury Undersecretary for Domestic Finance Peter Fisher has called an increase unnecessary. Deposit
insurance was originally meant to protect mom-and-pop savers amid the Great Depression. But Federal
Reserve data suggest that just 3% of savers today have uninsured deposits, and most are much wealthier
than the average depositor. Investors can also easily divide their $100,000 deposits among financial
institutions, thus insuring them all.
Small banks would be wise to remember that the thrift lobby's push for the 1980 increase contributed to that
industry's demise. Once the S&Ls started to unravel, well-run thrifts were punished along with the bad ones.
Legislators went on a regulation bonanza, which ultimately hampered smaller banks' ability to innovate.
Ordinarily, we'd expect the Senate to slow this giveaway, but election-year politics is also playing in that
chamber. The deposit-insurance bill was co-sponsored in the House by Republican Representative John
Thune, who is challenging South Dakota Senator Tim Johnson this fall. So Mr. Johnson has introduced his
own deposit-increase bill in the Senate and gained the support of Majority Leader, and fellow South Dakotan,
Tom Daschle. Add this to the farm bill and the ethanol boondoggle and this year's South Dakota Senate race
may turn out to be the most expensive election in American history.
Which means the buckpassing is probably going to stop once more with President Bush. We've suggested
the President start wielding his veto pen on spending bills, but an increase in taxpayer liabilities of this
magnitude also deserves a return to Congress.
(See related letter: "Letters to the Editor: Deposit Insurance Rise Protects Against Inflation" -- WSJ June 11,
2002)

Currency Trading:Dollar Is Expected to Consolidate or Rebound Slightly
Currency Trading: Dollar Is Expected to Consolidate or Rebound Slightly
By Paul Evans
Dow Jones Newswires
859 words
28 May 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
C11
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
TORONTO -- The dollar is expected to consolidate or even rebound slightly against other major currencies
this week, even as it remains under the shadow of concerns related to terrorism, conflicts in southwest Asia
and the Middle East, and the durability of the U.S. economic recovery.
Although these negative influences contributed mightily to the dollar's sharp retreat against many widely
traded currencies last week, currency watchers say there are signs that the dollar's fall may have run its
course for the time being.
"People have positioned for all that and we've had a run of pretty sustained moves in many currencies for
several weeks," said Lisa Finstrom, senior currency analyst at Salomon Smith Barney in New York. "This
week could be the breather from all that, and what we could be left with is a lull where the dollar is still
defensive, but not necessarily continuing to fall."
When full currency trading resumes today after Monday's Memorial Day holiday, the dollar is seen trading
between 124 yen and 126.50 yen against the Japanese currency while continuing a return from eight-month
lows against the euro to trade in a range between 92.00 U.S. cents and 91.00 U.S. cents to the euro.
Late Friday in New York, the euro was at 92.12 cents, up from 92.00 cents earlier in London but down from
92.17 cents late Thursday in New York. The dollar traded at 124.70 yen, down from 125.01 yen in earlier
London dealings and 124.90 yen in New York Thursday.
Last week's declines for the dollar occurred against a growing mood of risk aversion in global financial
markets, prompted by official warnings of terrorist strikes in the U.S., and the increasingly tense standoff
between nuclear rivals India and Pakistan.
Analysts acknowledge that the terrorism warnings and geopolitical concerns have put safe-harbor
considerations back into play in currency markets with a prominence not seen since the immediate post-Sept.
11 period, largely to the benefit of traditional safe harbors such as gold and the Swiss franc.
But in the absence of new attacks on the U.S. or an actual outbreak of war between India and Pakistan in the
immediate future, these concerns should wane somewhat beginning this week.
Even so, any potential gains for the dollar are likely to be limited by continuing misgivings about the vigor of
the U.S. economic recovery, along with the recent erratic performance of U.S. equity markets.
Recent economic data has given a mixed reading on the U.S. rebound, with last week's strong April
durable-goods orders report offset somewhat by a surprise downward revision of first-quarter growth in gross
domestic product to 5.6% from the original 5.8% estimate. Many analysts had been expecting an upward
revision to 6.0% or above.
This week features important U.S. consumer-confidence data with the May Conference Board consumer
confidence index today and the University of Michigan's final May consumer sentiment index on Friday.
These reports, along with Friday's May Chicago Purchasing Managers' index, are all expected to be relatively
solid, but likely won't lend much support to the dollar against the tense backdrop of war and terrorism threats.
"There's so much ambiguity that no one is betting the farm on this economic recovery gaining momentum,"
said Andrew Pyle, senior financial-markets economist at Scotia Capital Markets in Toronto, noting the
subdued currency market reaction that greeted last week's GDP and durable-goods orders data.

--- Page 232 ---
Page 232 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"The market will probably remain net negative on where the U.S. is currently, and retain the better flows into
currencies like the euro, pound sterling, and even peripheral currencies like Canada's or Australia's," he
added.
Prior to last week, the Japanese yen had been benefiting from a rosier economic outlook, rising to near
six-month highs against the dollar aided by a flood of foreign capital into Japanese equities, and perceptions
of a cyclical economic recovery in Japan.
The dollar fell from the 128-yen area to just below 124-yen in just three sessions early last week, a sudden
descent which prompted Japanese officials to back their verbal campaign for a weaker yen with two volleys of
direct currency-market intervention to slow its rise.
Additional Bank of Japan intervention is believed to still be a strong possibility if the yen resumes a strong
upward course toward 120 yen to the dollar, though most analysts believe the Japanese central bank is
interested mainly in braking the speed of further appreciation, rather than preventing it altogether.
"The Bank of Japan didn't necessarily want to reverse the price action in the yen, but just to slow the
process," said Ms. Finstrom of Salomon Smith Barney. "There are fundamental reasons behind the yen's
improvement, and though the outlook is still uncertain, the market's been able to decisively shift away from
the view of a continued death spiral for the Japanese economy."

Waiting for a Bull Market? It May Be Awhile
Abreast of the Market
Waiting for a Bull Market? It May Be Awhile
By E.S. Browning
1,467 words
28 May 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
THE ECONOMY IS getting better and there are signs that earnings are about to improve. So why can't the
stock market get going again?
The answer goes well beyond Osama bin Laden and Arthur Andersen. It lies in a fundamental shift in recent
years that has made the world a less-attractive place for investors, as a startling number of the market's
underpinnings have fallen away. As a result, many professional investors are warning that stocks aren't going
to perform the way they did in the late 1990s -- and may not for years to come.
Consider how many of the essential factors that drove the bull market have changed. Then, with the Cold
War over, money flowed easily from country to country and little challenged the American capitalist model.
Inflation fell to unheard-of lows. Falling interest rates spurred business and home ownership and helped
send stocks to dazzling highs. Creativity was high, too, as technology revolutionized the way people lived.
Across the world, people wanted to own U.S. stocks.
Some of those factors, of course, remain in place, but a surprising number don't -- making it very difficult to
muster arguments as to why anybody needs to buy stocks now.
Many people, especially in New York, find their minds focused on a new war against terrorism, which seems
to be here to stay. Economists who recently were forecasting government surpluses now warn of deficits that
could last for years. Communications and the Internet still are enhancing productivity, but companies simply
don't need to boost their purchases of tech gear as fast as they did. The dollar now is weakening and
foreigners are pulling back from U.S. stocks.
If companies start reporting stronger earnings, of course, some of the upbeat thinking could return. But for
now, the overriding sense of hope that drove stocks in the 1990s is fading -- partly as a result of doubts about
the validity of corporate numbers -- and many long-time investors doubt that it will come back the way it was.
"Investment psychology has changed dramatically over the past couple of years, and that will tend to limit the
upside" for the stock market, says Bruce Simon, chief investment officer at money-management firm
Glenmede Trust in Philadelphia. "It is going to foster a pretty fundamental change in the way investors
approach the markets," he says. "We need to condition ourselves and our clients for fairly unimpressive
single-digit returns over the next decade."
These professional investors aren't forecasting a renewed bear market. But they are signaling that the top
performers to come might not be the behemoths that seemed so invincible in the past. Some of the best
stocks could be those that were largely ignored in the 1990s: foreign stocks, or stocks of small companies
that make boring things like cement and wallboard.
"There is nothing written in stone that General Electric, [still the world's largest company based on total
stock-market value] should outperform the market for the next 10 years," says Mr. Simon of Glenmede.
"People will start to treat big U.S. stocks as just another asset class."
He is advising clients to shift some of their money toward foreign stocks and smaller U.S. stocks. GE, for its
part, is down more than 45% in less than two years.
Little wonder that last week, ahead of Monday's Memorial Day holiday, the major indexes found it impossible
to extend their gains from the week before. With a decline of 1.1%, or 111.82 points, on Friday, the Dow
Jones Industrial Average finished at 10104.26, down 2.4%, or 248.82 points, for the week. The Nasdaq
Composite Index, heavy in tech stocks, fell 4.6% on the week.

--- Page 234 ---
Page 234 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Investors say the stock doldrums, which have knocked the industrial average down 14% since early 2000,
and knocked the Nasdaq down 67%, are just the most obvious reflection of how much the investment climate
has changed.
Consider what the inflation rate has done. Falling inflation, and the falling interest rates that accompanied
it, were the engine of the stock-market gains in the 1990s. Low rates helped investors borrow money cheaply,
refinance homes and put money into stocks. They helped business prosper. They fell so low that some
investment strategists began talking about the death of inflation. Consumer prices went from an annual gain
of more than 6% at the end of 1990 to less than 2% by late 1997. Now, they are rising again.
They aren't soaring, of course. Consumer-price inflation still is below 2% and few economists think it is going
to become anything close to the problem it was in the 1970s. But with the unsettled world situation pumping
up both oil prices and the government deficit, with the dollar falling and trade tensions building, "the cyclical
disinflation that the economy has been enjoying for most of the last decade may be over," wrote Richard
Nash, chief investment strategist at Victory Capital Management, the money-management arm of Keycorp in
Cleveland, in a recent report to clients. "This is not to suggest that the economy will be returning to
1970s-style, or even troubling, levels of inflation soon, but the very favorable inflation environment seems to
be nearing an end."
A similar story is being told by the government surplus, by interest rates and by geopolitics. The yield of the
10-year Treasury note, an interest-rate benchmark, fell from 9.6% in 1987 to 4.2% this past October. Now it is
back above 5%. With the federal government planning to issue more bonds to finance its deficits, it would
take a truly weak economy to keep interest rates from rising now. (A few economists do forecast deflation
and a double-dip recession, which would knock interest rates back down, but that hardly would help stocks.)
None of this means that investors should abandon stocks. It doesn't mean there won't be sharp stock rallies.
Stocks, in fact, have had volatile ups and downs in recent months. But with all the volatility, the broad indexes
have had trouble sustaining gains. A fear is spreading among investors that the glory days for U.S. stocks
may not return anytime soon. And even when they start rising again, they may not regain their former luster.
"Most people I run into are just bored with the stock market right now," says Larry Baumgartner, a fund
manager at Banc One Investment Advisors, Bank One's money-management group in Columbus, Ohio.
"They are so beaten up that they don't want to hear about value stocks or growth stocks" or any other kind of
stocks, he says.
The mood of the market may best be captured in the price of gold. From late 1987 through the summer of
1999, as the bull market swaggered ahead, the price of gold fell almost in half, from $492 an ounce to about
$255. In those days, you had to be out of your mind to own gold when you could own stocks.
No more. Today, the price of gold is back above $317.
---
Friday's Market Activity
Sun Microystems' shares fell 7.4%, or 55 cents, to $6.86 in Nasdaq trading. The computer hardware and
software developer confirmed fiscal fourth-quarter forecasts in an outlook released late Thursday, in which it
promised again to show a profit for the period, but also acknowledged that orders haven't been coming in at
the pace seen last quarter.
Shares of other computer makers also weakened in the session. International Business Machines declined
1.25, or 1.5%, to 83.10. Oracle lost 52 cents, or 5.5%, to 8.85 on Nasdaq.
Biotechnology concern Biogen jumped 23%, or 9.11, to 49.47 on Nasdaq. A U.S. Food and Drug
Administration panel voted to recommend approval of the company's psoriasis drug Amevive. The panel's
decision is being viewed as a litmus test of the FDA's willingness to approve novel medications despite some
safety concerns.
-- Robert O'Brien

Ahead of the Tape
Ahead of the Tape
Ahead of the Tape
By Jesse Eisinger
427 words
22 May 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
[Today's Market Forecast]
Solid Gold
Gold, loved by pinkie-ring advocates but generally mocked by all but a few investors, has been staging a
substantial rally for more than a year. Can it be that the metal's bear market that has persisted since the early
1980s is over?
Well, gold at $800 an ounce -- the level hit back then -- isn't coming back soon. But gold might actually be
able to stay over $300 an ounce for a while. Arnhold and S. Bleichroeder's technical analyst John Roque,
who has been bullish on gold since late October, has a $340 an ounce target, though he thinks there are few
gold-company stocks that haven't moved up already. Gold yesterday traded at $315.80 an ounce, up more
than 13% this year.
The cliche is that gold is a "store of value." That means its price reflects investor jitters about stocks, bonds,
cash and currencies. To all but ardent gold diggers, the rising price can seem fairly nonsensical. Gold is
valuable because it always has been valuable and people agree all over the world that it is valuable. Yet
governments don't back their currencies with gold anymore and central banks in Europe have too much and
are perennial sellers. And industrial uses of gold are few.
For the skeptics, then, it might be worth simply considering that along with a weakening dollar, rising
long-term interest rates and a widening spread between government bonds and inflation-protected bonds, a
rising price of gold signals that investors expect inflation to come back. That should make even hardened
doubters pay attention.
View of nVidia
Today, nVidia, a chip maker that doubles as a lightning rod for short sellers, reports results that should look
strong. Be careful.
The company is still under the cloud of an SEC accounting probe. And investors are worried about its
exposure as a supplier of chips for Microsoft's Xbox, which has had disappointing sales. Sales to Microsoft,
which just lowered the price of its console, may have topped out. Even if they haven't, Microsoft might be able
to cut how much it pays for the chips.
Moreover, investors may want to look for more detail about nVidia's sales to middlemen distributors in Asia.
Skeptics say that the sales volumes from the region don't match demand. While nVidia has said everything is
fine, clearer disclosure would help.
---
Questions and comments should be sent to tape@wsj.com.

Thinking Things Over:Meanderings on Money
Thinking Things Over: Meanderings on Money
By Robert L. Bartley
973 words
20 May 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
A21
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Crosscurrents grip the current economy, frustrating its efforts to forge a clear theme. U.S. growth has
resumed after a short recession, but is backing off in the current quarter. The Federal Reserve keeps its
target rate at a historic low, without any evident effect on other interest rates.
The stock market has been trying to rally, but somehow no one's convinced. A split on the inflation outlook
emerges between top forecasters, and between the Fed and the European Central Bank. American
manufacturers whine about the high dollar, while the Bank of Japan doesn't like the yen recovery. What's this
all about, anyway?
Well, mostly it's about the mystery of monetary policy. The Fed cut its target federal-funds rate 11 times last
year, and this month kept it at a 40-year low of 1.75% But the benchmark 10-year bond rate has marched off
in another direction. The conventional wisdom expects the Fed to control bigger things such as production
and employment, that is, the real economy. But if it can't even control financial-sector benchmarks, how can it
affect the real sector?
As it happens, Alan Greenspan addressed this question in Senate Banking Committee testimony last year.
He said monetary policy may affect the real economy through different channels at different times, "But at
the end of the day it does seem to be effective."
Most of these channels rely on interest rate changes, however, to judge by a New York Federal Reserve
conference in April 2001. A summary by two Fed economists says, "The overall conclusion drawn from the
research presented is that monetary policy appears to have less of an impact on real activity than it once
had -- but the cause of that change remains an open issue."
The most prominent channel not focused on interest rates was "what might be described as a monetarist
channel." In popular terms, if the Fed creates new money, it has to go somewhere. Interestingly, the
monetary aggregates -- a monetarist economist's beloved money supply numbers, M1, M2 and so on -- do
not move in lockstep with the fed-funds target. They accelerated smartly until about year-end and have
leveled off since, a pattern consistent with recovery from recession and the more recent slowing.
But when the Fed's Open Market Committee held the target rate on at its May meeting, the next day the stock
market took off 300 points. After trading good and bad days, the Dow closed Friday up about 545 points from
where it was before the May 7 meeting. It's either saying that the real economy is going to accelerate further,
or that the Fed is creating liquidity that will flow into stock prices.
Inflationary dangers limit the Fed in trying to stimulate the real sector. A burst of money creation may give the
economy a temporary boost, but the fundamental long-term effect is on prices. And the real stimulus doesn't
work once the public picks up inflationary expectations. So the possibility of monetary stimulus turns
importantly on the inflation outlook.
Just before the FOMC meeting, Brian Wesbury, the top economic forecaster in this newspaper's 2001 survey,
made the case for an inflationary danger. He urged the Fed to start raising its target rate, pointing to the rising
price of gold, the dollar weakening from its previous heights, rising commodities, and the extraordinary
spread between the fed-funds target and longer interest rates. All are inflation warnings.
Wim Duisenberg, president of the European Central Bank, voiced similar concerns last week. Speaking after
German metal workers won a generous settlement with a series of one-day strikes, he said wage trends and
other indicators left the bank "less than entirely satisfied about recent price developments."
Others write off any near-term inflation danger. Ed Hyman, who also has a couple of victories in the WSJ
forecasting survey, says that you don't have inflation during recoveries, that producing more goods is not
inflationary. Fed officials point to the consumer price increase of only 1.4% in the last year, and similarly slow

--- Page 242 ---
Page 242 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
growth in more sophisticated indexes. New York Fed President William McDonough recently asked, "If that's
not price stability, what is?"
This difference reflects different conceptions of the central banking mission. The European Central Bank
sees its role as providing price stability; real sector stimulus is someone else's job. Mr. Wesbury's view draws
from the experience of the 1970s and 1980s, which led to the "supply-side" policy mix assigning price stability
to the Fed and stimulus to incentive tax cuts. The Federal Reserve is directed by statute to aim not only at
price stability but also at high employment.
If these crosscurrents are not complicated enough, there is also the international component. The foreign
exchange value of the dollar is also an artifact of monetary policy. When manufacturers ask for a lower
dollar, they are in effect asking for more inflation. Writing here recently, economist John Makin asked for
both a lower dollar and lower interest rates in Europe and Japan -- which translates into a heady
combination of an acceleration of money growth by foreign central banks and an even larger acceleration by
the Fed.
These meanderings are meant to suggest that monetary policy is a tricky tool. It can be powerful, but its
effects are complicated. I suspect our recent boom-and-bust experience already reflects an overreliance on
monetary policy to manage growth. Fiscal, regulatory and trade policies have pursued other goals. The
federal spending boom, resistance to incentive tax cuts, state tax boosts and creeping protectionism create a
climate not conducive to growth. It's a mistake to believe it can be overcome by heroics at the Federal
Reserve.

Page 243 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Keep the Dollar Strong
Keep the Dollar Strong
By Arthur B. Laffer
1,108 words
20 May 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
A20
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
With the U.S. unemployment rate hitting the 6% mark -- the highest rate since the early 1990s -- the stock
market having declined some 30% over the past two years, and an election looming large this November, the
value of the dollar is moving center stage in policy debates. The basic issue is whether the U.S. economy is
benefited or hurt by the extraordinary strength of the U.S. dollar in the foreign exchanges.
Even though the dollar has backed off a little bit from its recent highs, the U.S. trade deficit is enormous by
any standard. Weak-dollar advocates argue that the paucity of exports and the surfeit of imports are finally
wreaking havoc on our economy. In fact, playing a game of politics unseen in the U.S. for many, many years,
President Bush has imposed tariffs on imported steel and lumber. And, he turned protectionist all in the name
of saving American jobs and the economy. Gadzooks! If there were one lesson economists had taught
politicians, I would have said it was the absolute and inviolable benefits of free trade. Guess not.
But the question still remains, Which direction of the dollar is best for the U.S.? For my part, I think policies
that make for a strong dollar are great for the economy, and those policies that weaken the dollar are bad.
In the 1970s the dollar was weak because of a breakdown of the Bretton Woods fixed exchange rate
gold-exchange standard, profligate monetary policy, and high inflation. Paul Volcker, an ardent foe of the
U.S. "going off gold," in 1971 reversed the profligate monetary policy and, as a consequence, stopped
inflation dead in its tracks and started the reestablishment of credibility for the dollar. A strong dollar and high
U.S. inflation are not compatible. The choice should have been simple, and to Mr. Volcker it was.
Once a weak dollar catches hold, it tends to be reinforcing. Global currency holders tried to sell dollars and
buy stronger currencies. This further weakens the dollar and strengthens those already strong. In economics,
the phenomenon is called the "substitution of monies in demand," but in common parlance it's called "hot
money," a "run on the currency," or "capital flight." Mr. Volcker had his work cut out for him, but he was up to
the task.
That set the stage for a monumental turnaround in America's economic fortunes. In 1978, after a decade of
inflationary socialism and negative real after-tax returns on capital, the U.S. turned to a new paradigm. Mr.
Volcker reestablished a sound dollar, Howard Jarvis, Paul Gann and Ronald Reagan gave us real tax rate
cuts, and the president also ended price controls, held unions accountable for their actions, and rebuilt
America's defenses. After-tax returns soared, and everyone wanted to invest in America -- foreigners and
citizens alike. The strong-dollar decision was absolutely crucial to this recovery because exchange rates are
woven deeply into all aspects of the fabrics of economics.
Americans invest in America merely by accumulating capital. But foreigners can only invest in America by
moving goods to the U.S. in exchange for claims on U.S.-located assets. More simply, higher returns in the
U.S. induced foreigners and Americans to move capital to the U.S., causing an international capital surplus
for the U.S. A larger U.S. capital surplus is one and the same as a larger trade deficit. It's as simple as that.
But the micro-mechanism that induces Americans to buy more foreign goods and foreigners to buy less
American goods, thereby causing a larger trade deficit, is a change in the terms of trade, often called the real
exchange rate. A stronger dollar in the foreign exchange markets not offset by changes in foreign and
domestic prices will make U.S. goods less competitive. In this case, a stronger dollar means that we'll import
more and export less, all because good policies in the U.S. attracted foreign investors.
Immersed in a world of disbelievers (save Margaret Thatcher, of course), America's policies of the 1980s led
to a huge capital inflow into the U.S. and a strong, strong dollar. But with President Reagan's term coming to
an end, misgivings about new Fed Chairman Alan Greenspan, the original President Bush, and the early
Clinton administration reversed much of the optimism, and the dollar fell.

--- Page 244 ---
Page 244 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
But with the Democratic election catastrophe of 1994, Mr. Greenspan's settling into his own groove, and the
conversion of Mr. Clinton to pro-growth conservativism (only in economics, mind you), the U.S. once again
was the place to be. Japan was an economic disaster followed closely by almost every other country. Capital
moved -- and I do mean literally moved -- to the U.S. The euro, yen, and almost every other currency
collapsed as we sucked capital out of every corner of the globe. The dollar was rising and the U.S. trade
deficit was enormous. America prospered, unemployment tumbled, and asset values soared. It doesn't get
any better.
Now things have changed. The tech bubble, and every other asset bubble, has burst. The U.S. is engaged in
a global conflict without an obvious conclusion. State and local governments are raising taxes, President
Bush is imposing tariffs, and Mr. Greenspan's grip seems to be loosening. The U.S. just isn't the same as it
was under President Reagan or even President Clinton.
The rest of the world has changed too. Prime Minister Junichiro Koizumi of Japan is a huge improvement
over his predecessor, Yoshiro Mori. Germany is cutting taxes, Italy has moved radically toward free markets,
as have Spain and Ireland. With the latest election results in Portugal, Germany, and even France, more
change is in the offing. The rest of the world is definitely getting better -- and we are not.
Higher returns abroad and lower returns in the U.S. mean reduced U.S. capital inflows, lower U.S. trade
deficits, and, yes, a weaker dollar. If the U.S. wishes to maintain our leadership role in the world economy,
we've got to proceed undaunted in our pro-growth agenda. Just talking about a strong dollar won't cut it. In
the words of Nobel Prize-winning economist Robert Mundell, we need tight money and tax cuts -- and then
we'll have prosperity, asset appreciation, employment growth, and a strong dollar.
---
Mr. Laffer is founder and chairman of Laffer Associates, an economic research and consulting firm based in
San Diego.

Deals & Deal Makers:Bids & Offers
Deals & Deal Makers: Bids & Offers
765 words
17 May 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
C5
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
[Inside the World of Corporate Finance & Wall Street]
Duncan's Doughnuts
Call him a felon. Call him a turncoat. But don't call David Duncan cheap.
Testifying this week as the government's star witness in Arthur Andersen LLP's criminal trial, the former lead
partner on Enron Corp.'s audits said his most recent hourly billing rates at the firm ranged from $450 to $750.
Nearly all his work in recent years was for Enron. Tom Bauer, another partner on the Enron account, charged
about the same. Mr. Duncan earned more than $1 million a year at Andersen.
Andersen's billings for work by lower-rung partners and managers would make most New York City lawyers
blush as well. Hourly billing rates for work by Deb Cash and Roger Willard ranged from about $400 to $600,
Mr. Duncan said. Rates for managers on the Enron audit went from about $200 to $400 an hour. Meanwhile,
hourly rates for staff accountants ranged from $125 to $175, Mr. Duncan said.
Beaned
Having struck out in the corporate world, Arthur Andersen now has been knocked down by America's
pastime. Major League Baseball yesterday yanked the troubled accounting firm out of its lineup, where it had
been employed in an important "umpiring" role as far as memorabilia collectors are concerned: the official
witness to all the league's authorized autograph signings and to the removal of game-used items to
guarantee they are authentic. Andersen was replaced in this role by rival Deloitte & Touche LLP.
Tim Brosnan, executive vice president of business for Major League Baseball, said the switch had nothing to
do with Andersen's current troubles. Having had 108,000 items authenticated last year, the league needed to
step up its resources and opened it up to rival offers. "Everybody jumped into the bidding," he said. A
spokesman for Andersen, which had provided the service since the program was set up in January 2001,
declined to comment.
Separately, as Andersen's public-audit client base continues to melt away, Friendly Ice Cream Corp. was
among those yesterday to say they had dropped the firm as its auditor. The company, whose stock trades on
the American Stock Exchange, had used Andersen since 1972. Its new flavor is Ernst & Young.
Bankrupt Justice
The past year has been a bumper one for at least one group: bankruptcy judges.
With corporate scandals and the financial pressures on corporations and regular people, the number of
bankruptcy petitions filed in federal courts rose 15% in 12 months ended March 31. That breaks the record for
any one-year period, according to the Administrative Office of the U.S. Courts. During the period, 1,504,806
petitions were filed, 39,845 by businesses and 1,464,961 of the personal variety.
No new bankruptcy judgeships have been created since 1992, though a bankruptcy bill now stalled in
Congress would authorize 27 new ones. Meantime, current judges will have to shoulder ballooning
caseloads. New filings per judge rose to an average of 4,644 in the 12 months ended in March, compared
with 2,965 a decade ago.
NAV? Wham!
Spider-Man was already taken.

--- Page 246 ---
Page 246 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
A small mutual-fund company has turned to creating a superhero and publishing its own comic book to sell its
investment strategy. Kelmoore Investment Co., of Palo Alto, Calif., felt it needed a fresh approach to get
investors' attention when explaining its sophisticated stock-option investment approach.
"People's eyes were glazing over" with the standard brochures, said Jill Paiz, a spokeswoman for the fund
firm. "We wanted to make it a little more fun."
It's not a totally new idea: In the past, other financial institutions have similarly gone the comic-book route,
such as the Federal Reserve Bank of New York's comics that explain the mysteries of economics and
monetary policy.
Kelmoore is sending around 10,000 copies of a 16-page comic book featuring "Kaptain Kelmoore," a
blue-and-gold-clad mascot who flies around in tights and a mask. In the book, he doesn't have to save the
world, but merely explain how "call" and "put" options work.
"Fear not, Citizen Investor! For I, Kaptain Kelmoore, defender of truth, justice and a balanced portfolio, am
here to save you!" he tells one confused investor. Perhaps in the next issue, he'll explain why Kelmoore
Strategy Fund has fallen 23% in the past 12 months.

NEW YORK -- In a day of givebacks, small-cap stocks fell somewhere between blue chips and large tech
shares, although nobody lost more than they gained during Wednesday's monster rally.
Smaller stocks fell a shade more than the Dow Jones Industrial Average but fared better than big technology
issues. Being sandwiched is an unusual position for small caps; this year they have spent more time on the
outside, with a clearer path to adding gains.
But that has changed in the past couple of weeks, ever since the Standard & Poor's 600 SmallCap Index hit a
record high in mid-April. The question is whether small stocks are taking a pause that will refresh and allow
them to move even higher or beginning what technical analysts call a topping pattern, in which shares spend
some time near their highs but then start pulling back.
Yesterday it was a time for pulling back with the Russell 2000 Index of small-cap stocks falling 8.36 points, or
1.64%, to 501.39 and the Standard & Poor's SmallCap 600 Index dropping 3.79, or 1.49%, to 250.84. The
Nasdaq Composite Index slipped 45.80 points, or 2.70%, to 1650.49.
The unusual softness for small caps is on the minds of money managers who acknowledge it is becoming
harder to find good stocks at decent prices. But just as many small-cap strategists believe there is more room
to go and that big-cap investors remain much more challenged.
"You have your Ciscos, but then it's a new day with more news," said Tom Philips, chief investment officer at
Paradigm Asset Management, which invests money on behalf of small-cap investors. "The news today was
that Wal-Mart and other retailers had disappointing sales, which is largely a big-cap issue."
Also, during the day, word hit that International Business Machines, another big-cap stock, was planning
another round of layoffs in its U.S. operations.
Still, there are some events that send jitters through everyone. That happened yesterday, when news that
Federal Reserve members, including Chairman Alan Greenspan, were sent mail containing anthrax spores.
The letters were found at an off-site screening center. But stocks plunged on the news.

The Flip Side:Inflation Subdued, Top HawkAt Fed Frets Over the Opposite---Mr. Broaddus Helped ReachGoal of `Price Stability';But Is Deflat...
The Flip Side: Inflation Subdued, Top Hawk At Fed Frets Over the Opposite --- Mr. Broaddus Helped
Reach Goal of `Price Stability'; But Is Deflation a Danger? --- Depressing Lesson of Japan
By Greg Ip
2,848 words
8 May 2002
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
(Copyright (c) 2002, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
As an inflation fighter, J. Alfred Broaddus Jr. has defined the hard line. After becoming president of the
Federal Reserve Bank of Richmond in 1993, he preached tirelessly about the damage that inflation could do
even at the historically low rate of 3%. At Fed policy meetings, he dissented six times in favor of higher
interest rates, a record unbeaten by any Fed official in the past decade. The retired tennis pro who lives
across the street from Mr. Broaddus took to calling him "the hawk."
But more recently, his thinking has changed. The remarkable mix of low unemployment and low inflation in
the U.S. in the late 1990s and an unsettling trip to Japan have transformed Mr. Broaddus. After last fall's
terrorist attacks, he worried more about the potential harm of deflation, which has hobbled Japan, and helped
lead the charge to the lowest interest rates in the U.S. in 40 years.
Mr. Broaddus's conversion is part of a major shift in the thinking of many Federal Reserve officials, with
profound implications for the economy. They believe the Fed finally has achieved the elusive state of "price
stability." That's a central bankers' nirvana, in which inflation is so low that it doesn't figure in business or
consumer decisions, and their behavior thus helps reinforce steady prices over time.
That frees the Fed to be a more aggressive recession fighter. It can respond more rapidly to economic
weakness, as it did with last year's 11 interest-rate cuts, without fear of fueling inflation. And it can keep
rates low longer, as it is doing now. Even though the recession appears to be over, Fed officials kept their key
short-term interest-rate target at 1.75% at their meeting yesterday, citing continued uncertainty about the
strength of business and consumer spending.
There is a downside to price stability: The Fed must be equally vigilant against threats not just of inflation,
but also of deflation, when prices and wages fall, severely hampering a central bank's ability to stimulate the
economy. It would be "ironic to have fought all this time to bring the inflation rate down . . . and then lose
price stability on the down side," Mr. Broaddus said in a recent interview.
But the U.S. economy would have to be in much worse shape than it is now for deflation to become a real
threat. Inflation, meanwhile, isn't zero, but it is low. The consumer price index has climbed 1.4% over the
past year. The Fed's preferred inflation measure, an index of personal consumption expenditures, rose just
0.6% in the first quarter from a year earlier, the slowest gain since 1961. Excluding energy and food, it was up
only 1.2%, the slowest since 1965.
"If that's not price stability, what is?" New York Fed President William McDonough said recently. Fed
Chairman Alan Greenspan hasn't publicly declared victory, but in previous testimony he has indicated he
believes the U.S. is close to and perhaps at price stability.
Not everyone at the Fed agrees. "Price stability means you don't have inflation, and we have some modest
inflation by any measure," Kansas City Fed President Thomas Hoenig said in a recent interview, though he
added that inflation below 2% is "very acceptable." A few private economists are warning against
complacency, pointing to the falling dollar, rising gold prices and relatively brisk demand for the Treasury's
inflation-adjusted bonds as inflation storm warnings.
Since its creation in 1913, the Fed has simultaneously achieved stable prices and healthy economic growth
only twice, from 1922 to 1929 and again from 1959 to 1964, according to Allan Meltzer, a Carnegie Mellon
University economist who is writing a history of the Fed. Neither ended happily, though price stability wasn't
the cause. The first was followed by deflation and Depression. The second was followed by inflation stoked
by Vietnam War spending, which resulted in wage and price controls.
This generation's victory over inflation came gradually. The conviction grew first among central bankers here
and abroad -- and later, the politicians who appoint them -- that it was worth some short-term pain such as

--- Page 252 ---
Page 252 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
temporarily higher unemployment to achieve the long-run benefits of low inflation, such as better-functioning
markets and milder recessions. So the Fed cracked down early and hard when price pressures appeared.
Each time it did so, it nudged inflation down a bit more.
In the late 1990s, the battle against inflation got a big boost from faster productivity growth. Businesses
could produce more per worker, pay higher wages and earn higher profits, all without boosting prices.
Downward pressure on prices from deregulation and the Asian financial crisis contributed, too.
Mr. Broaddus agrees that all those factors contributed, but he says something else was critical: The Fed's
credibility, in tatters in the 1970s, had been restored with the central bank's successful pre-emptive strike at
inflation in 1994-95. Now that the public expected the Fed to keep inflation low, it behaved accordingly
when buying, selling and negotiating wages, anchoring inflation expectations at a low level.
Mr. Broaddus's anti-inflationary zeal is rooted in the lessons of the 1970s and 1980s, as well as local and
personal influences. His predecessor at the Richmond Fed was known as a hawk on inflation. There's also a
moral imperative at work, a "sense that ongoing inflation is dishonest, a way the government taxes the public
without representation," Mr. Broaddus says.
Mr. Broaddus's mother was an English teacher and his father a dentist who gave up practicing after he
became partially paralyzed when Mr. Broaddus was a child. He earned a Ph.D. in economics from Indiana
University on scholarship, and joined the staff of the Richmond Fed in 1970. The job gave him a ringside seat
during the great inflation of the late 1970s. By 1980, the inflation rate had risen to 13%, prompting Fed
Chairman Paul Volcker to raise interest rates sharply and drive the economy into a deep recession.
It wasn't a good time to be a central banker, even a low-level one. Mr. Broaddus recalls a speech he gave to
Virginia homebuilders, during which some audience members carried one-foot lengths of two-by-fours to
protest the damage high interest rates had inflicted on their industry. Mr. Broaddus also thought the Fed
shared responsibility for the economic pain -- not for having raised rates, but for having failed to keep
inflation in check in the first place.
"What was driving my thinking about policy ever after was: We don't ever want to go through that again," Mr.
Broaddus says.
Fed officials generally dislike being labeled "hawk" or "dove." Being pigeonholed can diminish one's influence
with different-minded colleagues. But Mr. Broaddus didn't shy away. In one speech pointing to faint signs of
inflation, he said: "It is a hawk's job to point out developments like these."
As he rose through the Richmond research department, which had traditionally specialized in public
information, banking and monitoring economic developments, Mr. Broaddus recruited Ph.D.s specializing in
monetary economics to give the bank more clout on monetary-policy matters. Among them was Marvin
Goodfriend, who after joining in 1978 became Mr. Broaddus's closest adviser and a key influence in his
recent change in thinking.
When Mr. Broaddus became the Richmond Fed's sixth president, he got a seat on the committee of
Washington-based Fed governors and regional bank presidents who set monetary policy. And every third
year, he got a vote. All 12 presidents participate in the discussions, but only five have votes at any one time:
the New York Fed president plus four of the others, who serve in rotation.
Mr. Broaddus, now 62 and still an avid runner, stands out among Fed colleagues for his plain-spoken and
self-deprecating manner. Talking about tight labor markets at one Fed meeting, he said he had seen a
want-ad for truck drivers on the mudflap of a tractor-trailer on Interstate 95 the previous day. "I have the
number if anybody would like it," he quipped.
What he talked about most was the need for tougher action against inflation. In May 1993, at only his third
meeting as a policy-maker, Mr. Broaddus pressed for an interest-rate increase, arguing, "In matters of
credibility, there is no substitute for action," according to a transcript. Mr. Greenspan and others disagreed.
The following year, the Fed did begin to raise rates. Mr. Broaddus now had a vote, and he promptly began
using it to push for even faster interest-rate increases to pre-empt an outbreak of inflation. Although the
tradition at the Fed favors unanimity, he dissented from the consensus three times that year. "I don't know
what the curve looks like . . . but I think we are still behind it," he warned in September 1994, Fed transcripts
show.
By spring 1995, the Fed had doubled its key interest rate to 6%. The economy slowed, and inflation stayed
near 3%. Mr. Broaddus wasn't satisfied. He argued that the Fed should set firm inflation targets to show it
was serious about achieving price stability, as the European Central Bank and the Bank of England have
done. He lost. When his turn came to vote in 1997, he dissented three more times, each time wanting higher
interest rates than his colleagues. Only Cleveland Fed President Jerry Jordan has dissented as often since

--- Page 253 ---
Page 253 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
1993, according to the Financial Markets Center, a research group in Philomont, Va., that critiques Fed
policy.
Between 1993 and 1998, the Richmond Fed also registered its anti-inflation zeal by submitting 68 requests
to raise the discount rate -- the largely symbolic rate the Fed charges on loans it makes to commercial banks.
Though discount-rate changes must be approved by the Federal Reserve Board, an individual bank's
requests serve to register its views on monetary policy. No other Fed bank was more persistent in its
demands for tighter policy, also according to the Financial Markets Center.
But in 1999, Mr. Broaddus began to realize that something had changed. The economy had been growing 4%
a year, well above what he and most economists thought it could sustain without fueling inflation. Yet
inflation kept edging lower. "The economy was really roaring. The unemployment rate dipped briefly below
4%, I'm thinking, `Got to go out and start looking for some inflation expectations out there.' And I couldn't find
any," Mr. Broaddus says.
Bond yields are Mr. Broaddus's preferred measure of inflation fears because bondholders are so sensitive to
how inflation can erode the value of the fixed income they earn on their investments. Rising yields suggest
expectations of higher inflation. Yields weren't rising nearly as much as they had in 1993-94.
Business executives told him repeatedly: We can't raise prices. One was Jeremiah Sheehan, then chief
executive of Richmond-based Reynolds Metals Co. and a member of the Richmond Fed board. He says the
late 1990s was the first boom he has lived through that gave so little scope to raise prices. "We'd go to each
customer and announce a price increase, and get substantial resistance," he says. A customer would
threaten to switch to another supplier, or "come to us and say, `We have a [lower] offer from someone -- do
you want to meet it?' And you'd typically meet it." But while ample global capacity hampered his industry's
pricing power, rising productivity also helped it hold down costs.
The economy of 1999 and early 2000 was the watershed for Mr. Broaddus. "The recognition that we had
achieved price stability came not with a bang but a whimper," he says.
But after that recognition, he then realized that the war on inflation could go too far. That sprang largely from
conversations with Mr. Goodfriend, who with other Fed officials had been studying the challenges low
inflation and deflation could pose, and the deflation then hobbling Japan.
In a normal economy, the central bank stimulates spending by cutting the "real" cost of borrowing, which is
the interest rate minus the inflation rate. It may even drive interest rates below inflation, to create a
negative real interest rate. That encourages consumers and businesses to borrow, on the belief that as their
incomes grow with inflation, their real debt burden will decline.
But in a weak economy such as Japan's through parts of the 1990s, the inflation rate can fall faster than the
central bank cuts interest rates. That translates into rising real interest rates, which curtails the spending
the economy needs. And if inflation falls to zero or, worse, turns to deflation, the central bank loses the
ability to use interest rates, since it can't cut rates below zero. Then, as incomes and sales fall along with
prices, debt burdens climb, triggering bankruptcies and spending cutbacks, and a deflationary spiral is born.
In July 2000, Mr. Goodfriend was invited to address a Bank of Japan conference on the problems for
monetary policy in periods of low inflation and deflation. Mr. Broaddus tagged along. There, foreign central
bankers and academics urged the Bank of Japan to conquer deflation by using the drastic and largely untried
step of printing yen to buy government bonds or dollars. Japanese officials rejected the advice. All agreed
Japan faced problems with little precedent. "I'm glad I'm not a Japanese central banker," Donald Kohn, then
Mr. Greenspan's top adviser, said at the end of the meetings.
Mr. Broaddus said little. But he remembers thinking: "It would be absolutely a tragedy if we didn't learn from
this experience."
The opportunity came quickly. When the Fed realized that the U.S. economy was slowing sharply in 2001, it
began to slash interest rates. At first, it wasn't worried about Japanese-style deflation. But it also wasn't
worried about inflation -- the first time in almost 50 years that it went into a recession unburdened by such
worries, according to an analysis by St. Louis Fed President William Poole.
Then came Sept. 11. The attacks seemed certain to hurt the economy. In October, the U.S. suffered its worst
one-month job loss in 21 years. With inflation and the federal-funds rate already low, the possibility of living
Japan's economic nightmare was no longer an academic discussion. Mr. Broaddus didn't think deflation was
imminent, but he didn't want to take a chance. Better for the Fed to fire as much ammo right away, rather
than hold back and risk deflation, which could render its interest-rate weapons useless.
On Nov. 5, three days after the October jobs report, Mr. Broaddus convened a special telephone meeting of
the Richmond Fed's directors, who are charged with recommending discount-rate changes to the Fed board

--- Page 254 ---
Page 254 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
in Washington. The Fed's anti-inflation credibility was no longer in question. The threat, Mr. Broaddus told
his board, was falling inflation and perhaps deflation.
"If ever there was a time to use our credibility, this is it," he told them. At his urging, the directors sent a
request to Fed headquarters in Washington for a half-percentage-point cut in the discount rate, the only one
of the 12 regional reserve banks to do so. The next day, the Fed's policy-making committee obliged, slashing
both the federal-funds and discount rates to levels below any in Mr. Broaddus's Fed career.
It was the Fed's third half-percentage-point cut in rates in the seven weeks since the attacks, and the cuts
yielded an immediate benefit. Jim Lowry, president of High Point Chevrolet Jeep LLC in High Point, N.C., and
one of Mr. Broaddus's business contacts, had seen sales plunge to fewer than 10 vehicles a week after the
attacks from 30 to 40 before. But after auto makers announced zero-interest financing, traffic rebounded to its
old levels. "The Fed's rate cuts combined with manufacturers taking advantage of those rates, has enabled
us to further stimulate the economy and drive people back into the show rooms," Mr. Lowry later reported to
Mr. Broaddus.
That a die-hard inflation hawk such as Mr. Broaddus now talks of the dangers of inflation going too low has
raised eyebrows both inside and outside the Fed. One fellow policy maker has started referring to him as the
"leading dove," Mr. Broaddus says.
Yet people who have watched Mr. Broaddus consider the apparent change consistent with his commitment to
price stability, which means battling both inflation and deflation -- and blurring old distinctions between
hawks and doves. "It's not a change of heart," says Mr. Meltzer, the Carnegie Mellon economist. "It's a
change of circumstance."
