Tech Industry's Woes Hit
Home in Certain Areas
The bursting of the technology bubble put the brakes on the home-price appreciation in tech-heavy metro
areas last year.
Not surprisingly, the San Francisco Bay area was hardest hit, according to the Federal Deposit Insurance
Corp. In San Jose, the heart of Silicon Valley, home prices jumped 24.4% in the first quarter of 2001 from a
year earlier. By the fourth quarter, year-over-year appreciation dove to 0.6%. Nearby Santa Cruz saw the rate
of appreciation dip to 5.9% from 25.7% between the first and fourth quarters, while the runup in San
Francisco slowed to 3.5% from 19.4%. Nationally, the annual rate of home appreciation slowed to 6.9% from
9.6%.
Northern California wasn't the only region to see marked softening in the home prices. In Austin, Texas, the
home-price appreciation rate dropped to 5% from 15.2% between the beginning and end of last year.
Lewiston-Auburn, Maine, experienced a slowdown to 7.1% from 14%, while price gains in Fargo, N.D.,
slipped to 4.6% from 11.1%.
The areas all shared sharp drops in employment. Austin saw job growth dip to 0.4% last year after growing at
annual average rates of 5.9% from 1998 to 2000, according to the FDIC. Employment in San Jose shrank by
1.3% after growing at annual average rates of 3.6% the previous three years.
But in the Bay area at least, says Kenneth Rosen, a real-estate professor at the University of California at
Berkeley, home prices are already rebounding as interest rates remain low and the economy begins to
recover.
-- Robert Gavin
Big Lobbyist
The insurance industry has more trade groups and businesses registered to lobby in the 50 state capitals
than any other industry. With 2,269 registered lobby groups, insurers top the health-services industry's 1,870
and educators' 1,859, according to a new report from the Center for Public Integrity, a Washington political
watchdog group. The figures reflect the number of lobby groups registered in 2000. Each group could employ
more than one lobbyist.
That the insurance industry has the most groups trying to be heard in state houses shouldn't come as a
surprise. States have almost the entire responsibility for regulating the industry.
Texas' 222 registered insurance lobbying groups are twice as many as second-place Florida. Why so many in
Texas? The state still regulates what insurance companies can charge while most other states allow
companies to set their own rates in a competitive market, says Jerry Johns, president of the Southwestern
Insurance Information Service, an Austin trade group of property and casualty insurers. Another reason:
Texas is in the midst of trying to determine how to handle homeowners' insurance claims of mold damage -- a
standard that other states may follow.
Texas also had the most energy-industry lobbying groups. California had the most registered lobby groups in
most other major industries, including health care, real estate, gambling, education and agriculture. Illinois
had the most banking-industry lobbying groups.
-- Russell Gold
Deregulation at Work

--- Page 256 ---
Page 256 of 256 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
With electricity deregulation stalling for lack of competition in many states, large manufacturers in Maryland
are finding a pleasant surprise as they shop for new power supplies: Bids are plentiful and prices are coming
in close to the government-regulated rates companies are now paying for electricity.
Under Maryland's 1999 deregulation law, rates for various customers were frozen for different periods to
allow for a gradual transition to a more competitive market. The rate freeze for large manufacturers expires
June 30. In the Baltimore area, where most of the state's manufacturers are based, industrial concerns report
getting prices that are within 6%, plus or minus, of the current frozen rates of about four cents a kilowatt hour.
"That is much better than we thought it was going to be," says Michael Powell, an attorney who represents 26
large industrial companies in the Baltimore area. Moreover, many of Mr. Powell's clients are receiving as
many as four bids from suppliers, giving the buyers some bargaining room.
The picture isn't uniformly bright, however. Mr. Powell isn't sure whether the current prices reflect a more
competitive market or if energy companies are just hustling for business in the wake of the recession.
Moreover, most of the suppliers bidding for his customers are affiliated with the region's local utilities, rather
than independent brokers and suppliers that deregulation proponents say are crucial to forming competitive
electricity markets.
-- Andrew Caffrey
